Training — Quality training for all employees, specialized training for quality team members. Supplier Quality — Incoming inspection, supplier scorecards, certification requirements. Internal Audits — Audit schedule, auditor qualifications, audit checklist, reporting format. Continuous Improvement — Kaizen events, suggestion system, improvement targets. Management Review — Quarterly quality review with leadership.

**Expected output:** Quality management system framework with metrics, processes, and improvement mechanisms
**Pro tip:** Quality is not what happens when you inspect — it's what happens when you design processes that prevent defects

### 3.17 Innovation Management and Idea Pipeline
**Use case:** Build systems to generate, evaluate, and implement innovative ideas
**Best for:** Innovation leads, R&D, product teams

Act as an innovation management consultant. I need to build an innovation system for my organization.

Company: [describe business] | Industry: [your sector] | Current innovation: [ad-hoc/structured/none] | Team: [who participates in innovation] | Resources: [time and budget for innovation] | Goals: [new products, process improvements, cost reduction] | Culture: [risk tolerance and openness to new ideas]

Design an innovation management system. Idea Generation Sources — Structured inputs: customer feedback, competitor analysis, technology trends, employee suggestions, academic research, adjacent industries. Idea Collection — Central repository for ideas with standard submission format: problem, proposed solution, expected impact, resources needed. Evaluation Framework — Score ideas on: strategic alignment, feasibility, market potential, uniqueness, effort required. Stage-Gate Process — Define stages: Ideation > Concept > Prototype > Pilot > Launch with go/no-go gates between each. Resource Allocation — Set aside dedicated innovation budget (suggest 10-15% of R&D time for exploration). Innovation Types — Balance: incremental (improve existing), adjacent (extend to new areas), transformational (breakthrough). Failure Tolerance — Define acceptable failure rates by stage (expect 70%+ to fail in ideation, 30% in development). Recognition — Reward system for ideas submitted, developed, and successfully implemented. Review Cadence — Monthly review of idea pipeline, quarterly innovation review with leadership.

**Expected output:** Innovation management system with pipeline, evaluation, and resource allocation
**Pro tip:** Most companies kill innovation by requiring ROI certainty before experimentation — create a "safe to fail" budget for high-risk ideas

### 3.18 Corporate Communication Strategy
**Use case:** Develop internal and external communication strategies
**Best for:** Communications directors, PR teams, executives

Act as a corporate communications consultant. I need to develop a comprehensive communication strategy.

Organization: [describe company] | Size: [employees] | Industry: [sector] | Current communication: [what exists] | Challenges: [message inconsistency, silos, low engagement] | Stakeholders: [employees, customers, investors, media, regulators] | Goals: [alignment, engagement, reputation, transparency]

Design a corporate communication strategy. Brand Voice and Tone — Define communication principles: how the company speaks internally and externally. Audience-Specific messaging frameworks. Internal Communications — Channels (all-hands, Slack/Teams, email, intranet), cadence, content types. Employee engagement metrics. External Communications — Media relations approach, spokesperson guidelines, press release protocol, bylined articles. Crisis Communication — Protocol, holding statements, escalation chain (builds on crisis management framework). Executive Communication — CEO messaging framework, town hall format, internal blog cadence, visibility expectations. Change Communication — Framework for communicating organizational changes: why, what, how, when, who affected. Measurement — Track: reach, engagement, sentiment, message recall, behavior change. Content Calendar — Quarterly themes, monthly planning, weekly execution cadence.

**Expected output:** Corporate communication strategy covering internal, external, crisis, and executive comms
**Pro tip:** The most important communication in any organization is the answer to "what's changing and why do I care?"

### 3.19 Competitive Intelligence System
**Use case:** Build systematic competitive monitoring and analysis
**Best for:** Strategy teams, product managers, marketing

Act as a competitive intelligence specialist. I need to build a competitive intelligence system.

Company: [describe business] | Industry: [sector] | Key competitors: [list 3-7] | Competitive landscape: [fragmented/consolidated/emerging] | Current CI approach: [none/ad-hoc/structured] | Users of CI: [product, marketing, sales, strategy] | Resources: [available budget/tools]

Build a competitive intelligence system. Competitor Profiles — For each competitor: product, pricing, target market, funding, strengths, weaknesses, strategic direction. Monitoring Sources — Track: websites, social media, job postings, review sites, press releases, investor calls, conferences, product launches. Intelligence Categories — Product (features, roadmap, pricing), Marketing (messaging, campaigns, channels), Sales (positioning, objections), Talent (hiring patterns, leadership changes), Financial (funding, revenue, valuation). Analysis Framework — SWOT analysis, competitive positioning map, feature comparison matrix, Porter's Five Forces. Intelligence Cadence — Weekly alerts, monthly competitive brief, quarterly deep-dive on top competitor. Distribution — Who gets what: executive summary for leadership, detailed analysis for product/marketing, battle cards for sales. Tools — Recommend: Crayon, Klue, or DIY dashboard with RSS feeds and web monitoring. Battle Cards — One-page competitive playbooks for sales: competitor strengths, weaknesses, positioning, common objections, responses.

**Expected output:** Competitive intelligence system with monitoring, analysis, and distribution
**Pro tip:** The most useful competitive intelligence comes from your own sales team — they hear objections and competitor mentions daily

### 3.20 Mergers and Acquisitions Strategy
**Use case:** Develop M&A framework for evaluating, executing, and integrating acquisitions
**Best for:** Executives, corporate development, finance

Act as an M&A strategy consultant. I need to develop a mergers and acquisitions framework.

Company: [describe business] | Strategy: [growth plan and gaps] | Financial capacity: [budget/debt capacity] | Industry: [sector and consolidation trends] | M&A experience: [none/some/extensive] | Goals: [technology acquisition, talent, market share, diversification]

Design an M&A strategy framework. Strategic Rationale — Define M&A thesis: what types of acquisitions create value. Adjacency map of attractive targets. Target Identification Criteria — Size (revenue, team), product/technology, customer overlap, cultural fit, geographic presence. Scoring Model. Sourcing — Channels: investment bankers, industry events, founder networks, competitive monitoring. Due Diligence Checklist — Comprehensive: financial, legal, technical, commercial, cultural, operational. Valuation Methods — DCF, comparable transactions, market multiples, synergy valuation. Integration Planning — Day 1 readiness, integration levels (full/partial/hold separate), integration team structure. Culture Integration — Most common failure cause; assessment and integration plan. Post-Merger KPIs — Revenue synergy achieved, cost synergy, retention, timeline adherence. Common Pitfalls — List of top 10 M&A mistakes and how to avoid them.

**Expected output:** M&A strategy framework with target criteria, due diligence checklist, and integration process
**Pro tip:** 70-90% of M&A deals destroy value — the difference between success and failure is almost always integration execution, not deal terms

### 3.21 Business Model Canvas Analysis
**Use case:** Evaluate and refine a company's business model across all nine building blocks of the canvas.
**Best for:** Founders, strategists, and innovation teams

Act as a business model strategist. Walk through each of the nine Business Model Canvas blocks for [company name]. For each block, assess the current state, identify gaps, and propose improvements aligned with [specific objective like profitability or market share growth]. Connect the blocks to show how changes in one area affect the others.

**Expected output:** A full nine-block canvas analysis with strengths, gaps, and an action plan for the three weakest blocks.
**Pro tip:** Run a live workshop where each block gets its own whiteboard section. This helps the team see interconnections they normally miss.

### 3.22 Value-Based Pricing Strategy
**Use case:** Set prices based on the perceived value delivered to customers rather than costs or competitors.
**Best for:** Product managers, pricing analysts, and startup founders

Act as a pricing strategist. For [product or service name], identify the key value drivers that matter most to [target customer segment]. Quantify the economic or emotional value each driver creates. Design a tiered pricing model that captures a fair share of that value while leaving customers feeling they got a good deal. Include price anchors and decoy options to guide choices.

**Expected output:** A value-based pricing model with 3-4 tiers, justification for each price point, and a summary of value drivers and their estimated worth to customers.
**Pro tip:** Interview your top 10 customers about what they would pay before you set prices. Their answers will surprise you.

### 3.23 Competitive Pricing Analysis
**Use case:** Analyze competitor pricing to find the optimal position in the market.
**Best for:** Marketing managers, business analysts, and founders

Act as a competitive pricing analyst. Identify [number] direct and indirect competitors for [company name] in the [industry] space. Map their pricing models, feature sets, and target segments. Identify pricing gaps, premium positioning opportunities, and segments where your offer beats competitors on value. Recommend a pricing position that avoids a race to the bottom.

**Expected output:** A competitive pricing matrix with competitor comparisons, a positioning map, and a recommended price band for your product.
**Pro tip:** Check competitor pricing every quarter. Prices change more often than you think, and you need to keep your map current.

### 3.24 Subscription Pricing Model Design
**Use case:** Design or optimize a recurring revenue pricing model that balances acquisition, retention, and expansion.
**Best for:** SaaS founders, subscription business owners, and pricing strategists

Act as a subscription pricing consultant. Design a pricing structure for [product or service name] that maximizes customer lifetime value while keeping churn low. Determine the right number of tiers, feature segmentation, billing cycles, and free trial or freemium options. Consider usage-based versus flat-rate models and where hybrid approaches make sense for [target market].

**Expected output:** A subscription pricing proposal with tier definitions, feature breakdowns, pricing per cycle, and projected impacts on acquisition and retention.
**Pro tip:** Keep your entry-level tier cheap enough to attract signups but valuable enough to reduce support burden from non-paying users.

### 3.25 Strategic Partnership Evaluation
**Use case:** Assess potential strategic partners for fit, value creation, and risk.
**Best for:** Business development managers, partnership leads, and CEOs

Act as a partnership strategist. Evaluate [potential partner name] as a strategic partner for [your company name]. Assess their market position, customer overlap, complementary capabilities, revenue model fit, and cultural compatibility. Map out a joint value proposition, estimate the revenue potential for both sides, and flag any risks like channel conflict or competitive overlap.

**Expected output:** A partnership scorecard with weighted criteria, a go/no-go recommendation, and a proposed partnership structure (referral, reseller, or integration).
**Pro tip:** Start with a small joint campaign before signing a long agreement. Real data beats spreadsheets every time.

### 3.26 Partnership Agreement Design
**Use case:** Structure the terms, incentives, and governance of a new business partnership.
**Best for:** Business development managers, legal teams, and partnership leads

Act as a partnership architect. Design the framework for a partnership between [your company] and [partner company]. Define the scope of the relationship, revenue sharing or commission structure, term length, exclusivity clauses, termination conditions, and governance cadence. Include performance milestones that unlock better terms for both sides.

**Expected output:** A partnership agreement outline with key clauses, incentive structures, performance milestones, and a quarterly review cadence.
**Pro tip:** Include a renewal clause that adjusts terms based on actual performance. Static partnerships often drift apart.

### 3.27 Vendor Selection and Evaluation
**Use case:** Select the best vendor or software provider through structured criteria and scoring.
**Best for:** Procurement managers, operations leads, and CTOs

Act as a procurement specialist. Develop a vendor evaluation framework for selecting [type of vendor or software] for [company name]. Define weighted criteria including cost, features, implementation timeline, support quality, security compliance, and scalability. Create a scoring system, shortlist questions, and a reference check process. Apply it to [number] shortlisted vendors.

**Expected output:** A vendor selection matrix with weighted scores, a shortlist comparison, and a final recommendation with rationale.
**Pro tip:** Talk to former customers of each vendor, not just the references they give you. Unprompted feedback is gold.

### 3.28 OKR Setting Framework
**Use case:** Align teams around measurable objectives and key results for a given quarter or year.
**Best for:** CEOs, team leads, and OKR champions

Act as an OKR coach. Guide [team or company name] through setting Objectives and Key Results for the [quarter/year]. Start with the company level objectives that support [annual strategy], then cascade to department and team levels. Ensure each objective is aspirational but grounded, and each key result is measurable and time bound. Avoid common pitfalls like cascading too many OKRs or setting results that feel like tasks.

**Expected output:** A complete OKR set for the organization with 3-5 company objectives, 2-4 key results each, and aligned team or department level OKRs.
**Pro tip:** Never tie OKR scores directly to compensation. If bonuses depend on hitting key results, teams will sandbag their targets.

### 3.29 KPI Dashboard Design
**Use case:** Build a real time dashboard of key performance indicators for operational visibility.
**Best for:** Operations managers, team leads, and data analysts

Act as an operations analytics consultant. Design a KPI dashboard for [department or company name] that tracks [number] key metrics aligned with [business goal]. Specify leading indicators that predict future performance and lagging indicators that confirm results. Include targets, thresholds, and alert triggers. Define the data sources, refresh cadence, and owner for each metric.

**Expected output:** A KPI dashboard mockup with metric definitions, targets, data sources, and alert thresholds for each indicator.
**Pro tip:** Pick 5-7 KPIs max per dashboard. More than that and people stop looking at all of them.

### 3.30 Business Turnaround Strategy
**Use case:** Develop a plan to reverse declining performance and restore profitability.
**Best for:** CEOs, interim executives, and board members

Act as a turnaround specialist. Diagnose the root causes of decline at [company name], whether they are operational, financial, strategic, or market driven. Develop a 90 day stabilization plan that addresses cash flow, cost structure, and quick revenue wins. Then outline a 12 month recovery roadmap with key milestones, resource requirements, and exit criteria for each phase.

**Expected output:** A turnaround plan with a 90 day cash stabilization phase, a 12 month recovery roadmap, and specific intervention points for each decline driver.
**Pro tip:** In a turnaround, cash is king. Cut anything that does not generate or preserve cash in the first 30 days.

### 3.31 Market Expansion Planning
**Use case:** Plan entry into a new geographic or demographic market.
**Best for:** Growth leads, marketing directors, and founders

Act as a market expansion strategist. Evaluate [new market name] as an expansion target for [company name]. Analyze market size, growth rate, competitive landscape, regulatory environment, and customer acquisition costs. Determine the best entry mode (organic, acquisition, or partnership), localize the value proposition, and build a phased rollout plan with resourcing and budget estimates.

**Expected output:** A market expansion plan with market analysis, entry strategy, localization requirements, financial projections, and a phased rollout timeline.
**Pro tip:** Rent a small presence first. A temporary office or local partner saves you from signing a long lease before you know the market works.

### 3.32 International Market Entry Strategy
**Use case:** Structure the legal, operational, and go-to-market approach for expanding across borders.
**Best for:** International business leads, expansion managers, and founders

Act as an international business strategist. Create an entry plan for [company name] into [target country or region]. Address legal entity setup, tax implications, hiring and employment law, currency risk, and intellectual property protection. Design a go-to-market approach that accounts for local buying habits, distribution channels, and pricing expectations.

**Expected output:** An international market entry playbook covering legal setup, operational logistics, go-to-market plan, and a 12 month budget.
**Pro tip:** Hire local leadership for the new market. Sending expats to run things almost always misses cultural nuance.

### 3.33 Lean Startup Methodology Application
**Use case:** Apply build-measure-learn cycles to validate a business idea before scaling.
**Best for:** Startup founders, product managers, and innovation teams

Act as a lean startup coach. Take [business idea or product concept] and design a rigorous validation process using the build-measure-learn loop. Define the key assumptions that must be true for the idea to work. Design the minimum viable product or experiment that tests the riskiest assumption first. Specify success metrics and decision criteria for whether to pivot or persevere.

**Expected output:** A lean validation plan with the riskiest assumption identified, an MVP or experiment design, success criteria, and a build-measure-learn timeline.
**Pro tip:** If you can test an assumption with a landing page and Google Ads instead of building software, do that first. Speed beats polish.

### 3.34 Business Process Automation Blueprint
**Use case:** Identify and prioritize manual processes for automation across the organization.
**Best for:** Operations managers, COOs, and automation leads

Act as a business automation consultant. Audit the current workflows at [company name or department] and identify [number] processes that are good candidates for automation. For each candidate, assess complexity, implementation cost, time savings, error reduction, and ROI. Recommend automation tools or platforms suited to your tech stack. Prioritize the automation roadmap based on quick wins versus high impact transformations.

**Expected output:** A process automation blueprint with a prioritized pipeline of automation candidates, tool recommendations, ROI estimates, and a 6 month implementation schedule.
**Pro tip:** Automate processes that your team hates doing. Those are usually the ones with the highest turnover and error rates.

### 3.35 Workflow Automation Prioritization
**Use case:** Rank automation opportunities by effort, impact, and organizational readiness.
**Best for:** Process improvement leads, ops managers, and IT directors

Act as a workflow optimization expert. For [company name], create a prioritization framework for workflow automation projects. Score each candidate process on effort to automate (technical complexity, integration needs, change management) and impact (time saved, error reduction, employee satisfaction). Plot them on a 2x2 matrix. Identify the low effort, high impact quadrant and build a 90 day sprint plan from those items.

**Expected output:** A prioritized automation backlog with effort-impact scoring, a 2x2 matrix view, and a 90 day implementation sprint plan.
**Pro tip:** Include change management time in your effort estimates. The technology is usually the easy part compared to getting people to change habits.

### 3.36 Cost Reduction Analysis
**Use case:** Identify and eliminate non-essential costs without damaging core operations.
**Best for:** CFOs, operations directors, and cost optimization teams

Act as a cost reduction analyst. Review the cost structure of [company name or department]. Categorize expenses into strategic (directly drive growth), operational (keep the lights on), and fat (low impact, high cost). Identify specific line items in the fat category that can be cut, renegotiated, or eliminated. For each suggestion, estimate the savings and any secondary effects on quality or capacity.

**Expected output:** A cost reduction plan with categorized expenses, specific cut recommendations, savings estimates, and a risk assessment for each change.
**Pro tip:** Cut costs in ways that customers never notice. If a reduction affects the user experience, you might save money now but lose revenue later.

### 3.37 Scalability Assessment
**Use case:** Evaluate whether current operations, systems, and team structure can handle rapid growth.
**Best for:** Founders, COOs, and growth stage executives

Act as a scalability consultant. Assess [company name]'s readiness to scale to [target size, e.g., 5x current revenue]. Evaluate four dimensions: operational processes (repeatable and documented?), technology infrastructure (can it handle the load?), team structure (will the current org chart work at scale?), and financial model (does unit economics improve or degrade with scale?). Identify the top three bottlenecks that will break first under growth pressure.

**Expected output:** A scalability assessment with scores across four dimensions, identified bottlenecks, and a strengthening plan for each weak area.
**Pro tip:** Your current best people will become your biggest bottlenecks at scale. Start hiring and delegating before you feel the pain.

### 3.38 Risk Management Framework
**Use case:** Build a structured approach to identifying, assessing, and mitigating business risks.
**Best for:** Risk managers, CFOs, and board members

Act as an enterprise risk management consultant. Design a risk management framework for [company name]. Identify risks across [number] categories: financial, operational, strategic, compliance, and reputational. For each risk, assess likelihood, impact, and current controls. Assign a risk owner and define mitigation actions, monitoring cadence, and escalation triggers. Include emerging risks that could reshape the landscape.

**Expected output:** A risk register with assessed and scored risks, mitigation plans, ownership assignments, and a monitoring and reporting schedule.
**Pro tip:** Review the risk register with the leadership team monthly, not annually. The biggest risks change fast and you want to catch them early.

### 3.39 Strategic Planning Retreat Agenda
**Use case:** Design a structured offsite agenda that produces actionable strategic outcomes.
**Best for:** CEOs, board members, and strategy leads

Act as a strategic planning facilitator. Design a [2-3] day strategic planning retreat agenda for [company name]. Include sessions for reviewing the current state, analyzing market trends, setting the vision and priorities for the next [year or quarter], and allocating resources. Build in solo thinking time, group discussions, and decision points. Include pre-work assignments so the team shows up ready to decide instead of catching up.

**Expected output:** A retreat agenda with session timings, objectives, facilitation methods, required pre-work, and expected outputs for each session.
**Pro tip:** End each day with a decision summary. A retreat without concrete decisions is just an expensive meeting.

### 3.40 Business Health Scorecard
**Use case:** Measure overall business health across financial, customer, operational, and people dimensions.
**Best for:** CEOs, board members, and investors

Act as a business performance analyst. Build a health scorecard for [company name] that tracks [number] metrics across four categories: financial health (revenue growth, gross margin, cash runway), customer health (NPS, churn rate, LTV:CAC ratio), operational health (capacity utilization, defect rate, on-time delivery), and people health (employee turnover, engagement score, time to hire). Define green, yellow, and red thresholds for each metric.

**Expected output:** A one page business health scorecard with traffic light indicators, trend arrows, and a commentary section for each category.
**Pro tip:** Share the scorecard with the whole company, not just leadership. Transparency builds trust and helps everyone prioritize.

### 3.41 Supply Chain Optimization
**Use case:** Improve supply chain performance to reduce costs, increase reliability, and manage disruptions.
**Best for:** Supply chain managers, operations directors, and logistics leads

Act as a supply chain optimization consultant. Analyze the current supply chain for [company name or product line]. Map the full flow from raw materials to end customer. Identify bottlenecks, single points of failure, excess inventory, and lead time issues. Recommend specific improvements like supplier diversification, inventory buffer adjustments, demand forecasting upgrades, or logistics route changes. Estimate the cost and impact of each change.

**Expected output:** A supply chain assessment with a current state map, bottleneck analysis, and prioritized improvement recommendations with ROI estimates.
**Pro tip:** Map your suppliers' suppliers. Your biggest disruption risk might come from a sub supplier you have never heard of.

### 3.42 Customer Lifetime Value Modeling
**Use case:** Calculate and improve the total value a customer generates over their relationship with your business.
**Best for:** Marketing analysts, finance teams, and growth leads

Act as a customer analytics expert. Build a CLV model for [company name]'s customer base. Calculate average purchase value, purchase frequency, customer lifespan, and gross margin per customer. Segment customers by acquisition channel, product line, and behavior to identify which segments have the highest and lowest lifetime value. Recommend specific actions to increase CLV for each segment through retention, upsell, or pricing changes.

**Expected output:** A CLV model with segment level analysis, key drivers of lifetime value, and a prioritized action plan to increase CLV across segments.
**Pro tip:** Track CLV by acquisition channel. A channel that brings cheap customers with low retention may not be as good as an expensive one that brings loyal buyers.

### 3.43 Unit Economics Analysis
**Use case:** Analyze the per unit profitability of a product or service to guide pricing and scaling decisions.
**Best for:** Startup founders, finance analysts, and product managers

Act as a unit economics analyst. Break down the unit economics for [product or service name] at [company name]. Calculate Customer Acquisition Cost (CAC), Average Revenue Per User (ARPU), gross margin per unit, contribution margin, and the CAC payback period. Compare these to industry benchmarks for [sector]. Identify which levers (price, retention, COGS reduction) would most improve unit economics and by how much.

**Expected output:** A unit economics breakdown with all key metrics, benchmark comparisons, sensitivity analysis on key levers, and a roadmap for improving the numbers.
**Pro tip:** If your CAC payback period is longer than 12 months, you need higher margins or more retention to make the math work at scale.

### 3.44 Fundraising Strategy Development
**Use case:** Prepare a fundraising strategy that aligns with business stage, goals, and founder preferences.
**Best for:** Startup founders and CEOs seeking external capital

Act as a fundraising advisor. Develop a capital raising strategy for [company name] based on its current traction, market size, and growth plan. Determine the right funding instrument (equity, convertible note, SAFE, or debt), target investor profile (angel, VC, or strategic), and valuation range. Build a fundraising timeline, target list of [number] investors, and a data room checklist. Include a strategy for managing the fundraising process without disrupting operations.

**Expected output:** A fundraising strategy covering instrument selection, target investor list, valuation rationale, data room contents, and a 6 month fundraising timeline.
**Pro tip:** Warm introductions convert 10x better than cold outreach. Spend the first month of fundraising getting referrals from your network.

### 3.45 Bootstrapping Growth Strategy
**Use case:** Grow a business profitably without external funding by optimizing cash flow and organic channels.
**Best for:** Bootstrapped founders, solo entrepreneurs, and small business owners

Act as a bootstrapping growth consultant. Design a capital efficient growth strategy for [company name] that does not rely on outside investment. Identify the highest ROI growth channels (organic content, referrals, partnerships, or paid channels with fast payback). Build a cash flow management system that keeps [number] months of runway. Plan hiring as a last resort, using contractors, automation, and outsourcing to stay lean.

**Expected output:** A bootstrapped growth plan with highest ROI channels identified, a cash flow management framework, and a lean hiring and operations strategy.
**Pro tip:** Profitability is your only moat when you are bootstrapped. If a growth channel does not pay back within 3 months, pause it.

### 3.46 Exit Strategy Planning
**Use case:** Prepare the business for a future sale, merger, or IPO to maximize owner value.
**Best for:** Founders, business owners, and board members

Act as an exit planning advisor. Develop an exit strategy for [company name] targeting a [timeframe like 3-5 years] horizon. Analyze whether a strategic sale, financial sale (PE), or IPO best suits the business profile and owner goals. Identify key value drivers that would increase the exit multiple: recurring revenue, customer concentration, gross margins, and IP portfolio. Build a preparation roadmap covering financial cleanup, operational standardization, management team strengthening, and timing considerations.

**Expected output:** An exit strategy plan with target buyer type, valuation range estimate, value driver improvement roadmap, and a preparation timeline.
**Pro tip:** Start preparing for exit 2-3 years before you plan to sell. Buyers pay for clean books and recurring revenue, not potential.

### 3.47 Business Continuity Planning
**Use case:** Ensure critical operations can continue during and after a major disruption.
**Best for:** Operations managers, risk officers, and IT directors

Act as a business continuity consultant. Build a continuity plan for [company name] that covers [types of disruptions like natural disaster, cyberattack, or supply chain failure]. Identify critical business functions, the maximum tolerable downtime for each, and the recovery strategies. Define communication protocols, backup locations, data recovery procedures, and a crisis management team structure with clear roles.

**Expected output:** A business continuity plan with critical function inventory, recovery time objectives, detailed recovery procedures, and a crisis communication tree.
**Pro tip:** Test your continuity plan with a live drill at least once a year. A plan that has never been tested is just a wish.

### 3.48 Corporate Innovation Frameworks
**Use case:** Build a systematic approach to innovation inside an established company.
**Best for:** Innovation leads, R&D directors, and corporate strategists

Act as a corporate innovation strategist. Design an innovation framework for [company name] that balances incremental improvements with breakthrough ideas. Recommend a portfolio approach (core, adjacent, and transformational initiatives) with resource allocation guidelines for each bucket. Include processes for idea sourcing, validation sprints, and scaling successful experiments. Address common barriers like risk aversion, budget silos, and short term performance pressure.

**Expected output:** An innovation framework with a portfolio model, idea management process, validation methodology, and governance structure.
**Pro tip:** Give innovation teams a separate budget and reporting line. If they report to the core business unit, incremental thinking will always win.

### 3.49 Strategic vs. Financial Buyer Analysis
**Use case:** Compare and contrast the advantages of selling to a strategic buyer versus a financial buyer.
**Best for:** Founders, M&A advisors, and business owners

Act as an M&A advisor. Analyze the tradeoffs between selling [company name] to a strategic buyer (a competitor or company in a related space) versus a financial buyer (private equity or family office). For each buyer type, assess likely valuation range, deal structure preferences, post acquisition integration requirements, management team retention expectations, and timeline flexibility. Recommend the optimal buyer type based on [company name]'s goals and profile.

**Expected output:** A buyer type comparison with valuation ranges, deal structure differences, integration requirements, and a recommendation tailored to company goals.
**Pro tip:** Strategic buyers often pay higher multiples but expect deeper integration. If you care about brand and culture, a financial buyer may be a better fit.

### 3.50 Operational Efficiency Audit
**Use case:** Identify waste, redundancy, and inefficiency across core business operations.
**Best for:** COOs, operations managers, and process improvement leads

Act as an operational efficiency auditor. Conduct a systematic audit of operations at [company name or department]. Review workflows, resource allocation, communication patterns, and technology usage. Identify [number] areas of waste: overproduction, waiting time, unnecessary movement, excess inventory, overprocessing, defects, and underutilized talent. For each waste type, quantify the cost and recommend a specific elimination strategy.

**Expected output:** An operational efficiency audit report with identified waste areas, cost impact estimates, and a prioritized elimination action plan.
**Pro tip:** Follow a worker through their day before you audit their process. You will see inefficiencies that no report will ever show you.




## 4. Sales & Lead Generation Prompts (40 Prompts)

### 4.1 Complete Sales Process Design
**Use case:** Design a repeatable, scalable sales process from prospecting to close
**Best for:** Sales leaders, founders, revenue operations

Act as a sales process architect. I need to design or redesign my sales process for consistency and scalability.

Company: [describe business] | Product: [what you sell] | Price point: [average deal size] | Sales cycle: [length in days/weeks] | Current process: [what exists] | Team size: [sales team members] | Target customer: [ideal customer profile] | Channels: [inbound/outbound/partners] | Conversion rates: [current metrics if available]

Design a complete sales process. Process Stages — Define 7-10 stages from prospecting to closed-won with clear entry/exit criteria for each. Stage Definitions: Lead Generation (prospecting activities, sources), Qualification (BANT, MEDDIC, or your criteria), Discovery (needs analysis, pain points identified), Proposal (solution presentation, demo), Negotiation (objection handling, pricing), Closing (contract, legal), Onboarding (handoff to CS). Deal Stages with Metrics — For each stage: expected duration, conversion rate to next stage, key activities required, exit criteria. Sales Methodology — Recommend and outline one core methodology (SPIN, Challenger, MEDDIC, Sandler, Value Selling). Tools and Tech Stack — CRM setup, sequencing tools, demo platforms, contract management. Content and Collateral — What sales materials needed at each stage: battle cards, case studies, proposals, ROI calculators. Pipeline Management — Weekly pipeline review cadence, deal scoring, next-step discipline. KPIs — Track: calls/emails per rep, meetings set, pipeline value, conversion rates, ACV, sales cycle length.

**Expected output:** Complete sales process design with stages, methodology, tools, and KPIs
**Pro tip:** A sales process isn't a restriction — it's a checklist that ensures reps don't skip critical steps when they're busy or overconfident

### 4.2 Ideal Customer Profile and Persona Development
**Use case:** Define and document target customers for precise sales targeting
**Best for:** Sales teams, marketing, product, founders

Act as a sales and marketing strategist. I need to develop detailed ideal customer profiles and buyer personas.

Company: [describe business] | Product: [what you sell] | Current customers: [describe best customers] | Industries served: [which verticals] | Deal sizes: [range of deal values] | Existing data: [CRM data available] | Sales feedback: [what reps say about best customers]

Create ICP and persona documentation. Ideal Customer Profile — Company-level criteria: industry, company size (revenue, employees), location, technology stack, growth stage, funding, business model, pain points that trigger need. Buyer Personas — For each persona (economic buyer, technical buyer, champion, user, influencer): role, seniority, goals, challenges, decision criteria, information sources, objections, relationships. Persona Depth — For each: typical career path, how they're measured, what keeps them up at night, why they'd buy, why they'd reject. Segmentation — Define segments: Tier 1 (perfect fit — high conversion), Tier 2 (good fit — nurture), Tier 3 (poor fit — disqualify early). ICP Scorecard — Create scoring template to evaluate leads against ICP criteria. Anti-Persona — Define who NOT to sell to (bad fit customers who churn or cost more than they're worth). Negative ICP saves sales time.

**Expected output:** Complete ICP and buyer persona documentation with scoring and negative ICP
**Pro tip:** Your best customer is not the one who pays the most — it's the one who gets the most value, stays longest, and refers most

### 4.3 Outbound Prospecting Engine
**Use case:** Build systematic outbound prospecting that generates qualified meetings
**Best for:** SDR/BDR teams, sales leaders, founders

Act as an outbound prospecting expert. I need to build a scalable outbound prospecting engine that consistently generates qualified meetings.

Company: [describe business] | Target market: [who you sell to] | ICP: [ideal customer profile] | Current outbound: [what's working/not working] | Team: [number of SDRs] | Tools: [current prospecting tools] | Monthly target: [meetings/pipeline needed]

Design an outbound prospecting system. Account Selection — Tier 1 accounts list building using: LinkedIn Sales Navigator, industry reports, competitor customers, conference attendee lists, 6sense/ZoomInfo. Priority ranking. Contact Research — For each target account: company news, tech stack (BuiltWith), recent funding (Crunchbase), leadership changes, strategic initiatives. Personalization framework. Sequence Design — Multi-channel sequences: Day 1: LinkedIn connection + personalized note, Day 3: Email 1 (value proposition), Day 7: Phone call, Day 10: Email 2 (case study), Day 14: LinkedIn follow-up, Day 21: Breakup email. Sequence variations by persona. Email Templates — Provide 10 email templates: initial outreach, follow-up, value-add, case study share, social proof, mutual connection, breakup, re-engagement. Each with personalization hooks. Call Scripts — 5 call scripts: gatekeeper bypass, discovery call, objection handling, voicemail, meeting confirmation. Objection Handling — Responses to top 10 objections: "not interested," "no budget," "not now," "happy with current," "send info," "too busy," etc. Cadence — Daily activity targets: 50 calls, 30 personalized emails, 20 LinkedIn actions per SDR. Metrics — Track: accounts contacted, response rate, meeting rate, conversion to opportunity, pipeline generated per rep.

**Expected output:** Complete outbound prospecting system with sequences, scripts, objection handling, and metrics
**Pro tip:** Personalization isn't mentioning the company name — it's referencing a specific challenge or initiative they're publicly working on

### 4.4 Inbound Lead Qualification System
**Use case:** Qualify and route inbound leads effectively to maximize conversion
**Best for:** SDR teams, marketing, sales operations

Act as a lead qualification specialist. I need to build an inbound lead qualification and routing system.

Lead sources: [website, content offers, webinars, trials, demos, events, referrals] | Monthly volume: [leads per month] | Current process: [manual/automated/none] | Team: [available SDRs] | CRM: [your CRM platform] | Scoring needs: [need lead scoring?]

Design an inbound qualification system. Lead Tiers — Define 4 tiers: Tier 1 (hot — call within 5 min), Tier 2 (warm — contact within 24 hours), Tier 3 (cold — nurture sequence), Tier 4 (MQL — marketing qualified, not sales-ready). Scoring Model — Points-based: demographics (industry, size, title), behavior (pages visited, content downloaded, demo viewed), engagement (email opens, webinar attendance). Thresholds for each tier. Qualification Framework — BANT (Budget, Authority, Need, Timeline) or MEDDIC (Metrics, Economic buyer, Decision criteria, Decision process, Identify pain, Champion). Qualification questions for each. Routing Rules — By territory, by company size, by product interest, by deal size. Round-robin vs assignment. SLA Definition — Time-to-contact: Tier 1 within 5 minutes, Tier 2 within 4 hours, Tier 3 within 24 hours. Response templates. Handoff Process — Marketing qualified alert, SDR contact script, CRM tracking, handoff to AE after qualification. Lead Nurture — Automated sequences for non-ready leads: educational content, case studies, webinar invites, re-engagement campaigns. Feedback Loop — SDRs report lead quality back to marketing monthly. Closed-loop reporting.

**Expected output:** Complete inbound lead qualification system with scoring, routing, SLAs, and nurturing
**Pro tip:** Speed to lead is the single highest leverage metric — calling within 5 minutes converts 100x better than calling after 30 minutes

### 4.5 Sales Discovery and Needs Analysis
**Use case:** Conduct effective discovery calls that uncover real needs and build value
**Best for:** AEs, sales professionals, founders

Act as a sales discovery expert. I need a framework for conducting effective discovery calls.

Product: [what you sell] | Typical buyers: [who you talk to] | Common use cases: [why customers buy] | Sales cycle: [length and complexity] | Current discovery: [strengths and weaknesses]

Design a discovery call framework. Pre-Call Research — 15-minute prep: company overview, recent news, LinkedIn profiles of attendees, tech stack, industry trends, competitors. Preparation checklist. Discovery Structure — 30/45/60-minute call structures with time allocation per section. Recommended: Rapport (5%), Agenda Setting (5%), Situation Questions (20%), Problem Questions (25%), Implication Questions (25%), Need-Payoff Questions (15%), Next Steps (5%). Question Bank — 50+ discovery questions organized by: Situation (current state), Problem (pain points), Implication (cost of problem), Need-Payoff (value of solution). Questions for different buyer personas (economic, technical, user). Active Listening Techniques — Summarize, validate, dig deeper with follow-up questions. When to talk vs listen (recommend 70% listening). Red Flags — Early warning signs: no budget, no timeline, not a priority, no authority, unclear decision process. Qualification Assessment — Post-call scoring against BANT/MEDDIC. Green/yellow/red for each criterion. Documentation — CRM notes template: key pain points, goals, decision criteria, competition, next steps.

**Expected output:** Complete discovery framework with question banks, call structure, and qualification criteria
**Pro tip:** The best discovery calls feel like a consultation, not an interrogation — ask questions that make the prospect think differently about their problem

### 4.6 Demo and Presentation Excellence
**Use case:** Deliver compelling demos and presentations that drive buying decisions
**Best for:** AEs, sales engineers, founders

Act as a sales presentation expert. I need to improve how my team delivers demos and presentations.

Product: [what you demo] | Typical audience: [who attends demos] | Current demo: [current approach and challenges] | Competition: [who you compete against] | Sales cycle stage: [when demos happen]

Design a demo excellence framework. Demo Structure — Problem-Agitate-Solution format: 10% context/agenda, 20% pain reaffirmation, 50% solution demonstration, 10% proof/case studies, 10% next steps. Discovery-Led Demo — Customize demo based on discovery findings. Map features back to prospect's stated pain points. Feature > Benefit > Value — For each feature shown: explain WHAT it does (feature), WHY it matters (benefit), and WHAT it means for them (value). Storytelling — Weave customer stories into demo: before (the pain), after (the solution), results (the ROI). Competitive Positioning — How to position against top 3 competitors without naming them. Difference without disparagement. Handling Interruptions — Techniques for getting back on track when prospect derails demo. Objection Handling During Demo — Real-time responses to demo objections. Trial Close — Check for buy-in throughout demo: "Would this solve [pain point] for you?" Call to Action — Clear next step: proposal, trial, POC, or close. Demo Checklist — Pre-demo tech check, materials ready, environment set up.

**Expected output:** Demo excellence framework with structure, storytelling, objection handling, and trial closes
**Pro tip:** The best demo shows the prospect their world AFTER your solution — don't demo features, demo the future they could have

### 4.7 Proposal and Quote Creation
**Use case:** Create compelling proposals and quotes that close deals
**Best for:** AEs, sales teams, founders

Act as a sales proposal expert. I need to improve how we create and present proposals.

Product/service: [what you sell] | Pricing model: [how you charge] | Typical deal size: [average value] | Competition: [who you compete against] | Current proposals: [format, strengths, weaknesses]

Design a proposal creation framework. Proposal Structure — Executive Summary > Problem Statement > Solution Overview > Implementation Plan > Pricing > ROI/Business Case > Case Studies > Terms > Next Steps. Value-First Pricing — Present pricing AFTER reinforcing value. Price anchoring techniques. Pricing page layout. ROI Calculator — Template to quantify value: current costs, solution costs, savings/revenue generated, payback period, 3-year ROI. Competitive Comparison — When and how to include competitive comparisons. Fact-based differentiation. Case Study Integration — Relevant case study per industry or use case aligned with prospect's situation. Social Proof elements. Visual Design — Proposal design principles: clean layout, data visualization, consistent branding, scannable sections. Delivery Strategy — Present in person if possible, walk through key sections, don't just email and pray. Follow-up cadence after sending. Negotiation Room — Build in strategic wiggle room: what can be discounted, what's non-negotiable, trade-off options. E-Signature — Include e-signature capability directly in proposal.

**Expected output:** Proposal creation framework with ROI calculator, structure, and negotiation strategy
**Pro tip:** Proposals should be presented, not sent — walking through the value in person converts at 3x the rate of emailing a PDF

### 4.8 Objection Handling Mastery
**Use case:** Prepare sales teams to handle any objection confidently and effectively
**Best for:** Sales reps, sales managers, SDRs

Act as a sales objection handling expert. I need to build a comprehensive objection handling system.

Product: [what you sell] | Common objections: [list top objections you face] | Competition: [main competitors and their positioning] | Price point: [how your pricing compares] | Sales cycle: [where objections typically arise]

Build an objection handling system. Objection Types — Categorize: Price (too expensive, no budget, can't justify), Product (missing features, integration concerns), Competition (using competitor, considering competitor), Authority (need to check with team), Timing (not now, too busy), Need (don't have that problem), Trust (unproven, too new). Response Framework for each — LAER: Listen (acknowledge and validate), Acknowledge (show understanding), Explore (ask questions to understand root cause), Respond (address specific concern). Price Objection Playbook — Top 10 price objection responses: value reframe, cost of doing nothing, ROI breakdown, payment terms, ROI calculator, comparison to competitor pricing, unbundling, phased approach, social proof. Competition Objection Playbook — Handling specific competitor mentions: acknowledge strengths honestly, differentiate on weaknesses, avoid trashing competitors. Product Objection Playbook — Missing features: roadmap discussion, workaround, partner integration, build vs buy analysis. Authority Objection — Creating a champion who sells internally, providing executive summary, offering to meet other stakeholders. Practice Drills — Weekly objection handling roleplay. Objection of the week with team practice.

**Expected output:** Complete objection handling system with categorized playbooks and practice drills
**Pro tip:** Every objection is either a request for more information or a concern that you haven't adequately addressed — never take objections personally

### 4.9 Sales Negotiation Tactics
**Use case:** Negotiate deals effectively while protecting value and relationships
**Best for:** AEs, sales leaders, founders

Act as a sales negotiation expert. I need to improve my team's negotiation skills.

Product: [what you sell] | Average deal size: [typical value] | Discounting history: [current discounting patterns] | Competition: [negotiation leverage points] | Common requests: [what customers typically ask for]

Design a sales negotiation system. Pre-Negotiation Preparation — BATNA (Best Alternative to Negotiated Agreement), reservation price, target price, walk-away point. Know your leverage. Concession Strategy — What to give, what to trade, what to never give. Make concessions slowly and conditionally ("If you... then I can..."). Value Before Price — Before discussing price, reinforce value delivered. ROI summary, case studies, unique differentiators. Anchoring — Let prospect state position first or anchor high with value justification. Bracketing technique. Common Tactics — How to handle: good cop/bad cop, nibbling (last-minute requests), higher authority ("I need to check with my boss"), time pressure, take-away, sympathy. Trade-Off Framework — When asked for discount, trade for: shorter term, upfront payment, longer commitment, referral, case study, reduced scope. Multiple Equivalent Simultaneous Offers (MESO) — Present 2-3 options with different value/price combinations to understand preferences. Silence — Use silence as a negotiation tool. After making an offer or hearing an objection, wait. Escalation Path — When to involve manager, when to walk away. Maintaining relationship while being firm.

**Expected output:** Sales negotiation system with tactics, concession strategy, and common scenario responses
**Pro tip:** The person who mentions a number first loses — unless you anchor with value so high that everything after seems reasonable

### 4.10 Closing Techniques and Strategies
**Use case:** Master closing techniques to consistently convert opportunities
**Best for:** AEs, sales professionals, founders

Act as a sales closing expert. I need to improve my team's ability to close deals.

Product: [what you sell] | Sales cycle: [typical length] | Current close rate: [current win rate] | Common stalls: [why deals get stuck] | Decision process: [typical buyer journey]

Design a closing mastery system. Closing Framework — Assume the close: act like the deal is already done. Transition from discovery/demo to close naturally. Trial Closes — Throughout the process: "If we could solve [problem], would you move forward?" "How does this compare to what you're using now?" Closing Techniques — 10 closing techniques with scripts: Assumptive Close, Alternative Choice Close (Option A or B), Urgency Close (limited time or capacity), Summary Close (recap all agreed value), Question Close ("Is there any reason we shouldn't move forward?"), Takeaway Close (remove offer to create desire), Sharp Angle Close (conditional response to objection), Ben Franklin Close (pros vs cons list), Standing Room Only Close (social proof), Direct Close (ask for the business). Trial Period/Pilot — When to offer a trial, how to structure it for conversion, success criteria for both sides. Handling Stalls — 5 most common stalls and how to overcome: "I need to think about it," "I need to talk to my team," "Send me information," "Let me get back to you," "I'm not ready." Closing Signals — Buying signals: discussing implementation, asking about pricing/terms/contract, involving others, discussing timelines, requesting references. Closing Plan — For each deal: close strategy, stakeholders to engage, obstacles to overcome, next step, close date.

**Expected output:** Closing mastery framework with techniques, stall handling, and deal-specific closing plans
**Pro tip:** The close doesn't start when you ask for the order — it starts when you first qualify the prospect. If you've done everything right, the close is just the logical next step

### 4.11 Account-Based Sales Development
**Use case:** Execute account-based sales strategies for high-value target accounts
**Best for:** Enterprise sales teams, ABM programs

Act as an account-based sales strategist. I need to design an account-based sales development program.

Target accounts: [number and profile of target accounts] | Average deal size: [target deal value] | Sales cycle: [expected length] | Current approach: [what's working] | Team: [available resources] | Tools: [available tech stack]

Design an account-based sales program. Account Selection — Tier accounts by fit and intent. Tier 1 (25 accounts): highest fit + active intent = full-court press. Tier 2 (100 accounts): good fit + some intent = targeted outreach. Tier 3 (500 accounts): good fit only = nurture and monitor. Account Research — Deep dive on each Tier 1 account: org chart, technology stack, strategic initiatives, recent news, competitors, pain points, trigger events. Stakeholder Mapping — Identify all decision makers, influencers, champions, blockers per account. Map relationships and influence. Multi-Channel Engagement — Per account playbook: personalized content, executive outreach, event invitations, social engagement, direct mail, custom demos. Account-Specific Content — Create tailored content for top accounts: case study of similar company, ROI analysis specific to their industry, custom integration plan, executive briefing document. Orchestration — Sales and marketing aligned on account sequence: when to call, what to send, who from the team engages. Tracking and scoring engagement. Meetings and Pipeline — Target: 3-5 stakeholder meetings per account, executive engagement. Pipeline value targets. Reporting — Account penetration metrics, engagement score, pipeline velocity, win rate by account tier.

**Expected output:** Account-based sales program with tiering, research, multi-channel plays, and orchestration
**Pro tip:** ABM isn't about doing more in each account — it's about doing different things. Spray and pray with personalized messages isn't ABM

### 4.12 Channel and Partner Sales Strategy
**Use case:** Build and manage a partner/channel sales ecosystem
**Best for:** Channel sales managers, partnerships, business development

Act as a channel sales strategist. I need to build or scale a partner/channel sales program.

Company: [describe business] | Product: [what partners would sell] | Current channel: [any existing partners] | Target partners: [types — resellers, agencies, tech partners, consultants] | Partner economics: [margin, commissions] | Competition: [competitor partner programs]

Design a channel sales program. Partner Types — Define partner categories and their business models: Resellers (buy and sell), Referral Partners (refer for fee), Technology Partners (integrate), Implementation Partners (implement/serve), Agency Partners (recommend). Partner Recruitment — ICP for partners: customer overlap, complementary product, geographic presence, technical capability, reputation. Recruitment channels and messaging. Partner Program Structure — Tiers: Silver, Gold, Platinum with different margins, support levels, and requirements. Program benefits for each tier. Enablement — Partner training: product certification, sales training, demo environment, battle cards, joint ROI calculator. Partner Portal requirements. Deal Registration — Partner deal protection: time-bound registration, discount on registered deals, conflict resolution process. MDF (Market Development Funds) — Funds for partners: how allocated, approved activities, claim process, proof of execution. Co-Marketing — Joint campaigns: MDF-funded activities, content co-creation, joint events, case studies. Performance Management — Quarterly business reviews, partner scorecards (revenue, certification, pipeline, satisfaction). Partner tiers review. Communications — Partner newsletter, quarterly kickoff, annual partner summit, partner advisory council.

**Expected output:** Channel sales program with partner tiers, enablement, deal registration, and performance management
**Pro tip:** Partners don't work for you — they work with you. The most successful channel programs make partners money in the first 90 days

### 4.13 Sales Enablement Content and Training
**Use case:** Build a sales enablement function that equips reps to sell effectively
**Best for:** Sales enablement managers, sales leaders, training

Act as a sales enablement expert. I need to build a sales enablement program for my team.

Team size: [sales team size] | Experience mix: [junior/mid/senior] | Product complexity: [simple/complex] | Sales cycle: [length] | Current enablement: [what exists] | Gaps: [where reps struggle most]

Design a sales enablement program. Onboarding Program — 30/60/90-day plan: Week 1-2: product training, Week 3-4: sales methodology and process, Week 5-6: discovery and demo training, Week 7-8: ride-alongs and roleplay, Week 9+: ramp to quota with mentorship. Content Library — Organize by sales stage: Awareness (case studies, whitepapers), Consideration (product sheets, comparison guides, ROI calculators), Decision (proposals, references, implementation plans). Training Curriculum — Monthly training topics: objection handling, negotiation, discovery, demo skills, industry knowledge, competitive positioning. Roleplaying — Weekly roleplay sessions: mock discovery calls, demo practice, objection handling. Record and review. Sales Playbooks — 10 playbooks: prospecting playbook, competitive playbook (per competitor), industry playbook (per vertical), deal rescue playbook, expansion playbook. Coaching Framework — Manager coaching: 1:1 ride-alongs weekly, deal reviews, call reviews using recording tools. MEDDIC scoring. Certification — Product knowledge certification, methodology certification. Annual re-certification. Technology Training — CRM proficiency, sales engagement tools, demo tools, analytics tools training. Metrics — Track: ramp time to quota, quota attainment, win rate, time spent on training, NPS.

**Expected output:** Sales enablement program with onboarding, training, playbooks, and coaching
**Pro tip:** Sales enablement is not about more training hours — it's about giving reps the right information at the moment they need it in the sales process

### 4.14 Revenue Operations Setup
**Use case:** Build a revenue operations function that aligns sales, marketing, and customer success
**Best for:** RevOps leaders, executives, ops teams

Act as a revenue operations consultant. I need to build or improve a RevOps function.

Company: [describe business] | Stage: [growth stage] | Current structure: [sales/marketing/CS separate or aligned] | Team: [current ops resources] | Tools: [current tech stack] | Pain points: [data silos, misalignment, reporting]

Design a RevOps framework. Organizational Model — Centralized RevOps (single team supporting all revenue functions) vs embedded (dedicated ops per function). Recommend based on company size. Technology Stack — Core stack: CRM (Salesforce/HubSpot), MAP (Marketo/HubSpot), Sales Engagement (Outreach/SalesLoft), CPQ, Analytics (Tableau), CS Platform. Stack optimization. Data Management — Single source of truth, data hygiene processes, deduplication, enrichment, standard definitions across teams. Process Integration — SLA definitions between marketing-sales (lead handoff), sales-CS (closed won handoff), CS-sales (expansion handoff). Reporting and Analytics — Full funnel reporting: Leads > MQLs > SQLs > Opportunities > Closed Won > Retention > Expansion. Common metrics for all teams. Compensation Alignment — Ensure sales comp, marketing goals, and CS incentives align with same company goals (ARR, retention, NRR). Forecasting — Pipeline coverage, weighted funnel, historical trends, rep-level forecasts. Forecast accuracy measurement. Tech Stack Management — Tool rationalization: eliminate redundant tools, ensure integrations work, control costs. Tech stack audit template. Deal Desk — Support for complex deals: pricing approval, discount authority, contract terms, non-standard requests.

**Expected output:** Revenue operations framework with tech stack, processes, analytics, and alignment
**Pro tip:** RevOps isn't about tools — it's about process. A well-designed process with basic tools beats a stack of best-in-class tools duct-taped together

### 4.15 Enterprise Sales Playbook
**Use case:** Develop enterprise sales capabilities for complex, large-deal selling
**Best for:** Enterprise AEs, sales leaders, founders moving upmarket

Act as an enterprise sales strategist. I need to develop an enterprise sales playbook.

Product: [what you sell] | Typical enterprise deal: [5-figure+/annual] | Enterprise ICP: [company characteristics] | Current enterprise sales: [experience, capabilities] | Competition: [who you face in enterprise deals]

Create an enterprise sales playbook. Enterprise Buying Process — Decision by committee (7-15 stakeholders typically), consensus-driven, procurement involvement, legal review. Map the journey. Stakeholder Mapping — Identify all roles: Executive Sponsor (economic buyer), Champion (internal seller), Technical Evaluator (assesses product), Procurement (negotiates terms), Legal (reviews contract), End Users (will use product). Engagement strategy per role. Champion Development — How to identify, recruit, and empower champions. Give them ammunition: internal memo templates, ROI data, competitive comparisons, implementation plan. Multi-Threading — Never depend on one relationship. Engage 3-5 stakeholders minimum. Lower, middle, and executive levels. Proof of Concept — Enterprise POC framework: success criteria defined upfront, timeline (30-60 days), executive sponsor on both sides, weekly check-ins, go/no-go decision framework. Security and Compliance — Security questionnaire response template, SOC2 reports, data processing agreement, penetration testing results. Enterprise procurement expectations. Legal and Contracting — Common enterprise contract terms: liability caps, SLAs, termination for convenience, renewal terms, data residency. Pre-negotiation checklist. Executive Engagement — C-level engagement strategy: executive summary, ROI analysis, peer case studies, executive-to-executive meetings. Implementation Planning — Detailed implementation plan: timeline, milestones, resource requirements, change management approach. Buyers need to see the full picture before committing.

**Expected output:** Enterprise sales playbook with stakeholder mapping, POC framework, and legal preparation
**Pro tip:** In enterprise sales, no single person can say "yes" to your deal — but several people can say "no." Find and neutralize every potential no

### 4.16 Sales Forecasting and Pipeline Management
**Use case:** Build accurate sales forecasting and healthy pipeline management practices
**Best for:** Sales leaders, RevOps, AEs

Act as a sales forecasting expert. I need to improve forecasting accuracy and pipeline management.

Team: [sales team size] | Deal stages: [current stages] | Average deal size: [typical ACV] | Sales cycle: [average length] | Current forecast accuracy: [current percentage] | Tools: [CRM and forecasting tools]

Design a forecasting and pipeline management system. Forecast Methodology — Bottom-up (rep-level forecasts aggregated) + Top-down (historical conversion rates applied to pipeline). Weighted forecast by stage probability. Pipeline Hygiene — Rules for: stale deals (auto-nudge after 30 days no activity), stage duration limits, deal age max (90/120 days), missing data alerts. Clean pipeline = accurate forecast. Deal Scoring — Score each deal: ICP fit (1-5), engagement level (1-5), champion strength (1-5), timeline clarity (1-5), budget confirmed (yes/no). Score threshold for forecast inclusion. Forecast Categories — Commit (high confidence, verbal yes), Best Case (strong pipeline, needs 1-2 things), Pipeline (early stage, long-term). Pipeline Coverage — Pipeline-to-quota ratio targets: 3x for new business reps, 2x for expansion. Coverage by stage. Forecast Cadence — Weekly pipeline review: rep self-forecast vs manager assessment. Monthly forecast to leadership. Quarterly rollup. Key Metrics — Track: pipeline generation rate, conversion by stage, average deal size, sales cycle length, win rate by source, by rep, by segment. Forecast Accuracy — Compare forecast to actual monthly. Root cause analysis for >20% variance.

**Expected output:** Forecasting system with methodology, pipeline hygiene, deal scoring, and accuracy measurement
**Pro tip:** Pipeline is not a number — it's a story about which deals will close and why. The best forecast comes from managers who deeply understand each deal

### 4.17 Customer Referral Program Design
**Use case:** Build a referral program that turns customers into a sales channel
**Best for:** Sales leaders, marketers, CS teams

Act as a referral program strategist. I need to design a customer referral program that generates consistent leads.

Company: [describe business] | Customer base size: [number of customers] | NPS: [current satisfaction] | Referral history: [any existing referrals] | Incentive willingness: [customer appetite for incentives] | Industry: [your sector]

Design a customer referral program. Program Structure — Referral mechanics: advocate refers friend, friend gets discount/gift card, advocate gets reward. Simple and easy to understand. Incentive Design — Recommended incentives by deal size: small deals ($100-500/month): $50 gift card per referral. Mid-market ($1-5K/month): One month free. Enterprise ($10K+/month): 10% of first year value or equivalent. Incentives for both referrer and referee. Referral Process — Customer refers via: dedicated URL, in-app widget, email, CS rep during QBR. Automated tracking. Simple 3-step flow: Refer > Notify > Reward. Customer Communication — Launch campaign: email sequence, in-app notification, CS team talking points, social media announcement. Ongoing promotion: customer newsletter mention, CS check-in mention, referral page on website. Rewards Fulfillment — Automated reward delivery: gift cards via email, account credits applied automatically, physical gifts shipped. Timeline: reward within 14 days of referee becoming customer. Tracking and Attribution — Referral source tracking, conversion rates, reward costs, ROI calculation (customer LTV vs referral cost). Program Optimization — A/B test incentives, test different messaging, optimize referral flow, analyze which customers refer most. Double down on top referrers. Best Practices — Ask for referrals at peak satisfaction (post positive support interaction, after product success milestone, post-QBR), make it easy, automate everything, say thank you.

**Expected output:** Customer referral program design with incentives, process, and promotion plan
**Pro tip:** The best time to ask for a referral is when a customer says something positive — train your CS team to ask immediately after "we love your product"

### 4.18 Sales Contest and Incentive Design
**Use case:** Design motivating sales contests and incentive programs
**Best for:** Sales leaders, RevOps, HR

Act as a sales incentive designer. I need to create sales contests and incentives that drive the right behaviors.

Team size: [sales team size] | Current comp: [base + commission structure] | Goals: [specific behaviors to incent] | Seasonality: [business cycles] | Budget: [available for contests] | Past contests: [what worked/didn't]

Design a sales contest and incentive framework. Contest Types — Presidents Club (top performers, annual trip), Monthly SPIFs (short-term, specific behavior), Team Contests (collaborative goals), Progress Contests (track progress, multiple winners), Bracket Contests (head-to-head), Mystery Incentives (surprise rewards). Design Principles — Contests should: drive specific behaviors, be winnable by multiple reps, be time-bound, be easy to track transparently, not cannibalize existing pipeline, not encourage bad practices (discounting, sandbagging). Contest Mechanics — Example monthly SPIFs: Most discovery calls set this week ($500), Highest demo-to-close conversion this month ($1000), First deal closed over $50K this quarter ($2000), Most accounts researched in a week ($250). Payout Structures — Flat reward (everyone who hits target gets $X), Tiered rewards (1st, 2nd, 3rd place), Percentage of accelerators, Non-cash rewards (experiences, merchandise, time off). Tracking and Transparency — Real-time leaderboard (daily update), Slack/Teams channel for contest updates, email summary of results. Transparency drives competition. Avoiding Gaming — Rules to prevent: hoarding deals, discounting to close faster, low-quality deals, team sabotage. Clean deal definitions. Post-Contest Analysis — Did the contest drive the intended behavior? Any negative side effects? ROI: incremental revenue vs contest cost. What to do differently next time.

**Expected output:** Sales contest framework with types, mechanics, tracking, and guardrails
**Pro tip:** The best contests create multiple winners — when only the top rep can win, most reps check out mentally after the first week

### 4.19 Territory Planning and Account Assignment
**Use case:** Design sales territories and assign accounts for maximum coverage and fairness
**Best for:** Sales leaders, RevOps

Act as a territory planning expert. I need to design sales territories and assign accounts.

Market: [geographic/vertical coverage needed] | Reps: [number and experience level] | Accounts: [total addressable accounts] | Current territories: [existing assignments] | Data: [available account and historical data] | Goals: [revenue targets, growth, coverage]

Design a territory planning system. Territory Design Principles — Fairness (equal revenue potential), Coverage (all accounts have owner), Accessibility (reasonable travel), Cohesion (similar industries/regions together), Stability (minimize changes). Data Preparation — Gather: account revenue history, account potential score, account location, industry, product usage. Clean data is essential. Territory Models — Geographic (by region/zip code/country), Vertical (by industry), Named Account (enterprise, assigned by strategic fit), Hybrid (vertical within geographic). Recommend based on company maturity. Balancing Territories — Calculate total addressable revenue per territory, adjust for: account density, travel time, rep experience, greenfield vs existing accounts. Aim for <20% variance between territories. Account Tiering — All accounts tiered: Strategic (Tier 1, named AEs), Mid-Market (Tier 2, assigned to reps), Commercial (Tier 3, inside sales/SDRs), Self-Serve (Tier 4, no touch). Account Assignment — Rules: strategic accounts by relationship/fit, mid-market by territory, commercial by round-robin. Consider rep strengths (industry expertise). Transition Plan — How to handle reassignments: communication to affected customers, warm handoff process, ramp period for reps, compensation protection (splits/credits). Optimization — Quarterly micro-adjustments, annual territory redesign. Track: territory attainment, rep satisfaction, customer coverage.

**Expected output:** Territory plan with fair assignments, account tiering, and transition process
**Pro tip:** Territory design is never perfect — aim for "good enough and fair" rather than optimizing for every last dollar of potential

### 4.20 Sales Kickoff Planning
**Use case:** Plan and execute an impactful sales kickoff event
**Best for:** Sales leaders, enablement, event planners

Act as a sales kickoff planner. I need to design a high-impact SKO for my team.

Team size: [number of attendees] | Duration: [1/2/3 days] | Budget: [available budget] | Location: [venue type — onsite/offsite/virtual] | Theme: [annual focus or goals] | Previous SKO feedback: [what worked/didn't]

Design a sales kickoff agenda and content plan. SKO Objectives — Define 3 primary objectives: e.g., announce strategy and targets, launch new product/playbook, build team cohesion, train new skills, celebrate wins. Theme and Messaging — Annual theme that aligns with company goals. Consistent narrative throughout event. Agenda Structure — Day 1: Strategy and Vision (keynotes, product roadmap, market intel), Day 2: Skills and Enablement (workshops, roleplay, product training), Day 3: Planning and Celebration (territory planning, team building, awards). Content Sessions — Types: Keynote (CEO/CRO vision), Product Sessions (what's new, roadmap), Training Workshops (sales methodology, skills practice), Breakouts (by role/segment), Panel Discussions (top reps share secrets), Customer Panels. Training and Roleplay — Dedicated 4+ hours for hands-on practice: new pitch practice, objection handling competitions, demo certifications. Most valuable part of SKO according to reps. Team Building — Activities that build cross-team relationships: social events, team competitions, volunteer events, dinner experiences. Awards and Recognition — Major awards: President's Club announcement, Top Rep, Most Improved, Rookie of the Year, Leadership awards. Peer recognition. Measurement — Post-SKO survey: session ratings, confidence levels before/after, intent to apply learning. Follow-up plan: 30-60-90 day reinforcement.

**Expected output:** Sales kickoff plan with agenda, content, training, and measurement
**Pro tip:** SKO is not a vacation — but it should feel like one. Great SKOs balance intense learning with genuine fun and connection

### 4.21 Outbound Sales Sequence (Prospecting)
**Use case:** Build a multi-touch outbound sequence that turns cold prospects into qualified meetings.
**Best for:** SDRs, BDRs, and sales development teams

Act as a sales development coach. Design a [number] step outbound prospecting sequence targeting [ideal customer profile] for [product or service name]. Include a mix of channels: email, LinkedIn, phone, and direct mail. Write the messaging for each touchpoint with personalization hooks, value propositions tailored to [specific pain point], and clear calls to action. Space the touches over [timeframe] and include a re-engagement track for non-responders.

**Expected output:** A complete outbound sequence with step by step messaging, channel mix, timing, and follow-up rules for each prospect response.
**Pro tip:** Personalize the first line of every email to something specific about the prospect's company. Generic openers get deleted immediately.

### 4.22 Outbound Sales Sequence (Follow-up and Nurture)
**Use case:** Convert prospects who went cold by staying top of mind with a structured follow-up cadence.
**Best for:** SDRs, account executives, and sales teams

Act as a sales sequencing expert. Design a long term follow-up and nurture sequence for prospects at [company name] who did not respond to the initial outreach. Create [number] touches spread over [timeframe like 90 days] using value-added content (case studies, industry reports, product updates) instead of just check-ins. Include trigger-based touches that respond to prospect behavior like visiting the pricing page or downloading a resource.

**Expected output:** A nurture sequence with timed touches, content assets, trigger based automation rules, and re-engagement criteria.
**Pro tip:** The best follow-up emails add value without asking for anything. Share a relevant article. Congratulate them on a company milestone. Stay useful.

### 4.23 Consultative Selling Framework
**Use case:** Sell by diagnosing customer problems and prescribing tailored solutions instead of pitching features.
**Best for:** Account executives, enterprise sellers, and solution consultants

Act as a consultative selling trainer. Create a framework for selling [product or service] using a consultative approach. Define a discovery and diagnosis process that uncovers the prospect's root causes, not just surface symptoms. Build a questioning framework that leads prospects to realize the cost of inaction. Map your solution's capabilities back to their specific problems and quantify the expected impact.

**Expected output:** A consultative selling framework with a discovery blueprint, root cause diagnosis questions, and a problem-to-solution mapping template.
**Pro tip:** If you are talking more than 30 percent of the time in a discovery call, you are losing. Let the prospect do the talking.

### 4.24 Sales Discovery Questions Bank
**Use case:** Uncover prospect needs, pain points, budget, authority, and timeline through structured questions.
**Best for:** SDRs, account executives, and sales teams

Act as a sales discovery specialist. Build a question bank of [number] discovery questions organized by sales stage and qualification dimension (pain, budget, authority, timeline, competition, and impact).
