Include open ended questions that reveal emotional drivers, diagnostic questions that uncover root causes, and future pacing questions that help prospects imagine the solution. Add follow-up probes for each primary question.

**Expected output:** A categorized discovery question bank with primary questions, follow-up probes, and guidance on which questions fit each stage of the sales cycle.
**Pro tip:** Ask "What have you tried so far?" early. It reveals budget, priority level, and what did not work, all in one question.

### 4.25 Sales Presentation Design
**Use case:** Create a persuasive sales deck that tells a compelling story and drives action.
**Best for:** Account executives, sales enablement teams, and founders

Act as a sales presentation designer. Structure a [number] slide sales presentation for [product or service name] targeting [buyer persona]. Follow a narrative arc: hook with a relatable problem, agitate the pain, present your solution as the natural answer, prove it with evidence (case studies, data, testimonials), and end with a clear, low friction next step. Each slide should support one clear point and avoid text clutter.

**Expected output:** A slide by slide sales presentation outline with narrative flow, key messages per slide, visual content suggestions, and speaker notes.
**Pro tip:** Put the customer's problem on slide two, not your logo slide. Buyers care about their problems, not your company history.

### 4.26 Sales Pipeline Management
**Use case:** Design a system to track, qualify, and advance deals through the sales pipeline predictably.
**Best for:** Sales managers, revenue operations leads, and sales teams

Act as a pipeline management consultant. Design a pipeline management system for [company name]'s sales team. Define pipeline stages from lead to closed won, with clear exit criteria for each stage. Set coverage ratios (pipeline value versus quota) and velocity metrics for each stage. Build a weekly pipeline review cadence with specific questions for each deal stage and a process for advancing or removing stalled opportunities.

**Expected output:** A pipeline management framework with stage definitions, exit criteria, coverage targets, velocity benchmarks, and a weekly review template.
**Pro tip:** If a deal has been in the same stage for more than two weeks, it is stuck. Do not let it sit there. Either advance it or take it out.

### 4.27 Sales Forecasting Model
**Use case:** Predict future revenue with accuracy using a structured forecasting methodology.
**Best for:** Sales managers, revenue operations teams, and CFOs

Act as a sales forecasting analyst. Build a revenue forecasting model for [company name]'s sales team. Use weighted pipeline methodology where each deal's expected value is adjusted by its stage probability. Include historical close rates, average deal size by segment, sales cycle length, and seasonality factors. Add a bottoms-up forecast from rep level rolled up to the team and a tops-down view based on historical conversion trends.

**Expected output:** A sales forecasting model with weighted pipeline calculations, historical conversion data, rep level rollups, and a variance tracking mechanism.
**Pro tip:** Forecasts are only as good as the data feeding them. Enforce strict deal stage definitions so reps cannot inflate pipeline by keeping dead deals in late stages.

### 4.28 Sales Acceleration Tactics
**Use case:** Shorten the sales cycle and increase win rates with targeted acceleration strategies.
**Best for:** Sales leaders, account executives, and revenue teams

Act as a sales acceleration specialist. Identify [number] specific tactics to shorten the sales cycle for [company name]'s target deals. Focus on strategies like creating urgency with scarcity or deadlines, offering streamlined proof of concepts, involving champions earlier, using social proof from similar customers, and removing internal blockers. For each tactic, describe the trigger, execution steps, and expected impact on cycle time.

**Expected output:** A sales acceleration playbook with actionable tactics, implementation steps, trigger conditions, and estimated cycle time reduction for each.
**Pro tip:** The fastest way to close a deal is to find the person who feels the pain most acutely and sell to them directly, not to a middleman.

### 4.29 Customer Pain Point Analysis
**Use case:** Map and prioritize customer pain points to sharpen messaging and product positioning.
**Best for:** Sales teams, product marketers, and product managers

Act as a customer insights analyst. Conduct a pain point analysis for [target customer segment] considering [product or service]. Categorize pain points into four types: financial (spending too much), productivity (wasting time), process (inefficient workflows), and emotional (frustration, anxiety, fear of being left behind). For each pain point, estimate severity, frequency, and the customer's awareness of it. Rank them by urgency and map your solution's capabilities to the top pains.

**Expected output:** A pain point map with categorized, ranked customer pains and a solution mapping that connects your product features to specific pains.
**Pro tip:** The pains customers talk about first are rarely the real ones. Keep asking "Why is that a problem?" until you hit the emotional root.

### 4.30 ROI Calculator Creation
**Use case:** Build a tool that quantifies the financial impact of your solution for prospects.
**Best for:** Sales enablement teams, product marketers, and revenue operations

Act as a ROI modeling specialist. Design an ROI calculator for [product or service name] that prospects can use to quantify the value of your solution. Define the inputs (current costs, time spent, inefficiency rates), the calculation logic (how your solution reduces costs or increases revenue), and the outputs (annual savings, payback period, five year net benefit). Make the calculator easy to customize with the prospect's own numbers.

**Expected output:** An ROI calculator framework with input fields, calculation formulas, output metrics, and a guide for sales reps on how to use it in conversations.
**Pro tip:** Let the prospect fill in their own numbers. Self generated ROI estimates are far more convincing than vendor provided ones.

### 4.31 Sales Playbook Development
**Use case:** Create a repeatable playbook for winning deals in a specific market segment or sales scenario.
**Best for:** Sales enablement leads, sales managers, and revenue teams

Act as a sales playbook author. Write a sales playbook for [company name] targeting [specific segment or sales scenario like competitive displacement or enterprise expansion]. Include the buyer persona profile, common objections and responses, competitive positioning, discovery questions specific to this scenario, proof points and case studies, and a step by step deal progression map with milestones. Keep it practical and action oriented.

**Expected output:** A sales playbook covering buyer persona, objection handling, competitive positioning, discovery questions, and a deal progression roadmap.
**Pro tip:** A playbook is only useful if reps actually use it. Keep each section to one page with bullet points, not paragraphs.

### 4.32 Sales Training Curriculum
**Use case:** Design a structured training program that builds core sales competencies across the team.
**Best for:** Sales enablement managers, sales leaders, and L&D teams

Act as a sales training curriculum designer. Build a [timeframe like 12 week] training curriculum for [company name]'s sales team. Cover modules on prospecting, discovery calls, product demonstrations, handling objections, negotiation, and closing. For each module, define learning objectives, training methods (role play, workshop, self study), assessment criteria, and reinforcement activities. Include a certification process at the end.

**Expected output:** A sales training curriculum with module outlines, learning objectives, delivery methods, practice exercises, and a certification exam.
**Pro tip:** Role play the most common objection scenarios until reps can handle them without thinking. That is where confidence comes from.

### 4.33 Competitor Battle Cards
**Use case:** Equip sales reps with quick-reference intelligence on key competitors and how to beat them.
**Best for:** Sales enablement teams, product marketers, and sales leaders

Act as a competitive intelligence analyst. Create battle cards for [number] key competitors of [company name]. For each competitor, cover: their positioning and messaging, target segments, strengths and weaknesses, common scenarios where they win or lose, specific objections they raise about your product, and your responses. Include a comparison table of features, pricing, and customer satisfaction data.

**Expected output:** A set of one page competitor battle cards with positioning, strengths, weaknesses, objection responses, and a features comparison matrix.
**Pro tip:** Never badmouth a competitor in a sales call. Acknowledge their strengths honestly, then shift the conversation to where you win.

### 4.34 Sales Qualification Frameworks (BANT, CHAMP, MEDDIC)
**Use case:** Apply structured qualification frameworks to prioritize the highest potential deals.
**Best for:** Account executives, SDRs, and sales managers

Act as a sales qualification expert. Design a qualification workflow for [company name] using multiple frameworks. Compare BANT (Budget, Authority, Need, Timeline), CHAMP (Challenges, Authority, Money, Prioritization), and MEDDIC (Metrics, Economic Buyer, Decision Criteria, Decision Process, Identify Pain, Champion). Recommend which framework fits your sales motion best and build a qualification scorecard with weighted criteria.

**Expected output:** A comparison of the three qualification frameworks, a recommended framework with rationale, and a qualification scorecard template for scoring and ranking deals.
**Pro tip:** Pick one framework and use it consistently across the team. Mixing frameworks confuses reps and makes pipeline data unreliable.

### 4.35 Enterprise Sales Process
**Use case:** Navigate the complex, multi-stakeholder sales cycles typical of enterprise deals.
**Best for:** Enterprise account executives, sales directors, and solutions consultants

Act as an enterprise sales strategist. Design an enterprise sales process for [company name] targeting organizations with [number] employees. Address the key challenges: multiple stakeholders with competing priorities, long evaluation cycles, procurement policies, security reviews, and legal negotiations. Build a stakeholder mapping framework, a multi-threaded engagement plan, and a stage by stage progression with milestones and exit criteria.

**Expected output:** An enterprise sales process with stakeholder mapping, multi-threaded engagement strategy, procurement and security review navigation, and stage progression criteria.
**Pro tip:** In enterprise sales, the person who wants your solution is rarely the person who signs the check. Map every stakeholder's agenda and sell to each one differently.

### 4.36 Channel and Reseller Sales Strategy
**Use case:** Build and manage a partner ecosystem that sells your product through indirect channels.
**Best for:** Channel sales managers, partnership leads, and VP of sales

Act as a channel sales strategist. Design a channel and reseller program for [company name]. Define partner types (resellers, referrals, affiliates, system integrators), recruitment criteria, onboarding process, training requirements, and tier structures. Build a partner compensation model (margin splits, bonuses, MDF funds) and a partner performance management system with quarterly business reviews.

**Expected output:** A channel sales strategy covering partner types, recruitment criteria, onboarding process, compensation model, and performance management framework.
**Pro tip:** Treat your top partners like an extension of your sales team. Give them leads, training, and support before you ask for more volume.

### 4.37 Sales Closing Techniques Comparison
**Use case:** Master multiple closing techniques and apply the right one for each deal scenario.
**Best for:** Account executives, sales managers, and sales enablement

Act as a sales closing specialist. Analyze [number] proven closing techniques: the assumptive close, the alternative choice close, the urgency close, the summary close, and the takeaway close. For each technique, describe when to use it, the exact phrasing, the psychological principle behind it, and the risks. Build a decision framework that helps reps choose the right close based on the buyer type and deal stage.

**Expected output:** A closing techniques guide with descriptions, scripts, psychological principles, scenario fit, and a decision tree for choosing the right close.
**Pro tip:** The best close is a natural next step that the prospect agrees to, not a dramatic ask. If you have done discovery right, closing should feel like a formality.

### 4.38 Sales Contract Negotiation
**Use case:** Negotiate terms, pricing, and contract structure to close deals while protecting margins.
**Best for:** Account executives, sales managers, and deal desk teams

Act as a sales negotiation coach. Build a negotiation framework for [company name]'s sales team. Define your walkaway point, target price, and concession currency (items you can trade that cost you little but feel valuable to the prospect). Create a negotiation playbook for common scenarios: price pushback, demands for extra services, requests for longer payment terms, and competitive threats. Include a discount approval workflow.

**Expected output:** A sales negotiation framework with walkaway points, concession planning, scenario playbooks, and a discount approval process.
**Pro tip:** Never give a concession without getting something in return. If they ask for a discount, ask for a longer contract term or a larger deal scope.

### 4.39 Upsell and Cross-Sell Strategy
**Use case:** Grow revenue from existing customers by expanding their usage and buying additional products.
**Best for:** Account managers, customer success teams, and sales leaders

Act as an expansion revenue strategist. Design an upsell and cross-sell program for [company name] targeting the existing customer base. Identify expansion triggers: usage milestones, contract renewals, new feature releases, organizational changes at the customer, or support requests that reveal unmet needs. Build a tiered approach: basic upsell (more seats), cross-sell (different product), and premium upsell (higher tier with more features). Train the team on timing and messaging for each approach.

**Expected output:** An upsell and cross-sell strategy with expansion triggers, tiered approaches, team training guidelines, and revenue expansion targets.
**Pro tip:** Ask your support team what customers complain about most. Those complaints are a roadmap of unmet needs that you can solve with an upsell.

### 4.40 Sales Territory Planning
**Use case:** Divide markets into territories that maximize coverage, minimize conflict, and balance rep earning potential.
**Best for:** Sales managers, revenue operations, and VP of sales

Act as a territory planning specialist. Design a territory plan for [company name]'s sales team of [number] reps covering [geographic region or market segment]. Use an account scoring methodology that balances account value, growth potential, and workload. Define rules for territory assignment, account handoffs, and lead routing. Include a territory review cadence and adjustment criteria for when rebalancing is needed.

**Expected output:** A territory plan with account assignments, scoring methodology, routing rules, and a quarterly review and adjustment process.
**Pro tip:** Let your top performers have input on their territory boundaries. If they feel the assignment is unfair, motivation drops and turnover follows.

## 5. Product Development & Technology Prompts (40 Prompts)

### 5.1 Product Strategy and Vision
**Use case:** Define product vision, strategy, and roadmap aligned with business goals
**Best for:** CPOs, product managers, founders

Act as a product strategy consultant. I need to define or refine my product strategy and vision.

Company: [describe company] | Product: [describe product] | Market: [target market, size] | Stage: [idea/early/growth/mature] | Competition: [competitive landscape] | Business goals: [revenue, users, engagement] | Resources: [team size, budget]

Create a product strategy framework. Product Vision — Define a compelling 5-year vision that describes the future you're creating. Google/Amazon press release technique: write the announcement of your product's future success. Strategic Pillars — 3-5 focus areas that support the vision (e.g., platform reliability, AI-powered features, vertical expansion, developer ecosystem). Market Definition — TAM, SAM, SOM. Target segment and beachhead market. Product Principles — 5-7 principles that guide product decisions (e.g., "simplicity over features," "secure by default," "fast over comprehensive"). Competitive Positioning — Differentiation strategy: what makes your product uniquely valuable. Positioning matrix vs competitors. Goals and OKRs — Annual product OKRs linked to business goals. North Star metric. Roadmap Framework — Now/Next/Later or 4-quarter roadmap. Themes over features. How to prioritize: RICE, weighted scoring, or opportunity solution trees. Innovation Approach — How you balance core improvements, adjacent opportunities, and transformational bets (70/20/25 model). Review Cadence — Quarterly strategy review, monthly roadmap review, weekly prioritization.

**Expected output:** Product strategy document with vision, pillars, roadmap framework, and OKRs
**Pro tip:** A product strategy is not a feature list — it's a set of choices about who you serve, what problems you solve, and what you deliberately won't do

### 5.2 Product Discovery and Validation
**Use case:** Validate product ideas before building to reduce wasted development
**Best for:** Product managers, founders, designers

Act as a product discovery expert. I need to validate a product idea before investing in development.

Product idea: [describe the concept] | Target users: [who would use it] | Problem: [what problem it solves] | Assumptions: [what must be true for success] | Resources: [available for discovery] | Timeline: [when you need to decide]

Design a product discovery process. Assumption Mapping — List all assumptions behind the product idea. Categorize as: Desirability (will people want it?), Viability (can we make money?), Feasibility (can we build it?). Prioritize by risk and uncertainty. Problem Discovery — Before testing solutions, validate the problem exists. Methods: user interviews (15-20 target users), ethnographic research, data analysis, support ticket review. The Mom Test framework. Solution Discovery — Once problem is validated: concept testing (show mockups, gauge interest), smoke test (pre-launch landing page with CTA), concierge test (deliver service manually), prototype testing (interactive prototype, observe usage). Experiment Design — For each assumption: hypothesis in If/Then format, minimum success criteria, experiment method, duration, sample size. Running lean experiments. Validation Metrics — Signal vs noise thresholds: willingness to pay, time spent using prototype, number of returning users, organic signups, NPS, qualitative feedback themes. Go/No-Go Decision — Did we meet success criteria? Critical assumptions confirmed? Known risks acceptable? Decision framework with clear criteria for proceed, pivot, or kill. Documentation — Discovery report: what we learned, key decisions, open questions, recommended next steps.

**Expected output:** Product discovery process with experiments, validation, and go/no-go criteria
**Pro tip:** The goal of product discovery is not to prove your idea is right — it's to learn the truth before you invest months building the wrong thing

### 5.3 User Research and Customer Insights
**Use case:** Build systematic user research capabilities for better product decisions
**Best for:** UX researchers, product managers, designers

Act as a user research specialist. I need to build a user research program for product decisions.

Product: [describe product] | Users: [target user base] | Research maturity: [none/occasional/structured] | Team: [available for research] | Tooling: [current research tools] | Questions: [what decisions need research input]

Design a user research program. Research Cadence — Continuous (ongoing): analytics, NPS, support ticket analysis. Monthly: usability tests (3-5 users), customer interviews (4-6 per month). Quarterly: strategic research (persona updates, JTBD deep dive), surveys. Research Methods — Generative (discover needs): user interviews, diary studies, field studies, contextual inquiry. Evaluative (test solutions): usability testing, A/B testing, prototype testing, heuristic evaluation. Behavioral (what people do): analytics, session recording, funnel analysis, cohort analysis. Attitudinal (what people say): surveys, NPS, customer interviews. Participant Recruitment — Source users: in-app recruitment, email outreach, social media, panels, user interviews service. Screening criteria. Incentives: $50-100 for 30-min session, $100-200 for 60-min session. Session Design — For interviews: 6-8 questions, open-ended, avoid leading questions. For usability tests: 5-8 tasks, think-aloud protocol, measure success rate and time on task. Analysis and Synthesis — Affinity mapping, themes identification, journey mapping, opportunity sizing. Research repository for findings. Insights Communication — One-page research summaries: what we learned, implications, recommendations. Video highlight reels for emotional impact. Insight presentation with decision recommendations. Research Repository — Central location for all research artifacts: interview transcripts, reports, journey maps, personas. Tagged and searchable. Democratization — Self-serve research toolkit: screening survey templates, discussion guides, usability test scripts, analysis templates.

**Expected output:** User research program with methods, cadence, recruitment, and insight communication
**Pro tip:** Watch 5 users struggle with the same thing and you'll never ship that feature without fixing it — research is cheapest when it prevents expensive mistakes

### 5.4 Technical Architecture Design
**Use case:** Design scalable, maintainable technical architecture for product development
**Best for:** CTOs, engineering leads, architects

Act as a software architect. I need to design or review the technical architecture for a product.

Product: [describe product] | Scale: [expected users/data] | Team: [team size, skill set] | Current stack: [existing technology] | Constraints: [budget, timeline, legacy systems] | Requirements: [functional and non-functional]

Design a technical architecture. Architecture Principles — Define guiding principles: modularity, scalability, security, maintainability, cost-efficiency, testability. Example: "Everything should be deployable independently." System Design — High-level architecture: client layer (web, mobile, API), application layer (services, microservices, serverless), data layer (databases, caches, queues), infrastructure (cloud, CDN, DNS). Diagram description. Technology Stack Selection — Choose: frontend framework, backend language/framework, database (SQL vs NoSQL), caching, message queue, cloud provider. Justification for each choice based on requirements. Data Architecture — Data model design, database choice (relational, document, graph, time-series), data flow, ETL/ELT pipelines, data warehouse/lake. Security Architecture — Authentication (JWT, OAuth, SSO), authorization (RBAC, ABAC), data encryption (at rest and in transit), API security, vulnerability management. Scalability Design — Horizontal vs vertical scaling, stateless services, database sharding/replication, CDN strategy, caching layers, auto-scaling configuration. Deployment Architecture — CI/CD pipeline design, containerization (Docker), orchestration (Kubernetes), environment management (dev/staging/prod), blue-green or canary deployments. Observability — Logging (structured), metrics (business and technical), tracing, alerting, dashboards. Error handling strategy. Cost Architecture — Cost estimation per component, optimization opportunities, reserved instances, serverless vs container trade-offs.

**Expected output:** Technical architecture document with stack, design decisions, scalability, and cost considerations
**Pro tip:** The best architecture is the one that's good enough for today's needs but easy to change tomorrow — over-engineering for scale you won't see for years is technical debt

### 5.5 Agile Development Process Design
**Use case:** Implement or improve agile development practices for engineering teams
**Best for:** Engineering managers, Scrum Masters, team leads

Act as an agile coach. I need to implement or improve agile practices for my development team.

Team size: [developers per team] | Current process: [waterfall/agile/hybrid/none] | Pain points: [missed deadlines, poor quality, low velocity] | Organization: [startup/small team/enterprise] | Remote/hybrid: [team distribution] | Tools: [current tools]

Design an agile development process. Methodology Selection — Scrum (best for product teams with clear priorities), Kanban (best for ops/support/continuous flow), Scrumban (hybrid). Recommendation with rationale. Scrum Framework — Sprints: 1-2 week cycles, sprint planning (2 hours), daily standup (15 min), sprint review (1 hour), retrospective (1 hour). Roles: Product Owner, Scrum Master, Development Team. Artifact definitions. Ceremony Guide — Daily Standup: what did I do yesterday, what will I do today, what's blocking me. No problem-solving in standup. Sprint Planning: capacity planning, story selection, task breakdown. Retrospective: start/stop/continue, action items with owners. Estimation — Story points vs hours. Planning poker. Relative estimation. Velocity tracking. Estimation accuracy vs consistency. Definition of Ready — Criteria for taking work into sprint: clear acceptance criteria, dependencies identified, design reviewed, estimated, feasible in one sprint. Definition of Done — Criteria for completing work: code reviewed, tested (unit, integration), documented, deployed to staging, acceptance criteria met. Scrum Master responsibilities — Facilitate ceremonies, remove blockers, coach the team, protect the team from interruption, improve process continuously.

**Expected output:** Agile development process with methodology, ceremonies, estimation, and definitions
**Pro tip:** Agile is not about going faster — it's about reducing the cost of changing direction. If your process makes it hard to pivot, you're doing it wrong

### 5.6 Code Review and Quality Standards
**Use case:** Establish code review practices that improve quality and team skills
**Best for:** Engineering managers, tech leads, senior engineers

Act as a code quality consultant. I need to establish or improve code review practices.

Team: [number of developers] | Languages: [primary languages] | Current reviews: [none/ad-hoc/formal] | Codebase age: [new/growing/mature] | Pain points: [bugs, slow reviews, inconsistent standards] | Tools: [current tools]

Design a code review system. Review Standards — What every review checks: correctness, security, performance, test coverage, style guidelines, documentation, error handling, edge cases. Checklist format for consistency. Review Process — Who reviews: minimum 1 approval for standard PRs, 2 for critical paths (auth, payments, data). PR size limits: max 400 lines, break larger changes into logical PRs. PR template. Review Cadence — SLA: initial review within 4 hours during business hours, 24 hours max for non-urgent. Merge button enabled only after approval. Automation — Automated checks before human review: linting, formatting, type checking, unit tests, security scanning (SAST), code coverage thresholds. Blocking vs advisory gates. Review Etiquette — Comments as suggestions, not orders. "What do you think about..." vs "This is wrong." Explain reasoning. Separate style preferences from functional issues. Approve vs Request Changes guidelines. Reviewer Rotation — Rotate reviewers to spread knowledge: primary reviewer (team lead or senior), secondary reviewer (rotating). No single reviewer bottleneck. Knowledge Sharing — Reviews as teaching moments: add comments explaining better approaches, link to documentation, suggest patterns. Junior dev paired with senior reviewer. Metrics — Track: time to first review, time to merge, review depth (comments per review), re-review rate, bug escape rate.

**Expected output:** Code review system with standards, process, automation, and metrics
**Pro tip:** The purpose of code review is not to catch bugs — tests should catch bugs. Code review is about knowledge sharing, design feedback, and team standards

### 5.7 Testing Strategy and Automation
**Use case:** Build a comprehensive testing strategy that ensures quality without slowing development
**Best for:** QA leads, engineering managers, tech leads

Act as a testing strategist. I need to build a comprehensive testing strategy for my product.

Product: [describe product — web, mobile, API, etc.] | Team: [developer and QA size] | Current testing: [what exists] | Quality issues: [common bugs, regressions] | Release frequency: [how often you ship] | Compliance: [any regulatory testing needs]

Design a testing strategy. Test Pyramid — Unit tests (70%, fast, isolated), Integration tests (20%, service boundaries), E2E tests (10%, critical user journeys). Focus on speed and reliability at each level. Unit Testing — What to test: business logic, utility functions, data transformations, edge cases. What not to test: framework behavior, constants, generated code. Coverage target: 80%+ for business logic. Integration Testing — Test: API contracts, database queries, service interactions, external service mocking. Use test containers for dependencies. E2E Testing — Critical paths only: signup flow, purchase flow, core feature flows. 10-15 critical journeys. Less is more for E2E (slow and flaky). Test Automation — CI pipeline integration: unit + integration on every PR commit, E2E on merge to main, nightly full suite. Test parallelization for speed. Flaky Test Management — Identify and quarantine flaky tests immediately. Dedicated sprint time to fix flaky tests. Ban flaky tests from blocking CI. Test Data Management — Factory/generator pattern for test data. Dedicated test database. Clean state between tests. Test environment management. Performance Testing — Load testing for critical APIs, stress testing for peak events, identify performance regressions. Tools: k6, Locust, JMeter. Testing Culture — Developers own test writing. QA focuses on exploratory testing and test strategy. Bug bash events before major releases. Testability as a design requirement.

**Expected output:** Testing strategy with pyramid, automation, performance testing, and quality culture
**Pro tip:** A test that fails randomly is worse than no test at all — it erodes trust in the entire test suite. Fix or delete flaky tests immediately

### 5.8 DevOps and Infrastructure Strategy
**Use case:** Build DevOps practices and infrastructure for reliable product delivery
**Best for:** DevOps engineers, CTOs, infrastructure leads

Act as a DevOps consultant. I need to build or improve DevOps practices and infrastructure.

Team: [dev team size] | Current infrastructure: [cloud provider, setup] | Current deployment: [manual/automated/frequency] | Pain points: [slow deployments, downtime, configuration drift] | Compliance: [SOC2, HIPAA, etc.]

Design a DevOps and infrastructure strategy. CI/CD Pipeline — Continuous Integration: build, lint, test on every commit. Artifact versioning. Continuous Deployment: automated deploy to dev/staging, manual gate to production. Pipeline as code (GitHub Actions, GitLab CI). Infrastructure as Code — Terraform or Pulumi for cloud resources. Configuration management (Ansible, Chef). Immutable infrastructure. Version-controlled infrastructure. Containerization — Docker for all services. Dockerfile best practices: multi-stage builds, minimal base images, no root user, security scanning. Environment Management — Dev: shared environment for development. Staging: production-like for final validation. Production: HA, multi-region. Environment parity principle. Monitoring and Observability — Metrics (CPU, memory, request rate, error rate, latency). Logging (centralized, structured). Tracing (distributed tracing for microservices). Alerting (on-call rotation, escalation policy). Incident Response — On-call schedule, incident severity levels, response SLAs, post-mortem process blameless culture. Security — Secrets management (vault, AWS Secrets Manager). Vulnerability scanning (container images, dependencies). Network security (VPC, security groups, WAF). Access control (IAM, least privilege). Disaster Recovery — Backup strategy (RPO/RTO goals), cross-region failover, backup restoration testing (quarterly). Cost Management — Resource tagging, right-sizing, reserved instances, auto-scaling, waste elimination. Cost monitoring and budgets.

**Expected output:** DevOps strategy with CI/CD, IaC, monitoring, security, and disaster recovery
**Pro tip:** DevOps is not a role or a tool — it's a culture where developers own their code from laptop to production. If you have a separate team "doing DevOps," you're doing it wrong

### 5.9 API Design and Development
**Use case:** Design robust, developer-friendly APIs following industry best practices
**Best for:** Backend engineers, API designers, platform teams

Act as an API design expert. I need to design or improve an API for my product.

API type: [REST/GraphQL/gRPC] | Consumers: [internal/external/partner] | Use cases: [what the API needs to do] | Scale: [expected requests/second] | Current API: [if redesign, what exists] | Standards: [versioning, auth, docs requirements]

Design an API development framework. API Design Principles — Consistency, predictability, statelessness, resource-orientation (REST), proper HTTP methods and status codes. Design-first approach (spec before implementation). RESTful Design — URL structure: /resources, /resources/:id, /resources/:id/sub-resources. Query params for filtering, sorting, pagination. Request/response formats (JSON:API or similar standard). Versioning — URL versioning (/v1/, /v2/) vs header versioning. When to version vs extend. Deprecation policy with timeline. Authentication and Authorization — API key, JWT, OAuth 2.0. Rate limiting per key. Scoped permissions. Error Handling — Consistent error format: error code, message, details, trace ID. HTTP status codes correctly used. Meaningful error messages that help developers debug. Pagination — Cursor-based vs offset-based. Default page size, max page size. Pagination metadata in response. Documentation — OpenAPI/Swagger specification as source of truth. Interactive docs (Swagger UI). Getting started guide. Authentication examples. SDK availability. Testing — Contract testing (Pact), integration tests, load tests. API mock server for consumers. Developer Experience — Consistent naming conventions, predictable patterns, complete examples, client libraries/SDKs, changelog, migration guides. Versioning, backwards compatibility, deprecation policies.

**Expected output:** API design framework with standards, security, documentation, and DX best practices
**Pro tip:** Your API is your product if you're a platform — and it's your integration contract if you're not. Either way, design it as if your business depends on developers loving it

### 5.10 Mobile App Development Strategy
**Use case:** Plan and execute mobile app development — native vs cross-platform decisions
**Best for:** Mobile leads, product managers, CTOs

Act as a mobile development strategist. I need to make technical decisions and build a plan for my mobile app.

App type: [consumer/enterprise/internal] | Target platforms: [iOS, Android, both] | Key features: [core functionality] | Timeline: [deadline for launch] | Team: [available mobile developers] | Budget: [development budget] | Performance needs: [high/low]

Create a mobile development strategy. Platform Decision — Native (Swift/SwiftUI for iOS, Kotlin/Jetpack for Android) vs Cross-platform (Flutter, React Native). Decision matrix with: performance, UI fidelity, code sharing, team skills, time to market, maintenance cost. Tech Stack Selection — For build: language, framework, state management, navigation, networking, local storage. For testing: unit, widget, integration, E2E. Architecture — Clean Architecture / MVVM pattern. Separation of concerns. Repository pattern for data access. Offline-first approach where applicable. CI/CD for Mobile — Build pipeline, code signing management (manual pain point), beta distribution (TestFlight, Play Console), app store deployment automation. Testing Strategy — Unit tests (business logic), widget tests (UI components), integration tests (flows), device lab testing (real devices), beta testing with analytics. Performance — App startup time (target <2s), UI responsiveness (60fps), memory management, network optimization, image caching, native performance profiling. App Store Optimization (ASO) — App name, subtitle, keywords, description, screenshots, preview videos, ratings and reviews management. Build variant strategy.

**Expected output:** Mobile development strategy with platform decision, architecture, CI/CD, and ASO
**Pro tip:** For most B2B apps, a well-designed responsive web app serves 90% of needs at 30% of the cost of native — only go native when you need camera, Bluetooth, offline, or AR

### 5.11 Platform and Ecosystem Strategy
**Use case:** Build a platform strategy that enables third-party developers and partners
**Best for:** Platform PMs, CTOs, product leaders

Act as a platform strategy consultant. I need to develop a platform and ecosystem strategy.

Product: [core product] | Current API: [any existing API] | Developer audience: [who would build on your platform] | Business goals: [ecosystem growth, revenue, stickiness] | Competition: [competing platforms] | Resources: [available for platform development]

Design a platform strategy. Platform Definition — What does "platform" mean for your product: API access, plugin architecture, marketplace, extension ecosystem? Define scope and value proposition for developers. Platform Architecture — API design, webhooks, SDKs, developer portal, plugin/extension architecture, marketplace infrastructure. Developer Experience (DX) — Onboarding experience: signup flow, API keys, first-call experience, SDK installation, getting started guide. Time to first "hello world" should be <15 minutes. Developer Portal — Documentation site, API reference, guides and tutorials, SDK downloads, API status page, community forum, changelog. Governance — What can partners build? Approval process for marketplace listings. Content guidelines. Quality standards. API versioning and deprecation policy. Monetization — Revenue models: API usage pricing (per-request, tiers), revenue sharing on marketplace (15-30%), white-label licensing, premium support tiers. Ecosystem Growth — Launch strategy: design partners, beta developers, launch partners. Recruitment channels: hackathons, conferences, content marketing, referral program. Developer Relations — DevRel team: developer advocacy, community management, content creation, event presence, support. KPIs. Success Metrics — Developer signups, active developers, apps/plugins published, API usage volume, marketplace revenue, developer NPS, time to first successful API call.

**Expected output:** Platform strategy covering architecture, DX, governance, monetization, and ecosystem growth
**Pro tip:** A platform is only valuable if the core product is already successful — don't try to build a platform to compensate for a weak product

### 5.12 Innovation Sprints and Hackathons
**Use case:** Design and run innovation sprints and hackathons that generate real value
**Best for:** Innovation leads, engineering managers, product teams

Act as an innovation program designer. I need to plan and run effective innovation sprints or hackathons.

Company: [describe business] | Team: [who participates] | Duration: [1-5 days] | Frequency: [quarterly/bi-annual/annual] | Goals: [new ideas, team bonding, prototyping] | Constraints: [budget, time, resources]

Design an innovation sprint/hackathon program. Event Types — Internal Hackathon (teams build anything, 24-48 hours), Themed Sprint (specific challenge or theme), Innovation Sprint (structured process: discover > ideate > prototype > test over 5 days), Fix-It Week (focus on tech debt, bugs, performance). Preparation (2-4 weeks before) — Theme selection, challenge definition, logistics (venue, food, tools), team formation (cross-functional encouraged), project proposals (pre-submitted). Rules and Guidelines — What's in bounds, intellectual property (company owns what's built), judging criteria (innovation, business value, technical quality, user impact, completeness), prize categories. Support During Event — Mentors available, technical support, business advisors, design help, lunch & learn sessions, checkpoints. Judging and Awards — Judging panel (execs, product leaders, external), pitch format (3 min demo + 2 min Q&A), prize categories: Grand Prize, Best Technical Achievement, Most Valuable to Customers, Best Design, People's Choice. Post-Event Follow-Up (critical, often missed) — Which projects go to prototype stage, which get dedicated resources, timeline for incubation, participants updated on progress. Metrics — Ideas generated, prototypes built, projects that shipped, employee engagement score, cross-team collaboration.

**Expected output:** Innovation sprint/hackathon program with preparation, execution, judging, and post-event follow-through
**Pro tip:** Hackathons that don't ship anything are just expensive parties — the real value is in the 10% of projects that make it to production. Design the event for that outcome

### 5.13 Technical Debt Management
**Use case:** Identify, prioritize, and systematically reduce technical debt
**Best for:** Engineering managers, CTOs, tech leads

Act as a technical debt management consultant. I need to build a system to identify and reduce technical debt.

Team: [dev team size] | Codebase age: [years in production] | Current tech debt: [known issues] | Impact: [velocity, bugs, morale] | Business context: [growth stage, release pressure] | Tools: [static analysis, code quality tools]

Design a technical debt management system. Debt Categories — Code quality (duplication, complexity, dead code), Architecture (coupling, lack of modularity, missing abstractions), Infrastructure (manual processes, outdated dependencies, poor CI/CD), Test debt (low coverage, flaky tests, slow test suite), Documentation (missing or outdated docs). Quantifying Debt — Effort to fix (S/M/L/XL) vs Impact (velocity, bugs, risk, developer frustration). Create a technical debt register with scores. Prioritization — Priority 1 (immediate): security vulnerabilities, data loss risk, blocking development. Priority 2 (this quarter): major velocity impediments, frequent bug sources. Priority 3 (this year): code quality, test debt, documentation. Allocation Model — Allocate 20% of capacity to debt reduction (the standard recommendation). Alternatively: "Scout Rule" (leave code better than you found it). Payment Strategies — Dedicated sprints (every 4th sprint is debt sprint), incremental (fix what you touch), big refactors (dedicated team for major rewrites), automated enforcement (linters, CI gates, architecture tests). Prevention — Standards (coding standards, architecture decision records), Automation (enforced by CI, not human review), Architecture (modular design limits debt propagation), Culture (talk about debt openly, don't blame). Communication to Business — Explain tech debt in business terms: "This is like paying credit card interest. We're paying 20% extra on every feature because of past shortcuts. Investing in reducing it will speed up all future work."

**Expected output:** Technical debt management system with quantification, prioritization, allocation, and prevention
**Pro tip:** Not all technical debt is bad — intentional debt taken to hit a market deadline can be a smart business decision. The problem is unacknowledged debt that compounds silently

### 5.14 Product Analytics Implementation
**Use case:** Implement product analytics to understand user behavior and drive decisions
**Best for:** Product managers, data analysts, growth teams

Act as a product analytics consultant. I need to implement or improve product analytics.

Product: [web/mobile/SaaS] | Current analytics: [what you track] | Tools: [current tools] | Team: [analytics capabilities] | Business model: [how you make money] | Key questions: [what you need to learn]

Design a product analytics implementation. Tracking Plan — Define events: page views, feature usage, key actions (signup, purchase, share), milestones (activation, retention trigger). Event naming convention (Object + Verb format: user.signed_up, order.completed). Properties per event. Instrumentation — Manual (code SDK) vs auto-track (tools like Heap, Mixology). Recommended: manual for key events, auto-track for exploration. Data layer design. User Identification — Anonymous user > identified user. Cross-device identity resolution. User properties for segmentation (plan type, signup date, cohort). Key Dashboards — Daily Active Users (DAU/MAU), User Growth (new users, total users), Activation Rate (% who reach key milestone), Retention Curve (% returning by day/week/month), Feature Adoption (% using each feature). North Star metric dashboard. Analysis Types — Cohort Analysis (how behavior changes by signup date), Funnel Analysis (conversion at each step), Retention Analysis (how many come back), Segmentation (behavior by user type), Behavioral Cohorts (users who did X vs Y). Activation Metrics — Define "aha moment": what behavior correlates with long-term retention. Time to activation. Activation flow analysis. Retention Analysis — Day 1, 7, 30 retention. Weekly retention curve. Compare cohorts (before/after feature changes). Features that drive retention. Experimentation — A/B test framework: hypothesis, minimum sample size, duration, success metric, statistical significance. Tool selection: LaunchDarkly, Optimizely, custom. Governance — Data privacy (PII handling), event governance (no event sprawl), naming conventions, documentation, access controls.

**Expected output:** Product analytics implementation with tracking plan, dashboards, analysis frameworks
**Pro tip:** Tracking everything is almost as useless as tracking nothing — focus on the 20% of events that answer 80% of your product questions

### 5.15 Release Management Process
**Use case:** Design a release management process for predictable, low-risk deployments
**Best for:** Engineering managers, DevOps, release managers

Act as a release management consultant. I need to design a release management process.

Team: [development team size] | Release frequency: [daily/weekly/monthly] | Product: [web/mobile/desktop/SaaS] | Environment: [cloud vs on-prem] | Compliance: [any regulatory requirements] | Current process: [strengths and pains]

Design a release management process. Release Types — Major (new features, breaking changes): version bump, release notes, coordinated marketing. Minor (features, improvements): version bump, release notes. Patch (bug fixes, hotfixes): expedited process, limited scope. Release Cadence — Recommended: continuous deployment for SaaS (multiple deploys daily), weekly releases for mobile (app store review), monthly for on-prem/enterprise. Find the cadence that matches your risk tolerance and customer expectations. Release Process — Development > Code Review > Merge > Automated Testing (unit, integration, E2E) > Staging Deploy > QA Verification > Stakeholder Review > Production Deploy > Post-Deploy Monitoring. Gate criteria at each stage. Versioning — Semantic Versioning (MAJOR.MINOR.PATCH). Pre-release identifiers for beta/RC. Tag releases in git. Changelogs. Release Gates — Automated gates: tests pass (100%), code coverage meets threshold, security scan passes, performance tests pass, no critical bugs open. Manual gates: QA signoff, product owner approval, compliance approval (if needed). Rollback Strategy — Automated rollback triggers: error rate spike, latency increase, business metric decline. Rollback process: revert code, revert DB migration if needed, notify team. Blue-green or canary deployments for zero-downtime rollback. Release Communication — Release notes: user-facing (new features, improvements, fixes) and technical (API changes, deprecations, migration steps). Communication channels: in-app notification, blog, email, Slack. Release Post-Mortem — For problematic releases: what went wrong, detection time, resolution time, prevention measures. Continuous improvement.

**Expected output:** Release management process with release types, gates, rollback, and communication
**Pro tip:** The best release process is invisible — if customers don't notice you deployed, and your team doesn't dread release day, you've nailed it

### 5.16 Building a Product Roadmap
**Use case:** Create and communicate a product roadmap that aligns team and stakeholders
**Best for:** Product managers, CPOs, founders

Act as a product roadmap consultant. I need to build a product roadmap that communicates strategy and priorities.

Product: [describe product] | Strategy: [product strategy] | Stakeholders: [who needs to see/approve] | Team: [engineering size] | Time horizon: [3/6/12 months] | Constraints: [resources, dependencies, commitments]

Create a product roadmap framework. Roadmap Formats — Theme-based (best for strategy communication): themes as outcomes, not features. Now/Next/Later (best for internal alignment): time-agnostic, shows priority buckets. Timeline (best for executive/stakeholder comms): quarterly with key milestones. Choose based on audience. Input Sources — Customer feedback (interviews, support tickets, NPS), data insights (analytics, usage patterns), competitive intelligence, company goals/OKRs, technical needs (debt, architecture), stakeholder input (sales, execs). Prioritization Framework — RICE Score: Reach (how many users affected), Impact (how much impact — 0.25x to 3x multiplier), Confidence (how sure — 0.5x, 1x, 1.5x), Effort (person-months or story points). Alternative: Value vs Effort matrix, Weighted scoring, Opportunity scoring. Stakeholder Management — How to handle: exec requests ("that's an important input, let me evaluate against other priorities"), sales requests ("I hear that customer need, here's where it fits"), "urgent" requests (evaluate against current sprint priorities). Saying no respectfully. Communication Cadence — Monthly internal roadmap update, quarterly roadmap review with stakeholders, bi-annual public roadmap (if applicable). Communication formats: all-hands presentation, written update, visual roadmap. Risk Management — On roadmap: identify dependencies, risky bets, assumptions to validate. Contingency plans. What we'll de-prioritize if things slip. Roadmap as Hypothesis — Frame roadmap items as experiments: "We believe [feature] will drive [metric] because [reason]. We'll validate by [measurement]."

**Expected output:** Product roadmap with prioritization, stakeholder management, and communication plan
**Pro tip:** A roadmap is not a commitment — it's a communication tool that shows your current best thinking about what will create the most value. Update it when you learn something new

### 5.17 Cross-Functional Product Development Process
**Use case:** Design a product development process that aligns design, engineering, and product
**Best for:** Product leaders, design managers, engineering managers

Act as a product development process designer. I need to improve how design, product, and engineering collaborate.

Team: [PMs, designers, engineers] | Current process: [what works, what doesn't] | Pain points: [handoff friction, misalignment, rework] | Product type: [web/mobile/platform] | Team culture: [collaborative/siloed]

Design a cross-functional product development process. Process Stages — Discover (research, problem definition, opportunity sizing) > Define (solution design, spec, design review) > Develop (sprint execution, standups, check-ins) > Deliver (QA, release, launch) > Debrief (metrics review, retrospective). Phase gates with cross-functional sign-off. Dual-Track Development — Discovery track (ongoing: research, prototyping, validation) + Delivery track (sprint execution). Discovery is 1-2 sprints ahead of delivery. Keeps the backlog validated. Collaborative Design — Design specs: prototypes, user flows, states (empty, error, loading, edge cases), design annotations. Design review: PM + engineering review before handoff. Handoff process with DevReady checklist. Sprint Rhythm — Planning: PM presents prioritized features with success metrics. Refinement: stories with clear acceptance criteria. Review: demo working software. Retrospective: continuous improvement. Standups: identify blockers early. Communication Templates — Feature Spec Template: problem statement, success metrics, scope, design mocks, technical considerations, open questions. PRD Template: background, user stories, acceptance criteria, design links, dependencies. Conflict Resolution — When design says one thing, engineering pushes back, and PM needs to decide: framework for decisions (user impact, development cost, timeline, strategic alignment). Escalation process. Post-Launch Review — 4 weeks after launch: did we move the metric? What did we learn? What would we do differently? Close the loop on the hypothesis.

**Expected output:** Cross-functional product development process with dual-track, ceremonies, and communication
**Pro tip:** The handoff between design and engineering is where most product schedule risk lives — invest in design specs that leave no ambiguity about states, edge cases, and behaviors

### 5.18 Design System Development
**Use case:** Build and maintain a design system for consistent, efficient product development
**Best for:** Design leads, frontend engineers, product teams

Act as a design system consultant. I need to build or scale a design system for my organization.

Product: [web/mobile/platform] | Team: [designers and developers] | Current components: [any existing design system] | Tech stack: [frontend framework] | Maturity: [none/atomic library/design-led] | Goals: [consistency, speed, brand cohesion]

Create a design system strategy. Naming and Organization — Atomic Design methodology: Atoms (buttons, inputs, icons), Molecules (form fields, search bars, cards), Organisms (headers, footers, product lists), Templates (page layouts), Pages (specific instances). Component Catalog — Priority order for building: Typography, Color system, Spacing (grid), Buttons, Forms (inputs, selects, checkboxes, toggles), Navigation, Modals, Notifications, Data display (tables, lists, charts). Design Tokens — Design decisions stored as data: colors, typography scale, spacing units, shadows, breakpoints, animation timing. Tokens bridge design and code. Documentation — Component documentation: purpose, props, states (default, hover, active, disabled, loading, empty, error), usage guidelines, code examples, accessibility notes, related components. Tooling — Figma (design), Storybook (development), Token management (Theo, Style Dictionary). Design-Dev workflow: Figma > Design Tokens > Code Components. Accessibility — Every component must meet WCAG 2.1 AA minimum. Focus states, color contrast, keyboard navigation, screen reader support. Accessibility checklist per component. Contribution Model — Centralized (design system team builds everything): fastest, but creates bottleneck. Federated (teams contribute): more scalable, consistency risk. Hybrid (core team builds primitives, teams build compositions). Versioning and Release — Semantic versioning for design system. Breaking changes communicated with migration guide. Release notes with visual changes. Adoption strategy for updates. Governance — Who approves new components, how to request, review process, deprecation policy. Design review for component additions.

**Expected output:** Design system strategy with component catalog, tokens, documentation, and governance
**Pro tip:** A design system isn't a project with an end date — it's a product for your internal teams with its own backlog, roadmap, and maintenance budget

### 5.19 Analytics and Data Infrastructure
**Use case:** Build analytics infrastructure that enables data-driven decisions across the company
**Best for:** Data engineers, CTOs, analytics leads

Act as a data infrastructure architect. I need to build or improve analytics data infrastructure.

Company: [describe business] | Data sources: [product, marketing, sales, finance, customer] | Volume: [data size and growth] | Current setup: [tools and processes] | Users: [who needs data — analysts, PMs, execs] | Use cases: [reporting, dashboards, ML, ad-hoc analysis]

Design analytics data infrastructure. Data Stack Architecture — Source > ELT/ETL > Warehouse > Transformation > BI/Analytics. Modern stack: Fivetran/Airbyte (ingestion), dbt (transformation), Snowflake/BigQuery/Redshift (warehouse), Looker/Mode/Metabase (BI), Census/Hightouch (reverse ETL). Data Modeling — Star Schema (facts and dimensions) for business analytics. dbt for transformations: staging > intermediate > marts. Naming conventions. Incremental vs full refresh models. Data Quality — Testing framework: uniqueness, not-null, referential integrity, accepted values, freshness. dbt tests or Great Expectations. Data quality dashboard. Data governance. Data Warehouse Design — Raw layer (source data, unchanged), Staging (cleaned, typed), Marts (business-facing, modeled for consumption). Partitioning and clustering strategy for performance. Pipeline Management — Orchestration (Airflow, Dagster): scheduling, dependency management, error handling, alerting, retries. SLA monitoring. Self-Serve Analytics — Semantic layer (tool-agnostic business definitions). Metric store (Dimitri or similar). Looker LookML or dbt metrics for consistent definitions. Analyst workflow guide. Data Catalog — Dataset documentation, owner, freshness, description, sample queries. Data lineage (where data comes from, how it's transformed). Access management. Reverse ETL — Sync modeled data back to operational tools: CRM (sales activity), marketing (user segments), support (customer data). Enables operational data use.

**Expected output:** Analytics data infrastructure design with stack, modeling, quality, and self-serve capabilities
**Pro tip:** The most expensive part of a data stack isn't the tools — it's the time analysts spend cleaning inconsistent data. Invest in data quality at the source

### 5.20 Machine Learning Integration Strategy
**Use case:** Plan and implement ML/AI features in your product effectively
**Best for:** ML engineers, CTOs, product managers

Act as an AI/ML strategy consultant. I need to integrate machine learning capabilities into my product.

Product: [describe product] | Data available: [what data you have for ML] | Use cases: [potential ML applications] | Team: [ML expertise available] | Infrastructure: [current technical setup] | Maturity: [no ML experience vs experienced]

Create an ML integration roadmap. Opportunity Identification — Where ML adds value: personalization (recommendations, content ranking), prediction (churn, LTV, demand), automation (classification, extraction, routing), optimization (pricing, scheduling), insights (anomaly detection, pattern recognition). Prioritize by impact and feasibility. Data Readiness Assessment — Do you have labeled data? Data volume (thousands, millions), data quality (completeness, accuracy), data access (pipeline, privacy). Data is the #1 success factor for ML. ML Maturity Model — Level 1 (Manual): decisions based on rules/heuristics. Level 2 (Assisted): ML provides recommendations, humans decide. Level 3 (Augmented): ML takes action, humans oversee. Level 4 (Autonomous): ML operates independently with guardrails. ML Stack — Training: Python, PyTorch/TensorFlow, Jupyter notebooks, experiment tracking (MLflow, Weights & Biases). Serving: model serving infrastructure (SageMaker, TorchServe, BentoML), feature store (Feast), model registry. MLOps — Pipeline automation, model versioning, A/B testing framework, monitoring (data drift, model decay), retraining cadence, rollback capability. Feature Engineering — Feature store: centralized, consistent features for training and serving. Real-time vs batch features. Feature creation pipeline. Model Evaluation — Offline metrics (accuracy, precision, recall, F1, AUC) vs Online metrics (business impact). Validation framework. Avoiding common pitfalls: data leakage, overfitting, imbalanced data. Ethics and Governance — Bias detection and mitigation. Model explainability (SHAP, LIME). Privacy (data minimization, differential privacy). Human-in-the-loop for high-stakes decisions.

**Expected output:** ML integration roadmap with opportunity assessment, data readiness, MLOps, and ethics
**Pro tip:** Start with simple heuristic or rule-based systems before ML — most products benefit more from good rules than bad ML, and you'll learn what data matters

### 5.21 Product Roadmap Prioritization Framework
**Use case:** Prioritize and sequence features for your product roadmap.
**Best for:** Product managers, CPOs, startup founders

Act as a senior product strategist. You have a list of [10-15 feature ideas] for [product name] targeting [target audience]. Apply the RICE scoring model (Reach, Impact, Confidence, Effort) to rank each feature. For each item, estimate the metric, explain your reasoning, and produce a prioritized roadmap for the next [3 months]. Flag any dependencies or sequencing conflicts between features.

**Expected output:** A ranked feature priority list with RICE scores, rationale, and a phased 3-month delivery timeline.
**Pro tip:** Run this prompt each quarter with updated confidence scores to keep your roadmap dynamic instead of static.

### 5.22 Feature Request Triage System
**Use case:** Systematically evaluate and route incoming feature requests.
**Best for:** Product managers, customer success leads, product ops

Act as a product operations manager. You receive [15-20 feature requests] from customers, sales, and support channels for [product name]. Categorize each request by type (new feature, enhancement, bug workaround, integration), assign a severity score based on [customer tier] and [frequency of request], and recommend whether to accept, defer, or decline. For accepted items, draft a brief product brief with success criteria.

**Expected output:** A triaged feature request table with category, severity score, disposition, and a one-paragraph product brief for each accepted item.
**Pro tip:** Create a shared scoring rubric beforehand so stakeholders understand why some requests get prioritized over others.

### 5.23 A/B Testing Plan for Product Features
**Use case:** Design and document A/B tests for proposed product changes.
**Best for:** Product managers, growth product managers, data analysts

Act as a growth experiment designer. You are testing [new feature or UI change] in [product name] to improve [primary metric, e.g., conversion rate]. Define the hypothesis, variant design, sample size calculation (minimum [X] users per variant), duration ([Y] days), success metrics, guardrail metrics (things that must not degrade), and a stop condition for statistical significance at [Z]% confidence. Include a ramp plan starting at [5%] traffic.

**Expected output:** A complete A/B test plan document with hypothesis, design, sample size, metrics, guardrails, and ramp schedule.
**Pro tip:** Always define guardrail metrics before the test starts to avoid cherry-picking results after launch.

### 5.24 Product Analytics Dashboard Setup
**Use case:** Define key events, properties, and dashboard layouts for product analytics.
**Best for:** Product managers, data analysts, growth leads

Act as a product analytics consultant. You are setting up analytics for [product name], a [B2B/B2C] product with [key user flows]. Define the top [10] events to track, required properties per event (user properties, event properties), and [5] core dashboards: acquisition, activation, retention, revenue, and referral. For each dashboard, specify the chart type, metric definition, and segment filters (by [plan type], [cohort date], [user source]).

**Expected output:** A tracking plan spreadsheet schema and 5-dashboard layout specification with event definitions, properties, and segment filters.
**Pro tip:** Over-track early and de-duplicate later you can always remove noisy events, but you cannot retroactively capture what you missed.

### 5.25 User Research Interview Guide
**Use case:** Prepare a structured interview guide for user research sessions.
**Best for:** Product managers, UX researchers, design leads

Act as a user research lead. You are investigating [specific problem or opportunity] for [product name] with [target user persona]. Write a [45-minute] interview guide with: [5-7] open-ended questions using the behavior-probing format ("Tell me about the last time you..."), [3] tasks for a live prototype test, and [2] follow-up prompts for emotional response ("How did that feel?"). Include a screener section to confirm the participant matches your persona. Add guidance on when to dig deeper versus move on.

**Expected output:** A structured 45-minute interview script with screener questions, behavior-based interview questions, prototype test tasks, and moderator tips.
**Pro tip:** Record and transcribe every session, then use thematic coding to find patterns across at least [8-10] interviews before drawing conclusions.

### 5.26 Product-Market Fit Survey Design
**Use case:** Design and analyze a product-market fit survey using the Sean Ellis method.
**Best for:** Product managers, founders, growth leads

Act as a growth strategist. You are launching a PMF survey for [product name] targeting [active user base of N users]. Write a survey with the core Sean Ellis question ("How disappointed would you be if you could no longer use [product name]?") plus [5-7] diagnostic questions about key features, value drivers, and alternatives. Specify the targeting criteria (users who have completed [key action] at least [X] times) and the response threshold you need ([minimum 100 responses]). Provide a framework for analyzing open-ended "What is the main benefit?" responses using frequency analysis.

**Expected output:** A ready-to-field PMF survey with the core question, diagnostic items, targeting rules, and an analysis playbook for interpreting results.
**Pro tip:** A score above 40% "very disappointed" predicts strong PMF, but below 25% means you should focus on retention before growth spending.

### 5.27 Pricing Page Copy and Structure
**Use case:** Write compelling pricing page copy that reduces friction and increases conversion.
**Best for:** Product marketers, growth managers, SaaS founders

Act as a SaaS pricing copywriter. You are writing the pricing page for [product name], a [B2B/B2C] tool with [3] plan tiers: [Starter, Professional, Enterprise]. For each tier, write a plan name, monthly price, [3-5] bullet features (framed as benefits, not just specs), and a call-to-action button. Above the tiers, write a [2-sentence] value proposition. Below the tiers, add an FAQ section addressing [3] common objections (e.g., "Can I switch plans?"). Include social proof elements (customer logo bar, testimonial, usage statistic).

**Expected output:** A full pricing page layout with tier cards, value proposition, FAQ section, and social proof markers.
**Pro tip:** Put your recommended tier preselected or visually highlighted to anchor customers toward your preferred plan.

### 5.28 Product Launch Checklist
**Use case:** Plan and coordinate a cross-functional product launch.
**Best for:** Product managers, launch coordinators, marketing leads

Act as a product launch program manager. You are launching [feature or product name] on [target date]. Build a [30-item] checklist organized by phase: Pre-Launch ([T-30] to [T-7] days), Launch Week ([T-7] to [T+0]), and Post-Launch ([T+0] to [T+14]). Cover these functions: engineering (code freeze, monitoring), product (internal demo, release notes), marketing (blog post, email sequence, social assets), sales (enablement deck, pricing update), support (FAQ training, known issues doc), and customer success (outreach script for top [20] accounts).

**Expected output:** A 30-item cross-functional launch checklist with owner assignments, due dates, and phase gates.
**Pro tip:** Schedule a launch rehearsal [1 week] before the date to catch broken flows, missing assets, and misaligned messaging early.

### 5.29 Beta Testing Program Design
**Use case:** Structure a beta testing program to validate features before general release.
**Best for:** Product managers, QA leads, program managers

Act as a beta program manager. Design a [4-week] beta program for [new feature] in [product name]. Recruit [30-50] participants from your existing user base with a screener survey. Define [3] success criteria (e.g., crash rate below [0.5%], task completion rate above [80%], NPS above [40]). Create a weekly engagement cadence: Week 1 onboarding and first tasks, Week 2-3 structured testing missions, Week 4 exit survey and wrap-up. Include a bug reporting template with severity labels and a private community channel for real-time feedback.

**Expected output:** A complete beta program playbook with recruitment screener, success criteria, weekly schedule, bug template, and communication plan.
**Pro tip:** Give beta testers a direct line to the product team not just a support ticket queue or they will disengage fast.

### 5.30 Churn Analysis for Product Teams
**Use case:** Diagnose why users churn and identify product-led retention opportunities.
**Best for:** Product managers, growth leads, data analysts

Act as a product growth analyst. Analyze churn for [product name] using [last 6 months] of user data. Segment churned users by [signup cohort], [plan type], and [usage tier]. Identify the top [3] behavioral leading indicators of churn (e.g., feature usage drops, login frequency decline, support ticket volume spike). Build a graph showing time-to-churn distribution (median days to churn: [X] days). Recommend [3] product changes that could address the most common churn triggers, with estimated retention impact per change.

**Expected output:** A churn analysis report with segment breakdown, leading indicator patterns, time-to-churn curve, and 3 product-led retention recommendations.
**Pro tip:** Track "first value time" (FVT) the time from signup to first key action users who hit it in under [X] minutes retain at [2X] the rate.

### 5.31 Onboarding Flow Optimization
**Use case:** Redesign the user onboarding flow to improve activation and time-to-value.
**Best for:** Product managers, growth leads, UX designers

Act as an onboarding optimization specialist. You are redesigning the first-run experience for [product name]. The current activation rate (users who complete [key action] in first [7] days) is [X%]. Design a [3-step] onboarding flow: Step 1 value prop confirmation (what the user will achieve), Step 2 quick setup wizard ([3-5] fields max), Step 3 success moment (user sees value immediately). Include a progressive disclosure strategy for advanced features, an email drip sequence ([3] emails over [5] days) for users who stall, and an in-app checklist with [5] core actions.

**Expected output:** A redesigned onboarding flow specification with wizard steps, email sequence, in-app checklist, and success metric targets.
**Pro tip:** Reduce the number of required fields for first signup to the absolute minimum you can prompt for everything else in context after the user sees value.

### 5.32 Product Experimentation Framework
**Use case:** Establish a systematic process for running product experiments.
**Best for:** Product managers, growth teams, data science leads

Act as a head of product experimentation. Build a company-wide experimentation framework for [product name]. Define [4] experiment types: A/B tests, multivariate tests, feature flag rollouts, and holdout tests. Create a standardized experiment template with fields for hypothesis, audience, duration, sample size, success metrics, and decision criteria. Specify the statistical threshold (p-value < [0.05], minimum detectable effect of [5%]). Document governance rules: who can launch experiments, how to handle significant negatives, and the experiment review cadence (bi-weekly).

**Expected output:** An experimentation framework document with experiment types, template, statistical standards, and governance rules.
**Pro tip:** Run a culture of "fail fast and document" where null results are shared publicly they save your team from repeating dead ends.

### 5.33 Data Privacy Compliance Strategy (GDPR/CCPA)
**Use case:** Plan and document a data privacy compliance approach for your product.
**Best for:** Product managers, legal teams, engineering leads

Act as a data privacy officer consulting for [product name], a product serving users in [EU, California, and other regions]. Outline the [6] key compliance areas: consent management (cookie banner, preference center), data inventory map (what data you collect, where it lives, how it flows), user rights request handling (access, deletion, portability), data retention policy (default [90] days for raw data, [24] months for aggregated), breach notification procedure (internal alert within [24] hours, customer notice within [48] hours), and vendor due diligence checklist for [3] subprocessors. Provide a compliance timeline with milestones for the next [3 months].

**Expected output:** A compliance action plan with data inventory, consent framework, rights request workflow, retention policy, and a 3-month implementation timeline.
**Pro tip:** Start with a data inventory map before writing policies you cannot govern data you do not know exists.

### 5.34 API Strategy and Developer Experience
**Use case:** Define the API product strategy, versioning approach, and developer experience standards.
**Best for:** Product managers (API/Platform), developer relations leads, engineering managers

Act as an API product strategist. Design an API strategy for [product name] targeting [developer audience]. Define the API scope (public, partner, internal), versioning approach (semantic versioning with [2] supported versions), authentication method ([API keys, OAuth 2.0]), rate limits ([1000] requests/hour for free tier, [10000] for paid), and documentation standards (OpenAPI 3.0 spec, interactive playground, SDKs for [Python, JavaScript, and one more language]). Include a deprecation policy: announce [12] months before removal, mark as deprecated in docs, provide migration guide.

**Expected output:** An API strategy document covering versioning, auth, rate limits, docs standards, SDK roadmap, and deprecation policy.
**Pro tip:** Ship your API with a public changelog and migration guides from day one your developers will trust you more when breaking changes are announced clearly.

### 5.35 Product Localization Strategy
**Use case:** Plan and execute product localization for new international markets.
**Best for:** Product managers, international growth leads, localization managers

Act as a localization program manager. You are localizing [product name] for [2 target markets, e.g., Japan and Brazil]. Create a localization priority matrix ranking [10] product surfaces (UI copy, emails, docs, support articles, marketing site, onboarding flows, error messages, legal pages, social media, in-product notifications) by user impact and engineering effort. Define a translation workflow: source strings in [i18n framework], machine translation pass with [DeepL/Google], human review by [2] native speakers per market, and a [2-week] QA cycle. Address region-specific concerns: date/number formats, payment methods ([Pix in Brazil, Konbini in Japan]), and cultural sensitivity notes.

**Expected output:** A localization priority matrix, translation workflow specification, and market-specific adaptation notes for each target region.
**Pro tip:** Localize error messages and empty states early, not last, because those are often the highest-friction touchpoints for non-English users.

### 5.36 Competitive Feature Analysis
**Use case:** Benchmark your product features against key competitors.
**Best for:** Product managers, competitive intelligence leads, product marketers

Act as a competitive intelligence analyst. Compare [product name] against [3] key competitors: [Competitor A, Competitor B, Competitor C]. Build a feature comparison matrix across [8] dimensions: core functionality, integrations, pricing, UX quality, mobile experience, reporting/analytics, security/compliance, and customer support. For each dimension, rate each product on a scale of [1-5] and write a [2-sentence] analysis. Identify [3] areas where you have a competitive advantage, [3] gaps where competitors lead, and [2] neutral zones where parity is sufficient.

**Expected output:** A feature comparison matrix with ratings, competitive advantage/gap analysis, and strategic recommendations for positioning.
**Pro tip:** Include "table stakes" features in your matrix but mark them neutrally the real strategic insight comes from differentiation features, not parity items.

### 5.37 Product Naming and Positioning
**Use case:** Generate and evaluate product or feature name candidates.
**Best for:** Product managers, product marketers, founders

Act as a brand naming consultant. You are naming a new [product or feature] that does [core function] for [target audience]. Generate [10] name candidates organized by strategy: descriptive (clear function), evocative (emotional feel), abstract (unique and ownable), and blended compounds. For the top [3] names, write a positioning statement using the "[Product X] helps [audience] achieve [outcome] by [mechanism]" format. Check each name for domain availability, trademark conflicts, and meaning in [Spanish, German, Japanese] to avoid cultural issues.

**Expected output:** 10 name candidates across 4 naming strategies, a positioning statement for the top 3, and a linguistic/cultural availability check.
**Pro tip:** Say the name aloud in a support conversation script if it sounds awkward or confusing when spoken, it will fail in word-of-mouth marketing.

### 5.38 Feature Deprecation and Retirement Strategy
**Use case:** Plan the graceful deprecation and retirement of an existing product feature.
**Best for:** Product managers, engineering managers, customer success leads

Act as a product lifecycle manager. You are deprecating [feature name], used by [X%] of active customers, in favor of [replacement feature].
