Design a [90-day] deprecation plan: Phase 1 ([Day 1-30]) in-app notice to users with "Learn more" link, Phase 2 ([Day 31-60]) email campaign to affected accounts with migration instructions and [2] migration support office hours sessions, Phase 3 ([Day 61-90]) automatic redirect of legacy API calls to the new feature with a clear header indicating deprecation. Define the criteria for exception handling (high-value accounts with complex migrations can get a [60-day] extension). Draft a support script for handling complaints.

**Expected output:** A 90-day feature deprecation plan with phased communication, migration support, exception criteria, and a support escalation script.
**Pro tip:** Announce deprecation deadlines with a specific date, not a quarter companies will delay until the last possible week no matter what you set.

### 5.39 Product Documentation Standards
**Use case:** Establish writing and formatting standards for product documentation.
**Best for:** Technical writers, product managers, developer relations leads

Act as a technical documentation lead. Create documentation standards for [product name]. Define page types: concept guides (what and why), tutorials (step-by-step for beginners), how-to guides (task-oriented for intermediate users), and reference docs (auto-generated from code). Specify the structure template for each type: title, context paragraph, numbered steps for tutorials, code blocks with copy buttons, and troubleshooting callout boxes. Set formatting rules: active voice ("Click Save"), second person ("You can configure"), consistent terminology list ([10] terms with standard usage), and screenshot guidelines ([1200px] width, annotated with red numbered callouts).

**Expected output:** A documentation standards guide with page type definitions, structure templates, formatting rules, and screenshot specs.
**Pro tip:** Add "docs as code" to your CI pipeline lint for broken links, outdated screenshots, and stale code examples on every pull request.

### 5.40 Product Feedback Loop from Support
**Use case:** Build a systematic pipeline for turning support conversations into product improvements.
**Best for:** Product managers, support leads, product ops

Act as a product operations manager. Design a feedback loop between support and product teams for [product name]. Define a tagging taxonomy for support tickets: [15] tags across [5] categories (bug, feature request, UX confusion, documentation gap, performance issue). Create a weekly triage process: support lead reviews tagged tickets, selects top [5] items by volume and severity, and presents to product team in a [30-minute] weekly feedback sync. Require that each item gets a disposition (accepted, queued, wontfix, needs more info) within [1] week. Track the closed-loop rate: percentage of submitted items that receive a disposition.

**Expected output:** A support-to-product feedback loop playbook with tag taxonomy, weekly triage workflow, accountability rules, and a closed-loop tracking metric.
**Pro tip:** Tag UX confusion tickets separately from bugs they signal documentation or design problems, not code defects, and need different owners.

## 6. Customer Success & Support Prompts (20 Prompts)

### 6.1 Customer Success Program Design
**Use case:** Build a customer success program that drives retention and expansion
**Best for:** CS leaders, founders, account managers

Act as a customer success strategist. I need to design a customer success program from scratch.

Company: [describe business] | Product: [what you sell] | Customer base: [number and segment] | Churn rate: [current churn] | CS team: [current team size or none] | Business model: [subscription/contract/usage-based] | Goals: [retention, expansion, advocacy]

Design a customer success program. Customer Journey — Map the customer lifecycle: Onboarding > Adoption > Value Realization > Retention > Expansion > Advocacy. Touchpoints and success milestones at each stage. Segmentation — Segment customers: Enterprise (dedicated CSM), Mid-Market (pooled CSM, 20-30 accounts each), SMB (digital-led, automated), Self-Serve (no-touch, product-led). Health Scoring — Define health score: product usage (frequency, feature adoption), engagement (support tickets, NPS responses, QBR attendance), sentiment (NPS, CSAT), business outcomes (goal progress, ROI realization). Green/Yellow/Red scoring. Onboarding Process — Day 1: welcome call, account setup. Week 1: implementation kickoff, success plan. Week 4: first value checkpoint. Week 8: onboarding complete, handoff to CSM. Success Plan — For each segment: define success milestones, timeline, metrics to track. Joint success plan document for enterprise customers. Renewal Management — 90-day renewal cycle: Day 90: value assessment, Day 60: renewal discussion, Day 30: proposal and negotiation, Day 0: close. Early renewals and multi-year incentives. Expansion Playbook — Triggers for expansion: product usage growth, new department adoption, feature request volume, champion promotion, ROI demonstrated. Upsell and cross-sell plays. Advocacy Program — Reference customers, case studies, testimonials, NPS promoters, advisory board, speaking opportunities.

**Expected output:** Complete customer success program with journey, segmentation, health scoring, and operations
**Pro tip:** The best customer success metric is time-to-value — every day a customer hasn't realized value is a day closer to churn. Accelerate their first win

### 6.2 Customer Onboarding Experience
**Use case:** Design a customer onboarding experience that drives activation and retention
**Best for:** CS teams, product managers, onboarding specialists

Act as a customer onboarding expert. I need to design or improve the onboarding experience.

Product: [describe product] | Complexity: [simple/complex] | Typical user: [tech savvy/not] | Current onboarding: [what exists] | Activation metric: [what defines a successful onboard] | Time to value: [current time] | Tools: [onboarding platform]

Design a comprehensive onboarding experience. Onboarding Philosophy — Activate (get user to first value fast) > Educate (teach advanced features) > Integrate (become part of their workflow). First 24 hours are critical. Pre-Onboarding — Welcome email sequence: confirmation, getting started guide, setup checklist, calendar link for kickoff call. Tech requirements checklist. Kickoff Call Structure — For high-touch: agenda, stakeholder alignment, success criteria definition, implementation timeline, roles and responsibilities, communication plan. Implementation Plan — Step-by-step: account setup, data migration/integration, configuration, team training, go-live, first value review. Timeline with milestones. Self-Service Onboarding — For low-touch/app-led: product tour (interactive), progress bar/wizard, empty states that guide actions, tooltips and hints, sample data to explore, templates for quick starts. Activation Milestones — Define 3-5 milestones that signal real adoption: e.g., imported data, created first project, invited team members, completed first workflow. Track completion rates. Time to Value Optimization — Identify the quickest path to first value. Remove friction, reduce steps, offer templates, auto-configure default settings. Every step removed improves activation. Training Resources — Knowledge base articles, video tutorials, webinars, certification program, best practices guides, community forum. Success Metrics — Time-to-activation (target varies by product), completion rate per onboarding step, support tickets during onboarding, NPS 30 days post-onboard.

**Expected output:** Onboarding experience design with pre-onboarding, implementation, activation milestones, and training
**Pro tip:** The single biggest onboarding improvement is removing a step — every click, form field, and email you eliminate directly increases activation rate

### 6.3 Customer Health Scoring System
**Use case:** Build a customer health scoring system that predicts churn risk
**Best for:** CS leaders, data analysts, RevOps

Act as a customer analytics consultant. I need to build a customer health scoring system.

Company: [describe business] | Customers: [number and segments] | Churn data: [historical churn patterns] | Available data: [product usage, support, NPS, billing] | Tools: [CRM, analytics platform] | Goal: [predict churn, identify expansion opportunities]

Design a customer health scoring system. Health Score Components — Product Usage (40% weight): login frequency, feature adoption, key action completion. Support Engagement (20%): tickets filed, resolution time, satisfaction, proactive vs reactive contact. Sentiment (15%): NPS score, CSAT, surveys, verbatim analysis. Business Outcomes (15%): progress against success plan, goal attainment. Relationship (10%): executive engagement, QBR participation, champion strength. Scoring Model — Each component scored 1-10, weighted average = total health score. Thresholds: Green (8-10, healthy), Yellow (5-7, at risk), Red (1-4, churn risk). Leading vs lagging indicators. Leading indicators (usage decline, support spike) predict churn before lagging indicators (NPS drop, missed renewal). Automation — Automated scoring updates daily. Slack alerts for score changes: red flag, yellow caution, green celebration. Playbook triggers per score. Churn Prediction — ML model (if data supports) or rule-based triggers: usage drop >30% for 2 weeks, zero logins for 7 days, support tickets >5 in a week, NPS drop >20 points, key stakeholder departure. Intervention Playbooks — Red: executive outreach, health call, root cause analysis, escalation process, discount/credit consideration. Yellow: CSM outreach with value review, additional training, feature introduction. Green: ask for referral, case study, upsell conversation. Scorecards — Customer-facing health scorecard: shared at QBR, shows progress. Helps customers see their own value realization.

**Expected output:** Customer health scoring system with components, automation, prediction, and interventions
**Pro tip:** Health scores are only useful if you act on them — a red account that nobody calls is just a data point. Drive interventions from score changes automatically

### 6.4 Customer Support Operations
**Use case:** Build or scale customer support operations for quality and efficiency
**Best for:** Support managers, customer experience leads

Act as a customer support operations consultant. I need to build or scale my support operations.

Company: [describe business] | Support volume: [tickets per month] | Channels: [email, chat, phone, social] | Team: [current team size] | Tools: [support platform] | Hours: [24/7 vs business hours] | Complexity: [simple/sophisticated product]

Design a customer support operations system. Channel Strategy — Email: for detailed/complex issues. Live Chat: for quick questions, high-volume issues. Phone: for urgent/complex/high-value customers. Self-Service: knowledge base, community, AI chatbot for Tier 0. Ticket Management — Ticketing system setup: auto-categorization, priority assignment, SLA enforcement, routing rules. Ticket status workflow (New > Open > Pending > Resolved > Closed). SLAs and Priorities — Priority matrix: Critical (system down, data loss) > respond 15 min, resolve 1 hour. High (major feature broken) > respond 1 hour, resolve 4 hours. Medium (minor issue) > respond 4 hours, resolve 24 hours. Low (question, request) > respond 24 hours, resolve 72 hours. Escalation Path — Tier 1 (frontline): handle common issues from knowledge base. Tier 2 (specialist): complex technical issues. Tier 3 (engineering): bugs, product issues. Escalation criteria and process. Knowledge Management — Knowledge base: articles with step-by-step solutions, screenshots, video walkthroughs. Internal (agents) and external (customer-facing) versions. Regular review and updates. Quality Assurance — QA program: review 5-10 tickets per agent per month. Score on: accuracy, tone, efficiency, follow-through, CSAT. Calibration sessions. Agent Tools — Macros/canned responses for common issues, AI-assisted responses (suggested answers), customer context sidebar (account info, history), next-best-action recommendations. Metrics — Operational: tickets resolved, first response time, resolution time, backlog, CSAT score. Business: retention rate of supported customers, escalation rate, self-service rate.

**Expected output:** Support operations framework with channels, SLAs, escalation, knowledge management, and QA
**Pro tip:** The most efficient support ticket is the one that never gets created — invest heavily in self-service and product improvements that prevent questions

### 6.5 Customer Feedback and VOC Program
**Use case:** Build a Voice of Customer program that systematically captures and acts on feedback
**Best for:** CS teams, product managers, CX leads

Act as a Voice of Customer consultant. I need to build a systematic VOC program.

Company: [describe business] | Feedback sources: [existing — support, surveys, reviews] | Current practice: [ad-hoc/structured] | Users: [who needs feedback insights] | Tools: [survey tools, CRM] | Goals: [product improvement, churn reduction, advocacy]

Design a Voice of Customer program. Feedback Sources — Structured: NPS (relationship survey, quarterly), CSAT (transactional, post-interaction), CES (customer effort score, post-task). Unstructured: support tickets (analysis), sales call transcripts, social media, review sites (G2, Capterra), community forum, product usage (behavioral feedback). Listening Posts — Map customer journey with feedback collection points: Onboarding > Day 7 check-in survey, Monthly > Product usage analytics touch points, Quarterly > NPS survey, Post-support > CSAT survey, Renewal > Exit interview (if churning). Survey Design Principles — Keep surveys short (3-5 questions max), ask one thing at a time, use clear language, offer open-ended for context, respect customer time. NPS: "How likely to recommend?" (0-10) + "Why?" Analysis — Quantitative: NPS trends by segment, CSAT scores over time, sentiment analysis on text. Qualitative: Thematic analysis on open-ended responses, identify recurring themes, tag and categorize, track frequency. Closed-Loop Feedback — Detractor (<7 NPS) > CSM reaches out within 24 hours. Passive (7-8) > Automated follow-up email with helpful resources. Promoter (9-10) > Thank them, ask for review, referral, or case study. Theme > Product team reviews quarterly, prioritized in roadmap. Reporting — Monthly VOC report to leadership: NPS trend, top praise themes, top complaint themes, action taken, closed-loop completion rate. Quarterly deep-dive with product team.

**Expected output:** Voice of Customer program with listening posts, surveys, analysis, and closed-loop process
**Pro tip:** Most companies listen to customers but don't close the loop — the real value of VOC is telling customers "we heard you and here's what we changed"

### 6.6 Customer Education and Training
**Use case:** Build a customer education program that drives product adoption and reduces support
**Best for:** CS teams, education managers, product marketing

Act as a customer education strategist. I need to build a customer education and training program.

Product: [describe product] | Users: [number and tech savviness] | Current training: [what exists] | Support volume: [tickets related to education gaps] | Content types: [preferred learning formats] | Budget: [available resources]

Design a customer education program. Learning Paths — By user role (admin, regular user, manager), by goal (get started, advanced features, admin tasks), by product area (reporting, integrations, configuration). Certification paths for power users. Content Formats — Written: knowledge base articles, how-to guides, best practices, FAQs. Video: tutorials (2-5 min), webinars (30-45 min), product demos. Interactive: in-app guides, tooltips, walkthroughs, product tours. Live: instructor-led training, Q&A sessions, office hours. Content Development — Content plan based on support ticket analysis (top causes of tickets = educational content priorities). Template for consistent formatting. Review cycle for accuracy (quarterly). Delivery Platform — LMS (Learning Management System) like Thinkific, Teachable, or product-integrated education. In-app education: Pendo, Appcues, WalkMe. Knowledge base: Help Scout, Zendesk Guide, Intercom Articles. Community — Community forum for peer learning and support. User groups, AMAs, best practice sharing, product updates. Moderation strategy. Certification Program — For power users: tiered certification levels, exam requirements, benefits (badge, recognition, early access, direct line to product team). Adoption and Impact — Track: course completion rates, certification earners, knowledge base article views/helpfulness, decrease in support tickets for trained topics, product adoption lift in educated users.

**Expected output:** Customer education program with learning paths, content formats, delivery platform, and impact measurement
**Pro tip:** Every dollar spent on customer education saves three dollars in support — a well-educated customer is your cheapest customer to serve

### 6.7 Churn Analysis and Prevention
**Use case:** Analyze churn patterns and build systematic prevention strategies
**Best for:** CS leaders, data analysts, executives

Act as a churn reduction specialist. I need to analyze churn and build prevention strategies.

Company: [describe business] | Churn rate: [monthly/quarterly/annual] | Churned customers: [recent churn examples] | Data available: [usage, support, billing, surveys] | Segments: [customer types] | Current retention: [existing retention efforts]

Design a churn analysis and prevention program. Churn Analysis — Quantitative: churn rate by segment, by tenure (when do customers churn?), by plan/price point, by acquisition channel, by region. Cohort analysis (do newer cohorts churn faster?). Qualitative: exit interviews for every churning customer (enterprise) or exit survey (SMB). Root cause analysis. Churn Reasons — Categorize: Product (missing features, bugs, performance), Value (not seeing ROI, not using enough), Experience (poor onboarding, bad support), Price (too expensive, not enough value for cost), Relationship (champion left, no relationship), Competition (found better alternative), Circumstance (business closed, no longer need). Retention Playbooks — At-Risk (health score yellow): value review call, additional training, feature introduction. High-Risk (health score red): exec sponsorship, root cause call, success plan reset, discount/temporary price adjustment if needed. Exiting (cancel requested): save attempt based on reason — pricing (offer discount), feature gap (roadmap commitment, workaround), usage (training, onboarding). Win-Back Program — For churned customers: re-engagement campaigns at 30, 60, 90 days post-churn. Product updates that address their reason for leaving. Special win-back offers. Competitive churn: track what they moved to, monitor for competitor weakness. Prevention System — Early warning triggers (from health scoring): usage decline, support spike, stakeholder change, NPS drop. Automated playbooks trigger on signals. Continuous improvement from root cause analysis.

**Expected output:** Churn analysis and prevention system with data analysis, retention playbooks, and early warning
**Pro tip:** If you don't know why customers churn, you can't prevent it — do exit interviews with EVERY churning customer. The pattern will emerge after 10-15 conversations

### 6.8 Customer Advocacy and Community Building
**Use case:** Build a customer advocacy program and community that drives growth
**Best for:** CS teams, community managers, marketing

Act as a customer advocacy consultant. I need to build a customer advocacy and community program.

Company: [describe business] | Customer base: [number and loyalty level] | NPS: [current promoter percentage] | Existing advocacy: [any testimonials, referrals] | Goals: [reviews, referrals, case studies, community] | Resources: [team and budget]

Design a customer advocacy program. Advocacy Types — Reviews (G2, Capterra, Google), Case Studies (written, video), Referrals (customer referral program), Speaking (customer at events), Community (forum, user groups), Product Advisory (feedback on roadmap), Beta Testing (early access to new features). Identification — Find advocates from: NPS promoters (9-10), long-tenured customers (2+ years), power users (high feature adoption), customers who give unsolicited positive feedback, social media mentions. Scoring and tiering. Advocate Journey — Recruit (invite to program with clear benefits) > Engage (start with low-effort asks — review, quote) > Nurture (provide value — early access, direct line to product, swag) > Amplify (case studies, events, speaking) > Reward (recognition, exclusive experiences, referral incentives). Community Platform — Choose: forum (Discourse, Circle), Slack/Discord community, user groups (local meetups). Community health metrics: active members, posts, replies, time-to-response. Content for community: best practices, Q&A, product updates, user spotlights. Advocacy ROI — Track: reviews generated, case studies produced, referral revenue, community-influenced deals, NPS of advocates vs non-advocates. Cost per advocacy activity. Advocate Management — CRM for advocates: track activities, rewards given, relationship strength. Advocate-only Slack/Community for exclusive access. Annual advocate event (virtual or in-person).

**Expected output:** Customer advocacy program with advocate journey, community platform, and ROI measurement
**Pro tip:** Your most valuable marketers are your happy customers — invest in making them successful and giving them ways to share their success naturally

### 6.9 QBR and Executive Business Reviews
**Use case:** Design and execute Quarterly Business Reviews that demonstrate value and drive retention
**Best for:** CSMs, account managers, executives

Act as a customer engagement consultant. I need to design an effective QBR process.

Company: [describe business] | Segments: [customer types] | CS team: [number of CSMs] | Current reviews: [what exists vs nothing] | Customer value: [average contract value] | Goals: [retention, expansion, advocacy]

Design a QBR framework. QBR Cadence — Enterprise customers: quarterly. Mid-Market: bi-annual. SMB: annual. Schedule: dedicated weeks per quarter, 45-60 min meetings. Pre-QBR Preparation — CSM preparation: account health review, usage analytics, support ticket summary, success plan progress, expansion opportunities identified, customer-specific presentation. Internal prep meeting. QBR Agenda — Standard agenda: Executive Summary (health score, key metrics) > Value Realization (ROI achieved, success milestones) > Product Usage (adoption trends, new features used) > Challenges/Concerns (openly address issues) > Product Roadmap (upcoming relevant features) > Opportunities (expansion, new use cases) > Strategic Planning (goals for next quarter) > Next Steps. Value Demonstration — Quantify value delivered: ROI calculator updated with actual results, metrics improvement (productivity, cost savings, revenue), business outcomes achieved. Compare to goals set in previous QBR. Customer Success Story — Create a "before/after" narrative: where they were, where they are now (with data), where they're going. Makes the value real and tangible. Expansion Discussion — Identify natural expansion opportunities: new departments, new use cases, increased usage, additional products. Present as "ways to get even more value." Post-QBR Actions — Summary document sent within 24 hours. Action items with owners and deadlines. Updated success plan. CRM updated with notes and next steps. Executive Alignment — For strategic accounts: executive sponsor attends QBR, builds relationship with customer executives. Executive-to-executive communication between QBRs.

**Expected output:** QBR framework with agenda, value demonstration, expansion discussion, and follow-through
**Pro tip:** A QBR should never be a "here's what happened" update — the customer should walk away feeling more confident about their investment and excited about what's next

### 6.10 Customer Success Technology Stack
**Use case:** Select and implement the right CS technology stack
**Best for:** CS leaders, RevOps, IT

Act as a CS technology consultant. I need to select and implement a customer success tech stack.

Company: [describe business] | Customer count: [number] | CS team: [size] | Current tools: [what you have] | Budget: [available for tools] | Integration needs: [CRM, product, billing integration]

Design a CS technology stack. Core Platforms — CRM (Salesforce/HubSpot): customer data, account hierarchy, contact management. CS Platform (Gainsight/Totango/ChurnZero/Catalyst): health scoring, playbooks, journey orchestration, surveys. Product Analytics (Amplitude/Mixpanel/Pendo): usage data, feature adoption, user behavior. Support Platform (Zendesk/Intercom/HelpScout): ticketing, knowledge base, chatbots. Survey Tools (Delighted/SurveyMonkey/Typeform): NPS, CSAT. Integration Architecture — Data flows: CRM <> CS Platform (account data sync), Product Analytics <> CS Platform (usage data to health score), Support Platform <> CS Platform (ticket data to health score), CS Platform <> Email/Slack (alerts and playbooks). BI/Reporting Layer — Data warehouse (Snowflake/BigQuery) with data from all sources. BI tool (Looker/Tableau) for cross-functional CS analytics. Executive dashboard. Automation — Workflow automation: health score changes trigger Slack alerts, NPS responses trigger tasks, renewal reminders sent automatically, onboarding task sequences. Tool Selection Criteria — Evaluate: integration with existing stack, ease of use, scalability, cost, support, data residency requirements. Demo with your data (trial period). Implementation Plan — Phase 1: Core setup (CRM + CS platform integration), Phase 2: Health scoring configuration, Phase 3: Playbook automation, Phase 4: Reporting and analytics. 30-60-90 day plan.

**Expected output:** CS technology stack with core platforms, integration, automation, and implementation plan
**Pro tip:** Don't buy a CS platform before you have a CS process — the tool should automate what you already know works, not define how you work

### 6.11 Customer Success Onboarding Program
**Use case:** Design a structured onboarding program that drives time-to-value for new customers.
**Best for:** Customer success managers, onboarding specialists, CS leaders

Act as a customer success onboarding lead. Design a [30-day] onboarding program for new [plan tier] customers of [product name]. Segment by customer size: [SMB] (self-serve with email touchpoints), [Mid-Market] (dedicated CSM with weekly calls), [Enterprise] (executive sponsor kickoff, dedicated team, custom success plan). For each segment, define Day [1] milestones (account setup, admin training), Day [7] (first workflow live), Day [14] (team rollout), Day [30] (success review and renewal prep). Include a health check at Day [14] with a [1-5] readiness score.

**Expected output:** A 30-day segmented onboarding playbook with milestones, touchpoint cadence, readiness scoring, and escalation triggers.
**Pro tip:** Customize the onboarding path based on the customer's use case during the discovery call not every customer needs the same tour.

### 6.12 Customer Health Scoring Model
**Use case:** Build a data-driven customer health scoring system to identify at-risk and high-potential accounts.
**Best for:** Customer success managers, CS operations, data analysts

Act as a customer success data analyst. Design a health scoring model for [product name] across [4] dimensions: product usage (login frequency, feature adoption, [key action] completion), support interaction (ticket volume, severity, response time satisfaction), business outcomes (goal tracking, ROI milestones), and sentiment (NPS, survey responses, executive engagement). Weight each dimension ([Usage: 40%, Support: 20%, Outcomes: 25%, Sentiment: 15%]) and define a [0-100] scoring scale. Specify thresholds: Green ([71-100]) healthy, Yellow ([41-70]) monitor, Red ([0-40]) at-risk. Define escalation triggers: Red score for [2] consecutive weeks triggers an executive call.

**Expected output:** A health scoring model with dimension definitions, weightings, threshold matrix, and automated escalation rules.
**Pro tip:** Pair health scores with revenue data a Green account on a small plan might need an upsell focus, while a Red account on a large plan needs immediate intervention.

### 6.13 Churn Prediction and Prevention Playbook
**Use case:** Predict which accounts are likely to churn and deploy targeted retention plays.
**Best for:** Customer success managers, retention specialists, CS leaders

Act as a churn prevention strategist. Analyze the customer base of [product name] ([N] accounts) and build a churn prediction model using [3] leading indicators: declining [weekly] active users, decreased [feature X] usage, and increased [support ticket volume]. Segment accounts into churn risk tiers: Low Risk ([0-20%] churn probability), Medium Risk ([21-50%]), High Risk ([51%+]). For each tier, prescribe retention plays: Low Risk gets [automated check-in email], Medium Risk gets [CSM outreach with business review offer], High Risk gets [executive sponsor call with discounted renewal or custom success plan]. Track play effectiveness with win-rate and saved revenue metrics.

**Expected output:** A churn prediction and prevention framework with risk tiers, targeted retention plays, and success tracking methodology.
**Pro tip:** Focus your retention efforts on High Risk accounts with high annual value those are where every point of recovery directly impacts revenue most.

### 6.14 Customer Feedback Collection System
**Use case:** Design a multi-channel feedback collection system that captures actionable customer insights.
**Best for:** Customer success managers, product managers, CX leads

Act as a customer experience strategist. Build a feedback collection system for [product name] across [5] touchpoints: post-onboarding (Day [7] survey), post-support interaction (CSAT survey), in-app NPS prompt (triggered after [3] sessions), quarterly business review (structured exec feedback form), and annual customer health assessment. For each touchpoint, specify the survey type, maximum questions ([3-5] to maintain completion rates), question format (rating scale, open-ended, multiple choice), and collection cadence. Define a feedback triage process: positive feedback goes to [marketing/testimonials], negative feedback creates a [task in product board] if it appears in [3+] responses.

**Expected output:** A multi-touchpoint feedback collection plan with survey specs, cadence, triage workflow, and cross-functional routing rules.
**Pro tip:** Only ask for feedback when you have a clear action to take on the results asking without closing the loop trains customers to ignore your surveys.

### 6.15 Customer Success Playbook for Account Types
**Use case:** Create standardized customer success playbooks for different account segments.
**Best for:** Customer success managers, CS team leads, CS ops

Act as a customer success program manager. Write [3] playbooks for [product name] segmented by account type: [High-Touch Enterprise] (ARR >[$50K]), [Mid-Touch Mid-Market] (ARR [$10K-$50K]), [Low-Touch SMB] (ARR <[$10K]). Each playbook must include: touchpoint cadence (calls, emails, reviews), success milestones with target dates, common risk signals specific to that segment, escalation path, and renewal approach. Enterprise playbook includes executive business reviews every [quarter] and a customer advisory board invitation. SMB playbook relies on automated triggers and digital engagement.

**Expected output:** Three customer success playbooks (Enterprise, Mid-Market, SMB) with cadence, milestones, risk signals, and renewal processes.
**Pro tip:** Build SMB playbooks for scale automate everything that does not require human judgment so your CS team can focus on Enterprise and Mid-Market accounts.

### 6.16 Customer Advocacy Program Design
**Use case:** Build a customer advocacy program that generates referrals, case studies, and social proof.
**Best for:** Customer success managers, marketing leads, community managers

Act as a customer advocacy program manager. Design a [3-tier] advocacy program for [product name]: Tier 1 Champions (reference calls, [1] case study per year, logo usage), Tier 2 Advocates (quarterly guest blog, webcast participation, product advisory feedback), Tier 3 Ambassadors (speaking slots at [conference name], co-branded content, early access to features). Define the application and selection process (NPS >= [9], active usage for [6+ months], willingness to participate). Create a rewards structure: Tier 1 gets [swag pack and LinkedIn shoutout], Tier 2 gets [free premium plan upgrade], Tier 3 gets [all-expenses-paid trip to user conference].

**Expected output:** A 3-tier advocacy program with selection criteria, participation activities, rewards structure, and recruitment campaign plan.
**Pro tip:** Start your advocacy program with your happiest customers ask them for a reference call first, then gradually invite them to higher-tier activities.

### 6.17 Support Ticket Triage and Routing System
**Use case:** Automate the categorization, prioritization, and routing of support tickets.
**Best for:** Support leads, customer success ops, engineering managers

Act as a support operations manager. Design a ticket triage system for [product name] handling [X] tickets per week. Define [4] priority levels: P1 (system down, data loss respond within [15] minutes), P2 (major feature broken respond within [2] hours), P3 (minor issue or bug respond within [24] hours), P4 (feature request or question respond within [72] hours). Create auto-routing rules: billing questions go to [billing team], technical bugs go to [Tier 2 support] with error logs attached, feature requests go to [product team] with customer context. Include an escalation policy: P1 unresolved after [1] hour escalates to [engineering manager], P2 after [8] hours to [team lead].

**Expected output:** A support ticket triage playbook with priority definitions, routing rules, escalation policies, and SLA commitments for each tier.
**Pro tip:** Integrate your ticket system with your product analytics if a P1 comes from a high-value account, flag it immediately for executive awareness.

### 6.18 Customer Success KPIs and Reporting Dashboard
**Use case:** Define the key metrics and build a reporting dashboard for customer success performance.
**Best for:** Customer success managers, CS leaders, operations teams

Act as a customer success analytics lead. Define the [7] core KPIs for [product name]'s customer success team: Net Revenue Retention (NRR) target [>=110%], Logo Retention Rate target [>=95%], Time-to-Value (median days to first key action) target [<=14 days], NPS target [>=50], CSAT target [>=4.5/5], Support Ticket Volume per account (target [<=3/month]), and Health Score coverage (target [>=90%] of accounts scored monthly). For each KPI, specify the formula, data source, reporting cadence (weekly for operational, monthly for strategic), and segment breakdown (by plan tier, customer age, region). Design a weekly [15-minute] standup agenda that reviews the top [3] KPIs and the [5] most at-risk accounts.

**Expected output:** A CS KPI framework with definitions, targets, data sources, and a weekly reporting dashboard layout with segment filters.
**Pro tip:** Lead your reporting with NRR and Time-to-Value everything else is a diagnostic for those two headline metrics.

### 6.19 Customer Retention Campaign Design
**Use case:** Design a multi-channel retention campaign to reduce churn and increase loyalty.
**Best for:** Customer success managers, retention marketers, CS leaders

Act as a customer retention strategist. Design a [90-day] retention campaign for [product name] targeting accounts showing early warning signs (declining usage over [2] consecutive weeks, no login in [14] days, or dropped [key action] completion). Segment by churn risk (Medium and High) and account value ([<$5K], [$5K-$20K], [>$20K]). For each segment, design a [3-touch] sequence: Touch 1 (automated email with helpful resource, no sales pitch), Touch 2 (CSM outreach with personalized check-in [7] days later), Touch 3 (special offer: [discount on renewal, free migration support, dedicated training session]). Track campaign effectiveness by response rate, re-engagement rate, and saved accounts.

**Expected output:** A 90-day retention campaign plan with risk segments, multi-touch sequences, special offers, and success metrics.
**Pro tip:** Make the first touch purely helpful no retention ask, no upsell just a genuinely useful resource. Trust rebuilds before you can retain.

### 6.20 Customer Win-Back Strategy
**Use case:** Develop a strategy to re-engage and win back churned customers.
**Best for:** Customer success managers, retention leads, growth marketers

Act as a customer win-back specialist. You are targeting [N] churned customers from [product name] who canceled in the last [6 months]. Segment them by reason for churn (competitor switch, budget loss, underutilization, feature gap) and original account value. For each segment, design a win-back sequence: competitor switchers get [product comparison sheet and migration support offer], budget losses get [scaled-down plan option at [30%] discount for [3] months], underutilizers get [success story from similar company and free onboarding session], feature gaps get [roadmap preview showing upcoming features]. Define the outreach cadence: email on Day [1], phone call on Day [7], final offer on Day [21], then suppress from campaigns for [6 months]. Track win-back rate (target [>=5%]) and re-churn rate of won-back accounts.

**Expected output:** A segmented win-back strategy with tailored offers, outreach cadence, success targets, and re-churn monitoring framework.
**Pro tip:** Churned customers who re-engage often become your most loyal segment if you address the original reason they left do not skip the root-cause analysis.

## 7. Human Resources & Talent Management Prompts (20 Prompts)

### 7.1 Talent Acquisition Strategy
**Use case:** Build a systematic approach to attracting and hiring top talent
**Best for:** HR leaders, founders, hiring managers

Act as a talent acquisition strategist. I need to build or improve our hiring process.

Company: [describe business] | Roles to hire: [positions needed] | Team size: [current and target] | Growth stage: [startup/scale/enterprise] | Budget: [per hire budget] | Current process: [what works/doesn't] | Employer brand: [current reputation]

Design a talent acquisition strategy. Sourcing Channels — Job boards (LinkedIn, Indeed, niche boards), Employee referrals (best source — build referral program), Recruiters (agency or internal), Passive sourcing (LinkedIn Recruiter, GitHub, Behance), Pipeline building (engage potential candidates before roles open), Campus recruiting, Social recruiting (Twitter/X, LinkedIn content). Employer Branding — Employee value proposition (EVP): why work here? Company culture content, employee testimonials, blog posts, social media presence, Glassdoor reviews management, awards and recognition. Candidate Experience — Application process (simple, <10 min), communication cadence (acknowledgment, status updates), interview experience (welcoming, structured, respectful), feedback (timely, constructive, always), offer experience (personalized, responsive). Interview Process Design — Stages: Screen (30 min, recruiter), Hiring Manager (45 min), Technical/Functional (60 min), Culture/Values (45 min), Executive (30 min, for senior roles). Structured interviews with consistent questions. Scorecards. Diversity Sourcing — Where to find diverse candidates: diverse job boards, professional associations, HBCUs/HSIs, conferences, employee referrals focused on diversity. Inclusive job descriptions (avoid gendered language). Diverse interview panels. Metrics — Time-to-hire, cost-per-hire, source efficiency, offer acceptance rate, candidate NPS, diversity pipeline metrics, quality of hire (performance after 6 months).

**Expected output:** Talent acquisition strategy with sourcing, employer branding, process design, and metrics
**Pro tip:** The best hires come from referrals and your network — invest in making your employees proud to refer. A referral program that pays 30-60 days after hire is the highest ROI recruiting channel

### 7.2 Interview and Assessment Design
**Use case:** Create structured, unbiased interview processes that predict job performance
**Best for:** HR, hiring managers, team leads

Act as an interview process designer. I need to design structured interviews and assessments.

Roles: [positions to design for] | Levels: [junior/mid/senior/lead] | Skills to assess: [technical, soft, leadership] | Current process: [what exists] | Company values: [what matters culturally]

Design an interview and assessment system. Competency Framework — For each role: define 5-7 key competencies. Technical: specific skills needed (coding, design, strategy). Behavioral: collaboration, communication, problem-solving. Leadership: team management, mentoring, strategic thinking. Interview Types — Structured Behavioral ("Tell me about a time when...") predicts future behavior based on past. STAR method (Situation, Task, Action, Result). Structured Technical — specific skills assessment (coding, case study, portfolio review). Situational ("What would you do if...") — how they think about scenarios. Take-Home Assignments — When to use: for roles where practical work is key evidence. Best practices: time-boxed (2-4 hours), real-world relevant, evaluate on rubric, blind review. Case Studies — For strategy, product, consulting roles: real-world business problem, evaluate structured thinking, frameworks used, creativity, communication. Assignment: 45-60 min. Interview Scorecards — Standard evaluation form: competencies rated 1-5, specific behavioral evidence recorded, hire/no-hire recommendation. Calibrated across interviewers. Panel Interview — For senior/leadership: multiple interviewers assess different competencies. Debrief session after all interviews. Consensus decision model. Avoiding Bias — Structured interviews reduce bias more than unstructured. Blind resume review (remove names, schools). Diverse interview panels. Consistent questions for all candidates. Score-first, discuss-second approach. Evaluation criteria defined before interviews.

**Expected output:** Interview system with competency framework, question types, scorecards, and bias prevention
**Pro tip:** The best predictor of job performance isn't the interview — it's a work sample test. Have candidates do actual work they'd do on the job, and evaluate the output

### 7.3 Onboarding and New Hire Experience
**Use case:** Design an onboarding experience that sets new hires up for success
**Best for:** HR, managers, team leads

Act as an onboarding specialist. I need to design a world-class onboarding experience.

Company: [describe business] | Role: [position type] | Team: [team structure] | Remote/office: [work model] | Current onboarding: [what exists] | Ramp time: [expected time to productivity]

Design a comprehensive onboarding program. Pre-Onboarding (Before Day 1) — Welcome email sequence, equipment shipping/tracking, IT account setup, access permissions, schedule setup, paperwork completed, intro to team Slack/Teams channel, onboarding buddy assigned. Day 1 — Welcome: office tour or virtual welcome, equipment setup, IT orientation, benefits overview, team lunch/coffee chat. First week: manager 1:1 daily, company orientation (culture, values, history), meet team members, read docs, set up dev environment. 30-60-90 Day Plan — 30 days: Learn (product training, team processes, meet stakeholders, complete onboarding tasks). 60 days: Contribute (take on small projects, begin independent work, receive feedback). 90 days: Own (full ownership of responsibilities, performance review, ramp complete). Onboarding Buddy — Assigned peer (not manager): first point of contact for questions, cultural guide, social integration, regular check-ins 2x/week for first month. Training Schedule — Product training (how product works, customer use cases), Tools training (internal tools, workflow), Process training (development process, communication norms), Company training (values, compliance, security). Role-Specific onboarding: deep dive into their function. Manager Check-Ins — Weekly 1:1s: how's it going, what's unclear, what support needed, feedback on process. 30-day review: onboarding progress. 60-day review: contributions and adjustments. 90-day review: performance baseline. Success Metrics — Time to first commit (engineering), time to first meeting (sales), time to first customer interaction (support), 30/60/90 day survey, new hire NPS, 6-month retention.

**Expected output:** Onboarding program with pre-boarding, 30-60-90 plan, buddy system, and training
**Pro tip:** The first week sets the tone for the entire employment — invest disproportionately in making Day 1 extraordinary. First impressions are lasting in employment relationships

### 7.4 Performance Management System
**Use case:** Design a performance management system that drives growth and accountability
**Best for:** HR leaders, managers, executives

Act as a performance management consultant. I need to design or improve our performance management system.

Company: [describe business] | Size: [number of employees] | Current system: [annual reviews/OKRs/ none] | Culture: [feedback culture maturity] | Pain points: [infrequent feedback, bias, disengagement] | Goals: [growth, accountability, development]

Design a performance management system. Philosophy — Continuous feedback culture vs annual event. Best practice: lightweight continuous with quarterly checkpoints + annual review. Goal Setting — OKRs (quarterly) or SMART goals. Cascade from company > team > individual. Goals aligned with business priorities. Check-Ins — Weekly 1:1s: manager + direct report. Agenda: priorities, blockers, feedback, development. Quarterly Reviews: goal progress, feedback, performance rating calibration. Annual Review: comprehensive evaluation, compensation, promotion decisions. Feedback Culture — Real-time feedback: specific, timely, actionable. Frameworks: SBI (Situation, Behavior, Impact) or Radical Candor. 360-degree feedback for development (not compensation). Performance Ratings — Calibration process: managers present ratings, leadership calibrates for consistency. Distribution guidelines (not forced curve but aware of patterns). Ratings: Exceeds, Meets, Developing, Improvement Needed. Development Plans — For each employee: strengths to leverage, areas to develop, growth opportunities, learning resources. IDP (Individual Development Plan) template. PIP (Performance Improvement Plan): structured plan for underperformance, clear expectations, regular check-ins, timeline for improvement. Compensation Review — Annual compensation cycle: merit increases, bonuses, promotions, equity adjustments. Performance-compensation link. Market adjustments. Career Progression — Career levels and expectations. Promotion criteria (skills, impact, tenure). Dual track: management vs individual contributor.

**Expected output:** Performance management system with goal setting, feedback, reviews, and career progression
**Pro tip:** The best performance system is one where no one is surprised at review time — if feedback is continuous, the annual review is just a summary

### 7.5 Learning and Development Strategy
**Use case:** Build a learning and development program that builds capabilities and retains talent
**Best for:** L&D managers, HR leaders, talent development

Act as a learning and development consultant. I need to build an L&D strategy for my organization.

Company: [describe business] | Size: [employees] | Skills gaps: [critical skills needed] | Current L&D: [what exists] | Budget: [training budget per employee] | Culture: [learning culture maturity] | Goals: [capability building, retention, engagement]

Design a learning and development strategy. Skills Assessment — Identify critical skills for business strategy (technical, leadership, domain). Current vs desired capability assessment. Skills gap analysis prioritized by business impact. Learning Modalities — Formal: courses, certifications, workshops, conferences. Social: mentoring, communities of practice, lunch & learns, peer coaching. Experiential: stretch assignments, job rotations, special projects, secondments. Content Sources — External: Coursera, Udemy, LinkedIn Learning, industry certifications. Internal: expert-led sessions, recorded knowledge shares, documentation, playbooks. Mix of curated and created content. Leadership Development — Programs for: first-time managers (fundamentals), mid-level managers (strategic thinking), senior leaders (executive presence, board readiness). Mentoring program pairing senior leaders with high-potentials. Technical Training — Role-specific: engineering (new languages, architecture), sales (methodology, product), CS (communication, product). Continuous learning budgets ($1000-5000 per employee/year). Compliance Training — Mandatory: security awareness, harassment prevention, data privacy, industry regulations. Automated assignment and tracking. Certification programs. Learning Culture — Manager support for learning (time, budget), knowledge sharing expectations, learning in performance conversations, internal mobility (learning = growth path). Measurement — Training completion rates, skills acquisition assessment, application on job (behavior change), business impact (productivity, quality, retention). ROI of L&D.

**Expected output:** Learning and development strategy with skills assessment, modalities, programs, and measurement
**Pro tip:** The most effective learning happens on the job, not in a classroom — invest more in stretch assignments and mentoring than in courses

### 7.6 Compensation and Benefits Design
**Use case:** Design competitive compensation and benefits packages that attract and retain talent
**Best for:** HR leaders, compensation analysts, executives

Act as a compensation consultant. I need to design or update our compensation and benefits philosophy.

Company: [describe business] | Stage: [startup/growth/enterprise] | Locations: [where employees are based] | Industry: [sector for benchmarking] | Current compensation: [existing structure] | Competitors: [who you compete for talent against] | Budget: [available for comp increases]

Design a compensation and benefits framework. Compensation Philosophy — Market position: pay at X percentile of market (typically 50th-75th for most roles, 90th+ for critical/key talent). Total compensation: base salary + bonus + equity + benefits. Transparency level. Salary Bands — Create bands by level, function, and location. Minimum, midpoint, maximum. Geographic differentials. Review and update annually with market data (Radford, Willis Towers Watson, Pave). Variable Compensation — Bonuses: annual performance bonus (target % of base: 10-20% for IC, 20-40% for management, 50-100%+ for executives). Commission plans for sales. SPIFs for short-term incentives. Equity Compensation — Options vs RSUs. Strike price, vesting schedule (4-year with 1-year cliff standard). Refresh grants. Early-stage vs late-stage equity strategy. 409A valuation implications. Benefits Package — Standard: health insurance, dental, vision, 401k/retirement. Differentiators: unlimited PTO, sabbatical, parental leave, mental health support, professional development budget, remote work stipend, wellness benefits. Market benchmarking annually. Total Compensation Statements — Show employees their full compensation value: base + bonus + equity + benefits. Increases transparency and appreciation. Promotion and Merit Cycles — Annual merit increase budget (typically 3-5% of payroll). Promotion increases (10-20%). Off-cycle adjustments for market or retention. Communication — Train managers on compensation conversations. Total rewards philosophy shared with all employees. Compensation data transparency appropriate for culture.

**Expected output:** Compensation framework with salary bands, equity strategy, benefits, and communication plan
**Pro tip:** Money doesn't motivate as much as you think — but bad compensation demotivates enormously. Get pay fair first, then invest in culture and growth

### 7.7 Employee Engagement and Culture
**Use case:** Measure and improve employee engagement and organizational culture
**Best for:** HR leaders, executives, people ops

Act as an employee engagement consultant. I need to measure and improve employee engagement.

Company: [describe business] | Size: [employees] | Current engagement: [anecdotal/surveyed/unknown] | Recent changes: [restructuring, growth, leadership change] | Culture: [current culture description] | Goals: [improve retention, productivity, satisfaction]

Design an employee engagement program. Engagement Survey — Survey design: 15-25 questions covering: satisfaction, belonging, growth, management, strategy, recognition, wellness. Standardized scales for benchmarking (eNPS, Gallup Q12). Anonymous. Frequency: pulse surveys monthly (3-5 questions), full survey bi-annual. Analysis — Results analysis by team, department, demographic, tenure. Identifying strengths, areas for improvement, and priority themes. Benchmark vs industry. Action Planning — Survey results shared transparently with all employees. Leadership discusses results openly. Team-level action plans with 2-3 focus areas. Track progress to previous survey. Culture Drivers — Define desired culture: values, behaviors, rituals. Assessment: current culture vs desired. Programs: recognition (peer-to-peer, manager-to-team), celebrations (milestones, wins), traditions (annual events, team building), rituals (all-hands, demos, show-and-tell). Retention Drivers — What keeps employees: meaningful work, good management, growth opportunities, fair compensation, work-life balance, belonging. Exit interviews: systematic collection and analysis of why people leave. Retention risk indicators. Wellness and Burnout Prevention — Workload management, boundaries (no late emails), mental health resources, flexible work, manager training to spot burnout signals. Engagement Drivers by Segment — Early career: growth, mentorship, challenging work. Mid-career: impact, autonomy, advancement. Late-career: purpose, flexibility, respect. Tailor programs accordingly.

**Expected output:** Employee engagement program with surveys, action planning, culture building, and retention analysis
**Pro tip:** The most engaged employees aren't the ones who get free lunch and ping pong — they're the ones who feel their work matters and their manager cares about their development

### 7.8 Organizational Design and Structure
**Use case:** Design organizational structure that supports strategy, growth, and effective collaboration
**Best for:** Executives, HR leaders, organization design consultants

Act as an organizational design consultant. I need to design or restructure my organization.

Company: [describe business] | Strategy: [current and future strategy] | Current structure: [functional/divisional/matrix] | Size: [employees] | Pain points: [silos, slow decisions, unclear ownership] | Growth plans: [projected growth trajectory] | Culture: [desired culture characteristics]

Design an organizational structure. Structure Options — Functional (by skill: engineering, marketing, sales): efficient but siloed. Divisional (by product/region): customer-focused but duplicated. Matrix: flexible but complex. Team/Tribe (Spotify model): autonomous but coordination-heavy. Recommend based on strategy and size. Principles — Clear accountability (every decision has an owner), span of control (5-8 direct reports optimal), layers (as flat as possible, as many as needed — aim for <7 layers from CEO to IC), clarity (roles, responsibilities, decision rights documented). Roles and Responsibilities — Role definition: mission, responsibilities, success criteria, key relationships. RACI matrix for cross-functional decisions (Responsible, Accountable, Consulted, Informed). Decision Rights — Who decides what: strategic decisions (board/CEO), operational decisions (functional heads), tactical decisions (team leads/ICs). Decision velocity as a design criterion. Coordination Mechanisms — How teams coordinate: cross-functional project teams, shared services (legal, finance, HR), communities of practice, communication channels, standing meetings. Design for the interfaces between teams. Scaling Considerations — Structure evolves with size: <25 people: informal, all-hands. 25-100: functional departments. 100-500: divisions or product teams. 500+: matrix, regions, business units. Plan ahead for next stage. Change Management — Communication strategy for restructuring: why, what's changing, what's not, timeline, who's affected. Transition plan: announcement, implementation period, stabilization. Managing uncertainty. Culture implications of structure change.

**Expected output:** Organizational design with structure recommendation, roles, decision rights, and change plan
**Pro tip:** Organizational structure is your operating system — no amount of culture or talent can overcome a structure that creates the wrong incentives and decision bottlenecks

### 7.9 Diversity, Equity, and Inclusion Strategy
**Use case:** Build a DEI strategy that creates a more equitable and inclusive workplace
**Best for:** DEI leaders, HR, executives

Act as a DEI strategist. I need to develop a diversity, equity, and inclusion strategy.

Company: [describe business] | Current DEI: [existing initiatives] | Representation data: [current diversity metrics] | Pain points: [lack of diversity, inclusion concerns] | Industry: [sector benchmarks] | Goals: [representation, inclusion, equity]

Design a DEI strategy. Representation — Sourcing: diverse job boards, partnerships with HBCUs/HSIs/ professional associations, inclusive job descriptions (gender-coded words audit), diverse hiring panels, blind resume screening. Targets for diverse candidate slates. Retention: ERGs (Employee Resource Groups), mentorship/sponsorship programs, inclusive manager training, career development for underrepresented groups. Equity — Pay equity: annual pay equity analysis (by gender, race/ethnicity). Correct inequities immediately. Promotions: analyze promotion rates by demographic, ensure equitable access. Performance reviews: audit for bias. Policies: equitable parental leave, flexible work, accommodation policies. Inclusion — Belonging survey questions ("I feel I belong here," "My voice is heard"). Inclusion training: bias awareness, inclusive language, allyship, inclusive meetings. ERG support: budget, executive sponsorship, meeting time. Recognition: diverse recognition programs. Measurement — Representation metrics by level and function (hire, promote, retain rates by demographic). Pay equity analysis. Inclusion survey scores (belonging, psychological safety). ERG participation and impact. Goals and accountability: DEI goals in executive OKRs. Accountability — Leadership accountability: DEI goals tied to executive compensation. DEI council: cross-functional leadership group. Reporting: annual DEI report transparently shared. Accountability without blame. Community Impact — Diverse supplier program. Community partnerships. Social impact initiatives aligned with DEI.

**Expected output:** DEI strategy with representation, equity, inclusion, measurement, and accountability
**Pro tip:** DEI is not a program with a end date — it's a continuous practice of examining who gets opportunities, who feels they belong, and whether the system is fair for everyone

### 7.10 People Analytics and HR Technology
**Use case:** Build people analytics capabilities and select the right HR technology stack
**Best for:** HR leaders, people ops, analytics teams

Act as a people analytics consultant. I need to build people analytics capabilities and select HR technology.

Company: [describe business] | Employees: [number] | Current HR tech: [existing systems] | Data available: [HRIS, payroll, engagement, performance] | Analytics maturity: [none/basic/advanced] | Goals: [retention, productivity, hiring efficiency]

Design a people analytics and HR tech strategy. HR Technology Stack — Core: HRIS (Workday, Rippling, BambooHR) for employee records, payroll, benefits. Talent: ATS (Lever, Greenhouse) for recruiting, LMS for learning, Performance management tools. Engagement: survey tools (Culture Amp, Qualtrics, Peakon). Productivity: collaboration tools (Slack/Teams), project management (Asana/Jira). Integration: data flows between systems. People Analytics — Key Metrics: Headcount and growth, turnover rate (voluntary/involuntary), time-to-hire, cost-per-hire, promotion rate, engagement score, performance distribution, L&D completion, pay equity. Analytics Use Cases — Retention: predict flight risk using engagement, tenure, performance, manager, commute. Identify top retention drivers. Hiring: source effectiveness (best channels for quality/ cost), hiring manager satisfaction, new hire retention. Performance: performance distribution, promotion velocity, high-potential identification. Comp: market position, internal equity, comp-to-performance correlation. Talent Analytics Team — Skills needed: HR domain knowledge, data analysis (SQL, Excel, Python), data visualization (Tableau, Power BI), storytelling. Build vs buy analytics capability. Data Governance — Data privacy (employee consent, anonymization), data quality (clean, consistent), access control, compliance (GDPR, CCPA, local labor laws). Reporting Cadence — Monthly: headcount, turnover, hiring dashboard. Quarterly: engagement trends, promotion analysis. Annual: full people analytics review, DEI report. Leadership: executive dashboard.

**Expected output:** People analytics and HR tech strategy with metrics, use cases, tools, and governance
**Pro tip:** People analytics is most valuable when it predicts outcomes, not just describes them — move from "what happened" to "what will happen" and "what should we do about it"

### 7.11 Remote Team Management Framework
**Use case:** Build a management framework that keeps remote teams aligned, engaged, and productive.
**Best for:** Engineering managers, team leads, HR business partners

Act as a remote work strategist. Design a remote management framework for a [team size] person team at [company name]. Define [4] pillars: communication guidelines (async-first, Slack for quick questions, Loom for complex topics, daily [15-minute] standup), documentation culture (all decisions recorded in [Notion/Confluence], single source of truth for each project), availability and hours (core overlap hours [10am-2pm UTC], flexible outside that), and team bonding (monthly [remote social event], annual [in-person offsite]). Include a manager playbook for 1-on-1s (weekly [30-minute] video calls with the same structured agenda template) and performance visibility (weekly written updates, quarterly OKR reviews).

**Expected output:** A remote team management framework with communication protocols, documentation standards, team bonding cadence, and manager playbook.
**Pro tip:** Async-first does not mean never meet be intentional about synchronous time use it for decisions and connection, not status updates.

### 7.12 Employee Engagement Survey Design
**Use case:** Design and deploy an employee engagement survey that surfaces actionable insights.
**Best for:** HR managers, people ops leads, culture leads

Act as an employee engagement specialist. Design a [biannual] engagement survey for [company name] ([N] employees). Use the [eNPS] format as the core metric plus [10] questions across [4] dimensions: growth and development ("I have a clear path for advancement at this company"), management effectiveness ("My manager gives me regular, constructive feedback"), culture and belonging ("I feel comfortable being myself at work"), and resources and workload ("I have the tools I need to do my job effectively"). Add [2] open-ended questions ("What keeps you here?" and "What would improve your experience?"). Specify anonymity protection (minimum [5] respondents per group before results display) and a post-survey action plan template managers must use to close the loop within [2] weeks.

**Expected output:** A survey instrument with core metric, dimensional questions, open-ended items, anonymity rules, and a manager action plan template.
**Pro tip:** The survey itself does not improve engagement. The visible action you take after publishing results is what moves the needle share results company-wide within [1] week.

### 7.13 Performance Improvement Plan Framework
**Use case:** Create a fair and structured performance improvement plan process.
**Best for:** HR managers, people ops, managers

Act as an HR performance management specialist. Design a PIP (Performance Improvement Plan) framework for [company name]. Define the [3] triggers that warrant a PIP: consistent missed targets over [2] consecutive review periods, repeated behavioral issues after verbal coaching, and critical skill gaps affecting team output. Create a PIP template with: specific performance gap description, [3-5] measurable success criteria with deadlines, support resources (training, mentoring, reduced scope), check-in schedule (weekly [30-minute] progress reviews), and duration ([30/60/90] days). Define the possible outcomes: pass (return to standard expectations), extend (additional [30] days with modified criteria), or fail (transition out). Include manager training guidelines to ensure PIPs are applied consistently and not punitively.

**Expected output:** A PIP framework with trigger criteria, template document, check-in cadence, outcome definitions, and manager training guide.
**Pro tip:** A PIP should always be a surprise no one there is nothing worse for trust and culture than learning about performance issues for the first time on a PIP document.

### 7.14 DEI Strategy and Action Plan
**Use case:** Develop a diversity, equity, and inclusion strategy with measurable goals.
**Best for:** HR leaders, DEI leads, executive teams

Act as a DEI strategist. Build a [12-month] DEI strategy for [company name] ([N] employees, currently [X%] underrepresented groups in leadership). Define [4] pillars: diverse hiring (blind resume screening, diverse slate requirement [at least 2 underrepresented candidates per final round], interview panel diversity), equitable processes (standardized interview scorecards, pay equity audit [annual], promotion criteria transparency), inclusive culture (employee resource groups with [budget] and executive sponsorship, inclusive language guide, regular belonging survey), and external impact (diverse supplier program, community partnerships with [2] organizations). Set [3] measurable goals for the year with quarterly checkpoints.

**Expected output:** A 12-month DEI action plan with pillar definitions, measurable goals, quarterly milestones, and accountability structure.
**Pro tip:** Tie DEI goals to business OKRs, not just HR metrics diverse teams outperform on innovation and revenue, so frame the strategy around business outcomes not compliance.

### 7.15 Career Development Framework
**Use case:** Build a transparent career progression framework with defined levels and competencies.
**Best for:** HR managers, people ops, engineering leads

Act as a career development architect. Design a career framework for [job family, e.g., Engineering, Marketing, Sales] at [company name] with [5] levels: L1 (Entry/Junior), L2 (Mid-level), L3 (Senior), L4 (Lead/Staff), L5 (Principal/Director). For each level, define [4] competency dimensions: technical/functional skill, project scope and complexity, collaboration and influence, and strategic impact. Write level-appropriate expectations for each dimension in measurable terms. Include both individual contributor and management tracks with equivalent levels so ICs are not forced into management for advancement. Add typical time-in-level guidelines ([2-3] years per level) with variance notes for high performers.

**Expected output:** A career progression framework with 5 levels, 4 competency dimensions per level, dual-track (IC and management) structure, and time-in-level guidance.
**Pro tip:** Publish the framework openly to all employees the transparency eliminates the "what do I need to do to get promoted?" ambiguity and reduces bias in promotion decisions.

### 7.16 Compensation Benchmarking Analysis
**Use case:** Conduct a compensation benchmarking analysis to ensure competitive and equitable pay.
**Best for:** HR managers, compensation analysts, finance leads

Act as a compensation analyst. Benchmark [10] key roles at [company name] against market data from [Radford, Payscale, Levels.fyi] for [industry] in [geographic region]. For each role, collect: base salary range (P10, P25, P50, P75, P90), target bonus percentage, and equity grant range. Compare your current pay against market P50 and calculate compa-ratio for each role. Identify roles that fall below [P25] (urgent adjustment needed) and roles above [P75] (check for budget efficiency). Include a geographic adjustment multiplier for remote roles in [high-cost and low-cost regions]. Provide a total compensation philosophy statement: target market [P50/P75] for base, with bonus and equity bringing total compensation to [P75] for top performers.

**Expected output:** A compensation benchmarking report with role-level market comparison, compa-ratios, gap analysis, geographic adjustments, and compensation philosophy statement.
**Pro tip:** Benchmark against companies you compete for talent against, not just companies in your industry the talent market cares about role, not sector.

### 7.17 Employee Recognition Program Design
**Use case:** Create a meaningful employee recognition program that reinforces company values.
**Best for:** HR managers, people ops, culture champions

Act as an employee recognition program designer. Design a [3-tier] recognition program for [company name] ([N] employees). Tier 1 Peer-to-Peer: public shoutouts in [#channel] on Slack with [company values] tags, small point-based rewards ([$25] gift cards redeemed at [50] points). Tier 2 Manager Nomination: monthly awards for [3] categories ([Value Champion, Innovation Award, Customer Hero]) with a [$200] bonus and plaque. Tier 3 Leadership Selection: quarterly awards for [2] top contributors with a [$1000] bonus, [extra PTO day], and company-wide announcement. Include program governance: budget ([$X per employee per year]), nomination review committee ([3] cross-functional members), and a rule against same-person repeat wins in [consecutive] periods.

**Expected output:** A 3-tier recognition program with reward structures, nomination processes, budget guidelines, and governance rules.
**Pro tip:** Peer-to-peer recognition drives more engagement than top-down awards because the frequency and authenticity of recognition from colleagues is harder to replicate.

### 7.18 Conflict Resolution Process
**Use case:** Design a structured conflict resolution process for workplace disputes.
**Best for:** HR managers, people ops, team leads

Act as an HR conflict resolution specialist. Design a [4-step] conflict resolution process for [company name]. Step 1 Direct Conversation: the involved parties attempt resolution in a facilitated conversation using ["I feel" statements and active listening]. Step 2 Manager Mediation: if unresolved, the direct manager mediates with a structured agenda (share perspectives, identify interests, brainstorm solutions, agree on action). Step 3 HR Facilitation: if still unresolved, HR leads a formal mediation session with ground rules, signed agreements, and a [30-day] follow-up. Step 4 Formal Resolution: if all steps fail, HR issues a formal decision with any necessary accommodations or separations. Include a [48-hour] cooling-off period between steps if emotions are escalated. Provide a confidential reporting channel for cases where power dynamics make direct conversation unsafe.

**Expected output:** A 4-step conflict resolution process with facilitation guides, timeframes, escalation rules, and confidential reporting options.
**Pro tip:** Train all managers on Step 1 facilitation skills the earlier a conflict is resolved, the less productivity and team morale it costs.

### 7.19 Exit Interview Analysis and Insights
**Use case:** Conduct and analyze exit interviews to identify systemic retention risks.
**Best for:** HR managers, people ops, talent leads

Act as an HR data analyst. Analyze [last 12 months] of exit interviews from [N] departing employees at [company name]. Categorize departure reasons into [5] buckets: compensation, career growth, management, work-life balance, and role fit. Calculate voluntary turnover rate by department, tenure band ([0-6 months], [6-12 months], [1-3 years], [3+ years]), and manager. Identify the top [3] systemic themes (e.g., "manager effectiveness in Department X" or "compensation lag in Engineering"). For each theme, estimate the impact (if [X] more employees leave for the same reason, potential cost is [$Y] in replacement). Recommend [3] policy changes with expected retention improvement.

**Expected output:** An exit interview analysis report with departure reason breakdown, thematic analysis, cost impact estimates, and policy recommendations.
**Pro tip:** Track "regrettable vs non-regrettable" turnover separately losing a high performer to a competitor is a different problem from a low performer leaving voluntarily.

### 7.20 Talent Retention Strategy
**Use case:** Build a comprehensive talent retention strategy to reduce voluntary turnover.
**Best for:** HR leaders, talent managers, executive teams

Act as a talent retention strategist. Design a [12-month] retention strategy for [company name] ([N] employees, current voluntary turnover rate [X%]). Address [5] drivers: compensation (annual market adjustment cycle, spot bonuses for overperformance), career growth (individual development plans for every employee, internal promotion rate target [>=60%]), management quality (manager training program, 360-degree feedback for all managers, skip-level meetings [quarterly]), culture and flexibility (remote/hybrid options, wellness stipend, [3]-day weekends [quarterly]), and recognition (peer-to-peer program, tenure milestones, project completion celebrations). Set a turnover reduction target (reduce from [X%] to [Y%] within [12] months). Create a quarterly retention dashboard with leading indicators (engagement survey scores, promotion rate, internal mobility).

**Expected output:** A 12-month retention strategy with multi-dimensional action plan, turnover reduction targets, and quarterly leading indicator dashboard.
**Pro tip:** The single highest-leverage retention lever is manager quality an employee with a great manager stays [2-3x] longer than one with a poor manager, regardless of compensation.

## 8. AI, Technology & Future Trends Prompts (180 Prompts)

### 8.1 AI Adoption Strategy for Business
**Use case:** Develop a strategy for adopting AI across business operations
**Best for:** Executives, CTOs, digital transformation leads

Act as an AI adoption strategist. I need to develop a strategy for integrating AI into my business operations.

Company: [describe business] | Industry: [your sector] | Current AI maturity: [none/exploring/implementing] | Business goals: [efficiency, innovation, customer experience] | Data available: [what data you have] | Team: [technical capabilities] | Budget: [available for AI initiatives] | Risk tolerance: [conservative/moderate/aggressive]

Design an AI adoption strategy. Opportunity Assessment — Identify high-impact AI use cases across: customer service (chatbots, sentiment analysis), marketing (personalization, content generation), sales (lead scoring, forecasting), operations (automation, optimization), product (recommendations, insights). Prioritize by impact and feasibility. AI Maturity Roadmap — Level 1: Experiment (pilot projects, low-risk, learn). Level 2: Operationalize (integrate AI into existing workflows). Level 3: Transform (AI-native processes, competitive advantage). Timeline and milestones for each level. Data Foundation — Data readiness: collection, cleaning, labeling, storage, governance. Data infrastructure investments needed before AI can succeed. Team and Skills — Build vs buy: when to hire ML engineers vs when to use AI SaaS products. Training existing team on AI literacy. Partner with AI vendors or consultants. AI Vendors and Tools — Evaluate: AI platforms (OpenAI, Anthropic, Google AI), vertical AI tools (Jasper for content, Gong for sales), ML platforms (AWS SageMaker, GCP Vertex AI). Build vs buy decision framework. Risk and Governance — AI ethics: bias detection and mitigation. Data privacy: customer data in AI training. Hallucination risks: human-in-the-loop for critical decisions. AI policy: acceptable use guidelines for employees. Change Management — Communication: AI as augmentation, not replacement. Training: how to work with AI tools. Managing fear and resistance. Success Stories: quick wins to build momentum. Measurement — ROI framework: cost savings, revenue uplift, productivity gains, customer satisfaction. Tracking adoption rates across teams. Continuous evaluation of AI tool performance.

**Expected output:** AI adoption strategy with opportunity assessment, roadmap, vendor selection, and governance
**Pro tip:** The biggest mistake in AI adoption is trying to solve everything at once — pick one high-impact, low-risk use case, prove it works, then expand

### 8.2 Generative AI Content Strategy
**Use case:** Leverage generative AI for content creation while maintaining quality and brand voice
**Best for:** Content teams, marketers, creative directors

Act as an AI content strategist. I need to develop a strategy for using generative AI in content creation.

Company: [describe business] | Content needs: [types and volume] | Current team: [writers, designers] | Brand voice: [brand personality] | Quality standards: [current quality bar] | Risk concerns: [accuracy, plagiarism, brand safety] | Tools: [current AI tools or none]

Design a generative AI content strategy. Use Case Mapping — Where AI adds value: drafting (blog posts, social, emails), editing (grammar, style, consistency), ideation (headlines, angles, topics), personalization (email, ads, landing pages), translation, image generation, video scripting. Where humans must remain: strategy, fact-checking, final approval, creative direction. AI + Human Workflow — AI drafts > Human edits > AI polishes > Human approves. Clear handoff points. Quality bar: AI output is starting point, not finished product. Prompt Engineering System — Standardized prompt templates for different content types. Brand voice prompts: tone, vocabulary, examples, constraints. Style guides encoded into prompts. Prompt library for the team. Review and iterate prompts based on output quality.
