Content Automation — Batch content generation (SEO articles, social posts), personalization at scale (email variations, ad copy), content repurposing (blog to social, video to blog), translation and localization. Quality Control — Fact-checking process: AI-generated stats, claims, quotes must be verified. AI detection: ensure appropriate human authorship. Plagiarism checking: run AI output through originality checkers. Brand voice audit: regular review of AI content for consistency. Governance and Policy — AI content policy: disclosure requirements, prohibited use cases, approval workflows, data privacy (don't put proprietary info into public AI tools). Training — Team training: prompt engineering, editing AI output, recognizing AI limitations, responsible use guidelines. Measurement — Productivity: time saved per content piece. Quality: engagement metrics, brand consistency scores. Volume: content output increase. Cost: cost per piece reduction.

**Expected output:** Generative AI content strategy with workflow, prompt system, quality control, and governance
**Pro tip:** The best AI content is invisible — readers should never think "this sounds like AI wrote it." Your human editors are more important than ever

### 8.3 Automation and Workflow Optimization
**Use case:** Identify and implement automation opportunities across the business
**Best for:** Operations leads, CTOs, process improvement teams

Act as an automation strategist. I need to identify and implement automation opportunities in my business.

Company: [describe business] | Department focus: [which area to automate first] | Current manual processes: [repetitive tasks] | Tools: [existing tech stack] | Team: [who would implement] | Budget: [available for automation] | Goals: [cost savings, speed, accuracy, scalability]

Design an automation strategy. Automation Opportunity Assessment — Identify candidates: repetitive (same steps every time), rule-based (if/then decisions), high-volume (done frequently), error-prone (human mistakes common), time-consuming (takes significant manual effort). Prioritization matrix: effort vs impact. Automation Types — Robotic Process Automation (RPA): UiPath, Automation Anywhere for UI-based tasks. Workflow Automation: Zapier, Make, n8n for connecting apps. AI Automation: document processing, data extraction, classification, content generation. Custom Automation: scripts, APIs, internal tools. Process Mapping — Document current process steps, decision points, inputs/outputs, systems involved. Identify automation candidates at each step. As-is vs to-be process maps. Implementation Approach — Quick wins first (implement in days, not months). Crawl-walk-run: start with simple automations, add complexity over time. Measure ROI before scaling. Build vs buy: off-the-shelf automation tools vs custom development. Change Management — Communicate automation purpose: reduce drudgery, not replace people. Reskill team members whose roles change. Involve process owners in automation design. Monitoring and Optimization — Track: time saved, error reduction, cost savings, throughput increase. Monitor automation health (failure rates). Continuous improvement: iterate on automations as processes change.

**Expected output:** Automation strategy with opportunity assessment, tools, implementation plan, and change management
**Pro tip:** Before automating a process, ask "should this process exist at all?" Automating a bad process just gives you faster bad results

### 8.4 Digital Transformation Roadmap
**Use case:** Plan and execute digital transformation initiatives across the organization
**Best for:** CIOs, CTOs, digital transformation leads

Act as a digital transformation consultant. I need to develop a digital transformation roadmap.

Company: [describe business] | Industry: [sector] | Current digital maturity: [low/medium/high] | Business challenges: [competition, efficiency, growth] | Technology: [current systems] | Culture: [change readiness] | Budget: [transformation budget]

Design a digital transformation roadmap. Current State Assessment — Technology audit: systems, integrations, tech debt, cloud maturity. Process audit: manual vs digital, bottlenecks, inefficiencies. Skills audit: team capabilities, gaps. Customer experience: digital channels, satisfaction, friction points. Vision and Principles — Define digital transformation vision: what does "digitally transformed" look like? Guiding principles: cloud-first, data-driven, customer-centric, agile, security-by-design. Transformation Pillars — Customer Experience: omnichannel, personalization, self-service, digital-first interactions. Operations: automation, digitized processes, real-time data, integrated systems. Technology: cloud migration, modern architecture, API strategy, data platform. Culture: digital skills, agile methods, innovation mindset, data literacy. Phased Roadmap — Phase 1 (0-6 months): Quick wins, pilot projects, build foundation (cloud migration plan, data strategy). Phase 2 (6-18 months): Scale successful pilots, implement core platforms, process digitization. Phase 3 (18-36 months): Full transformation, AI/ML integration, ecosystem expansion, continuous innovation. Governance — Transformation office: executive sponsor, program manager, cross-functional team. Steering committee. KPIs: project milestones, adoption metrics, business outcomes. Risk management. Change Management — Communication plan: vision, progress, wins. Training programs: digital skills building. Change champions network. Managing resistance. Culture change: from "why change?" to "how can we improve?"

**Expected output:** Digital transformation roadmap with current state, vision, phased plan, and change management
**Pro tip:** Digital transformation is 20% technology and 80% culture and change management — the hardest part isn't the software, it's getting people to work differently

### 8.5 Cybersecurity and Data Privacy Program
**Use case:** Build a cybersecurity program that protects the business and builds customer trust
**Best for:** CISOs, security leads, IT managers

Act as a cybersecurity consultant. I need to build or improve a cybersecurity program.

Company: [describe business] | Industry: [sector] | Company size: [employees] | Data types: [customer data, PII, financial, IP] | Current security: [existing measures] | Compliance: [applicable regulations] | Budget: [security budget] | Threat landscape: [specific industry threats]

Design a cybersecurity program. Security Framework — Choose: NIST CSF, ISO 27001, CIS Controls. Framework provides structure for all security activities. Risk Assessment — Asset inventory: all systems, data, devices, applications. Threat modeling: identify threats per asset. Vulnerability assessment: scan for known vulnerabilities. Risk scoring: likelihood x impact. Prioritized remediation plan. Security Controls — Access control: least privilege, MFA, SSO, PAM (privileged access management). Network security: firewall, segmentation, VPN, zero trust. Endpoint security: EDR, antivirus, device management, encryption. Application security: secure SDLC, SAST/DAST scanning, dependency scanning, WAF. Data security: encryption at rest and in transit, DLP, data classification, backup. Incident Response — IR plan: preparation, detection, containment, eradication, recovery, lessons learned. IR team: roles and contact information. Tabletop exercises quarterly. Communication plan for stakeholders. Compliance — Regulatory requirements: GDPR, CCPA, HIPAA, SOC2, PCI-DSS, SOX. Compliance calendar. Audit preparation. Evidence collection. Training — Security awareness training: phishing simulations, secure practices, reporting incidents. Role-based training: developers (secure coding), executives (risk management), all employees (basics). Vendor Security — Third-party risk management: vendor security assessments, contract security requirements, ongoing monitoring. Data processing agreements. Continuous Monitoring — SIEM: log collection, correlation, alerting. SOC (in-house or MSSP). Weekly/monthly review cadence. Continuous improvement.

**Expected output:** Cybersecurity program with risk assessment, controls, incident response, and compliance
**Pro tip:** The best security investment is security awareness training for employees — 90% of breaches start with a human error. A trained employee is your best defense

### 8.6 Cloud Migration Strategy
**Use case:** Plan and execute migration of infrastructure and applications to the cloud
**Best for:** CTOs, infrastructure leads, DevOps

Act as a cloud architect. I need to develop a cloud migration strategy.

Company: [describe business] | Current infrastructure: [on-prem/hybrid/co-located] | Target cloud: [AWS/Azure/GCP/multi-cloud] | Workloads: [applications and data to migrate] | Drivers: [cost, scalability, innovation, disaster recovery] | Constraints: [compliance, latency, legacy systems] | Team: [cloud skills available]

Design a cloud migration strategy. Migration Strategies (7 Rs) — Rehost (lift and shift): fastest, least modification. Replatform: optimize for cloud (e.g., move to RDS). Refactor/Re-architect: rebuild as cloud-native (containers, serverless). Repurchase: move to SaaS. Retire: decommission. Retain: keep on-prem for now. Relocate: move to a different cloud region. Assessment — Workload inventory: applications, dependencies, data volumes, performance requirements. Migration prioritization: low-hanging fruit first (simple apps, dev/test), complex migrations later (legacy, regulated). Tagging and documentation. Landing Zone — Multi-account strategy (management, security, dev, staging, prod). Network design: VPC, subnets, connectivity (VPN/Direct Connect). Identity and access management (SSO, roles, policies). Governance: tagging, budgets, compliance guardrails. Migration Process — Wave planning: group workloads into migration waves. Runbook: step-by-step migration process per workload. Testing: pre and post migration validation. Rollback plan for each wave. Optimization — Right-sizing: match resources to actual usage. Reserved instances: commit to savings for stable workloads. Auto-scaling: for variable workloads. Cost monitoring and budgeting. Continuous optimization. Security in Cloud — Shared responsibility model: understand what cloud provider secures vs customer. Cloud security tools: Security Hub, GuardDuty, Config. Encryption: KMS, Cloud HSM. Compliance in cloud: configure for regulatory requirements. Organizational Change — Cloud Center of Excellence (CCoE): governance, best practices, training. Cloud training for team: certifications, hands-on labs. New roles: cloud architect, FinOps specialist. Cultural shift: from managing servers to managing services.

**Expected output:** Cloud migration strategy with workload assessment, landing zone, migration waves, and optimization
**Pro tip:** Lift-and-shift rarely saves money — the real cloud savings come from refactoring for cloud-native architectures. Plan to rearchitect after migrating

### 8.7 Data Strategy and Governance
**Use case:** Develop a comprehensive data strategy that treats data as a strategic asset
**Best for:** CDOs, data leaders, CTOs

Act as a data strategist. I need to develop a data strategy for my organization.

Company: [describe business] | Data maturity: [aware/exploring/defined/managed/optimized] | Data sources: [internal systems, external, third-party] | Use cases: [analytics, AI, reporting, operations] | Current challenges: [data silos, quality, access, governance] | Goals: [data-driven decisions, AI, efficiency]

Design a data strategy. Data Vision and Principles — Vision: what does data-driven mean for the company? Principles: data as an asset, privacy by design, quality over quantity, democratize access, govern responsibly. Data Architecture — Modern data stack: ingestion (Fivetran/Airbyte), storage (data lake/lakehouse), transformation (dbt), serving (BI tools, ML models). Architecture decision: data warehouse vs data lake vs lakehouse. Real-time vs batch. Data Governance — Data catalog: inventory of all data assets, metadata, lineage. Data ownership: each dataset has an owner responsible for quality and access. Data quality: standards, monitoring, remediation processes. Data privacy: classification (public/internal/confidential/restricted), access controls, masking. Data Lifecycle: creation, storage, usage, archival, deletion. Data Culture — Data literacy: training programs for all roles. Self-service analytics: empower teams to answer their own questions. Data-driven decision framework: decisions backed by data, not hierarchy. Celebrating data wins. Data Organization — Centralized (data team serves all): consistent but bottleneck. Decentralized (data embedded in teams): scalable but inconsistent. Hub-and-spoke: central platform team + embedded analysts. Recommended model. Data Monetization — Internal: better decisions, operational efficiency, customer insights. External: data products, data as a service, insights for partners. Measurement: value of data initiatives. Data Ethics — Responsible AI: bias detection, fairness, transparency. Data use boundaries: what data can and cannot be used for. Consent management. Customer trust as competitive advantage.

**Expected output:** Data strategy with vision, architecture, governance, culture, and organization
**Pro tip:** Data strategy fails when it's driven by technology instead of business outcomes — start with "what decisions do we need to make better?" not "what data do we have?"

### 8.8 Product-Led Growth Strategy
**Use case:** Build a product-led growth motion that drives acquisition, retention, and expansion
**Best for:** Growth PMs, founders, product leaders

Act as a product-led growth (PLG) strategist. I need to design a PLG strategy.

Company: [describe business] | Product: [describe product] | Current growth: [sales-led/marketing-led/hybrid] | Business model: [SaaS/usage-based] | User base: [current users] | Self-serve capability: [can users get value without sales] | Goals: [acquisition, conversion, expansion]

Design a product-led growth strategy. PLG Motion — Free tier or trial: what's the entry point? Free tier: limited features (drive upgrade). Time-limited trial: full features (drive urgency). Usage-limited: free up to X (drive growth). Value-first: core value free forever (drive adoption). Activation — Define "aha moment": what action predicts long-term retention. Activation flow: user signs up > experiences value > recognizes value > converts. Optimize each step. Funnel metrics: signup > activation > retention > referral > revenue. Virality — Built-in sharing: collaborative features (multiplayer), shared documents, team invites. Viral coefficient: users invited per user. Viral cycle time: how fast do invites convert. Network effects: product gets better with more users. Monetization — Freemium to paid conversion. Pricing: usage-based, tiered, or hybrid. Upgrade triggers: feature limits, usage limits, team size. Self-serve purchase flow: frictionless credit card, automated provisioning. Expansion — Usage growth: more users, more usage, more features. Account expansion: team grows within org. Product-qualified leads (PQLs): users showing buying signals get sales outreach. Customer success at scale for self-serve users. Product Analytics for PLG — Tracking: signups, activation rate, time-to-value, feature adoption, NPS, viral coefficient, conversion rate, expansion rate, churn. Cohort analysis for retention. Funnel analysis for conversion. Product Dashboards for growth team. PLG + Sales Hybrid — When to add sales: high-value accounts, complex use cases, enterprise requirements. Sales-assist model: product identifies PQLs, sales closes. Product-led sales: sales uses product data to personalize outreach.

**Expected output:** Product-led growth strategy with activation, virality, monetization, and analytics
**Pro tip:** The most powerful growth engine is a product so good that users invite others without being asked — design for indispensability, not virality hacks

### 8.9 Innovation and R&D Management
**Use case:** Build an innovation pipeline and manage R&D investment for future growth
**Best for:** CTOs, innovation leads, R&D directors

Act as an innovation management consultant. I need to build a systematic innovation and R&D capability.

Company: [describe business] | Industry: [sector] | Innovation history: [what's worked/didn't] | R&D budget: [current spend] | Team: [R&D/innovation resources] | Market: [technology trends affecting your industry] | Goals: [new products, competitive advantage, future-proofing]

Design an innovation and R&D management system. Innovation Portfolio — Horizon model: Horizon 1 (core business improvements, 70% of investment), Horizon 2 (adjacent opportunities, 20%), Horizon 3 (transformational bets, 10%). Balance short-term ROI and long-term bets. Innovation Sources — Internal: R&D team, employee ideas (hackathons, suggestion system), customer feedback (NPS comments, support tickets, interviews). External: technology scouting (patents, startups, universities), partnerships (joint R&D, acquisitions), open innovation (crowdsourcing, hackathons). Technology Scouting — Systematic monitoring of emerging technologies relevant to your industry. Tools: patent databases, academic papers, startup tracking (Crunchbase), conferences, analyst reports (Gartner Hype Cycle). Technology radar: assess, prioritize, act. R&D Process — Stage-Gate for innovation: Idea > Concept > Feasibility > Development > Testing > Launch. Gates: go/kill/adjust decisions at each stage with clear criteria. Running multiple parallel experiments. Innovation Metrics — Input: ideas generated, projects in pipeline, R&D spend. Process: ideas-to-launch cycle time, stage conversion rates, kill rate (high kill rate = good discipline). Output: new product revenue, ROI of innovation projects, patents, competitive differentiation. Culture of Innovation — Psychological safety: fail fast, learn fast. Exploration time: 10-20% time for new ideas (Google's 20% time). Recognition: celebrate learning from failures, not just successes. Cross-functional innovation teams. Leadership support for experimentation. Build vs Buy vs Partner — Build: when technology is core to competitive advantage. Buy: when speed matters and good solutions exist. Partner: when capability is needed temporarily or for non-core areas. Decision framework.

**Expected output:** Innovation and R&D management system with portfolio, process, metrics, and culture
**Pro tip:** The kill rate of your innovation pipeline is the best indicator of its health — if too few projects are killed, you're not taking enough risk or not being honest about what's working

### 8.10 Future of Work Strategy
**Use case:** Prepare your organization for the future of work — AI, remote, gig economy, and changing expectations
**Best for:** Executives, HR leaders, strategists

Act as a future of work strategist. I need to prepare my organization for the evolving world of work.

Company: [describe business] | Industry: [sector] | Workforce: [current composition] | Trends: [AI, remote, automation impacting your industry] | Current practices: [work model, culture, technology] | Goals: [attract talent, stay competitive, adapt to change]

Design a future of work strategy. AI and Augmentation — How AI will change roles in your organization. Not replacement but augmentation: AI handles routine, humans handle judgment, creativity, relationships. Reskilling strategy: identify roles most impacted, create retraining programs. New roles: AI prompt engineers, automation architects, data storytellers. Work Model — Hybrid work: optimal balance of remote and in-office. Office purpose: collaboration, culture, connection, not individual work. Remote infrastructure: async-first communication, digital HQ. Flexibility as competitive advantage for talent attraction. Talent Model — Full-time employees + contractors + gig workers + AI agents. Core (strategic, full-time) + Flexible (project-based, contractors) + Augmented (AI, automation). Talent ecosystem management. Skills-Based Organization — Shift from job titles to skills. Skills taxonomy: technical, soft, leadership. Skills gap analysis: current vs future needs. Skills-based hiring: hire for capability, not credentials. Internal mobility: move people to where skills are needed. Continuous learning as expectation. Employee Value Proposition — Purpose-driven work: connection to mission. Flexibility: when, where, how to work. Development: continuous learning, growth. Wellness: mental health, boundaries, work-life integration. Financial: fair comp, equity, benefits. Connection: belonging, community, relationships. Technology Enablement — AI-powered productivity tools, collaboration platforms, knowledge management, learning platforms, wellness technology. Digital employee experience. Change Leadership — Leading through uncertainty: transparent communication, involvement in decisions, experimentation mindset. Manager transformation: from command-and-control to coach-and-enable. Building organizational adaptability.

**Expected output:** Future of work strategy covering AI augmentation, work models, talent, skills, and EVP
**Pro tip:** The organizations that thrive in the future won't be the ones that predict it best — they'll be the ones that adapt fastest. Build adaptability into your DNA

### 1.52 Market Sizing and TAM Analysis
**Use case:** Calculate Total Addressable Market (TAM), Serviceable Addressable Market (SAM), and Serviceable Obtainable Market (SOM)
**Best for:** Founders, product managers, investors

Act as a market research analyst specializing in market sizing. I need to estimate the market opportunity for my product or service. Begin by asking me these questions one at a time: what category does my product serve, what is the average revenue per customer in my space, what geographic markets do I target, and what is my primary customer segment. Once I answer, calculate TAM using the top-down approach (total market revenue from industry reports), SAM using bottom-up (average revenue per customer x total potential customers who fit my ideal profile), and SOM using value theory (what share of SAM I can realistically capture based on competitive differentiation, distribution channels, and marketing budget). Provide your calculations step by step, cite any assumptions you make, and highlight the three biggest risks to your estimates.

**Expected output:** A structured TAM/SAM/SOM analysis with clear calculations, data sources cited, and risk assessment
**Pro tip:** Investors prefer bottom-up SOM calculations over top-down TAM — show you understand how you'll actually reach customers

### 1.53 Content Repurposing Engine
**Use case:** Transform one piece of content into 15+ formats across channels
**Best for:** Content marketers, social media managers

Act as a content repurposing strategist. I will give you a piece of content (blog post, video transcript, podcast episode, or report). You will analyze it and generate a repurposing plan that extracts every possible format from it. For each format — social posts (LinkedIn, Twitter/X, Instagram), email newsletter, infographic script, short-form video script (Reels/TikTok), podcast discussion script, SlideShare deck outline, Quora answer, Reddit post, guest post pitch, and internal memo — provide the angle, the target audience for that platform, and a brief adaptation strategy (what to keep, what to change, what to add). Prioritize the formats by expected ROI based on the content type. Also suggest the optimal posting cadence for the repurposed pieces so they don't cannibalize each other.

**Expected output:** A detailed repurposing calendar with 15+ format adaptations prioritized by channel ROI
**Pro tip:** The 80/20 rule applies: 80% of repurposing value comes from adapting for LinkedIn, email, and one short-form video platform. Do those first before branching out

### 1.54 Pricing Strategy Optimizer
**Use case:** Determine optimal pricing for a product or service based on value, competition, and psychology
**Best for:** Founders, product managers, marketers

Act as a pricing strategist with experience across SaaS, e-commerce, and service businesses. I will describe my product and you will help me set the optimal price. First, ask me these questions: what is my cost structure, who are my main competitors and their prices, what is my unique value proposition, and what is my target customer's willingness to pay. Then analyze three pricing models: value-based (price set by perceived value to customer), cost-plus (cost + margin), and competitor-based (market positioning). Recommend a specific price point with rationale. Then design a tiered pricing structure with three plans, applying psychological pricing tactics: charm pricing ($X.99), decoy effect (a middle tier that makes the top tier look valuable), and anchoring (show a high-priced option first). For each tier, specify features included, the monthly/annual price, and the messaging angle to position that tier.

**Expected output:** A complete pricing strategy with three tiers, dollar amounts, positioning angles, and psychological triggers
**Pro tip:** Never compete on price alone — the middle tier should be your "decoy" that makes your top tier look like the best value

### 1.55 Viral Referral Program Designer
**Use case:** Design a customer referral program with viral mechanics
**Best for:** Growth marketers, founders, product managers

Act as a growth marketing specialist who has designed referral programs generating 30%+ of new customers for companies like Dropbox, Airbnb, and Uber. I need you to design a referral program for my business. Start by asking me: what is my product, what is my average customer LTV, what is my current acquisition cost, and who are my power users. Then design a complete referral program with: reward structure (dual-sided incentive — reward both referrer and referee), referral mechanics (how users share — unique link, email invite, social share, QR code), friction reduction (pre-filled messages, one-click sharing, address book import), viral coefficient target (how many new users each referrer should bring), and program rules (limits, fraud prevention, payout timing). Also include: the referral ask — the exact moment in the customer journey to ask for referrals (typically after a success moment like a purchase or positive support interaction), the email/SMS copy for the referral invitation, the landing page the referred user sees, and how to track and optimize the viral loop.

**Expected output:** A complete referral program spec with rewards, mechanics, messaging, and growth metrics
**Pro tip:** The best time to ask for a referral is immediately after a customer achieves a "wow moment" — not on signup, not on purchase, but when they first experience your core value

### 1.56 Competitor Intelligence System
**Use case:** Build a systematic competitive monitoring and analysis framework
**Best for:** Product marketers, strategists, founders

Act as a competitive intelligence analyst. I need to set up an ongoing system to track and analyze my competitors. Start by asking me: who are my top 3-5 competitors, what is my product category, what are my key product features, and what channels do I sell through. Then build a competitive intelligence framework with these components: monitoring sources (competitor websites, social media, review sites, job postings, press releases, investor updates, product hunt launches, app store updates, pricing pages), tracking cadence (daily: social mentions/reviews, weekly: blog posts/content, monthly: product updates/pricing, quarterly: strategic analysis), analysis template (feature comparison matrix, pricing comparison, positioning/messaging comparison, strengths/weaknesses, strategic moves, and threats/opportunities), and reporting format (a weekly 5-bullet competitive update and a monthly deep-dive for each competitor). Also provide a competitive response decision framework: when to react (feature parity, pricing changes, market entry) vs when to ignore (distractions, non-overlapping segments, unproven strategies).

**Expected output:** A competitive intelligence system with monitoring sources, cadence, analysis templates, and response framework
**Pro tip:** Your competitor's job postings tell you more about their strategy than their blog or press releases — track their hiring patterns religiously

### 1.57 Email Sequence A/B Testing Framework
**Use case:** Design and execute A/B tests for email marketing sequences
**Best for:** Email marketers, growth marketers

Act as an email marketing optimization specialist. I need to set up a systematic A/B testing program for my email sequences. Start by asking: what email platform do I use, what sequences do I currently have running, what are my current metrics (open rate, click rate, conversion rate), and what is my minimum sample size per test. Then design an A/B testing framework covering: what to test (subject lines, preview text, sender name, send time/day, CTA button text/color/placement, email length, personalization depth, image vs no-image, offer type), testing methodology (A/A tests to establish baseline, one variable at a time, statistical significance at 95% confidence, minimum sample size calculation), test calendar (which tests to run in which order, prioritizing subject lines and CTAs first as highest impact), winner implementation (how to roll out winners — update sequence, measure uplift, document learnings), and a learning log template to capture every test result and insight for future sequences.

**Expected output:** An A/B testing framework with test variables, methodology, calendar, and knowledge repository template
**Pro tip:** Test your subject lines on a 10% segment first before full send — if open rate drops more than 5%, revise before sending to the rest

### 1.58 Customer Segmentation Clusterer
**Use case:** Segment customers into actionable groups based on behavior, demographics, and value
**Best for:** Marketing managers, product managers, founders

Act as a customer analytics expert. I need to segment my customer base into meaningful groups for targeted marketing. Ask me these questions: what customer data do I have (demographics, purchase history, website behavior, support tickets), what is my total customer count, what are my key business goals for segmentation, and what tools do I use for analytics. Then create a segmentation model with at least 4-5 segments using these criteria: behavioral (purchase frequency, average order value, product categories, engagement score), demographic (industry, company size, role, location), psychographic (pain points, goals, buying motivations, risk tolerance), and value (LTV, acquisition channel, referral activity, churn risk). For each segment, provide: segment name, size (% of customer base), key characteristics, primary needs/pain points, recommended marketing approach, channel preference, messaging angle, and suggested offer type. Also identify which segments are most profitable, which have highest growth potential, and which are at highest churn risk.

**Expected output:** 4-5 customer segments with detailed profiles, marketing approaches, and prioritization matrix
**Pro tip:** The most actionable segments are based on behavior, not demographics — group by what customers DO, not who they ARE

### 1.59 Social Media Content Calendar Builder
**Use case:** Plan 30 days of social media content across platforms with themes and formats
**Best for:** Social media managers, content marketers

Act as a social media content strategist. I will tell you my business type, target audience, and platforms. You will build a 30-day content calendar optimized for engagement and conversion. First ask me: what is my business, who is my target audience, which platforms do I use (Instagram, LinkedIn, Twitter/X, TikTok, Facebook), and what are my content goals (awareness, engagement, traffic, sales). Then create a 30-day calendar with: weekly themes (e.g., Education Week, Behind-the-Scenes Week, Social Proof Week, Promotion Week), content ratio (40% value/education, 25% engagement/polls, 20% social proof/testimonials, 15% promotional), content formats per platform (carousels for Instagram, thread-style for Twitter/X, thought leadership for LinkedIn, how-to for TikTok), specific post ideas for each day with caption angle and visual description, optimal posting times per platform, hashtag strategy (3-5 high-volume + 3-5 niche tags), and engagement plan (how to respond to comments, DMs, and mentions to maximize algorithmic reach).

**Expected output:** A 30-day content calendar with daily post ideas, formats, timing, and engagement strategy per platform
**Pro tip:** The algorithm rewards consistency over perfection — post 5-6 days per week at 70% quality rather than 2-3 days at 100%

### 1.60 Lead Magnet Concept Generator
**Use case:** Create high-converting lead magnets for email list growth
**Best for:** Email marketers, content marketers, founders

Act as a lead generation specialist who has built email lists from zero to 100,000+ subscribers. I need you to help me create a lead magnet that will attract my ideal customers. Ask me these questions: what is my product or service, who is my target customer, what is their biggest pain point, and what format do they prefer (PDF, video series, template, toolkit, checklist, webinar). Then based on my answers, generate 5 lead magnet concepts ranked by conversion potential. For each concept provide: the title (specific, benefit-driven, curiosity-inducing), format (PDF guide, video course, template pack, toolkit, scorecard, challenge), description of what the lead magnet contains, how it solves the specific pain point, where in the customer journey it fits (top of funnel awareness vs middle of funnel consideration), and a sample landing page headline and bullet points. Also recommend the delivery mechanism (welcome email, thank you page, content upgrade popup), and a bridge strategy — how each lead magnet naturally leads into a product or service offer.

**Expected output:** 5 lead magnet concepts ranked by conversion potential with titles, formats, content outlines, and bridge strategies
**Pro tip:** The best lead magnets solve ONE specific problem immediately — don't try to cover everything. A "5-day challenge" consistently outperforms a "complete guide"

### 1.61 Brand Partnership Pitch Generator
**Use case:** Craft compelling brand partnership proposals for collaborations and sponsorships
**Best for:** Marketing managers, partnership managers, founders

Act as a brand partnership strategist who has brokered deals between startups and major brands. I need you to help me pitch a partnership to another brand. Ask me: what is my brand, what are my audience demographics and reach, what type of partnership am I seeking (co-marketing, sponsorship, affiliate, co-product, content collaboration), and who is the target brand I want to partner with. Then craft a complete partnership pitch that includes: subject line for the outreach email, opening hook that references something specific about their brand, value proposition for them (what they gain — access to your audience, content, expertise, distribution), partnership structure (what each side contributes and receives), proposed timeline and deliverables, past success stories or credibility signals, and a specific low-friction first step (e.g., "a 30-minute call to explore" or "a co-branded social post as a test"). Also provide a follow-up sequence: day 3 (add value — share something relevant), day 7 (re-engage with new angle), day 14 (final reach-out with urgency or alternative).

**Expected output:** A complete partnership pitch email and 3-part follow-up sequence
**Pro tip:** The best partnerships are asymmetric — offer more value than you receive in the first deal. Long-term relationships come from making the other party look good internally

### 1.62 Marketing Budget Allocator
**Use case:** Optimize marketing budget allocation across channels for maximum ROI
**Best for:** Marketing directors, founders, CMOs

Act as a marketing finance analyst. I need to allocate my marketing budget across channels for maximum return. Ask me: what is my total monthly marketing budget, what channels am I currently using, what are my current costs and returns per channel, what is my primary marketing goal (revenue, leads, brand awareness, engagement), and what is my customer acquisition cost target. Then build a budget allocation model that: analyzes historical performance per channel (cost per acquisition, conversion rate, average order value, customer LTV), applies the 70-20-10 rule (70% proven channels, 20% growth channels, 10% experimental), recommends specific dollar amounts per channel with justification, projects expected outcomes (leads, customers, revenue) based on historical conversion data, includes a reallocation trigger (when to shift budget: if a channel's CPA exceeds X%, if a new channel shows promise, if seasonal trends change), and adds a testing budget (recommended % for experimenting with new channels or tactics). Also provide tracking recommendations: which metrics to monitor weekly, monthly, and quarterly to validate the allocation.

**Expected output:** A budget allocation spreadsheet with channel-specific amounts, projected ROI, reallocation triggers, and testing budget
**Pro tip:** Don't optimize for the cheapest channel — optimize for the channel that delivers the highest LTV customers. A $50 CPA with $500 LTV beats a $10 CPA with $50 LTV every time

### 1.63 Webinar Script Architect
**Use case:** Write a complete webinar script that converts attendees into customers
**Best for:** Marketing teams, founders, sales teams

Act as a webinar scriptwriter who has created webinars generating 30%+ conversion rates. I will tell you my topic, target audience, and offer. You will write a complete 45-minute webinar script. First ask me: what is the webinar topic, who is the target audience, what is the offer at the end, and what is the main transformation attendees will experience. Then write a full script structured as: hook (first 2 minutes — a shocking stat, story, or question that grabs attention), problem amplification (minutes 2-10 — describe the pain and cost of not solving it), solution introduction (minutes 10-20 — present your framework/methodology, not yet your product), credibility section (minutes 20-25 — case study or testimonial of someone who used your approach), implementation preview (minutes 25-35 — show exactly how it works with a live demo or walkthrough), pitch transition (minutes 35-37 — the pivot from value to offer), offer presentation (minutes 37-42 — what they get, bonuses, guarantee, pricing, urgency), and call to action (minutes 42-45 — clear next step with urgency). Include speaker notes (what to say verbatim), slide descriptions (what to show on screen), and engagement prompts (polls, questions, chat prompts) every 5-7 minutes.

**Expected output:** A complete 45-minute webinar script with speaker notes, slide descriptions, and engagement timestamps
**Pro tip:** Never pitch until you've delivered 80% of the value first. The longer you provide pure value before the offer, the higher your conversion rate

### 1.64 Google Ads Keyword Research Blueprint
**Use case:** Conduct comprehensive keyword research for Google Ads campaigns
**Best for:** PPC marketers, digital marketers

Act as a Google Ads keyword research specialist. I need to build a keyword list for my Google Ads campaign. Ask me: what is my product or service, what is my industry, what are my main product categories, what is my target geography, and what is my daily budget. Then conduct a comprehensive keyword research process: seed keywords (start with 10-15 core terms describing my product), keyword expansion using the "3-2-1 method" — 3 synonyms per seed keyword, 2 related concepts per synonym, 1 long-tail variation per concept, match type strategy (broad modified, phrase, exact — with percentages of budget per type), negative keyword identification (list terms to exclude to avoid wasted spend), keyword grouping into ad groups by search intent (informational, commercial, transactional), competitive keyword analysis (which keywords competitors are bidding on that I'm missing), and volume/cost estimates (estimated monthly searches, suggested bid range, competition level for each keyword). Provide the final list organized by ad group with match types, estimated volumes, and priority rankings.

**Expected output:** A keyword research document with 100+ keywords organized by ad group with match types and estimates
**Pro tip:** Start with exact match and phrase match only — broad match should only be added after you have 100+ conversions and strong negative keyword lists

### 1.65 Customer Avatar Visualization Script
**Use case:** Create a detailed, emotionally-resonant customer avatar for empathetic marketing
**Best for:** Copywriters, marketers, founders

Act as a customer empathy expert trained in human-centered design. I need you to help me build a deep, emotionally-nuanced customer avatar. Ask me: what does my product solve, who currently buys it, and what transformation do they experience. Then guide me through an avatar creation process using the "5 Whys" technique — for each surface-level customer attribute, ask "why does that matter" five times to reach the emotional core. Once complete, write a narrative customer profile that includes: their morning routine (what frustrates them before they find my product), their emotional state (what they fear, what they hope for, what keeps them up at night), their social influences (who they trust, whose opinion matters, what communities they belong to), their decision-making style (impulsive vs methodical, price-sensitive vs value-driven, research-heavy vs trust-recommendation), their language (exact words and phrases they use to describe their problem — capture their actual voice), their objections (the real reasons they hesitate — not the surface reasons but the emotional fears underneath), and their transformation story (a narrative of their journey from frustrated to fulfilled). End with a one-page "user manual" that tells any marketer exactly how to speak to this person.

**Expected output:** A deep customer avatar narrative with emotional drivers, language patterns, objections, and a one-page user manual
**Pro tip:** The most powerful marketing insight is the exact phrase your customer uses to describe their pain — use their words, not your jargon

### 1.66 Social Proof Collection System
**Use case:** Systematically collect and deploy customer testimonials, reviews, and case studies
**Best for:** Marketers, founders, customer success teams

Act as a social proof strategist. I need to build a system for collecting and deploying customer testimonials and reviews. Ask me: what is my product, how many customers do I have, what is my current NPS or satisfaction score, and what channels do customers interact on. Then design a social proof collection system: collection triggers (specific moments to ask — after successful outcome, after positive support interaction, after renewal/upgrade, after NPS promoter response), collection channels (email, in-app prompt, SMS, post-purchase page, review platform), ask templates (3 versions per channel — short/long/incentivized), incentive strategy (discount, feature access, gift card, or simply the dopamine of being featured), review platform strategy (which platforms to target — G2, Capterra, Google Reviews, Trustpilot — and how to funnel customers to them), case study creation process (identify ideal candidates, interview script, approval workflow, format options), content repurposing (testimonial → social post → website section → sales deck → ad creative), and a deployment calendar showing which social proof asset goes where and when. Include a response management system: how to handle negative reviews, how to amplify positive ones, and how to track social proof ROI.

**Expected output:** A complete social proof system with collection triggers, templates, incentives, platform strategy, and deployment calendar
**Pro tip:** Video testimonials convert 3x better than text. Ask customers to record a 30-second testimonial on their phone — the raw, unpolished ones perform best

### 1.67 Crisis Communication Template
**Use case:** Prepare a crisis communication plan for brand reputation management
**Best for:** PR managers, founders, communications teams

Act as a crisis communication specialist with experience managing brand reputation emergencies. I need to prepare a crisis communication plan for my business. Ask me: what is my business type, what are my biggest reputation risks, what is my current brand sentiment, and who are my key stakeholders (customers, investors, employees, partners). Then create a crisis communication framework that covers: risk assessment matrix (identify 5-10 potential crises ranked by likelihood and impact — product failure, data breach, PR scandal, competitor attack, regulatory issue, leadership controversy, employee incident, supply chain disruption), response tiers (Level 1: minor issue — social media response within hours; Level 2: moderate — press release + email + social within 4 hours; Level 3: major — full plan with press conference, all-stakeholder communication, legal review within 2 hours), response templates for each level (holding statement, full statement, FAQ, social media posts, email to customers, email to employees, investor note), approval workflow (who writes, who reviews — legal, PR, executive, who approves), channel strategy (where to communicate first — usually direct to affected parties before public), monitoring plan (what to track, how often, escalation triggers), and post-crisis recovery (reputation repair steps, commitment tracking, follow-up communication cadence). Include a "golden hour" checklist — the first 60 minutes of crisis response.

**Expected output:** A crisis communication plan with risk matrix, response tiers, templates, approval workflow, and golden hour checklist
**Pro tip:** The first rule of crisis communication: respond within the first hour, acknowledge the issue, express empathy, and state what you're doing — even if you don't have all the answers yet. Silence is the worst response

### 1.68 Event Promotion Campaign Blueprint
**Use case:** Plan and execute a multi-channel promotion campaign for a live or virtual event
**Best for:** Event marketers, community managers

Act as an event marketing director with experience launching industry conferences and virtual summits. I need to create a promotion campaign for my event. Ask me: what is the event (name, date, format), who is the target audience, what is the ticket price or is it free, and what speakers/guests are confirmed. Then design a comprehensive promotion campaign timeline: 8 weeks out (announce date, open speaker applications, early bird pricing), 6 weeks out (launch speaker lineup, open registration, early bird deadline, partner outreach), 4 weeks out (content marketing push — blog posts from speakers, social media campaign, email sequence starts), 2 weeks out (urgency campaign — last chance pricing, scarcity messaging, social proof from registrants), 1 week out (final push — daily emails, behind-the-scenes content, logistics information), day of event (live coverage, engagement prompts, FOMO for next year), post-event (recordings, highlights, next event announcement, feedback survey). For each phase specify: channels (email, social, paid, PR, partnerships), content types needed (graphics, copy, video teasers, speaker interviews), key metrics (registrations, email open rate, social engagement, ticket revenue), and specific messaging angles. Include a partnership promotion package — what partners/sponsors receive and how they promote the event to their audiences.

**Expected output:** A phased event promotion campaign with timeline, channel strategy, content needs, and partnership packages
**Pro tip:** The #1 driver of event registrations is speaker promotion. Give your speakers a complete promo kit (email templates, social posts, graphics) and make it dead simple for them to promote

### 1.69 Churn Prediction and Prevention Model
**Use case:** Identify at-risk customers and deploy targeted retention campaigns
**Best for:** Marketing managers, customer success teams, SaaS founders

Act as a customer retention analyst. I need to build a system to predict which customers are likely to churn and proactively intervene. Ask me: what is my business model (subscription, transactional), what customer data do I have access to (usage logs, support tickets, payment history, engagement metrics), what is my current churn rate, and what retention campaigns do I currently run. Then design a churn prediction and prevention system: churn indicators (red flags to monitor — decreased login frequency, reduced feature usage, support ticket sentiment, payment failures, account setting changes, team member departures, competitor mentions), risk scoring model (assign weighted points to each indicator, calculate a composite churn risk score, define thresholds for low/medium/high risk), intervention playbooks for each risk level (low risk: automated nurture emails, feature tips; medium risk: personalized email from CSM, check-in call, targeted offer; high risk: executive reach-out, discount/retention offer, product demo of unused features), trigger timing (when to intervene based on indicator patterns — e.g., 7 days of decreased usage triggers medium-risk protocol), escalation path (automated → CSM → manager → executive based on customer value and risk level), and recovery tracking (monitor re-engagement metrics, track win-back rate, measure retention offer ROI). Also include a win-back sequence for customers who have already churned.

**Expected output:** A churn prediction system with indicators, risk scoring, intervention playbooks, and recovery tracking
**Pro tip:** The best retention strategy is adoption — customers who use your product daily almost never churn. Focus your retention efforts on getting new customers to form a habit within the first 14 days

### 1.70 Micro-Influencer Outreach Campaign
**Use case:** Identify, vet, and partner with micro-influencers for authentic brand promotion
**Best for:** Brand marketers, social media managers

Act as an influencer marketing strategist specializing in micro-influencer campaigns. I need to launch a campaign partnering with micro-influencers (1K-50K followers). Ask me: what is my brand, what is my product, what is my target audience, what is my budget for influencer partnerships, and what platforms are most relevant (Instagram, TikTok, YouTube, LinkedIn). Then design a complete micro-influencer campaign: sourcing strategy (where to find influencers — hashtag searches, competitor follower lists, influencer databases, manual discovery, audience overlap tools), vetting criteria (engagement rate > 3%, audience authenticity score, content quality, brand alignment, past sponsored content performance, audience demographics match), outreach approach (DM vs email, personalization template, what to include in the pitch — product offer, creative freedom promise, compensation, timeline), partnership structure (gifted product only, flat fee + commission, affiliate link, performance bonus), creative brief (what to include: brand guidelines, key messages, do's and don'ts, mandatory disclosures, content specs, approval process), campaign tracking (UTM parameters, promo codes, affiliate links, engagement metrics, conversion tracking), and relationship management (how to build long-term partnerships, how to get repeat collaborations, ambassador program design for top performers). Include a budget allocation model showing expected ROI at different spending levels.

**Expected output:** A micro-influencer campaign plan with sourcing, vetting, outreach, partnership structure, creative brief, and tracking
**Pro tip:** Micro-influencers with 5K-15K followers consistently have the highest engagement rates AND the most authentic audience relationships. They're also more negotiable on price and more enthusiastic about creative freedom

### 1.71 StoryBrand Marketing Framework
**Use case:** Apply Donald Miller's StoryBrand framework to clarify brand messaging
**Best for:** Founders, marketers, copywriters

Act as a StoryBrand-certified messaging strategist. I will guide you through the StoryBrand 7-part framework to clarify your brand messaging. Ask me these questions one at a time: who is your customer (the hero), what does your customer want (their desire), what is their problem (external, internal, and philosophical), what guide/mentor figure does your brand represent, what plan can you give them, what is the call to action (direct and transitional), and what does success (and failure) look like. Then craft a complete StoryBrand BrandScript: one sentence that captures the essence of your message using the formula: "A [hero] who wants [desire] meets [brand] who helps them overcome [problem] by [plan] so they can [success]." Then write the following assets based on the BrandScript: website homepage messaging (headline, subheadline, 3 bullet points, CTA), email sequence hook (the first email in a nurture sequence), elevator pitch (30-second version), about page narrative, and social media bio. Each piece should follow the StoryBrand principle of making the customer the hero and your brand the guide.

**Expected output:** A complete StoryBrand BrandScript with homepage messaging, email hook, elevator pitch, about page, and social bio
**Pro tip:** The most common StoryBrand mistake is making your brand the hero. Your customer is Indiana Jones — you're just the map. Make sure every piece of copy answers "what's in it for me" from the customer's perspective

### 1.72 Product Launch Email Sequence
**Use case:** Write a complete pre-launch and launch email sequence for a new product
**Best for:** Email marketers, product marketers, founders

Act as a direct-response copywriter specializing in product launch email sequences. I need a complete email sequence to launch my product. Ask me: what is the product name, what problem does it solve, when is the launch date, what is the price, and what is the launch offer. Then write a 7-email launch sequence following the "story-selling" framework: Email 1 (The Problem): describe the problem in vivid detail — the pain, the frustration, the cost of not solving it. Email 2 (The Discovery): tell the story of how you discovered/found the solution — personal story or customer story. Email 3 (The Solution): introduce your product — what it is, how it works, the core transformation. Email 4 (Social Proof): share testimonials, case studies, early adopter results. Email 5 (The Offer): present the complete package with pricing, bonuses, guarantee. Email 6 (Scarcity/Urgency): remind them the offer is closing, last chance, what they'll miss. Email 7 (Last Call): final reminder with strongest testimonial and most compelling offer summary. For each email provide: subject line (curiosity + benefit), preview text, body copy, CTA, and PS line. Also include the send schedule (which day relative to launch and what time).

**Expected output:** A 7-email launch sequence with subject lines, body copy, CTAs, and scheduling strategy
**Pro tip:** The best launch emails don't feel like launch emails — they tell a story your audience sees themselves in. Spend 3 emails on the problem before you even mention your product

### 1.73 Analytics Dashboard Requirements Document
**Use case:** Define requirements for a marketing analytics dashboard
**Best for:** Marketing analysts, marketing directors, BI teams

Act as a marketing analytics consultant. I need to define the requirements for a marketing analytics dashboard that gives me a single view of my marketing performance. Ask me: what channels am I using, what tools am I currently using for analytics, what are my key business goals, and who will use the dashboard (executives, marketing team, channel managers). Then design a marketing analytics dashboard with these layers: executive view (top-line metrics — revenue, CAC, LTV, ROAS, total leads, conversion rate — with trend lines and month-over-month comparison), channel performance view (per-channel breakdown: spend, impressions, clicks, conversions, CPA, ROAS, with channel comparison charts), campaign view (individual campaign performance with budget tracking, pacing alerts, and creative variation comparison), funnel view (top-of-funnel → middle → bottom — traffic sources, lead conversion, opportunity creation, deal closure with drop-off rates at each stage), cohort analysis (customer retention by acquisition channel and month), and alerts (automated notifications when metrics go outside thresholds — CPA spikes, traffic drops, conversion rate anomalies). For each view specify: chart type, data source, refresh frequency, filters, and the decision each view supports. Also include implementation considerations: data integration requirements, attribution model selection (first-touch, last-touch, multi-touch, data-driven), and rollout approach (MVP first with core metrics, then expand).

**Expected output:** A marketing dashboard requirements document with views, metrics, visualizations, data sources, and implementation roadmap
**Pro tip:** Don't track everything — track the 5 metrics that drive decisions. The best dashboards show less data, not more. Start with: revenue, CAC, ROAS, lead conversion rate, and channel contribution

### 1.74 Community Engagement Playbook
**Use case:** Build and nurture an engaged online community around your brand
**Best for:** Community managers, brand marketers

Act as a community building expert who has grown multiple online communities to 10,000+ active members. I need to create a community engagement playbook for my brand. Ask me: what is my brand, who is my target community, what platform will the community live on (Discord, Slack, Facebook Group, Circle, custom), and what is the primary purpose of the community (support, discussion, networking, co-creation). Then design a community engagement playbook covering: community structure (channels/categories, member roles — admin, moderator, contributor, member, newbie, onboarding flow), engagement mechanics (daily prompts, weekly threads, monthly challenges, AMAs, member spotlights, expert sessions, virtual meetups, collaborative projects), content cadence (daily: discussion prompt or question; weekly: resource share, member spotlight, themed thread; monthly: challenge, expert session, feedback forum; quarterly: virtual event, community survey, roadmap sharing), growth loops (how members invite others — referral incentives, shareable content, guest access, value-driven invitations), moderation guidelines (rules, enforcement levels, escalation path, automod setup), metrics to track (active members, retention rate, posts per member, conversion to customers, NPS), and community lifecycle (tourist → regular → contributor → moderator → ambassador). Also include specific templates for welcome messages, weekly digests, event announcements, and moderation messages.

**Expected output:** A community engagement playbook with structure, cadence, growth loops, moderation, and lifecycle management
**Pro tip:** The #1 community killer is over-moderation. Let conversations breathe, tolerate mild off-topic posts, and only step in when they violate core values. The best communities feel slightly chaotic, not sterile

### 1.75 B2B Account-Based Marketing Strategy
**Use case:** Design an account-based marketing (ABM) strategy for targeting high-value B2B accounts
**Best for:** B2B marketers, sales teams, founders

Act as an ABM strategist who has run successful account-based marketing programs for enterprise B2B companies. I need to design an ABM strategy for targeting high-value accounts. Ask me: what is my product, what is my ideal customer profile (industry, company size, revenue, decision-maker roles), what is my average contract value, and what is my current sales process. Then design a complete ABM program: account selection (firmographic criteria, intent signals, technographic data, existing relationships, scoring model to prioritize top 20-50 accounts), account research template (company overview, org chart with decision-makers, technology stack, recent news/funding, pain points and initiatives, competitive landscape, personalization opportunities), multi-channel engagement plan (direct mail — personalized package; LinkedIn — connection requests, content sharing, InMails; email — personalized sequence; events — exclusive dinner/invite; advertising — account-targeted ads), content personalization per account (custom case studies, ROI calculators, industry-specific landing pages, executive summary with their company name throughout), sales-alignment (weekly ABM meeting structure, lead handoff process, shared account timeline, feedback loop from sales on engagement quality), measurement (pipeline influenced, engagement score per account, account penetration rate, meetings booked, deal velocity, win rate, revenue attribution), and technology stack (CRM, ABM platform, LinkedIn ads, intent data tools, personalization tools). Include the 1:1 (enterprise), 1:Few (mid-market), and 1:Many (SMB) ABM tiers.

**Expected output:** A complete ABM strategy with account selection, research template, multi-channel plan, and measurement framework
**Pro tip:** ABM fails when marketing and sales don't share ownership. Set a shared pipeline target, have a weekly joint review, and make both teams responsible for account penetration, not just leads

### 1.76 Visual Brand Identity Brief
**Use case:** Create a comprehensive creative brief for brand identity design
**Best for:** Marketing managers, founders, brand designers

Act as a brand identity strategist who bridges the gap between business goals and visual design. I need to create a creative brief for designing (or redesigning) my brand's visual identity. Ask me: what is my business, who is my target audience, what are my brand values and personality, who are my main competitors, and where will the brand primarily appear (digital, print, packaging, physical space). Then create a comprehensive brand identity brief: brand essence (the core idea in 2-3 words), brand personality (using the brand archetype framework — Hero, Outlaw, Lover, Jester, Sage, Magician, Caregiver, Creator, Explorer, Innocent, Ruler, Everyman), competitive landscape analysis (3 competitors' visual identities — what works, what doesn't, where the whitespace is), target audience visualization (mood board description — words, images, feelings that resonate with them), design direction (style descriptors — modern vs traditional, minimal vs detailed, bold vs subtle, warm vs cool, playful vs serious), color strategy (primary palette rationale, secondary palette, psychological associations, cultural considerations, accessibility requirements), typography direction (serif vs sans-serif, display vs body, hierarchy suggestions), logo requirements (lockups needed, variations, minimum size, clear space, usage guidelines), application guidelines (how the identity adapts across business cards, website, social media, packaging, signage, presentations), and do's and don'ts (what to embrace and what to avoid visually). Include reference brands that embody the desired direction as inspiration.

**Expected output:** A complete brand identity brief with brand essence, competitive analysis, design direction, color, typography, and application guidelines
**Pro tip:** A great brand identity brief answers "how will this brand make people FEEL" not just "what should it look like." The emotional response is the design North Star

### 1.77 Marketing Automation Workflow Builder
**Use case:** Design automated marketing workflows for lead nurturing, onboarding, and re-engagement
**Best for:** Marketing automation managers, growth marketers

Act as a marketing automation architect. I need to design automated workflows for my marketing stack. Ask me: what marketing automation platform do I use (HubSpot, Marketo, ActiveCampaign, Klaviyo, Mailchimp), what are my main marketing goals for automation (lead nurturing, onboarding, re-engagement, cross-sell, event promotion), and what segments do I currently have. Then design the following automated workflows with full logic: Lead Nurture Flow (trigger: new lead from [source], branches by lead score — high score: send to sales with intro email; medium score: 5-email nurture sequence with content based on their source/interest; low score: monthly newsletter with re-engagement check at 90 days), Customer Onboarding Flow (trigger: first purchase/signup, day 1: welcome + getting started guide, day 3: check-in + tips, day 7: advanced feature showcase, day 14: success story + community invite, day 30: review request + upsell opportunity, day 60: at-risk check if low engagement), Re-engagement Flow (trigger: 90 days inactive, email 1: "we miss you" with what's new, email 2: exclusive offer or content, email 3: final "is this goodbye" with feedback survey, if no response: move to dormant, if response: re-enter active nurturing), Cross-sell/Upsell Flow (trigger: based on purchase/usage behavior, recommend complementary products with social proof from similar customers). For each workflow specify: triggers, conditions/branches, timing, email content type, success metric, and fallback behavior.

**Expected output:** 4 automated marketing workflows with complete logic trees, timing, content requirements, and success metrics
**Pro tip:** The most effective automation workflows are the simplest ones. A 3-email sequence with strong personalization outperforms a 12-email sequence with generic content every time

### 1.78 Product Positioning and Messaging Document
**Use case:** Define how your product is positioned in the market and craft messaging that resonates
**Best for:** Product marketers, founders, product managers

Act as a product positioning strategist. I need to develop a clear positioning and messaging framework for my product. Ask me: what is my product and what does it do, who is it for, what is the primary problem it solves, who are the main competitors, and what makes my product different. Then create a comprehensive positioning document using the following framework: competitive alternatives (what customers would use if my product didn't exist — including "do nothing" and "build in-house"), unique differentiation (the specific, defensible advantage that competitors cannot easily copy), target audience definition (primary and secondary segments with their unique needs), market category (the category we compete in, and whether we're creating a new category or challenging an existing one), positioning statement using the formula: "For [target customer] who [need], [product name] is a [category] that [key benefit]. Unlike [competitor], we [unique differentiator]." Then craft messaging for each audience segment: messaging pillar 1 (the rational benefit — features, specs, ROI), messaging pillar 2 (the emotional benefit — how they feel using it), messaging pillar 3 (the social benefit — how it makes them look to others). For each pillar, write: headline, 3 supporting proof points, and the key differentiator. Include a "say this, not that" guide for sales and marketing teams.

**Expected output:** A product positioning document with competitive analysis, positioning statement, messaging pillars, and sales guide
**Pro tip:** The best positioning makes your competitor a specific alternative, not an abstract concept. "The Salesforce for plumbers" is clearer than "revolutionary field service management"

### 1.79 Viral Content Hook Generator
**Use case:** Generate attention-grabbing hooks for social media content
**Best for:** Social media managers, content creators

Act as a viral content strategist who has studied what makes content spread on every major platform. I need you to help me generate hooks for my content. Tell me: what is my content topic, what platform am I posting on, and who is my target audience. Then use the following hook frameworks to generate 20 hooks: curiosity gap ("I tried [X] for 30 days and here's what happened"), controversy ("Why [common belief] is actually wrong"), social proof ("How [famous person/company] does [activity]"), how-to ("How to [achieve result] without [common mistake]"), number/list ("5 [surprising adjective] ways to [goal]"), pattern interrupt ("Stop doing [common activity]. Do this instead."), identity-based ("If you're a [identity], you need to know this"), prediction ("What nobody is talking about in [industry] in 2026"), challenge ("I did [challenge] for [timeframe] and the results were [surprising]"), story ("The [timeframe] story of how I [achievement]"), fear ("The mistake 90% of [audience] make with [topic]"), insider access ("What [industry] insiders won't tell you about [topic]"). For each hook, provide: the hook itself, why it works (psychological trigger), target platform, and a sample first paragraph that delivers on the hook's promise. Indicate which hooks are best for which platform based on the platform's algorithm and audience behavior.

**Expected output:** 20 content hooks using 12 different frameworks, each with explanation and platform fit
**Pro tip:** The best hook doesn't tell the whole story — it creates a gap between what the reader knows and what they want to know. If they can guess your entire post from the hook, it's not a hook, it's a summary

### 1.80 Channel Attribution Model Builder
**Use case:** Build a marketing attribution model to understand which channels drive conversions
**Best for:** Marketing analysts, marketing directors, founders

Act as a marketing attribution specialist. I need to build a channel attribution model to understand the true impact of each marketing channel. Ask me: what channels do I use, what is my sales cycle length, what tools do I use for tracking (analytics, CRM, ad platforms), and what type of attribution reporting does my organization need now. Then design an attribution model strategy: model comparison (first-touch — credits the first interaction; last-touch — credits the last click before conversion; linear — equal credit to all touchpoints; time-decay — more credit to recent interactions; U-shaped — 40% first touch, 20% middle touches, 40% last touch; W-shaped — 30% first, 30% lead creation, 30% deal close, 10% middle; data-driven — algorithm assigns credit based on incremental impact), recommendation of which model(s) to use based on sales cycle length, number of touchpoints, and organizational maturity, implementation guide (how to set up tracking — UTM parameters, cross-domain tracking, offline conversion import, call tracking, CRM integration), common attribution pitfalls (over-crediting last touch, ignoring offline influence, device switching not tracked, cookie loss, view-through vs click-through), and a staggered approach (start with simple U-shaped or W-shaped model, graduate to data-driven once you have 12+ months of clean data and 500+ conversions per channel). Provide a sample attribution report format showing how to present the data to executives — channel contribution %, ROI, and actionable insights per channel.

**Expected output:** An attribution model strategy with model comparison, implementation guide, common pitfalls, and executive report template
**Pro tip:** Don't chase a perfect attribution model — there is no perfect model. The best model is the one that gives you actionable insights and gets organizational buy-in. U-shaped (40/20/40) is a great starting point for most B2B companies

### 1.81 Seasonal Marketing Campaign Planner
**Use case:** Plan marketing campaigns around seasonal events, holidays, and industry trends
**Best for:** Marketing managers, content marketers

Act as a seasonal marketing strategist. I need to plan marketing campaigns around upcoming seasonal events and holidays relevant to my business. Ask me: what is my business/industry, who is my target audience, what season or holiday am I planning for, and what is my campaign budget. Then create a complete seasonal campaign plan: campaign concept (the big idea connecting the season to my product), timeline backward from the event date (content creation deadline: 4 weeks before; design deadline: 3 weeks before; campaign launch: 2 weeks before; peak push: 1 week before; event day: real-time engagement; post-event: follow-up and recap), channel-specific tactics (email: themed sequence with countdown; social: seasonal content series with themed visuals; paid ads: seasonal keywords and creative; website: seasonal landing page and banners; partnerships: co-marketing with complementary seasonal brands), content requirements (blog posts, social graphics, email templates, ad copy, landing page, video/Reel script, gift guide or seasonal resource), budget allocation (ad spend, content creation, design, partnerships, tools, contingency), success metrics (sales/revenue during campaign period, traffic increase, email engagement rate, social reach, new customers acquired, ROI), and post-campaign analysis (what worked, what didn't, insights for next season, template for repeating). Include specific calendar dates for content milestones.

**Expected output:** A complete seasonal campaign plan with timeline, channel tactics, content requirements, budget, and measurement
**Pro tip:** Start planning seasonal campaigns 8-12 weeks in advance. The brands that win holidays start their campaigns when most people are still thinking about the previous season

### 2.36 SEO Long-Form Article Writer
**Use case:** Write a comprehensive, search-optimized long-form article with proper structure
**Best for:** Content writers, SEO specialists, bloggers

Act as an SEO content strategist and senior writer. I will give you a topic and target keyword, and you will write a complete long-form article outline and introduction. First, ask me these questions: what is the primary keyword, what is the search intent (informational, commercial, transactional, navigational), who is the target reader, and what is my brand's content style (professional, conversational, educational, provocative). Then conduct a quick SERP analysis based on the keyword and identify: what format ranks best (listicle, guide, comparison, review), what angle competitors are missing (content gap), what questions people also ask related to this topic, and what keyword clusters to include. Then produce: a compelling title (with the primary keyword near the beginning, under 60 characters), a meta description (under 160 characters, includes keyword and CTA), a complete H2/H3 outline with 10-15 sections, key points to cover in each section, internal linking opportunities, FAQ schema candidates, and a 300-word introduction that hooks the reader, establishes the problem, previews the solution, and includes the keyword naturally. Each section should flow logically, answer the reader's next question, and include transition sentences.

**Expected output:** Complete article brief with SEO title, meta description, full H2/H3 outline, and a polished introduction
**Pro tip:** The post that ranks #1 on Google is almost never the longest — it's the most comprehensive for the searcher's intent. Match search intent perfectly before adding word count

### 2.37 Viral Twitter/X Thread Creator
**Use case:** Write engaging, high-engagement Twitter/X threads that drive followers and traffic
**Best for:** Twitter/X marketers, content creators, personal brands

Act as a Twitter/X growth strategist who has written threads with millions of impressions. I need you to write a thread about a topic I specify. First, ask me: what is the thread topic, what is my niche/industry, what is the goal (followers, traffic, sales, authority), and what is the main takeaway I want readers to remember. Then write a complete Twitter/X thread following the proven viral thread structure: Hook tweet (first tweet) — creates curiosity gap, makes a bold claim, or asks a provocative question. Must stand alone as an interesting tweet. 2-5 context tweets — establish the problem, share the backstory, or define the framework. 5-10 value tweets — each tweet delivers ONE specific insight, tip, or data point. Each tweet should be understandable on its own. Use storytelling devices: contrast ("most people think X, but actually Y"), specificity ("on day 3, I..." instead of "eventually"), and social proof ("after working with 50+ companies"). 1-2 CTA tweets — summarize the thread and tell them what to do (follow, bookmark, click link, reply). Each tweet should be under 280 characters, use line breaks strategically, and include relevant emojis sparingly. Also recommend the best posting time, hashtag strategy (0-2 max), and engagement follow-up (reply to top comments within 2 hours).

**Expected output:** A complete 10-18 tweet thread with viral hook, value-packed body, and clear CTA
**Pro tip:** The #1 predictor of a viral thread is the hook tweet. Spend 50% of your thread-writing time on the first tweet alone. If it doesn't grab attention in 2 seconds, the rest doesn't matter

### 2.38 Email Newsletter Content Calendar
**Use case:** Plan 3 months of email newsletter content with themes and article ideas
**Best for:** Newsletter writers, content marketers

Act as an email newsletter strategist who has grown newsletters from zero to 50,000+ subscribers. I need to plan 3 months of newsletter content. Ask me: what is my newsletter topic, who is my target reader, what is my current send cadence (weekly, bi-weekly, monthly), and what is the primary goal (traffic, affiliate revenue, authority building, community). Then create a 12-week newsletter content plan with: monthly themes (one theme per month that ties the weeks together), weekly topics that ladder into the monthly theme, format rotation (essay week, roundup week, case study week, interview week, opinion week, tools/resources week), content pillars (60% core educational content, 20% personal stories/opinions, 10% curated resources/tools, 10% promotional/offers), subject line options (3 per issue with different angles — curiosity, benefit, urgency, controversy), call to action for each issue (read article, reply with thoughts, check out product, share with a friend, join community), content repurposing notes (which newsletter pieces can become blog posts, social threads, or podcast topics), and performance benchmarks to track (open rate target, click rate target, reply rate, growth rate, unsubscribe rate). Include a content batching workflow — which pieces to write each week and how to stay ahead of schedule.

**Expected output:** A 12-week newsletter content plan with themes, formats, subject lines, CTAs, and repurposing opportunities
**Pro tip:** The most successful newsletters have a clear "what to expect every time" promise. "Every Tuesday, one framework to grow your business" is better than "random thoughts whenever"

### 2.39 LinkedIn Thought Leadership Post Generator
**Use case:** Write LinkedIn posts that build authority, engagement, and professional credibility
**Best for:** LinkedIn marketers, founders, executives, personal brands

Act as a LinkedIn content strategist who has helped executives build personal brands with 100K+ followers. I need to write a thought leadership post.
