Ask me: what is my expertise area, what is the single insight I want to share, who is my target audience on LinkedIn, and what is the goal (engagement, traffic, leads, speaking inquiries). Then write a complete LinkedIn post following proven formats: choose the best format for my content — "I used to think X, but now I believe Y" (contrast/pivot), "Here's a framework I use for Z" (educational), "I made a mistake with X and here's what I learned" (vulnerability), "3 lessons from [experience]" (list), "Hot take: [controversial opinion]" (debate starter). Write the full post with: hook (first 2-3 lines that stop the scroll — use line breaks for readability), body (5-10 short paragraphs, each with a single idea, use formatting tools — bold for emphasis, line breaks for rhythm, emojis sparingly), call to action (what do you want them to do — comment with their experience, DM for resource, click link, follow), comment strategy (pre-write 3-5 thoughtful comments to reply to anticipated responses). Also write 3-5 optional "alt hooks" (different angles for A/B testing). Include best posting time (Tuesday-Thursday 8-10am or 12-1pm in target timezone), hashtag strategy (3-5 relevant tags, mix of broad and niche), and how to repurpose the post as a carousel, article, or newsletter.

**Expected output:** A complete LinkedIn post with hook, body, CTA, comment strategy, and alt hooks for testing
**Pro tip:** LinkedIn's algorithm favors "dwell time" over likes. Write posts that make people stop and think — questions, frameworks, and counter-intuitive insights keep people reading longer

### 2.40 Video Script for YouTube/TikTok/Reels
**Use case:** Write a high-retention video script optimized for platform-specific algorithms
**Best for:** Video creators, content marketers, founders

Act as a video content strategist who has produced content generating millions of views across YouTube, TikTok, and Instagram Reels. I need a video script for a topic I specify. Ask me: what platform is this for, what is my topic, who is my target audience, and what is the desired outcome (views, followers, engagement, traffic, sales). Then write a complete video script following the platform-optimized structure: For YouTube (8-15 min): hook (first 30 seconds — pattern interrupt, big promise, or shocking statement), setup (minute 1-2 — context and stakes), body (minutes 2-12 — structured value delivery with chapter markers, visual cues, and verbal signposts), CTA (final 30 seconds — like, subscribe, comment, watch next). For TikTok/Reels (15-60 seconds): hook (first 2 seconds — visual hook + verbal hook together, must grab attention instantly), pattern (demonstrate problem → show reaction → reveal solution → result), CTA (last 3 seconds — follow for more, comment your take, check link). For each script provide: visual descriptions (what's on screen), audio notes (voiceover tone, background music, sound effects), pacing markers (fast, slow, dramatic pause), text overlay suggestions (for captions and subtitles), and hook analysis (why this hook works for this platform's algorithm). Include production notes — lighting, framing, background, props needed.

**Expected output:** A complete video script with visual notes, audio cues, timing, text overlays, and hook analysis
**Pro tip:** The first 3 seconds of any video should make the viewer think "I NEED to watch this" — not "this looks interesting." Use specific numbers, surprising visuals, or direct address to the viewer's pain point

### 2.41 Podcast Episode Outline and Script
**Use case:** Structure a podcast episode with engaging narrative, guest questions, and listener takeaways
**Best for:** Podcasters, content creators, interviewers

Act as a podcast producer and scriptwriter. I need to outline and script a podcast episode. Ask me: is this a solo episode or interview, what is the topic or guest name and expertise, who is the target listener, and what is the episode goal (education, inspiration, entertainment, promotion). Then produce a complete episode structure: for solo episodes — cold open (30-second teaser of the most compelling moment), introduction (host intro, episode topic, why it matters now, what listener will learn), body (3-5 segments with clear transitions, each with a key point, example/story, and actionable takeaway), recap (key takeaways summarized), CTA (rate/review, subscribe, join newsletter, check sponsor), outro (what's coming next week). For interviews — cold open (best 30-second clip from the interview), host intro (guest background, why they're qualified, why this topic matters), segment 1 (guest's origin story — how they got into their field, key turning point), segment 2 (core expertise — the main framework/method/insight they're known for), segment 3 (practical application — how the listener can apply this today), segment 4 (contrarian opinions — what the guest disagrees with in their industry), rapid-fire questions (5 quick questions for personality), guest's CTA (where to find them, what they're offering), host outro (key takeaways, sponsor message, next episode preview). For each segment provide: suggested questions (for interviews) or talking points (for solo), estimated timing, and the emotional arc (what the listener should feel). Also include pre-interview research questions for the host.

**Expected output:** A complete podcast episode outline with timing, segments, questions/talking points, and emotional arc
**Pro tip:** The best podcast episodes have a clear "why this, why now" for the listener. If you can't summarize the episode value in one sentence, the episode isn't focused enough

### 2.42 E-book Chapter Outline and Sample
**Use case:** Structure an e-book or book chapter with compelling narrative and clear takeaways
**Best for:** Authors, content marketers, course creators

Act as a book coach and developmental editor who has helped write multiple bestselling business books. I need to outline a chapter for my e-book or book. Ask me: what is the book topic, what is the chapter topic, who is the target reader, and what is the core message of this chapter. Then produce a complete chapter outline: chapter title (intriguing but clear, includes the core benefit), opening hook (a story, statistic, or question that pulls the reader in and establishes why this chapter matters), key concepts (3-5 main ideas the reader needs to understand, each explained with a metaphor or analogy), supporting evidence (data points, research citations, expert quotes, or case studies for each concept), practical application (step-by-step exercises, worksheets, or action items the reader can do after the chapter), transition (bridge sentence to the next chapter that creates curiosity), chapter summary (3-5 bullet-point key takeaways), and reflection questions (3-5 questions for the reader to journal about). Then write the first 1,000 words of the chapter as a sample, demonstrating the tone, pacing, and level of detail. Also provide writing craft notes: pacing advice (when to slow down for emotion, when to speed up for action), voice guidance (first person vs third, formal vs conversational, humor level), and common pitfalls to avoid in this type of chapter.

**Expected output:** A complete chapter outline with hook, concepts, evidence, exercises, and a 1,000-word sample
**Pro tip:** Every chapter should answer one core question and make exactly one point the reader absolutely must remember. If a chapter tries to do two things, split it into two chapters

### 2.43 Case Study Interview Script
**Use case:** Conduct a structured customer interview to gather case study material
**Best for:** Content marketers, case study writers, customer success teams

Act as a case study writer who has produced case studies for companies like Salesforce, HubSpot, and Shopify. I need to interview a customer to write a compelling case study. Provide me with a complete interview script for extracting a customer success story. First, tell me: what is the customer's industry and role, what is my product/service, and what is the desired outcome for the case study (specific metric, transformation story, technical validation). Then write a structured interview script with these sections: warm-up (2-3 rapport-building questions — their role, how long they've used the product, general context), before (the situation before using your product — what was their old process, what were their frustrations, what did they try that didn't work, what was the cost of the old way, what was the emotional state), the decision (what made them choose your solution — what alternatives they considered, what was the deciding factor, any skepticism or concerns they had), implementation (how they got started — was it easy or hard, how long to see results, any challenges during setup), results (the transformation — specific metrics before vs after, time saved, revenue impact, team feedback, unexpected benefits), the future (how their process has changed permanently, would they go back, what's next for them with your product), and recommendation (what would they say to someone considering your product, one piece of advice for a new user). After the script, provide guidance on how to follow up on vague answers, how to dig for specific numbers when the customer gives ranges, and how to identify the "golden quote" moments during the interview.

**Expected output:** A complete case study interview script with question categories, follow-up techniques, and quote-spotting guidance
**Pro tip:** The best case study quotes come from asking about EMOTIONS, not just metrics. "How did that feel when you saw the results?" generates more powerful quotes than "what were the results?"

### 2.44 Landing Page Copy Architect
**Use case:** Write high-converting landing page copy using proven direct-response principles
**Best for:** Copywriters, marketers, founders

Act as a direct-response copywriter specializing in landing page optimization. I need to write a landing page for a specific offer. Ask me: what is the product/service, who is the target customer, what is the primary benefit, and what is the single call to action. Then write complete landing page copy using these sections: Headline (the single most important line — communicates the #1 benefit in under 20 words, uses the "4U" formula: useful, urgent, unique, ultra-specific), Subheadline (expands on the headline, adds a second benefit or audience targeting, under 30 words), Hero section (headline + subheadline + supporting visual description + primary CTA button copy), Pain point section (2-3 paragraphs describing the problem in vivid detail — the reader should feel understood), Solution section (how your product solves the problem — features become benefits using "so that" language), Social proof (testimonials, logos, stats — "join X customers who have achieved Y"), Features/Benefits section (3-5 features with benefit-driven headlines and supporting detail), Objection handlers (1-2 sections that pre-empt common objections — "but what about..." with compelling rebuttals), Risk reversal (guarantee, free trial, money-back promise), CTA section (restated headline-level benefit + CTA button + reminder of what they get), and FAQ (3-5 questions that address remaining objections). For each section explain the psychology behind the copy choice and what emotional trigger it hits.

**Expected output:** Complete landing page copy with 10 sections, each with psychological rationale and CTA optimization
**Pro tip:** Your headline should pass the "chicken test" — if you replaced your product name with "chicken," would the headline still make sense? If yes, it's too generic. Make your headline specific to your unique value

### 2.45 Press Release and Media Pitch
**Use case:** Write a newsworthy press release and pitch it to journalists and publications
**Best for:** PR managers, founders, marketing teams

Act as a PR specialist and former journalist who knows exactly what reporters look for in a story. I need to announce something newsworthy about my company. Ask me: what is the announcement (product launch, funding, partnership, milestone, award), what is my company and what do we do, who is the target audience for this announcement, and what is the news hook (why does this matter NOW). Then write two pieces: a press release and a pitch email. The press release should follow AP style with: embargo/dateline, headline (under 90 characters, includes strong action verb and key benefit), subheadline (expands on headline with key detail, under 150 characters), dateline (city, state, date), lead paragraph (inverted pyramid — who, what, when, where, why, how in the first paragraph, under 35 words), body paragraphs (2-3 quotes — CEO quote about vision, customer quote about impact, partner/investor quote about credibility, supporting details and context), boilerplate ("About Company" paragraph — standard company description), media contact (name, email, phone, website), and availability (interview, demo, images, spokesperson). The pitch email should be personalized to a specific journalist/publication with: subject line (under 10 words, avoids "press release" or "announcement", teases the story), greeting (personalized to their beat and past coverage), hook (why this matters to their readers now), story (2-3 sentences with the key angle), availability (what you can provide — interview, exclusive data, demo), and PS (a specific detail that shows you read their recent work).

**Expected output:** An AP-style press release and a personalized media pitch email for a specific journalist
**Pro tip:** Don't send the press release as an attachment. Paste the full text in the email body. Journalists are 3x more likely to read it if they don't have to download a file. Also, CCs = instant delete — pitch one journalist at a time

### 2.46 Explainer Video Script (60 seconds)
**Use case:** Write a concise, compelling explainer video script that clarifies a complex product
**Best for:** SaaS companies, product marketers, founders

Act as an explainer video scriptwriter with experience producing videos for tech startups. I need a 60-second explainer video script for my product. Ask me: what is my product and what does it do in one sentence, what problem does it solve, who is the target customer, and what is the key differentiator. Then write a complete 60-second script structured as: 0-10 seconds (The Problem Hook): visually show the painful situation your customer experiences daily — make it relatable and slightly exaggerated. 10-25 seconds (The Solution Reveal): introduce your product as the answer — "what if there was a way to..." 25-40 seconds (How It Works): show 3 simple steps — keep it visual, minimal text, show the product in action. 40-50 seconds (The Transformation): show the "after" state — the relief, the time saved, the results achieved. 50-60 seconds (The CTA): clear, single action — sign up, start free trial, get early access. For each 5-second segment provide: visual description (what's on screen), voiceover script (exact words), on-screen text (any text overlays), sound/music direction (tone — playful, professional, dramatic), and transition notes (how each segment flows to the next). Also provide 3 versions of the script for different tones: professional/corporate, friendly/approachable, and humorous/memorable.

**Expected output:** A 60-second explainer video script with 5-second segments, visual/audio notes, and 3 tone variations
**Pro tip:** In a 60-second explainer, every word costs about 2-3 seconds of screen time. Read your script aloud and time it — if it's over 65 seconds, cut ruthlessly. The first 10 seconds are the most expensive real estate

### 2.47 White Paper Executive Summary
**Use case:** Write a compelling executive summary for a white paper or research report
**Best for:** Content marketers, B2B marketers, researchers

Act as a white paper writer specializing in B2B thought leadership. I need to write an executive summary for my white paper. Ask me: what is the white paper topic, what is the core argument or thesis, who is the target reader (C-suite, manager, technical), and what data or research supports the thesis. Then write a comprehensive executive summary that follows this structure: Opening hook (1-2 sentences that state the urgent problem or opportunity — use a compelling statistic or provocative question), Context (3-4 sentences establishing why this topic matters now — market trends, industry shifts, regulatory changes), Thesis statement (1 sentence that clearly states the paper's central argument), Key findings (3-5 bullet points or short paragraphs, each summarizing a major finding with supporting data), Implications (2-3 sentences on what these findings mean for the reader's business or industry), Recommendations (2-3 actionable recommendations based on the findings), Call to action (what the reader should do next — read the full paper, schedule a consultation, attend a webinar). Additionally provide: an alternative 50-word version (for social media promotion), an alternative 100-word version (for email promotion), and a list of key statistics (5-7 data points from the paper that journalists would quote). The language should be authoritative but accessible — C-suite ready. Avoid jargon, but demonstrate deep subject matter expertise.

**Expected output:** A full executive summary with short/long versions, key statistics for PR, and recommendation summaries
**Pro tip:** Most executive summaries are too long. If a busy CEO can't understand your entire argument in 60 seconds, your executive summary needs editing, not your white paper. Lead with the conclusion, not the methodology

### 2.48 Product Launch Social Media Blitz
**Use case:** Create a coordinated social media campaign for a product launch across platforms
**Best for:** Social media managers, product marketers

Act as a social media campaign strategist. I need to create a product launch social media blitz. Ask me: what is the product, what is the launch date, what platforms are we using, and what is the launch offer. Then create a complete social media launch campaign with: Teaser Phase (7-14 days before launch — 3-5 posts per platform building curiosity without revealing the product — hint at problem, tease the solution, countdown, "something big is coming"), Reveal Phase (launch day — coordinated posts across all platforms announcing the product, each platform gets platform-native content not just cross-posting), Social Proof Phase (days 1-3 after launch — customer testimonials, early adopter screenshots, media mentions, user-generated content), Urgency Phase (days 4-7 — limited-time offer, "X customers joined in first 24 hours," scarcity, bonus ending soon), Content Mix (for each phase and platform specify: format — image, video, carousel, text, story/reel; copy angle; visual direction; posting time; engagement strategy — how to respond to comments, DMs, shares), Paid amplification (recommended budget allocation per platform per phase, targeting parameters, creative variations), and Measurement (key metrics per phase — reach, engagement, click-through, conversion, hashtag performance, sentiment). Include a content matrix showing which content goes where on which day.

**Expected output:** A complete social media launch campaign with phased content plan, platform-specific posts, paid strategy, and measurement
**Pro tip:** The biggest launch mistake is going silent after launch day. The second day after launch is when most people actually see your content — have 5-7 pieces of post-launch content queued up. The launch is a week-long event, not a single day

### 2.49 Infographic Content and Structure Brief
**Use case:** Plan the content, structure, and data visualization for an infographic
**Best for:** Content marketers, graphic designers, data storytellers

Act as a data visualization and infographic designer. I need to create a brief for designing an infographic. Ask me: what is the topic, what is the key data or information to visualize, who is the target audience, and where will the infographic be published (blog, social, print, presentation). Then create a complete infographic brief: Core message (the single takeaway someone should get after looking at the infographic for 5 seconds), Target format (vertical vs horizontal, ideal dimensions, file size limit), Color palette recommendation (2-3 primary colors that match the brand, 1-2 accent colors for data highlights, accessibility considerations for colorblind viewers), Typography (heading font, body font, data font — with rationale), Visual hierarchy (what catches the eye first, second, third — use the "F-pattern" or "Z-pattern" for information flow), Section breakdown (3-5 sections, each with: headline, supporting fact, visual format recommendation — bar chart, pie chart, icon array, timeline, flowchart, map, illustration), Data points to include (key statistics, percentages, comparisons, rankings, dates, with source citations for each), Icon/illustration needs (what visual metaphors to use for each section, style guide for consistency), Text length limits (maximum words per section — infographics should be skimmable), Brand elements (logo placement, website URL, social handles, brand marks), and Call to action (what to include at the bottom — embed code, share button, link to full report). Also include 3 reference infographics that match the desired style for the designer to study.

**Expected output:** A complete infographic brief with core message, visual hierarchy, section structure, data points, and design specifications
**Pro tip:** The best infographics communicate ONE idea per section and use visuals to replace text wherever possible. If you need more than 50 words in a section, the visual isn't doing its job

### 2.50 Email Cold Outreach Sequence
**Use case:** Write a multi-touch cold email sequence for B2B prospecting
**Best for:** Sales teams, business development, founders

Act as a cold email copywriter who has generated millions in pipeline through cold outreach. I need to write a cold email sequence to prospect for my business. Ask me: what is my product/service, who is my ideal prospect (title, industry, company size), what is the main value proposition, and what is the desired next step (call, demo, trial, reply). Then write a 5-email sequence: Email 1 (Value First): personalized opening referencing something specific about their company/role, value proposition focused on a specific outcome, low-friction CTA (reply with "interested" or click a case study). Email 2 (Follow-up + Social Proof): reference to email 1 ("might have missed my last email"), social proof example (customer result), alternative CTA (less commitment — "worth a 5-minute call?"). Email 3 (Value Add): no ask — share a relevant resource (article, tool, template, insight) that genuinely helps them regardless of whether they buy. Email 4 (Objection Handler): address a common objection directly ("you might be thinking..."), provide counter-evidence (case study, data point), direct CTA. Email 5 (Breakup): final email — respect their decision, leave the door open, sometimes a "breakup email" gets responses from people who were interested but procrastinating. Each email should: be under 150 words, use plain text format (no HTML templates), include personalization beyond first name (company initiative, recent news, mutual connection), and have a single CTA. Include ideal send timing (day of week, time of day, gap between emails), subject line options (3 per email), and follow-up rules (when to move to next email, when to remove from sequence).

**Expected output:** A 5-email cold outreach sequence with personalization strategy, timing, and subject line variations
**Pro tip:** Email 3 (the pure value add with no ask) is the highest-converting email in most sequences. It builds trust and makes your follow-ups feel helpful, not pushy. Never skip it

### 2.51 Brand Voice and Tone Adaptation
**Use case:** Adapt a piece of copy to match different brand voices and tones
**Best for:** Copywriters, content teams, agencies

Act as a brand voice specialist. I will provide you with a piece of source content and a target brand voice description. You will rewrite the content in the target voice and explain the changes made. Provide me: the original content (paste below), and the target brand voice description (e.g., "casual and witty like Mailchimp," "authoritative and data-driven like Harvard Business Review," "empathetic and supportive like Headspace," "bold and irreverent like Oatly," "minimalist and confident like Apple"). Then rewrite the content in the target voice, documenting: voice characteristics (tone — formal vs casual, sentence structure — simple vs complex, vocabulary — industry jargon vs plain language, rhythm — short punches vs flowing prose, personality markers — humor, empathy, authority, irreverence), specific changes made (word choice substitutions, sentence restructuring, added/removed elements, punctuation and formatting changes), before/after comparison table showing key phrases transformed from original to new voice, and a 5-point brand voice guide based on the adaptation (rules for staying in this voice across future content). Also identify which elements of the original content shouldn't change — facts, core message, key benefits, calls to action — to ensure the rewrite maintains effectiveness while changing tone.

**Expected output:** A brand voice adaptation of the source content with documented changes, comparison table, and a mini brand voice guide
**Pro tip:** Brand voice is about CONSISTENCY, not creativity. The goal isn't to make every piece of content brilliant — it's to make every piece sound like it came from the same brand. Constraints create coherence

### 2.52 About Us Page Storyteller
**Use case:** Write a compelling About Us page that builds trust and emotional connection
**Best for:** Founders, marketers, brand storytellers

Act as a brand storyteller who specializes in writing About Us pages. I need to write or rewrite an About Us page for my business. Ask me: what is my company's origin story, what problem were you founded to solve, what are the core values, what is the company mission, and who are the key team members. Then write a complete About Us page that follows this narrative arc: The Origin (the moment of insight or frustration that led to founding — make it personal, specific, and emotionally resonant), The Mission (what the company exists to do — stated in one powerful sentence that balances ambition with specificity, includes the customer transformation), The Values (3-5 core values, each with a short story or example that shows what the value looks like in practice — avoid generic "integrity" and "innovation" without concrete examples), The Team (brief introduction of 3-5 key team members — not just titles and backgrounds, but what they uniquely bring to the mission), The Customer (show that you understand and care about customers — include customer photos, quotes, or a customer story), The Future (where the company is heading — a compelling vision that invites the reader to be part of the journey), Call to action (what should someone do after reading — try the product, join the team, follow the journey). For each section provide the copy itself plus a "why this works" note explaining the psychological principle behind the section. Also include an alternative "minimalist" version (for social media or footer) and an alternative "detailed" version (for investors or partners).

**Expected output:** A complete About Us page with 7 sections, psychological rationale, and alternative length versions
**Pro tip:** The best About Us pages aren't about the company — they're about the customer's transformation and the founder's vulnerability. Share the struggle, not just the success. Imperfection builds trust

### 2.53 Content Gap Analysis Report
**Use case:** Identify content opportunities by analyzing competitors' content and search intent gaps
**Best for:** SEO strategists, content marketers

Act as an SEO content strategist specializing in content gap analysis. I need to identify content opportunities that my competitors are missing. Ask me: what is my website/domain, who are my top 3-5 content competitors, what is my primary topic cluster, and what format performs best in my industry. Then conduct a structured content gap analysis: Topic overlap matrix (mapping which topics my competitors cover vs what I cover — identify topics all competitors cover (must-haves), topics only some cover (opportunities), and topics no one covers (blue ocean)), Keyword gap analysis (keywords competitors rank for that I don't — categorize by search volume, difficulty, and relevance to my business), Content format gaps (formats competitors use that I don't — video, infographics, interactive tools, podcasts, original research, templates, calculators), Funnel gaps (content stages competitors cover that I miss — top-of-funnel awareness content, middle-of-funnel comparison content, bottom-of-funnel decision content), Quality benchmarking (compare my content depth vs competitors — word count, readability, visual quality, data citations, update frequency, author expertise). For each gap identified, provide: the specific opportunity (topic + format recommendation), the search volume estimate, the difficulty level (how hard to rank), and a content brief (recommended word count, key points to cover, internal linking strategy, citation sources needed). Prioritize gaps by expected ROI — quick wins (high search volume, low competition, easy to produce) vs strategic plays (high value, higher effort).

**Expected output:** A content gap analysis with topic, keyword, format, and funnel gaps, prioritized by expected ROI
**Pro tip:** Don't compete on keywords your competitors are dominating — find the keywords they're NOT using but should be. The best content gap is the one your competitors overlooked because they were all chasing the same high-volume terms

### 2.54 Client Proposal Writing Template
**Use case:** Write a persuasive client proposal that wins projects and sets clear expectations
**Best for:** Freelancers, agencies, consultants

Act as a proposal writing expert who has won millions in client work through compelling proposals. I need to write a proposal for a potential client. Ask me: what is the client's name/company, what is the project scope, what is my proposed solution, what is the timeline and pricing, and what makes my approach unique. Then write a complete proposal following this structure: Executive Summary (3-4 sentences summarizing the client's challenge, your solution, and the expected outcome — this is the most-read section, make it powerful), Understanding of the Problem (show you deeply understand their situation — paraphrase their needs back to them, demonstrate you've listened, identify the root cause not just symptoms), Proposed Solution (detailed description of what you'll do, broken into phases or workstreams with deliverables and timelines for each — use specific, measurable language), Methodology (how you work — your process, tools, frameworks, communication cadence, reporting), Timeline (visual or detailed timeline showing start date, milestones, delivery dates, review points), Investment (pricing broken down by phase or deliverable, payment terms, what's included and excluded), ROI / Expected Outcomes (what results the client should expect — specific metrics where possible, case studies of similar work), Why Us (team qualifications, relevant experience, client testimonials, industry recognition), Next Steps (how to accept — e-signature, deposit, kickoff call scheduling, what you need from them to start). Also provide: a "quick read" version (1-page summary for busy stakeholders), a comparison table showing your proposal vs alternative approaches (in-house, other vendors, do nothing), and objection-handling footnotes (addressing common concerns like timeline, budget, or scope).

**Expected output:** A complete client proposal with detailed sections, quick-read version, and comparison table
**Pro tip:** The most powerful section of any proposal is the "Understanding of the Problem" section. If the client reads that and thinks "they get me," you've won the work. Spend 30% of your proposal writing time on this section alone

### 2.55 Grant Application Narrative
**Use case:** Write a compelling grant application narrative that secures funding
**Best for:** Non-profits, startups, researchers

Act as a grant writing specialist with experience securing funding from government agencies, foundations, and corporate grant programs. I need to write a grant application for a project or program. Ask me: what is the project/program name, what organization is applying, what is the funding amount requested, what type of grant is this (government, foundation, corporate), and who is the target funder. Then write a grant narrative following the standard structure: Executive Summary (1 paragraph — project name, applicant, problem, solution, amount requested, expected impact), Statement of Need (the problem you're solving — supported by data, statistics, research citations, and beneficiary testimonials — make the case urgent and compelling), Project Description (what you'll do — activities, methodology, timeline, milestones, deliverables, who will benefit and how), Goals and Objectives (SMART goals — specific, measurable, achievable, relevant, time-bound), Evaluation Plan (how you'll measure success — metrics, data collection methods, reporting cadence), Organizational Capacity (why your organization is qualified — mission alignment, team expertise, past success, partnerships, infrastructure), Budget Narrative (how the funds will be used — line items explained, cost justification, matching funds or in-kind contributions), Sustainability (how the project will continue after grant funding ends — other funding sources, revenue model, community support). For each section provide: the actual narrative text, word count suggestions (most grants have strict limits), and tips for aligning with the funder's priorities. Include a checklist of required attachments and submission requirements.

**Expected output:** A complete grant narrative with all required sections, word counts, and funder alignment tips
**Pro tip:** Funders read hundreds of applications. Your executive summary is the only section they're guaranteed to read — spend the most time on it. Lead with IMPACT, not process. "We will train 500 teachers" is better than "we will conduct 10 training sessions"

### 2.56 Social Media Caption Variation Generator
**Use case:** Generate multiple caption variations for the same visual content, optimized for different platforms and tones
**Best for:** Social media managers, content creators

Act as a social media copywriter who can write in multiple brand voices. I will give you a description of a visual asset (photo, video, graphic) and your target audience. Write 10 different caption variations for the same visual, each optimized for a different purpose or platform: Short & Punchy (20-40 characters for Instagram grid or TikTok — focuses on the visual, minimal text, emoji-friendly), Storytelling (100-200 words for LinkedIn or Facebook — narrative arc, personal insight, lesson learned, 3-paragraph structure), Educational (80-150 words — tip, how-to, or insight with clear takeaway, bullet points or numbered steps), Conversational (50-100 words for Twitter/X — casual, opinionated, invites replies, uses colloquial language), Inspirational (60-120 words — motivational tone, aspirational, uses quotes or affirmations, emotional appeal), Question-Based (30-60 words — starts with a question to drive comments, creates curiosity gap, engagement bait that adds value), Controversial/Hot Take (40-80 words — provocative opinion that challenges conventional wisdom, backed by your unique perspective), Promotional (50-80 words — clear CTA, urgency or scarcity element, benefit-focused, "limited time" or "exclusive"), Humorous (30-60 words — witty, playful, unexpected, uses wordplay or irony, self-deprecating where appropriate), Community-Focused (60-100 words — invitation to share, user-generated content prompt, tag-a-friend, celebrate the community). For each variation specify: best platform fit, character count, tone, and the psychological trigger it uses.

**Expected output:** 10 caption variations for the same visual, each with platform fit, tone, and psychological trigger
**Pro tip:** The same visual can perform completely differently depending on the caption. For high-engagement content, lead with a question or controversial opinion. For click-through, lead with curiosity. Never use the same caption across platforms — adapt the angle to each platform's culture

### 2.57 Webinar Follow-Up Sequence
**Use case:** Write a multi-touch follow-up sequence for webinar attendees and no-shows
**Best for:** Marketing teams, demand generation

Act as a webinar follow-up strategist. I need to create a follow-up sequence for my upcoming webinar. Ask me: what is the webinar topic, what is the offer at the end, what date was the webinar, and how many people registered vs attended. Then write a complete follow-up sequence with separate tracks for attendees and no-shows:

ATTENDEE TRACK: Thank You + Replay (sent within 1 hour of webinar ending — thank you, link to replay, key takeaway summary, CTA for offer with bonus expiration), Day 1 Follow-up (top question from the webinar answered in depth, testimonial from a satisfied customer related to the topic), Day 3 Follow-up (case study or success story that reinforces the webinar's main point, reminder that the offer expires), Day 7 Follow-up (FAQ document addressing questions asked during the webinar, last chance for the offer), Day 14 Follow-up (survey — what did you think, what topics do you want next, what did you implement from the webinar).

NO-SHOW TRACK: Missed You + Replay (sent within 2 hours of webinar ending — "we missed you" tone, link to full replay, key highlight to entice watching, CTA for offer with shorter expiration), Day 2 Follow-up (curated highlight reel — top 3 moments from the webinar in bullet points with timestamps, reminder that content is only available for X more days), Day 5 Follow-up (most impactful slide from the webinar with brief explanation, offer reminder), Day 10 Follow-up (the single best insight from the webinar — make it valuable enough that they regret missing it, final offer extension).

For each email provide: subject line, preheader text, body copy, CTA, and timing rationale. Also provide SMS versions (160 characters max) for each email to use as text message reminders.

**Expected output:** A two-track webinar follow-up sequence (attendee + no-show) with emails, SMS versions, and timing
**Pro tip:** The no-show track is often more valuable than the attendee track because no-shows are people who were interested enough to register but not motivated enough to attend. A well-crafted follow-up can convert them at the same rate as attendees

### 2.58 Ad Copy A/B Testing Hypothesis Generator
**Use case:** Generate structured A/B testing hypotheses for paid social and search ads
**Best for:** Paid media buyers, growth marketers

Act as a paid advertising strategist specializing in ad creative testing. I need to generate A/B testing hypotheses for my ad campaigns. Ask me: what platform (Facebook, Google, LinkedIn, TikTok), what is the product/service, what is the target audience, what ad format (single image, carousel, video, text), and what is the current ad creative approach. Then generate 10 structured A/B testing hypotheses, each with: Variable to test (the specific element you'll change — headline, CTA, image, body copy, offer, social proof type, format, angle, audience, landing page), Hypothesis (specific prediction — "changing the headline from [current] to [new] will increase CTR by X% because [reason]"), Control description (what the current version is), Variation description (exactly what the new version will be), Success metric (primary metric to measure — CTR, CVR, CPA, ROAS), Sample size needed (estimated conversions needed for statistical significance at 95% confidence), Test duration (how many days to run based on daily budget and estimated conversion rate), Risk level (low — safe change that provides learning, medium — moderate change with potential upside, high — completely different approach). Prioritize the hypotheses by expected impact and ease of implementation. Also include a testing roadmap — which tests to run first, second, third based on dependencies and parallel testing opportunities (avoid testing two variables that interact with each other simultaneously).

**Expected output:** 10 structured A/B testing hypotheses with variable, prediction, control/variation, success metric, and testing roadmap
**Pro tip:** Test ONE variable at a time. If you test headline AND image AND CTA simultaneously, you'll never know which change caused the result. The fastest path to a winner is slow, disciplined testing

### 2.59 Personal Brand Bio Generator
**Use case:** Write a compelling personal brand bio for different platforms and contexts
**Best for:** Founders, executives, freelancers, personal brands

Act as a personal branding consultant. I need to write or rewrite my personal bio for multiple platforms. Ask me: what is my name and title, what do I do (expertise), what is my unique perspective or methodology, who do I help, what are my key accomplishments, and what is my personality style (professional, casual, witty, inspirational). Then write 6 versions of my bio optimized for different platforms: LinkedIn About Section (2,000+ characters — narrative format with origin story, expertise journey, value proposition, social proof, CTA), LinkedIn Headline (220 characters max — includes keyword-rich title + value prop + social proof element), Twitter/X Bio (160 characters max — punchy, personality-driven, includes what you tweet about and why follow), Instagram Bio (150 characters max — visual-focused, emoji-friendly, includes category and CTA link), Personal Website Hero (100-200 characters — headline + subheadline that communicates your value in 5 seconds), and Email Signature (50-80 characters — title + company + what you do + phone/link). For each version provide: the bio text itself, character count, key elements included (keywords, social proof, personality marker, CTA), and the psychological principle behind the structure (authority, likeability, social proof, specificity, curiosity). Also include a "bio building blocks" section — reusable phrases and power words they can mix and match for future bio needs.

**Expected output:** 6 platform-optimized bio versions with character counts, element breakdown, and psychological rationale
**Pro tip:** Your LinkedIn bio should tell a story, but your Twitter bio should make a promise. Different platforms have different visitor mindsets — LinkedIn visitors want to understand you, Twitter visitors want to know what value you'll give them

### 2.60 Monthly Content Performance Review
**Use case:** Analyze past month's content performance and create an optimization plan
**Best for:** Content strategists, editors, content teams

Act as a content performance analyst. I need to review my content performance from the past month and create a plan for improvement. Ask me: what content did I publish last month (blog posts, videos, social posts, emails), what platform metrics do I have (traffic, engagement, conversions), and what were my content goals for the month. Then produce a content performance review with: Top Performers (top 3-5 pieces of content ranked by goal achievement — for each: title, metric performance, analysis of why it worked), Bottom Performers (bottom 3-5 pieces — for each: title, metric performance, diagnosis of what went wrong), Channel Analysis (per-platform performance — which channels drove the most valuable traffic, which channels need attention, engagement trends), Content-Type Analysis (which formats performed best — long-form vs short-form, video vs text, listicle vs guide, case study vs opinion), Topic Analysis (which topics resonated most, which fell flat, content coverage gaps), Distribution Analysis (which promotion channels drove the most engagement per piece, which didn't pull weight), Competitive Benchmarking (how your content compares to competitors on key metrics — frequency, engagement, topics, formats), and Action Plan (3-5 specific, prioritized recommendations for next month with expected impact level and implementation effort). Include a template for next month's content brief that incorporates the lessons learned.

**Expected output:** A content performance review with top/bottom performers, channel/format/topic analysis, and action plan
**Pro tip:** Don't optimize for vanity metrics. A blog post with 100 highly-targeted readers that converts to 5 customers is worth more than a viral post with 10,000 views and 0 conversions. Measure content against business goals, not platform metrics

### 3.21 Business Model Canvas Builder
**Use case:** Complete a full Business Model Canvas for a new or existing business
**Best for:** Founders, strategists, entrepreneurs

Act as a business model innovation consultant. I need to complete a Business Model Canvas for my business idea or existing company. Ask me these nine questions one at a time: what customer segments do you serve, what value proposition do you offer each segment, through what channels do you reach customers, what type of customer relationships do you establish, what revenue streams do you have, what key resources do you need, what key activities does your value proposition require, what key partnerships do you rely on, and what is your cost structure. After I answer all nine, synthesize everything into a complete Business Model Canvas with each block filled in specifically and actionably. For each block, identify the top risk or assumption that needs validation and suggest the cheapest way to test it. Then analyze how the nine blocks interconnect — which changes in one block affect others, and what the most critical dependency is. Finally, suggest three modifications to the model that could improve it: one to increase revenue, one to reduce costs, and one to differentiate from competitors.

**Expected output:** A complete Business Model Canvas with risk analysis, dependency mapping, and three optimization suggestions
**Pro tip:** The cost structure and revenue streams blocks should be filled last — they're outputs of the other seven blocks, not inputs. Start with customer segments and value proposition

### 3.22 Process Optimization Workflow
**Use case:** Analyze and optimize a business process to reduce waste and increase efficiency
**Best for:** Operations managers, business owners, process improvement leads

Act as a Lean Six Sigma process improvement specialist. I need to analyze and optimize a business process. Describe the process to me step by step: what is the process name, what triggers it to start, what are the sequential steps, who is responsible for each step, how long does each step take, what is the output or success condition, and what are the common problems or bottlenecks. Then I will analyze the process using Lean methodology: value stream mapping (identify value-added vs non-value-added steps), waste identification (apply the 8 wastes framework — defects, overproduction, waiting, non-utilized talent, transportation, inventory, motion, extra-processing), bottleneck analysis (which step constrains the overall throughput — calculate cycle time and identify the slowest step), root cause analysis (for each problem, use the 5 Whys technique to find the true root cause), and improvement recommendations (specific, actionable changes for each issue found — eliminate, simplify, combine, or automate each step). Present the analysis as a before/after comparison: current state process map vs optimized future state process map, with estimated improvement in cycle time, cost reduction, and quality improvement.

**Expected output:** A complete process optimization analysis with current/future state maps, waste identification, and quantified improvement estimates
**Pro tip:** The biggest process improvement usually comes from eliminating steps entirely, not speeding them up. Ask "why do we do this at all?" before asking "how can we do this faster?"

### 3.23 KPI Tree and OKR Alignment
**Use case:** Define and align Key Performance Indicators (KPIs) with Objectives and Key Results (OKRs)
**Best for:** CEOs, department heads, operations managers

Act as a performance measurement consultant. I need to define KPIs and align them with my company's OKRs. Ask me: what is my company's mission and vision, what are our top 3-5 objectives for this quarter/year, what departments exist, and what is the current measurement system. Then create a complete KPI tree: Top-Level KPIs (3-5 company-wide metrics that directly measure strategic success — revenue growth, customer satisfaction, market share, employee retention, operational efficiency), Department-Level KPIs (3-5 per department that ladder up to company KPIs — marketing: CAC, MQL conversion, channel ROI; sales: win rate, deal velocity, quota attainment; product: NPS, activation rate, churn; operations: cycle time, cost per unit, quality rate), Leading vs Lagging indicators (lagging: measure results — revenue, churn, profit. leading: predict future results — pipeline generated, product engagement, support tickets), KPI definition sheet for each metric (name, formula, data source, frequency, target, threshold, owner, and review cadence), OKR-KPI alignment map showing which key results are measured by which KPIs, and a dashboard layout recommendation (top KPIs on one page, drill-down to department level). Include a quarterly review process — how to review KPI performance, when to adjust targets, and how to cascade changes through the organization.

**Expected output:** A complete KPI framework with company/department-level metrics, leading/lagging indicators, OKR alignment, and review process
**Pro tip:** Don't track more than 5 KPIs per person. If everything is a KPI, nothing is a priority. The best KPIs are the ones that, if they move in the right direction, automatically pull other metrics with them

### 3.24 Meeting Effectiveness Audit
**Use case:** Audit and optimize meeting culture to reduce wasted time and increase productivity
**Best for:** Team leads, operations managers, executives

Act as a organizational productivity consultant specializing in meeting optimization. I need to audit and improve my organization's meeting culture. Answer these questions: how many meetings does your team have per week on average, how long are they, what is the average attendance, what types of meetings do you have (standup, weekly review, 1:1, all-hands, brainstorming, status update), and what are the common complaints about meetings. Then conduct a meeting audit with: Meeting Waste Calculator (calculate the cost of meetings — multiply average hourly rate × number of attendees × meeting duration × number of meetings per week — show the annual cost). Meeting Type Analysis (categorize all recurring meetings by purpose — decision-making, information-sharing, brainstorming, relationship-building, status-updating — recommend which types to keep, reduce, or eliminate), RACI Assessment (for each meeting, identify who should be Required, who should be Accountable, who should be Consulted, who should be Informed — reduce attendance to only Required and Accountable), Structural Recommendations (for each kept meeting — ideal duration, agenda requirement, pre-read policy, output expectation, decision documentation), Async Alternatives (for each meeting type, suggest an async alternative — Loom video, Slack thread, shared doc, project management update), and a Meeting Charter Template (a one-page document every recurring meeting must complete: name, purpose, frequency, required attendees, agenda lead, expected output, max duration). Provide a 30-day implementation plan with week-by-week changes.

**Expected output:** A complete meeting audit with waste calculation, type analysis, async alternatives, and 30-day implementation plan
**Pro tip:** The most impactful meeting change is instituting a "no meeting Wednesday" or "maker mornings" policy — 4-hour blocks with no internal meetings. This single change typically recovers 15-20 hours of productive time per person per month

### 3.25 Vendor Selection and Evaluation Framework
**Use case:** Evaluate and select third-party vendors, software, or service providers
**Best for:** Operations managers, procurement, founders

Act as a procurement and vendor management consultant. I need to evaluate and select a vendor for my business. Answer these questions: what service or product do you need, what is your budget range, what is the implementation timeline, who are the stakeholders involved in the decision, and what are your top 3-5 requirements (must-haves vs nice-to-haves). Then create a vendor evaluation framework: Requirements Definition Document (functional requirements — what the vendor must do; technical requirements — integration, security, scalability; commercial requirements — pricing model, contract terms, support SLAs), RFP/RFI Template (standardized questions to send to all vendors covering: company background, product/service capabilities, pricing structure, implementation process, support model, security/compliance, references), Scoring Matrix with weighted criteria (30% functionality fit, 20% pricing, 15% implementation ease, 15% support quality, 10% company stability, 10% references — with a 1-5 scoring scale and rationale for each score), Due Diligence Checklist (security audit questions, data privacy compliance, financial health review, customer reference questions, trial/pilot evaluation plan), Decision Framework (how to weigh tradeoffs — vendor A has better features but higher price vs vendor B has adequate features but better support), and Contract Negotiation Points (key terms to negotiate — pricing discounts for annual commitment, data export guarantee, SLA penalties, termination notice period, price lock-in duration). Include a decision timeline from initial research through contract signing.

**Expected output:** A complete vendor evaluation framework with requirements, RFP template, scoring matrix, due diligence checklist, and negotiation points
**Pro tip:** The cheapest vendor is almost never the cheapest in the long run. Calculate total cost of ownership (TCO) including implementation, training, migration, and ongoing support — not just subscription price

### 3.26 Risk Management Register
**Use case:** Identify, assess, and mitigate business risks across all departments
**Best for:** Operations managers, founders, risk officers

Act as an enterprise risk management consultant. I need to build a risk register for my business. Answer these questions: what industry are you in, what is your company size, what are your key business processes, and have you identified any risks already. Then create a comprehensive risk register using this framework: Risk Categories (strategic — competition, market shifts, regulation; operational — supply chain, systems, people, process failures; financial — cash flow, credit, currency, fraud; compliance — legal, regulatory, data privacy; reputational — brand damage, customer dissatisfaction, PR crisis), Risk Assessment Matrix (for each risk: name, description, category, likelihood on 1-5 scale, impact on 1-5 scale, risk score = likelihood × impact, risk rating — low/medium/high/critical), Mitigation Strategies for each risk (avoid — eliminate the activity causing the risk; reduce — implement controls to lower likelihood or impact; transfer — insurance, outsourcing, contracts; accept — acknowledge and budget for the risk), Action Plan for high and critical risks (specific owner, mitigation actions, timeline, monitoring frequency, contingency plan), Key Risk Indicators (KRIs) — early warning metrics that signal a risk is materializing (e.g., customer support ticket volume as KRI for product quality risk), Review Cadence (how often to review each risk level — critical: monthly, high: quarterly, medium: biannually, low: annually), and Escalation Path (who to notify based on risk level and when). Present as a structured risk register table with all fields filled.

**Expected output:** A complete risk register with 15-20 identified risks, scored and rated, with mitigation strategies, owners, and monitoring plan
**Pro tip:** The biggest risk most businesses ignore is "unknown unknowns" — risks you haven't even thought of. Schedule a quarterly "black swan" session where the team brainstorms the most unlikely but catastrophic scenarios

### 3.27 Hiring Process Design
**Use case:** Design a structured hiring process that attracts and selects the best candidates
**Best for:** HR managers, founders, team leads

Act as a talent acquisition specialist. I need to design or improve my hiring process for a specific role. Answer these questions: what role am I hiring for, what department, what is the team size, what is the current hiring process (if any), and what are the biggest hiring challenges (too many applicants, not enough, wrong fit, slow process). Then design a complete hiring process: Job Description Template (role title, mission statement, key responsibilities, requirements — must-have vs nice-to-have, team culture description, benefits, compensation range, application instructions — focus on selling the opportunity, not just listing requirements), Sourcing Strategy (best channels for this role — LinkedIn, industry job boards, communities, referrals, recruiters — with a sourcing mix recommendation), Screening Process (resume review criteria, phone screen questions — 30-minute call to assess motivation, communication, and basic fit), Interview Process Design (2-3 rounds maximum — round 1: skills assessment or take-home project; round 2: technical/deep-dive with team; round 3: culture add and leadership. Each round has: purpose, interviewer(s), specific questions or exercises, scoring rubric, and time allocation), Assessment Methods (beyond interviews — work sample test, case study presentation, skills assessment, personality assessment, reference checks), Interview Scorecard (standardized evaluation form per candidate with 1-5 ratings on: skills, experience, culture add, communication, problem-solving — with behavioral anchors for each rating level), Decision Framework (how to compare candidates objectively, tie-breaking criteria, involving team input), and Offer Process (verbal offer call, written offer, negotiation strategy, closing timeline). Also include the candidate experience map — every touchpoint from application to offer/rejection, with communication templates and timeline.

**Expected output:** A complete hiring process with job description, sourcing strategy, interview design, scorecard, and offer process
**Pro tip:** The best predictor of job performance is a work sample test, not an interview. Ask candidates to do a 2-4 hour project that mirrors actual job work — it's 2x more predictive than any interview question

### 3.28 Customer Support Knowledge Base Builder
**Use case:** Create a structured knowledge base for customer support that reduces tickets and improves response quality
**Best for:** Customer support managers, operations managers

Act as a knowledge management specialist. I need to build a customer support knowledge base. Answer these questions: what is your product/service, what are the top 10 customer questions or issues, what support channels do you use (email, chat, phone, self-service), and how large is your support team. Then design a complete knowledge base structure: Article Types (troubleshooting guides — step-by-step problem resolution; how-to guides — task-based instructions; FAQ — quick answers to common questions; onboarding guides — setup and first steps; policy articles — billing, shipping, returns, terms; integration guides — how your product works with others), Article Template (title: problem-focused or task-focused; metadata: tags, related articles, product version; difficulty level: beginner/intermediate/advanced; body: problem statement, step-by-step instructions with screenshots, expected outcome, troubleshooting tips; last updated date), Categorization Structure (hierarchy of categories and subcategories based on how customers actually search — use support ticket data to identify natural groupings, not internal org structure), Search Optimization (SEO for internal search — keywords in titles, headers, and first paragraph; synonym mapping; redirects from common misspellings), Content Creation Workflow (who writes: support team or product team; review process: technical accuracy + readability; approval path; publishing; maintenance schedule — review each article every 90 days for accuracy), Self-Service Success Metrics (article view-to-ticket rate — how often does reading an article prevent a ticket?; search success rate — do customers find what they search for?; article satisfaction score — thumbs up/down per article), and Maintenance Process (outdated article flagging, deprecation workflow, update triggers — when product changes, related articles must be updated within 48 hours).

**Expected output:** A complete knowledge base structure with article types, templates, categorization, SEO, workflow, and metrics
**Pro tip:** The best knowledge base articles are written from support tickets, not from product documentation. The language customers use in tickets is the language your knowledge base should use — write in their words, not your internal terminology

### 3.29 Inventory Management System Design
**Use case:** Design an inventory management system for physical product businesses
**Best for:** E-commerce operators, retail managers, product business owners

Act as a supply chain and inventory management consultant. I need to design an inventory management system. Answer these questions: what products do you sell (how many SKUs), what is your sales volume (units per month), where are your products stored (warehouse, retail, dropshipped), and what is your current inventory process. Then design a complete inventory management system: Inventory Classification (apply ABC analysis — A items: top 20% of products generating 80% of revenue, tight control; B items: next 30%, moderate control; C items: bottom 50%, simplified control), Reorder Point Calculation (formula: reorder point = lead time demand + safety stock — calculate for each inventory class), Safety Stock Formula (safety stock = Z-score × standard deviation of demand × √lead time — with Z=1.65 for 95% service level, Z=2.33 for 99% service level), Economic Order Quantity (EOQ = √(2DS/H) where D=annual demand, S=order cost, H=holding cost per unit per year), Cycle Counting Process (count A items weekly, B items monthly, C items quarterly — with reconciliation and adjustment procedures), Warehouse Organization (slotting strategy — fast-moving items at waist-to-eye level in closest pick location; bin location system with zone, aisle, rack, shelf, position), Technology Recommendations (what features an inventory management system needs — real-time tracking, barcode scanning, low-stock alerts, demand forecasting, multi-channel sync, reporting), Physical Inventory Procedure (annual full count preparation, execution, reconciliation, adjustment approval workflow), and Shrinkage Management (shrinkage causes: theft, damage, miscounting, supplier errors — with prevention and detection measures for each). Include a dashboard of key inventory metrics: inventory turnover ratio, days of inventory outstanding, stockout rate, carrying cost, and fill rate.

**Expected output:** A complete inventory management system design with classification, reorder points, cycle counting, warehouse organization, and metrics
**Pro tip:** Most businesses over-order C-items (slow movers) and under-order A-items (fast movers). The Pareto principle means you should check A-item stock daily and C-item stock quarterly. The money tied up in excess C-item inventory is usually a hidden profit killer

### 3.30 Employee Onboarding Program
**Use case:** Design a structured onboarding program that accelerates new hire productivity
**Best for:** HR managers, team leads, founders

Act as an employee onboarding and experience specialist. I need to design an onboarding program for new hires. Answer these questions: what is my company size and industry, what roles are we typically hiring for, what is the current onboarding process (if any), and what is our current time-to-productivity for new hires. Then design a comprehensive onboarding program broken into phases: Pre-Arrival (week -2 to day 0 — equipment ordering and setup, account creation, workspace preparation, welcome email with first-day details, manager prepares 30-60-90 day plan, team introduction email sent in advance), Week 1 (Orientation — company mission and values deep-dive, org structure and team introductions, HR policies and benefits enrollment, IT setup complete, first project starts — a small win they can achieve in the first week, buddy assignment, daily 15-minute check-ins with manager), Week 2-4 (Immersion — product/service training sessions, meet key cross-functional stakeholders, attend team meetings, complete first meaningful task, weekly 30-minute 1:1 with manager, schedule customer calls ride-along or shadowing), Month 2 (Contribution — take ownership of a real project or task, begin working more independently, bi-weekly 1:1s with manager, feedback session on onboarding experience, introduction to performance expectations and review process), Month 3 (Independence — manage their first project end-to-end, present to team or all-hands, set goals for next quarter, feedback from team members, 90-day performance review). For each phase specify: who is responsible (HR, manager, buddy, IT), key activities, desired outcomes, and success metrics. Include templates for: 30-60-90 day plan, first week schedule, buddy guide, manager onboarding checklist, and 90-day review form.

**Expected output:** A complete onboarding program with 4 phases, responsibility matrix, templates, and success metrics
**Pro tip:** The most important day in onboarding is day 1. Have everything ready before they arrive — equipment, accounts, desk, a warm welcome. The first impression sets the tone for their entire tenure. A great first week can cut time-to-productivity in half

### 3.31 Cash Flow Forecasting Model
**Use case:** Build a cash flow forecast to predict and manage liquidity
**Best for:** Founders, CFOs, financial managers

Act as a financial planning consultant. I need to build a cash flow forecast for my business. Answer these questions: what is your business model (subscription, project-based, retail, SaaS), what are your main revenue streams, what are your major expense categories, what is your current cash balance, and do you have any debt or financing. Then build a cash flow forecasting framework: Revenue Forecasting (for each revenue stream — expected monthly revenue with low/medium/high scenarios, payment terms, collection timing, seasonality adjustments, historical collection rates for accounts receivable), Expense Forecasting (fixed costs — rent, salaries, insurance, subscriptions with known dates; variable costs — COGS, marketing spend, contractor payments with estimation basis; one-time expenses — equipment, legal fees, project costs with planned dates), Cash Flow Statement Structure (opening balance + cash inflows - cash outflows = closing balance — project monthly for the next 12 months), Working Capital Management (accounts receivable days, accounts payable days, inventory days — with optimization recommendations for each — collect faster, pay slower, reduce inventory), Scenario Planning (base case — most likely scenario; upside case — 20% revenue increase; downside case — 20% revenue decrease + 10% expense increase — show cash balance in each scenario monthly), Runway Calculation (if revenue stopped today, how many months can you operate — current cash / monthly burn rate), Warning Triggers (cash balance thresholds that trigger action — 6 months runway: comfortable; 3 months: alert — review expenses, delay hires; 1 month: critical — immediate financing or drastic cost cutting), and Financing Options (line of credit, invoice factoring, term loan, equity — when to consider each based on the forecast). Include a dashboard of key cash metrics: days cash on hand, cash conversion cycle, burn rate, and runway.

**Expected output:** A cash flow forecasting framework with revenue/expense projections, scenario analysis, runway calculation, and trigger thresholds
**Pro tip:** Cash flow is more important than profit. A profitable company can go bankrupt from poor cash flow management. Always track your cash conversion cycle — the days between paying for inventory/services and getting paid by customers — and work to shorten it

### 3.32 Business Continuity Plan
**Use case:** Prepare a business continuity plan for disruptions — natural disasters, cyber attacks, pandemics, supply chain failures
**Best for:** Operations managers, founders, risk officers

Act as a business continuity planning specialist. I need to create a business continuity plan for my business. Answer these questions: what is your business type, what are your critical business functions, what are your key dependencies (suppliers, technology, people, facilities), and what geographic risks do you face (natural disasters, political instability, infrastructure risks). Then create a complete business continuity plan: Business Impact Analysis (identify critical functions — what must continue for the business to survive; for each function: maximum tolerable downtime, recovery time objective, recovery point objective, staffing requirements, technology dependencies, revenue impact per hour of downtime), Risk Scenarios (top 5-7 disruption scenarios ranked by likelihood and impact — data breach, server outage, supplier failure, key person loss, facility inaccessibility, payment system failure, regulatory action), Response Plans for each scenario (immediate actions — first 15 minutes; short-term actions — first 24 hours; medium-term actions — first week; communication plan — who to notify, how, when, what to say), Technology Disaster Recovery (data backup strategy — frequency, location, restoration testing; system redundancy — failover plan, cloud backups, offline access; communication recovery — alternative communication channels if primary fails), Alternate Work Arrangements (remote work enablement — if facility inaccessible; temporary location; cross-training coverage for key roles; reduced operations plan — what we stop doing to preserve cash), Crisis Communication Templates (internal: employee notification, leadership updates; external: customer notification, supplier communication, media statement, regulatory notification), Plan Testing Schedule (tabletop exercise — quarterly; full drill — annually; backup restoration test — monthly), and Plan Maintenance (quarterly review and update process, change management — when major changes occur (new systems, key personnel changes, new locations), the plan must be updated within 30 days).

**Expected output:** A complete business continuity plan with impact analysis, scenario response plans, technology recovery, communication templates, and testing schedule
**Pro tip:** Most business continuity plans fail because they're never tested. A tabletop exercise (sitting around a table talking through a scenario) takes 2 hours and reveals 80% of plan weaknesses. Do one this quarter

### 3.33 Pricing Model Comparison and Selection
**Use case:** Evaluate different pricing models and select the best one for your product or service
**Best for:** Founders, product managers, financial analysts

Act as a pricing strategy consultant. I need to evaluate pricing models for my product or service. Answer these questions: what is your product/service, who is your target customer, what is your cost structure, who are your competitors and their pricing, and what is your target revenue and margin. Then evaluate the following pricing models with a recommendation: One-Time Purchase (pros: simple for customer, immediate revenue; cons: no recurring revenue, higher churn, lower LTV), Subscription/Monthly (pros: predictable recurring revenue, lower upfront barrier, built-in relationship; cons: requires ongoing value delivery, higher customer acquisition cost needed), Tiered (pros: captures different willingness-to-pay, upgrade path, serves multiple segments; cons: complexity, feature selection is hard, tier confusion), Usage-Based (pros: aligns price with value, low barrier to start, scales with customer success; cons: unpredictable revenue, customer anxiety about bill shock), Freemium (pros: massive adoption, viral potential, natural upgrade path; cons: high support cost for free users, low conversion rates typically 2-5%), Per-User/Seat (pros: clear value, scales with customer growth; cons: disincentivizes usage across org, admin overhead), Value-Based (pros: captures full value, can charge premium; cons: hard to determine value, requires deep customer understanding, complex to communicate). For each model provide: how to calculate pricing (recommended price range with rationale), expected impact on acquisition and retention, implementation complexity, and success metrics to track. Then recommend the best model for your specific business with a transition plan if switching from an existing model.

**Expected output:** A pricing model evaluation covering 7 models with analysis, pricing calculations, and final recommendation
**Pro tip:** The best pricing model is the one that aligns your revenue with your customer's success. If your customer succeeds and grows, your revenue should grow naturally. Usage-based and value-based pricing achieve this alignment best

### 3.34 Quality Assurance Process Design
**Use case:** Design a quality assurance process for products, services, or deliverables
**Best for:** Operations managers, product managers, team leads

Act as a quality management specialist. I need to design a QA process for my business. Answer these questions: what type of output does your business produce (physical product, software, service, content, consulting), what are the common quality issues you face, and what is the current review/check process. Then design a complete QA process: Quality Standard Definition (for each deliverable type — what "good" looks like: accuracy standards, completeness criteria, formatting requirements, performance benchmarks, customer satisfaction targets), Inspection Points (at what stage in the process quality is checked — input quality: verify raw materials/information before work begins; in-process: check at milestone points during production; output: inspect finished deliverable before delivery; post-delivery: customer feedback and defect tracking), QA Checklist Template (standardized checklist per deliverable type with pass/fail criteria — include: functional requirements, accuracy, completeness, formatting, compliance, performance), Sampling Methodology (for high-volume outputs — random sampling with sample size calculation based on acceptable quality level; for low-volume: 100% inspection for critical items), Defect Classification and Tracking (critical — causes failure or safety issue; major — significantly impacts functionality; minor — cosmetic or low-impact issues. For each: tracking method, resolution timeline, root cause analysis requirement), Root Cause Analysis Process (when defects exceed threshold — use 5 Whys or fishbone diagram to identify systemic causes, not just fix symptoms), Continuous Improvement Loop (collected defect data → trend analysis → process improvement → updated standards → re-training), and QA Metrics (defect rate, first-pass yield, rework cost, customer-reported defects, audit score, and trend over time). Include roles and responsibilities — who performs QA (dedicated QA team, peer review, automated checks, or combination).

**Expected output:** A complete QA process with standards, inspection points, checklists, defect tracking, RCA, and metrics
**Pro tip:** The most cost-effective quality improvement is catching defects at the earliest possible stage. A defect found during design costs $1 to fix; during production costs $100; after delivery costs $10,000. Invest in input and in-process QA, not just output inspection

### 3.35 Strategic Partnership Evaluation
**Use case:** Evaluate potential strategic partnerships and structure the collaboration
**Best for:** Business development, founders, partnership managers

Act as a strategic partnership consultant. I need to evaluate a potential partnership opportunity. Answer these questions: what is my company, what does the potential partner do, what type of partnership are we considering (co-marketing, distribution, technology integration, co-product, referral, joint venture), and what are our respective goals. Then create a partnership evaluation framework: Strategic Fit Assessment (mission alignment — do we share values and vision?; audience overlap — do we serve the same or complementary customers?; product complementarity — do our products work better together?; market position — what does each bring to the table?; competitive implications — does this partnership affect relationships with other partners or competitors?), Value Projection Model (projected value for each partner: incremental revenue, new customer acquisition, cost savings, technology acceleration, market access, brand lift — estimate with low/medium/high scenarios), Partnership Structure Options (recommended structure with rationale: referral agreement — pay per lead/customer; revenue share — split revenue from joint offering; co-marketing — shared campaign costs and leads; technology integration — build API or native integration; joint venture — create new entity together), Term Sheet Template (key terms: duration, exclusivity, territory, IP ownership, data sharing, revenue split, termination conditions, governance — meeting cadence, decision rights, dispute resolution), Risk Assessment (what could go wrong — partner doesn't deliver, brand damage, customer confusion, competitive risk, lock-in — with mitigation strategies for each), and Success Metrics (KPIs to track partnership health: leads generated, revenue attributed, customer satisfaction, partner satisfaction, time-to-value, net promoter score from joint customers). Include a scoring matrix to compare multiple potential partners quantitatively.

**Expected output:** A partnership evaluation with strategic fit, value projection, structure options, term sheet, risk assessment, and success metrics
**Pro tip:** The best partnerships are asymmetric — each partner brings something the other can't easily build. If both sides bring the same thing (both have audiences, both have products), there's no partnership; there's just a trade. True partnerships create net new value neither could create alone

### 3.36 Expense Policy and Approval Workflow
**Use case:** Design a company expense policy and approval workflow
**Best for:** Finance managers, founders, operations managers

Act as a finance and operations consultant. I need to design an expense policy and approval workflow for my company.
