Answer these questions: how many employees, what types of expenses do they incur (travel, software, supplies, client entertainment, remote work), what is the average monthly spend, and what is the current approval process. Then design a complete expense management system: Expense Policy Document (categories of expenses with per-category rules — what's reimbursable, limits, documentation requirements, pre-approval needs; travel policy — flight class, hotel rate limits, per diem, mileage rate, booking channels; software/SAAS policy — approval needed, standard vendors, cancellation process; entertainment policy — per-person limits, client vs internal, documentation), Approval Workflow by Amount (under $50: no approval needed, receipt required; $50-$500: manager approval; $500-$5,000: manager + department head approval; $5,000-$25,000: executive approval; over $25,000: CEO/board approval. Each level: who approves, documentation needed, timeline for decision), Expense Reporting Process (submit within 30 days, required receipts for all expenses over $25, digital submission via [tool], reimbursement within 5 business days of approval, audit triggers — random 10% audit, 100% audit for high-spend employees, quarterly pattern review for anomalies), Corporate Card Program (who gets cards, spending limits per card, automatic categorization, personal vs business expense separation, liability and misuse consequences), Non-Compliance Handling (first offense: warning and education; second offense: manager notification and coaching; third offense: loss of card privileges or escalation to HR), Periodic Review (quarterly policy effectiveness review, annual policy update based on inflation and business changes, employee expense trend analysis). Include template text for each policy section that can be adapted.

**Expected output:** A complete expense policy and approval workflow with tiers, processes, compliance handling, and review cadence
**Pro tip:** The best expense policy balances control with trust. Too strict: employees waste time on small approvals and feel distrusted. Too loose: money leaks. The sweet spot is high trust on small expenses ($50-100 threshold) and rigorous review on large ones ($500+)

### 3.37 Customer Feedback Loop System
**Use case:** Design a systematic process for collecting, analyzing, and acting on customer feedback
**Best for:** Product managers, customer success, operations

Act as a customer experience and feedback systems consultant. I need to design a systematic customer feedback loop. Answer these questions: what is your product/service, how do you currently collect feedback, how do you use it, and what tools do you have (CRM, survey tool, analytics). Then design a complete feedback loop system: Collection Channels (in-app surveys — targeted by user behavior, max 2 questions, triggered after key actions; email NPS surveys — quarterly, with follow-up question for detractors; support ticket analysis — categorize and quantify issues from tickets; social media monitoring — brand mentions, sentiment analysis; customer interviews — monthly, 30 min, structured format; user testing — quarterly, observe users using your product; review platforms — monitor and respond to public reviews), Categorization Framework (feedback types — bug report, feature request, usability issue, support quality, pricing concern, praise; severity — critical, major, minor, suggestion; product area mapping — tag feedback to specific product features or processes), Analysis Process (weekly triage — categorize and prioritize all feedback from all channels; monthly trends report — top issues, sentiment trends, request frequency; quarterly strategic review — patterns across quarters, roadmap impact, competitive feedback comparison), Action Loop (feedback → analyze → decide → act → close the loop with customer. For each piece of feedback: acknowledge receipt within 24 hours, estimate when it will be addressed, update customer on progress, close the loop when resolved. Public roadmaps: share what you're working on based on feedback), Closed-Loop Communication Templates (acknowledgment: "Thanks for your feedback on X, we're reviewing it"; under review: "Your feedback on X has been shared with the product team"; shipped: "The feature you requested is now live — here's how to use it"; unable to address: "Here's why we can't implement X and what we're doing instead"), and Metrics (feedback response time, % of feedback with closed loop, NPS trend, customer satisfaction with resolution, feature request-to-launch rate, feedback volume trends). Include a weekly feedback review meeting agenda template.

**Expected output:** A complete customer feedback loop system with collection, categorization, analysis, action, and close-the-loop process
**Pro tip:** Most companies collect feedback but never close the loop. The competitive advantage isn't having more feedback — it's telling customers what you did with theirs. A simple "you asked, we built" email campaign can increase NPS by 10+ points

### 3.38 Remote Work Policy and Playbook
**Use case:** Design a remote work policy for a hybrid or fully distributed team
**Best for:** HR managers, founders, operations

Act as a future-of-work consultant specializing in remote team management. I need to design a remote work policy for my company. Answer these questions: what is my company size, what is our current work model (in-office, hybrid, remote), what roles do we have, and what are our biggest remote work challenges. Then create a comprehensive remote work policy and playbook: Work Model Definition (fully remote — no office, async-first; hybrid — 2-3 days in office, schedule coordination; office-flex — primarily office but flexible days. For each: expectations, who qualifies, transition process), Communication Guidelines (async-first — use written communication, documentation, recorded updates; synchronous when needed — regular team meetings, 1:1s, decision-making huddles; response time expectations: Slack messages within 4 hours during core hours, email within 24 hours, urgent issues via phone call), Core Hours (defined overlap hours for synchronous work — e.g., 10am-3pm ET — with flexibility outside those hours; time zone policies for distributed teams), Home Office Setup (equipment provided — laptop, monitor, keyboard, mouse, headset, webcam; internet reimbursement — monthly stipend or actual cost; ergonomic assessment — virtual or reimbursement for chair/desk), Meeting Culture (camera-on norms — on for team meetings, 1:1s, client calls; meeting-free blocks — 4-hour maker time, no internal meetings; async-first review — share documents 24 hours before discussion; meeting agenda and notes required for all recurring meetings), Performance Management (output-focused — measure results, not hours; regular check-ins — weekly 1:1, monthly goal review, quarterly performance review; async updates — weekly written update to team, monthly update to manager), Team Connection and Culture (virtual team building — monthly social event, game sessions, coffee chats; annual in-person retreat — team offsite; recognition program — peer-to-peer recognition, shoutouts in team channel; onboarding buddy — remote-specific support for new hires), and Wellness and Boundaries (encourage breaks, no after-hours messaging expectations, mental health days, internet/tech stipend, home office budget renewal). Include a manager's guide to remote leadership: how to coach, motivate, and support remote team members.

**Expected output:** A complete remote work policy and playbook covering communication, core hours, equipment, meetings, performance, culture, and wellness
**Pro tip:** The biggest remote work challenge is not productivity — it's connection and career growth. Remote employees often feel invisible for promotions and miss informal learning. Institute a formal mentorship program and ensure remote employees get equal visibility for growth opportunities

### 3.39 Compliance and Regulatory Checklist Builder
**Use case:** Build a compliance checklist for industry-specific regulations and standards
**Best for:** Compliance officers, operations managers, founders

Act as a regulatory compliance consultant. I need to build a compliance checklist for my industry and business type. Answer these questions: what industry are you in (healthcare, finance, SaaS, e-commerce, manufacturing, professional services), what geographies do you operate in, what is your company size, and do you handle sensitive data (PII, PHI, financial data, children's data). Then create a comprehensive compliance framework: Applicable Regulations by industry and geography (GDPR — EU data protection; CCPA — California privacy; HIPAA — US healthcare; SOC2 — SaaS security; PCI-DSS — payment card data; SOX — financial reporting; ISO 27001 — information security; industry-specific regulations — with applicability assessment for your business), Compliance Requirements Checklist organized by domain (Data Privacy — data inventory, consent management, data subject access requests, breach notification, data retention/deletion, privacy policy, cookie consent; Information Security — access control, encryption, vulnerability management, incident response, vendor risk management, employee training, physical security; Financial Compliance — segregation of duties, audit trail, approval workflows, financial reporting controls, fraud prevention; Employment Compliance — labor laws, equal opportunity, harassment prevention, workplace safety, leave policies, contractor classification), Documentation Requirements (policies needed — data protection policy, information security policy, acceptable use policy, incident response plan, business continuity plan, employee handbook; record-keeping requirements — retention periods, format, accessibility for audits), Audit Preparation (internal audit checklist — quarterly self-assessment; external audit preparation — document collection, evidence organization, staff interview preparation, remediation plan template; common audit findings by industry — most frequently cited deficiencies and how to avoid them), Training Requirements (mandatory training topics by role — data privacy for all employees, security training for engineers, compliance training for finance; training frequency and tracking), and Compliance Calendar (annual compliance tasks by month: data privacy audit, security assessment, policy reviews, training completion, external audits, regulatory filings). Include risk level indicators for each requirement — critical, high, medium, low — based on penalty severity for non-compliance.

**Expected output:** A compliance framework with applicable regulations, requirements checklist, documentation, audit prep, training, and annual calendar
**Pro tip:** Compliance is not a one-time project — it's an ongoing operational process. The companies that treat compliance as "check the box once a year" are the ones that fail audits. Build compliance into your daily workflows, not just your annual review

### 3.40 Customer Journey Optimization Strategy
**Use case:** Map and optimize the complete customer journey from awareness to advocacy
**Best for:** Marketing managers, product managers, founders

Act as a customer experience and journey optimization consultant. I need to map and improve my customer journey. Answer these questions: what is your product/service, what are the key stages a customer goes through (awareness, consideration, purchase, onboarding, retention, advocacy), and what do you know about customer behavior at each stage. Then create a complete customer journey optimization strategy: Current State Journey Map (for each stage: customer actions — what they do; touchpoints — where they interact with your brand; emotions — how they feel (frustrated, confused, excited, delighted); pain points — what's difficult or unclear; opportunities — where improvement would have most impact). For each stage, specify: Awareness Stage — channels customers use to discover you, their trigger to start searching, first impression of your brand, content they consume; Consideration Stage — how they evaluate options, comparison criteria, information sources they trust, common questions; Purchase Stage — barriers to purchase (price, risk, complexity), decision-making process, checkout/purchase experience, post-purchase emotions; Onboarding Stage — first experience with product, time-to-value, support interactions, learning curve; Retention Stage — regular usage patterns, engagement triggers, reasons for churn, loyalty drivers; Advocacy Stage — what would make them refer, how they share their experience, incentives for advocacy. Then provide optimization recommendations for each stage (quick wins — implement in 1-2 weeks; strategic changes — 1-3 months; transformation — 3-6 months), prioritized by impact and effort. Include measurement plan — KPIs per stage and how to track them.

**Expected output:** A customer journey map with current state analysis and optimization recommendations at each stage, prioritized by impact and effort
**Pro tip:** Most customer journey maps are aspirational — they show the ideal journey, not the real one. The real journey has dead ends, detours, and drop-offs. Map the actual journey by talking to customers who churned, not just the ones who succeeded

### 3.41 Data Privacy and Governance Framework
**Use case:** Establish data privacy policies and governance practices for handling customer and company data
**Best for:** Compliance officers, data managers, founders

Act as a data privacy and governance consultant. I need to establish data privacy and governance practices. Answer these questions: what type of data do you collect (customer PII, payment data, analytics, employee data), where is it stored, who has access, and what regulations apply. Then create a data privacy and governance framework: Data Inventory and Mapping (catalog all data types collected: categories, collection points, storage locations, processing purposes, sharing with third parties, retention periods, deletion procedures — create a data flow diagram showing how data moves through your systems), Privacy Policy Template (sections required: what data is collected, how it's collected, how it's used, who it's shared with, how long it's kept, user rights (access, correction, deletion, portability), security measures, cookies, children's privacy, policy changes, contact information — tailored to your data practices), Consent Management (consent mechanisms — opt-in checkboxes, cookie consent banner, preference center; consent records — store who consented, when, to what; withdrawal process — how users can withdraw consent and what happens to their data), Data Subject Access Request (DSAR) Process (steps to handle a DSAR: verify identity → locate all data → compile response → deliver within required timeframe (usually 30 days) → document completion — with templates for each step), Data Security Measures (access controls — role-based access, principle of least privilege; encryption — at rest and in transit; monitoring — access logs, anomaly detection; breach response — detection, containment, notification, documentation, review), Vendor/Third-Party Assessment (questions to ask vendors about their data practices: where is data stored, who has access, what sub-processors do they use, security certifications, breach history, data processing agreement required), Employee Training (mandatory privacy training content: recognizing PII, handling data requests, breach reporting, secure data practices, consequences of non-compliance; annual refresher required), and Governance Structure (data owner per data category, privacy committee, regular audit schedule, policy update process, escalation path for privacy incidents).

**Expected output:** A data privacy and governance framework with data inventory, policies, consent management, DSAR process, security measures, and training
**Pro tip:** Data privacy is a competitive advantage, not just a compliance burden. Customers increasingly choose brands they trust with their data. A clear, customer-friendly privacy policy and transparent data practices can increase conversion rates and customer loyalty

### 3.42 Capacity Planning and Resource Allocation
**Use case:** Plan team capacity and allocate resources effectively across projects and initiatives
**Best for:** Operations managers, project managers, team leads

Act as a resource management consultant. I need to plan capacity and allocate resources for my team. Answer these questions: what is your team size and composition (roles, skills), what projects/initiatives are currently active or planned, what is the current workload per person, and what is the planning horizon (weekly, monthly, quarterly). Then create a capacity planning system: Demand Forecasting (for each initiative: estimated hours required per week, duration, start/end dates, skill sets needed, priority level — critical, important, nice-to-have), Capacity Calculation (total available hours per person = working hours per week × weeks in period — PTO, holidays, meetings, admin time (typically 20-30% overhead); net available hours per person = total - overhead; total team capacity = sum of all net available hours), Allocation Mapping (assign initiatives to team members based on skills, interest, and development goals; track allocation % per person — target 80-90% utilization for sustainability, 100%+ means overworked and at risk of burnout), Bottleneck Identification (which skill sets are over-demanded vs underutilized; which projects are competing for the same people; which timelines are unrealistic given capacity), Tradeoff Recommendations (if demand exceeds capacity: which projects to delay, descope, or cancel; what to hire for; what to outsource; what to automate), Tracking and Adjustment Cadence (weekly: 30-minute resource review — check actual vs planned, adjust for priority changes; monthly: capacity reforecast for next month; quarterly: strategic resource review — hiring needs, skill development, project portfolio adjustments). Provide a resource allocation dashboard format showing: per-person allocation, team utilization rate, demand vs capacity gap, and project health indicators.

**Expected output:** A capacity planning system with demand forecasting, allocation mapping, bottleneck identification, and tracking cadence
**Pro tip:** The #1 cause of missed deadlines is not poor execution — it's overcommitment at the planning stage. Add a 20% buffer to all capacity estimates for unexpected work (urgent requests, bug fixes, meetings that run long). If you think something takes 10 hours, plan for 12

### 3.43 Feedback Culture Implementation Guide
**Use case:** Build a culture of constructive feedback that improves team performance and trust
**Best for:** Team leads, HR managers, founders

Act as an organizational culture and feedback specialist. I need to build a feedback culture in my team or organization. Answer these questions: what is the current feedback culture (do people give feedback openly, avoid it, or only give negative feedback), what is the team size, and what are the common challenges with feedback in your organization. Then create a feedback culture implementation plan: Feedback Principles (specific — focus on observable behaviors and impact, not personality; timely — give feedback as close to the event as possible; actionable — include a suggestion for improvement; balanced — recognize what's working too; requestable — make it safe to ask for feedback; kind — deliver with genuine care for the person's growth), Feedback Models (SBI Model — Situation: "In yesterday's client meeting...", Behavior: "when you interrupted the client three times...", Impact: "it made the client feel unheard and we lost the follow-up."; Situation-Behavior-Impact is clean and non-judgmental. Plus/Delta Model — "What worked well was... What could be different next time is..." — good for team retrospectives. Start/Stop/Continue — "Start doing X, Stop doing Y, Continue doing Z" — good for manager feedback), Giving Feedback Training (workshop structure: why feedback matters, how to use the models, practice sessions with real scenarios, receiving feedback gracefully, common mistakes to avoid), Receiving Feedback Skills (listen without defending, ask clarifying questions, thank the giver, take time to process, share your action plan), Feedback Systems (regular 1:1s with built-in feedback time, retrospectives for team feedback, anonymous feedback option (but encourage named), 360-degree feedback process — annually, peer-to-peer, manager-to-direct, direct-to-manager), Psychological Safety Building (leaders go first — ask for feedback publicly, share their own growth areas, respond to feedback with gratitude not defensiveness; normalize learning — mistakes are learning opportunities, not failures; model vulnerability — admit when you don't know something), and Feedback Metrics (frequency of feedback given and received, quality ratings, psychological safety survey scores, team satisfaction with feedback culture). Include specific conversation starters: "One thing I'd love to see you do more of is...", "I noticed in the last sprint that...", "What's one thing I could do differently to make your work easier?"

**Expected output:** A feedback culture implementation plan with principles, models, training, systems, and measurement
**Pro tip:** The single most effective thing a leader can do to build a feedback culture is ask for feedback on themselves — publicly, regularly, and with genuine receptivity. When the boss asks "what could I do better?" and actually changes, permission spreads through the entire organization

### 3.44 Exit Interview and Offboarding Process
**Use case:** Conduct meaningful exit interviews and create a smooth offboarding process
**Best for:** HR managers, team leads, founders

Act as an employee experience and retention consultant. I need to design an exit interview and offboarding process. Answer these questions: what is your company size, what is your current turnover rate, and what is the current offboarding process (if any). Then design a complete offboarding and exit interview system: Exit Interview Process (timing — conduct on the last week, not the last day (less emotional, more honest); format — 30-45 minute conversation with someone not the direct manager (HR, skip-level, external); setting — neutral, confidential, comfortable. Pre-interview: review employee's tenure, contributions, performance, and any flags), Exit Interview Questions structured by theme: Reasons for Leaving (what prompted you to start looking, what was the final straw, was there anything we could have done, what did the new role offer that we didn't), Work Environment (what did you enjoy most, what frustrated you most, how was work-life balance, how was recognition and feedback, were resources adequate), Management (how would you describe your manager's leadership style, what could they do better, did you feel supported in your growth), Culture (how would you describe our culture, what would you change, did you feel included and valued, did you understand how your work contributed to company goals), Growth (were there growth opportunities, did you receive enough training, were your career goals discussed and supported), Compensation (was compensation competitive, were benefits adequate, was the total rewards package clear), Honest Advice (what advice would you give leadership, is there anything you want to share that I haven't asked, would you recommend us as an employer — why or why not), Offboarding Process Checklist (announce departure to team — timing and messaging; knowledge transfer — documentation, handoff meetings, access transfer; equipment return — company laptop, phone, keys, access cards; account deactivation — email, Slack, CRM, code repositories, SaaS tools; final paycheck and benefits — final payment, COBRA, PTO payout, stock options; exit interview — schedule and conduct; alumni program — add to alumni database, LinkedIn alumni group, alumni newsletter, alumni events), Knowledge Transfer Requirements (document all active projects, key contacts, pending decisions, recurring tasks, passwords/document locations; record 2-3 Loom videos walking through critical processes; schedule handoff meetings with team members and stakeholders), Alumni Program Design (maintain relationship — LinkedIn group, alumni newsletter alumni events, alumni directory; rehire policy — alumni welcome to return, no waiting period, previous tenure counts for benefits; alumni benefits — continued access to certain resources, employee referral bonus for alumni who refer). Include templates for: transition plan, knowledge transfer document, offboarding checklist, and alumni outreach.

**Expected output:** A complete exit interview and offboarding process with interview questions, offboarding checklist, knowledge transfer, and alumni program
**Pro tip:** The best time to reduce turnover is day 1, not the exit interview. Most people decide to leave 3-6 months before they actually tell you. Monitor engagement signals — decreased participation, reduced discretionary effort, more sick days — and have stay conversations before they decide to leave

### 3.45 Business Metric Waterfall Analysis
**Use case:** Break down a business metric into its component drivers for root cause analysis
**Best for:** Operations managers, financial analysts, founders

Act as a business analytics consultant. I need to understand what drives changes in a key business metric using waterfall analysis. Tell me which metric you want to analyze (e.g., revenue, profit, customer count, conversion rate, churn) and what time period you're comparing (e.g., this month vs last month, this quarter vs last year). For example, if analyzing revenue: Revenue = Traffic × Conversion Rate × Average Order Value. For each component, I will: measure the actual change (e.g., traffic up 10%, conversion rate down 5%, AOV up 3%), calculate the contribution of each component to the total change using the waterfall method (isolate each variable's impact by holding others constant), identify which component had the largest impact on the total change, and analyze why that component changed (dig into the drivers of that specific metric — for traffic: which channels changed, what campaigns drove the change, any seasonal or external factors). Then generate recommendations: if traffic drove the change — optimize channels with best ROI; if conversion drove the change — review funnel steps, pricing, or competitor activity; if AOV drove the change — analyze product mix, upselling effectiveness, or pricing changes. Show the waterfall in a visual format using text: start at the base value, add/subtract each component's effect sequentially to arrive at the ending value, with each step clearly labeled and explained.

**Expected output:** A waterfall analysis of the chosen metric with component decomposition, contribution analysis, driver identification, and recommendations
**Pro tip:** Waterfall analysis is most powerful when you segment the data before analyzing it. Instead of analyzing total revenue, analyze revenue by customer segment (new vs existing), by channel (organic vs paid), or by product line. The same total change can have very different stories hiding inside it

### 3.46 Workflow Automation Opportunity Assessment
**Use case:** Identify and prioritize business processes that can be automated
**Best for:** Operations managers, founders, efficiency leads

Act as a workflow automation consultant. I need to identify which of my business processes can and should be automated. Answer these questions: what are the top 10 most time-consuming manual tasks in your business, how many hours per week does each take, who performs them, and what tools do you currently use. Then create an automation opportunity assessment: Automation Candidate Scoring (rate each task on: time spent — hours per week (1-5), frequency — daily/weekly/monthly (1-5), complexity — how hard to automate (1-5, higher = harder), error sensitivity — cost of human error (1-5), employee satisfaction — how much people hate doing it (1-5), strategic value — would automation free up high-value time (1-5). Calculate a composite score for each task and rank them), Automation Approaches by task type (data entry — spreadsheet automation, API integration, OCR; reporting — automated dashboard, scheduled report generation, data pipeline; communication — email templates, auto-responders, chatbot; approval workflows — automated routing, conditional logic, e-signature; file management — auto-filing, naming conventions, backup automation; customer service — knowledge base auto-suggest, ticket routing, canned responses; social media — scheduling, cross-posting, analytics aggregation), Technology Recommendations (no-code tools: Zapier/Make for simple integrations; low-code: Power Automate, n8n for complex workflows; built-in: use your existing tool's automation features first; custom: when no off-the-shelf solution fits), Implementation Prioritization (quick wins — high score, low complexity, implement in days; strategic projects — high score, medium complexity, 1-4 weeks; major initiatives — high score, high complexity, 1-3 months — may require custom development or new tools), ROI Estimation (for each automated process: hours saved per month × hourly cost = cost savings; error reduction value; employee satisfaction improvement; speed improvement; and implementation cost: tool subscriptions, development hours, training time. Calculate payback period and 12-month ROI), and Implementation Roadmap (Phase 1 — weeks 1-4: automate top 3 quick wins; Phase 2 — weeks 5-8: automate next 3-5 opportunities and monitor Phase 1; Phase 3 — weeks 9-12: strategic projects, evaluate results, plan next quarter). Include a note on what should NOT be automated — tasks requiring human judgment, creativity, empathy, or relationship building.

**Expected output:** An automation opportunity assessment with scored candidates, approach recommendations, ROI estimates, and 12-week implementation roadmap
**Pro tip:** Don't automate a bad process — you'll just get bad results faster. Before automating any workflow, first simplify and optimize the manual process. Remove unnecessary steps, clarify handoffs, and fix errors. Then automate the optimized version

### 3.47 Pricing Page Psychology and Architecture
**Use case:** Design a pricing page layout and copy that maximizes conversion
**Best for:** SaaS companies, product marketers, founders

Act as a pricing page conversion optimization specialist. I need to design a pricing page that drives conversions. Answer these questions: what is your product, how many pricing tiers do you want, what is the pricing for each tier, and who is your target customer. Then design a complete pricing page strategy: Pricing Architecture (recommended number of tiers — 3 is optimal (low, mid, high). The middle tier is your "best value" anchor; the high tier makes the middle look reasonable; the low tier captures price-sensitive customers. Consider a "hidden" enterprise tier for custom quotes. Decoy effect pricing: if you want to sell the middle tier, make the high tier more expensive than needed so the middle looks like great value), Feature Differentiation (for each tier: which features to include/exclude — enough differentiation that higher tiers feel valuable, but lower tiers still feel useful. Don't remove core value from the lowest tier or it won't convert. Recommended: limiting features vs usage vs support level), Pricing Visual Design (high tier highlighted or "most popular" badge — placement usually left-to-right: low, popular (highlighted), high; or center-popular with flanking tiers; annual vs monthly toggle — show annual savings prominently; per-seat or per-unit pricing clearly labeled; total price calculation visible before checkout), Psychological Elements (anchoring — show highest price first or most prominently; framing — "less than $1/day" vs $29/month; scarcity — "limited spots at this price" or "early adopter pricing"; social proof — "join 500+ companies" or logos of customers at each tier; risk reversal — "cancel anytime" or "14-day free trial" / guarantee badges), Pricing Page Copy (for each tier: tier name — aspirational not descriptive, one-line value proposition, key features (5-7 bullet points, benefit-focused), CTA button text — "Start Free Trial," "Get Started," "Contact Sales" — matched to commitment level), Pricing Page Optimization (A/B test ideas: annual vs monthly prominence, feature ordering, tier names, CTA colors, social proof placement, background vs highlighted tier, testimonials, FAQ accordion, comparison table vs feature list), and Common Mistakes (hiding pricing — reduces trust; too many tiers — choice paralysis, no clear winner; too few tiers — no upgrade path; all tiers equally highlighted — no decision guidance; weak differentiation between tiers — customers can't decide; missing FAQ — unaddressed questions create friction). Provide a final pricing page structure recommendation with wireframe description and copy for each tier.

**Expected output:** A pricing page design strategy with tier architecture, feature differentiation, psychology elements, copy, and optimization roadmap
**Pro tip:** The most effective pricing page change is often simply adding a "Most Popular" badge to your middle tier. This single change can increase conversion to that tier by 30-40%. Customers want guidance, not unlimited choice

### 3.48 Lean Experiment Design for Business Decisions
**Use case:** Design a low-cost experiment to test a business hypothesis before making a big investment
**Best for:** Founders, product managers, innovators

Act as a lean startup and experimentation consultant. I need to design an experiment to test a business hypothesis before committing significant resources. Tell me your hypothesis — the assumption you want to validate. I will guide you through the lean experiment design process using this framework: Hypothesis Statement (we believe that [this action] will result in [this outcome] for [this audience]. We'll know we're right when we see [this measurable signal]. We'll know we're wrong when we see [this counter-signal]), Minimum Success Criteria (what result would make this hypothesis worth pursuing further — specific, measurable, time-bound), Experiment Type recommendation based on what you're testing (demand — pre-sell, landing page with waitlist, fake door test, crowdfunding campaign; usability — smoke test with mockup, concierge test, Wizard of Oz test, paper prototype; pricing — price comparison test, Van Westendorp survey, willingness-to-pay question; retention — cohort commitment test, deposit/pre-order, subscription signup; channel — small ad buy, content test on one platform, cold outreach test with 50 prospects), Experiment Design (sample size needed — how many participants/data points for statistical significance; duration — how long to run; control group — what you compare against; success metric — the primary metric that determines go/no-go; data collection method — survey, analytics, observation, interview), Resource Requirements (time to set up, time to run, money needed, people needed, tools needed), Go/No-Go Decision Criteria (explicit thresholds — if metric reaches X%, we proceed; if Y%, we pivot; if Z%, we kill the idea. Pre-commit to these thresholds before running the experiment to avoid confirmation bias), and Learning Capture (what we expect to learn; what we'll do if we're right; what we'll do if we're wrong; what unexpected insights we'll watch for). Include a one-page experiment canvas template that can be filled out in 30 minutes for any future hypothesis.

**Expected output:** A complete lean experiment design with hypothesis, success criteria, experiment type, sample size, resources, and decision thresholds
**Pro tip:** The best experiments are the ones that tell you you're wrong as fast as possible. If your experiment can't disprove your hypothesis, it's not a good experiment. Design for falsifiability, not validation. "I was wrong" is the most valuable outcome of any experiment

### 3.49 Delegation Matrix for Leaders
**Use case:** Determine what to delegate, to whom, and how to develop team members through delegation
**Best for:** Managers, team leads, founders

Act as a leadership development consultant. I need to improve how I delegate tasks to my team. Answer these questions: what is your role, what are your main responsibilities, what tasks are you currently doing that could be delegated, and what are your team members' skills and development goals. Then create a delegation matrix using these frameworks: Delegation Levels (Level 1: Do exactly what I ask — tell them what, how, and when; Level 2: Research and report back — investigate and bring recommendations; Level 3: Do it and let me know — make the decision and inform me; Level 4: Do it and only tell me if it fails — full ownership, inform on exceptions; Level 5: This is your area now — full ownership and decision authority. Assign each task a delegation level based on the person's experience with that task type and their development goal), Task Audit (list all your tasks and categorize: stop doing (delegate or eliminate), delegate (hand off with appropriate delegation level), automate (set up system to handle), do (must be done by you). Use the Eisenhower Matrix: urgent/important, not urgent/important, urgent/not important, not urgent/not important), Person-Task Matching (for each team member: current capability (1-5), aspiration (1-5 — how much they want to do this), development opportunity (what skill this develops), and delegation level (based on capability + aspiration). Match tasks to the person who has the highest combination of capability and aspiration, or to someone who needs to develop that skill), Delegation Brief Template (what needs to be done, why it matters, what success looks like, what the constraints are, what authority they have, what resources they can use, who they can ask for help, when it's due, how we'll check in, what level of delegation this is), Check-in Cadence by delegation level (Level 1-2: daily check-in; Level 3: check-in at milestones; Level 4: weekly status update; Level 5: monthly review), Common Delegation Mistakes (delegating without context — explain the "why"; delegating without authority — give them decision power; over-delegating to top performers and under-delegating to developing team members; reverse delegation — letting them hand the task back to you; hovering — checking in too frequently for the delegation level assigned).

**Expected output:** A delegation matrix with task audit, person-task matching, delegation brief template, and check-in cadence by level
**Pro tip:** The goal of delegation is not just to free up your time — it's to develop your team. Delegate tasks that are slightly beyond someone's current capability but within their aspiration. The "stretch zone" is where growth happens. If you always delegate what people already know, you're managing, not developing

### 3.50 OKR Cascading and Alignment Process
**Use case:** Cascade company-level OKRs to departments, teams, and individuals
**Best for:** Leadership teams, OKR champions, HR

Act as an OKR implementation consultant. I need to cascade our company OKRs through the organization. Answer these questions: what are your company's top 3-5 objectives for this quarter, what is your organizational structure (departments/teams), and have you used OKRs before. Then create an OKR cascading and alignment process: Top-Down Context Setting (company OKRs defined first — 3-5 objectives with 3-5 key results each. Company OKRs answer "where are we going?" and "how will we know we're making progress?" Each key result must be measurable and have a specific target — e.g., "increase monthly recurring revenue from $50K to $75K" not "grow revenue"), Department/Team OKRs (each team creates their own OKRs that answer "how do we contribute to company OKRs?" Team OKRs should directly ladder up to at least one company key result. Teams have autonomy on their key results — they decide the "how" while the company sets the "what." Cross-functional dependencies identified: if Team A's OKR depends on Team B delivering something, flag it during alignment review), Individual OKRs / Personal Objectives (individuals set personal OKRs that ladder up to team OKRs. Not every individual needs OKRs — for roles with well-defined tasks, performance goals may be more appropriate. Individuals should have a mix of work-related and personal development objectives), OKR Drafting Guidelines (objectives: inspirational, qualitative, time-bound — "deliver a world-class onboarding experience." Key results: quantitative, measurable, specific — "achieve NPS of 60+ on 30-day onboarding survey." 3-5 KRs per objective. All key results must have a baseline and target. Avoid "binary" key results like "launch feature X" — use outcome-based KRs like "achieve 20% adoption of feature X in first month"), Alignment Review Process (after all teams draft OKRs: horizontal alignment — no conflicting or overlapping KRs between teams; vertical alignment — does each team's OKRs clearly support company OKRs; dependency mapping — what each team needs from others. Alignment meeting: 2-hour session with all department heads presenting OKRs and identifying cross-team dependencies), OKR Tracking Cadence (weekly check-in: 15 minutes per person — what did I accomplish, what's on track, what's blocked, what needs adjustment; monthly review: 1-hour team review — are key results progressing, do any need to be reprioritized or changed; quarterly close: retrospective — what worked, what didn't, final scoring, lessons for next quarter). OKR Scoring (0-1.0 scale: 0.7-1.0 = delivered; 0.4-0.6 = made progress but didn't fully deliver; 0.0-0.3 = missed. The target is NOT 1.0 — if you consistently hit 1.0, your OKRs aren't ambitious enough. Ideal score: 0.6-0.8).

**Expected output:** OKR cascading and alignment process with company → department → individual flow, drafting guidelines, alignment review, tracking cadence, and scoring
**Pro tip:** OKRs fail when they become a performance evaluation tool. OKRs are for alignment and ambition, not compensation. If you tie OKRs to bonuses, people will set easy OKRs they know they can hit. Keep OKRs aspirational and use a separate system for performance evaluation

### 4.21 Sales Playbook for New Product Launch
**Use case:** Create a sales playbook for launching a new product to existing customers and prospects
**Best for:** Sales teams, revenue operations, product marketers

Act as a sales enablement strategist. I need to create a sales playbook for launching my new product. Answer these questions: what is the new product, who is the target buyer, what is the current product portfolio, and what is the launch timeline. Then create a complete sales playbook: Product Positioning (one-liner, key differentiators vs competitors, target customer profile, use cases by industry/role), Ideal Customer Profile (company size, industry, role, pain points that trigger the need, buying committee members), Messaging by Audience (CEO: revenue impact, competitive advantage, ROI; Department Head: efficiency, team productivity, specific feature benefits; End User: ease of use, time savings, how it makes their job better), Sales Process Steps (outreach: who to contact, what to say in email/LinkedIn/phone — templates for each; discovery: key questions to qualify — budget, authority, need, timeline; demo: key features to show by pain point, competitive comparison points, objection handling; proposal: pricing structure, discount authority, proposal template; close: common objections and rebuttals, urgency triggers, negotiation boundaries), Competitive Battle Card (top 3 competitors: their positioning, weaknesses in areas where you excel, how to handle comparisons, specific responses to "why should I choose you over X"), Pricing and Packaging (pricing tiers, discount limits by role, annual vs monthly positioning, bundling options, contract terms), Common Objections and Responses (objection 1: "too expensive" — response: value comparison, ROI calculation, case study; objection 2: "happy with current solution" — response: identify gaps in current solution, share unique differentiators, offer proof; objection 3: "not the right time" — response: cost of delay, limited-time offer, pilot option), and Sales Tools and Resources (demo scripts, proposal templates, case studies, technical documentation, competitor comparisons, ROI calculator, FAQ). Include a "first 30 days" plan for ramping new sales reps on this product.

**Expected output:** A complete sales playbook with positioning, ICP, messaging, process, battle cards, and objection handling
**Pro tip:** The best sales playbooks are living documents, not one-time projects. Update them quarterly with real objections and responses from your sales team. The most effective playbook entries come from actual sales calls, not marketing assumptions

### 4.22 Sales Territory Planning and Optimization
**Use case:** Design and optimize sales territories for balanced coverage and maximum revenue
**Best for:** Sales leaders, revenue operations

Act as a sales operations consultant specializing in territory design. I need to design or optimize sales territories for my team. Answer these questions: how many sales reps do you have, what geographic regions or market segments do you cover, what is your current revenue by territory/segment, and what is your sales model (field, inside, hybrid). Then create an optimized territory plan: Territory Design Principles (balance — each territory has equal revenue potential; accessibility — territories are geographically logical for travel; minimal overlap — clear boundaries between territories; account ownership — one rep owns the relationship per account; growth potential — each territory has room to grow), Data Requirements for Mapping (current customer concentration by region/segment, lead/opportunity density, market size by territory, travel time/cost between accounts, existing rep-customer relationships, industry vertical concentrations), Territory Assignment Methods (geographic — by region, time zone, or metro area; segment — by company size, industry, or vertical; account-based — named accounts for enterprise; hybrid — geographic + vertical overlay; algorithmic — use data to maximize coverage and minimize travel). For each method: pros, cons, and when to use), Optimization Metrics (territory potential vs quota balance — max 15% variance between best and worst territory; coverage ratio — accounts per rep should be achievable; travel efficiency — optimize route density; revenue per rep — benchmark against industry averages), Territory Quota Setting (bottom-up: sum of account potential in each territory; top-down: company revenue target split by territory potential; market-based: territory market share × growth rate. Use a blend of all three approaches), Account Assignment Protocol (new leads: auto-assign by territory; account moves: when companies change location or segment; account splits: when accounts grow beyond territory capacity; reassignment process: annual review with documented change management), and Annual Territory Review Process (data collection — revenue, activity, coverage by territory; rep feedback — what's working, what's not; adjustment recommendations; approval and communication timeline; territory transition plan for affected accounts). Include a territory health scorecard: coverage, balance, potential vs attainment, rep satisfaction.

**Expected output:** A territory plan with design principles, data requirements, assignment method, optimization metrics, and review process
**Pro tip:** The most common territory mistake is over-allocating to existing revenue and under-allocating to growth potential. A territory with $1M in current revenue but limited growth potential is worth less than a territory with $200K in current revenue in a high-growth market. Design for future potential, not just current revenue

### 4.23 Sales Meeting Preparation Template
**Use case:** Prepare thoroughly for a sales meeting with research, strategy, and talking points
**Best for:** Sales reps, account executives, SDRs

Act as a sales effectiveness coach. I need to prepare for an upcoming sales meeting with a prospect. Answer these questions: what is the prospect's company name and industry, what is your product/service, what is the meeting type (discovery, demo, proposal, close), and what do you already know about the prospect. Then create a meeting preparation document: Pre-Meeting Research (company overview — size, revenue, funding, recent news, leadership changes, growth stage; technology stack — what tools they use, what they might need; their customer — who they serve, their value proposition; competitive landscape — who they compete with, what that means for needs; contact research — prospect's role, tenure, LinkedIn activity, content they share, mutual connections). Meeting Strategy (primary goal — what must happen for this meeting to be successful; secondary goals — ideal outcomes beyond the primary; must-ask questions — information gaps you need filled; must-learn — what you need to understand about their situation to qualify), Talking Points by Meeting Phase (opening — 2 minutes: thank them, state the agenda and time commitment, get agreement on outcomes; discovery — focus on asking vs telling: uncover their current situation, pain points, desired outcomes, decision process, budget, timeline, authority; presentation/pitch — connect features to THEIR specific needs, use their language, reference their industry; objection handling — anticipate 3 objections they're likely to raise, have responses ready; next steps — propose specific action with timeline and ownership, get commitment before leaving), Personal Preparation (your energy and mindset, what to listen for, what to avoid (talking too much, pitching too early, not qualifying), and how to handle common meeting derailments — late start, wrong attendees, technical issues, hostile prospect), and Post-Meeting Actions (what to document in CRM immediately, follow-up timeline and specific actions, internal notes for team, meeting debrief questions: what went well, what could improve, what did we learn). Include a one-page meeting prep sheet template that can be completed in 15 minutes.

**Expected output:** A complete meeting preparation document with research, strategy, talking points, personal prep, and post-meeting actions
**Pro tip:** The best sales reps spend 30 minutes preparing for every 60-minute meeting. Your preparation should focus more on what you'll ASK than what you'll SAY. The best discovery question format: "Tell me about a time when..." followed by silence. Let the prospect fill the space

### 4.24 Enterprise Sales Negotiation Framework
**Use case:** Negotiate enterprise deals with multiple stakeholders and complex requirements
**Best for:** Enterprise account executives, sales leaders

Act as an enterprise sales negotiation expert. I need to navigate a complex enterprise deal negotiation. Answer these questions: what is the deal value, who are the stakeholders involved, what is the prospect's budget range, what is your product/service, and what are the main sticking points. Then create a negotiation framework: Stakeholder Mapping (identify all decision influencers — economic buyer (controls budget), technical buyer (evaluates solution), user buyer (will use the product), champion (wants you to win), blocker (prefers competitor or status quo). For each: their priorities, concerns, authority level, and relationship to you), Negotiation Leverage Analysis (your strengths — unique capabilities, ROI evidence, social proof, time sensitivity, relationships; your weaknesses — feature gaps, long implementation, new company, limited references; their pressures — deadline, competitor evaluation, internal mandate, budget approval), Concession Strategy (what you can give: payment terms — monthly vs annual, net 30 vs net 60; scope — additional users, modules, services; pricing — discount %, but always in exchange for something; implementation — priority support, training, dedicated CSM; contract terms — duration, renewal terms, exit clauses. Never give a concession without getting something in return — "if I can do X, will you commit to Y?"), Common Enterprise Objections and Responses (objection: "your price is too high" — response: total cost of ownership comparison, value ROI, flexible payment options; objection: "we need to evaluate other options" — response: understand timeline, offer to help evaluate, share competitive comparison; objection: "we need legal to review" — response: provide pre-approved legal terms, offer to join legal call, share customer-standard agreements), BATNA (Best Alternative to a Negotiated Agreement) — what you'll do if this deal doesn't close. Your BATNA determines your walk-away point. If you have a strong pipeline, you can negotiate harder. If this is your only deal, you have less leverage. Know your walk-away before you start negotiating), Closing the Negotiation (when to stop negotiating — have the prospect confirm all terms verbally, send the contract with exactly what was agreed, set a deadline for signature, follow up persistently but professionally. "I think we've addressed everything — shall I send the contract over with these terms?"), and Post-Negotiation (document what was agreed and why, update CRM, start implementation prep, maintain relationship with all stakeholders, deliver on every commitment made during negotiation).

**Expected output:** An enterprise negotiation framework with stakeholder map, leverage analysis, concession strategy, objection responses, and closing approach
**Pro tip:** In enterprise sales, the person who speaks first about price loses. Let them name their budget range before you propose pricing. If they ask "what does it cost?" before you've built value, say "it varies based on scope — let me understand your needs first, then I'll put together a specific proposal"

### 4.25 Sales Demo Script and Framework
**Use case:** Deliver a structured, outcome-focused product demo that drives decisions
**Best for:** Sales reps, solutions consultants, account executives

Act as a sales demo coach and enablement specialist. I need to improve my product demo effectiveness. Answer these questions: what is your product, who is the typical demo audience (roles), what are the main use cases you demonstrate, and what is the current demo structure. Then create a demo framework: Pre-Demo Qualification (before the demo, confirm: who will attend and their roles, what specific problems they want solved, how long they have, what stage of evaluation they're in, what they've seen/done so far. Send a pre-demo questionnaire or have a 10-minute call to set up the demo for success), Demo Structure by Time (5% — context and agenda setting: restate their needs, what you'll show, what success looks like; 70% — targeted demonstration: show specific features that solve their specific problems, not a generic product tour; 15% — value summary and proof: recap how your solution addresses each need, share relevant case study; 10% — next steps: clear agreement on what happens next, timeline, ownership), Storytelling in Demos (use the "Before/After/Bridge" format: Before — describe their problem vividly, make them feel understood; After — paint the picture of how their life improves with your solution; Bridge — show how your product gets them from Before to After. Use customer stories instead of feature lists. "A customer similar to you had this problem... here's how they solved it with our platform"), Multi-Stakeholder Demo Strategy (when multiple roles attend: start with the executive view — dashboard, ROI, high-level value; then dive deep for technical users; then end with actionable insights for everyone. Ask each stakeholder what they specifically want to see at the start of the demo), Common Demo Mistakes (talking too much — demo should be 40% you showing, 60% them engaging; showing too many features — focus on what matters to them; technical difficulties — have a backup plan, offline demo environment, screenshots; reading slides — use the product, not a slide deck; not handling questions well — "great question, I'll address that in the next section" or "let me show you exactly that"), Objection Handling During Demo (when they raise an objection: acknowledge it, validate the concern, address it specifically, confirm resolution — don't dismiss or minimize), and Post-Demo Actions (send a personalized recap within 2 hours highlighting: what you showed that addresses their needs, next steps and timeline, answers to any open questions, relevant case study or ROI data, call-to-action for next meeting). Include a pre-demo checklist and a post-demo email template.

**Expected output:** A complete demo framework with structure, storytelling, stakeholder strategy, objection handling, and follow-up process
**Pro tip:** The best demos don't show the product — they show the prospect their future with the product. Every feature you demonstrate should be framed as an answer to a specific problem they described. If you haven't heard them say the problem, don't show the feature

### 4.26 Sales Pipeline Hygiene and Management
**Use case:** Clean, manage, and optimize the sales pipeline for accurate forecasting
**Best for:** Sales reps, sales managers, revenue operations

Act as a sales pipeline management consultant. I need to improve the health and accuracy of my sales pipeline. Answer these questions: what is your current pipeline value, how many deals are in each stage, what is your average deal size and sales cycle length, and what are your current win rates by stage. Then create a pipeline management system: Pipeline Stages and Definitions (each stage must have clear entry and exit criteria: 1 — Lead/Prospecting: initial contact made, interest expressed; 2 — Discovery: needs identified, budget confirmed, decision process understood; 3 — Demo/Presentation: solution presented, value demonstrated; 4 — Proposal: detailed proposal sent, pricing shared; 5 — Negotiation: terms being discussed, legal review; 6 — Closed Won/Lost. For each: typical duration, expected actions, documentation requirements), Pipeline Metrics to Track Weekly (total pipeline value — weighted by stage probability; deal count — pipe coverage ratio (total pipeline / quota target — target 3x); average deal age — how long deals sit in each stage (aging deals are stale); stage conversion rates — % of deals advancing from each stage to the next; win rate — by rep, by source, by segment; velocity — average days from creation to close), Pipeline Cleaning Criteria (deals to remove or reclassify: no contact in 30+ days — move to "nurture" or remove; no defined next step — requires immediate action or remove; not qualified — budget, authority, need, timeline criteria not met; ghosting — prospect stopped responding after 3 follow-ups; stalled at same stage for 3x normal duration; deal value unrealistic — drastically different from average), Pipeline Management Cadence (daily: update deal stages, log activities, schedule next actions; weekly: 30-minute pipeline review — add new deals, advance staged deals, clean stalled deals; monthly: 1-hour pipeline deep-dive — forecast accuracy review, stage-by-stage analysis, coaching opportunities; quarterly: pipeline strategy review — source analysis, rep coaching plan, market changes), Forecast Accuracy Improvement (commit vs forecast vs pipeline: commit = deals you're confident will close this period; forecast = best estimate of what will close; pipeline = every active deal. Track forecast accuracy: actual / forecast — target 90%+ confidence; identify patterns in forecast misses: overly optimistic timing, discount expectations, missing stakeholders), and CRM Hygiene Rules (update deal stage within 24 hours of change; log all calls and emails; complete all required fields; attach relevant documents; set next steps for every active deal). Include a weekly pipeline review meeting agenda template.

**Expected output:** A pipeline management system with stage definitions, metrics, cleaning criteria, cadence, and forecasting process
**Pro tip:** The health of your pipeline is more important than the size. A $500K pipeline with 30% stage-to-stage conversion is healthier than a $1M pipeline with 10% conversion. Track leading indicators — meeting-to-demo conversion and demo-to-proposal conversion — before they show up in revenue

### 4.27 Channel Partner Enablement Program
**Use case:** Design an enablement program for channel partners, resellers, or affiliates
**Best for:** Channel sales managers, partnership managers

Act as a channel sales and partner enablement specialist. I need to design an enablement program for my channel partners. Answer these questions: what is your product/service, what type of partners do you work with (resellers, affiliates, referrals, integrators), how many partners do you have, and what is your current partner program. Then create a complete partner enablement program: Partner Onboarding (week 1: partner agreement signed, welcome kit sent, portal access granted; week 2: product training — overview, positioning, competitive landscape; week 3: sales training — pitch, demo, objection handling, pricing; week 4: shadow sales calls, co-sell with your team; week 5: first deal support — your team helps close their first deal; month 2: certification and independent selling; quarter 1 review: performance check, coaching, tier assignment), Training Content Library (product demo videos — 5-10 minutes each, organized by use case; sales presentation deck with speaker notes; competitive comparison documents; objection handling playbook; pricing and packaging guide; case studies by industry; FAQ document; partner portal with all resources searchable), Sales and Marketing Support (co-branded marketing materials; joint webinar templates; email templates for outreach; social media content for partner promotion; event-in-a-box for partner-hosted events; MDF (Market Development Funds) guidelines — how partners can apply for funding support; lead sharing process — how leads are distributed and tracked; deal registration process — protect partner deals), Partner Incentives and Tiers (tier 1 — registered: basic training, standard commission, lead sharing; tier 2 — certified: advanced training, higher commission, MDF eligibility, dedicated partner manager; tier 3 — premier: all benefits plus co-selling support, executive sponsorship, joint marketing, early access to products. Each tier has clear requirements and benefits), Performance Management (metrics to track: pipeline generated, deals closed, revenue attributed, partner satisfaction score, certification completion rate; monthly partner business review: pipeline review, training needs, support needs; quarterly business review: performance against targets, growth plans, market feedback; annual partner summit: awards, strategy, networking), Communication Cadence (weekly: newsletter with product updates, sales tips, success stories; monthly: partner training webinar on specific topic; quarterly: all-hands partner call with leadership; annual: partner conference), and Partner Feedback Loop (quarterly partner survey, partner advisory board (select top partners), regular check-ins with partner managers, product feedback collection from partners selling your product). Include a partner portal requirements document.

**Expected output:** A complete partner enablement program with onboarding, training, support, incentives, tiers, and communication cadence
**Pro tip:** Partners don't fail because they don't want to sell your product — they fail because they don't know HOW. The most important enablement asset is a short, compelling demo script they can deliver in 10 minutes. If they can't demo your product simply, they won't sell it

### 4.28 Sales Activity Metrics and Goal Setting
**Use case:** Define sales activity metrics and set goals for SDRs, AEs, and sales managers
**Best for:** Sales leaders, revenue operations

Act as a sales performance consultant. I need to define the right activity metrics and goals for my sales team. Answer these questions: what roles are on your sales team (SDR, AE, AM, BDR), what is your sales process, what are your current conversion rates at each stage, and what is your revenue target. Then design a sales activity framework: Activity Metrics by Role (SDR/BDR: calls dialed (daily target), conversations had (qualified conversations), emails sent, LinkedIn touches, meetings booked, meetings held. AE: meetings held, demos delivered, proposals sent, follow-up actions completed, deals advanced stage. AM: account touches, upsell conversations, renewal conversations, QBRs completed, referrals requested. CSM: check-in calls, health checks, training sessions, escalation responses, NPS surveys), Conversion Rate Benchmarks (lead-to-meeting: 10-25% for outbound, 20-40% for inbound; meeting-to-demo: 60-80%; demo-to-proposal: 40-60%; proposal-to-close: 30-50% — vary by deal size and industry. Calculate YOUR conversion rates at each stage as the foundation for goal setting), Goal Setting Methodology (bottom-up: activity volume × conversion rates = output. If you need $1M in new revenue, average deal is $10K, close rate is 25%, you need 400 proposals. With a 50% demo-to-proposal rate, you need 800 demos. With a 70% meeting-to-demo rate, you need ~1,143 meetings. Then divide by number of reps to get individual targets. Top-down validation: do the activity targets require more hours than available? A rep has about 120 hours/month. If they need 80 prospecting calls AND 10 meetings AND 5 demos AND proposals AND CRM time, is that realistic?), Leading vs Lagging Metrics (leading indicators — activities that predict future revenue: meetings held, pipeline created, proposals sent, conversations had; lagging indicators — results that measure past performance: revenue closed, deals won, commissions earned. Track leading metrics daily/weekly and lagging metrics monthly/quarterly), Activity Quality vs Quantity (dialing 100 numbers with no research vs 30 targeted calls with personalization — quality wins. Define minimum quality standards: research before contact, personalize outreach, capture notes in CRM, follow up within agreed timeframe. Monitor conversion rates as quality indicators — if activity is up but conversion is down, quality is dropping), Coaching Based on Metrics (rep with high activity but low conversion: needs messaging and qualification coaching; rep with low activity but high conversion: needs pipeline generation coaching; rep with everything low: needs skill assessment and potentially role reassessment; rep hitting all metrics: time to stretch goals or mentor others). Include a weekly activity dashboard template showing: activity vs target, conversion rates trend, pipeline generated, and coaching flags.

**Expected output:** A sales activity framework with role-specific metrics, conversion benchmarks, goal calculation methodology, and coaching framework
**Pro tip:** Activity metrics without quality standards drive the wrong behavior. A rep who makes 100 calls with no personalization to get 2 meetings is worse than a rep who makes 30 well-researched calls to get 5 meetings. Measure outcomes (meetings, pipeline) more than inputs (calls, emails). Input metrics are for coaching; output metrics are for evaluation

### 4.29 Sales Coaching and Development Program
**Use case:** Design a structured sales coaching program to improve rep skills and performance
**Best for:** Sales managers, sales leaders

Act as a sales coaching and development specialist. I need to design a coaching program for my sales team. Answer these questions: how many reps do you manage, what are their experience levels (junior, mid, senior), what are the biggest skill gaps, and what coaching do you currently do. Then create a sales coaching program: Coaching Philosophy (coaching is about developing the person, not fixing the problem. The best coaches ask questions, don't give answers. "What could you have done differently?" vs "you should have done X." Coach the person, manage the deal. Focus on skills that transfer across deals, not specific deal fixes), Coaching Frequency and Structure (weekly 1:1: 30 minutes per rep — review pipeline, identify coaching opportunities, practice one skill. Monthly ride-along/call review: observe a real call or demo, debrief with structured feedback using "keep/stop/start" format. Quarterly skills assessment: evaluate each rep on core competencies: discovery, demo, objection handling, close, account management, pipeline management, negotiation), Core Competency Framework (define 7-8 core sales competencies with proficiency levels: Beginning — needs guidance, can execute with supervision; Developing — executes independently in familiar situations; Proficient — executes consistently across all situations; Advanced — coaches others, handles complex situations. For each competency (Discovery, Demo, Objection Handling, Negotiation, Closing, Pipeline Management, Account Planning, Communication), describe what each proficiency level looks like with specific behaviors), Coaching Methods (role-play: practice specific scenarios — objection handling, discovery questions, cold call opening; call shadowing and debrief: observe and provide structured feedback; deal review: walk through a specific deal together — what's working, what's blocking, what's the next move; skill drill: focused 10-minute practice on one micro-skill — tonality differentiator, power pause, assumptive close; peer coaching: pair reps to practice together and give each other feedback; self-assessment: have reps rate themselves before you coach — compare their self-assessment with your observation), Deal Coaching Framework (for each deal in pipeline: what stage is it, what do we know, what don't we know, what's the prospect's timeline, what are the biggest risks, what's the next action, what can the rep do differently, what support do they need from me), Coaching Documentation (track each coaching session: skill practiced, feedback given, rep's commitment to practice, follow-up date. Review coaching effectiveness: are reps improving on the competencies you're coaching on? Are deals progressing after coaching?), and Manager Coaching on Coaching (train your managers how to coach effectively: ask don't tell, praise in public coach in private, be specific, focus on behavior not personality, follow up on commitments, document progress). Include a coaching session template and coaching log.

**Expected output:** A complete sales coaching program with philosophy, cadence, competency framework, methods, deal coaching, and documentation
**Pro tip:** The most effective sales coaching happens in the moment, not in the quarterly review. Listen to a live call with your rep, text them one specific observation during the call ("great discovery question — now ask about budget"), and debrief for 5 minutes after. Micro-coaching between calls compounds faster than weekly coaching sessions

### 4.30 Price Increase Communication Strategy
**Use case:** Communicate a price increase to existing customers while minimizing churn
**Best for:** Sales leaders, customer success, founders

Act as a customer retention and pricing communication specialist. I need to communicate a price increase to my existing customers. Answer these questions: what is your product, what is the current pricing, what is the new pricing, how much is the increase (percentage and dollar amount), and when will it take effect. Then create a complete price increase communication strategy: Customer Segmentation for Notification (tier 1 — VIP customers (high revenue, long tenure): personalized call from account manager or executive before any written communication. Tier 2 — standard customers: personalized email from account manager with 60+ days notice. Tier 3 — self-serve/low-touch: email notification with 30-45 days notice. Tier 4 — at-risk customers (recent churn signals, low usage): special handling — consider grandfathering or discount to retain), Messaging Framework (acknowledge the change upfront — don't bury it; explain WHY — value added since original pricing: new features, improved support, increased costs, market positioning; focus on VALUE delivered, not cost increase: "We've added X new features since you joined, and we're committed to continuing to invest in improving your experience"; be transparent about the amount and timing; offer a way to lock in current pricing: annual prepay, multi-year commitment, grandfather period), Communication Template (subject line: "An important update about your [product] plan"; opening: thank them for their partnership; context: what's changed since their original pricing — new features, improved service, team growth; the change: new pricing, when it takes effect (specific date), how it affects their account (new monthly/annual price); lock-in option: available to maintain current price; what's NOT changing: core commitment, service quality, support; closing: reiterate value, provide contact for questions, thank them), Grandfathering and Transition Options (option 1: lock in current price for 12 months by switching to annual billing; option 2: current price frozen for 6 months; option 3: new price takes effect at next renewal only; option 4: for at-risk customers — maintain current price permanently or with smaller increase), Risk Mitigation (identify customers most likely to churn: low usage + price sensitive + competitor options available. Preemptive outreach: call these customers before the increase notice, discuss their usage, offer help getting more value, introduce any loyalty programs or hardship accommodations), Customer Objection Handling (objection: "the price is too high now" — response: value recap, usage review, help them get more value; objection: "we'll need to evaluate other options" — response: understand concerns, offer to help evaluate, highlight switching costs; objection: "this feels unfair" — response: acknowledge the feeling, explain the reasons, offer a transition option; objection: "we can't afford it" — response: explore downgrade options, hardship accommodation, extended timeline), Communication Timeline (T-60 days: VIP customers contacted personally; T-45 days: standard customers emailed; T-30 days: all customers notified with full details; T-14 days: reminder for customers who haven't acknowledged; T-0: new pricing takes effect, any lock-in options expire; T+30 days: follow-up with customers who transitioned, check satisfaction). Include a customer-facing FAQ document addressing the most likely questions.

**Expected output:** A price increase communication strategy with customer segmentation, messaging framework, transition options, risk mitigation, and timeline
**Pro tip:** The most important rule of price increases: give more notice than you think you need. A 60-90 day notice period allows customers to plan, reduces the feeling of being ambushed, and gives you time to address concerns before they escalate. Customers who feel respected during a price increase are more likely to stay

### 4.31 Sales Referral Program Design
**Use case:** Design a customer referral program that generates qualified leads
**Best for:** Sales leaders, customer success, founders

Act as a sales referral and advocacy specialist. I need to design a referral program that generates qualified leads from existing customers. Answer these questions: what is your product, who is your ideal customer, what is your NPS/customer satisfaction level, and what incentive do you currently offer for referrals (if any). Then create a complete referral program: Referral Program Structure (incentive type: dual-sided — reward both referrer and referee (proven to be 3x more effective than single-sided). Referrer reward: cash, account credit, gift card, charitable donation, or product discount. Referee reward: discount off first purchase, free month, upgraded features, free onboarding. Determine the reward value: typically 10-20% of referral's first-year value or 1 month of their subscription), Referral Ask Timing (when to ask: after a success moment — positive support interaction, feature adoption milestone, NPS promoter response, renewal/upgrade, positive social mention. Never ask during onboarding or immediately after a problem. Best timing: 30-90 days after they've experienced core value), Referral Process (customer receives referral link or unique code; customer shares with their network; prospect clicks link, sees personalized landing page; prospect signs up or requests demo; referral is tracked in CRM; when prospect becomes customer, both sides get reward; reward delivered automatically or with simple claim process), Referral Enablement Materials (email templates: "I'm loving [product] and thought you might find it useful — here's a special link"; social sharing: pre-written posts for LinkedIn, Twitter, email; in-app prompts: "Love us? Share the love and earn rewards"; one-pager: what their friend gets, what they get, how it works; video testimonial: short clip of customer explaining why they refer), Tracking and Attribution (unique referral links per customer; cookie duration — typically 30-90 days for attribution; automatic tracking via referral software; manual approval process for exceptions; dispute resolution process if two customers claim the same referral), Program Rules (eligibility: all active customers in good standing; reward limits: monthly cap to prevent abuse; fraud prevention: same IP, same company, self-referral detection; reward delivery timeline: within 30 days of referee's first payment; tax implications: inform customers if referrals are taxable income), and Performance Optimization (metrics to track: referral rate (% of customers who refer), referral conversion rate, average referral value, cost per referral, referral program ROI; A/B test ideas: reward amount ($50 vs $100), reward type (cash vs credit vs gift card), ask timing (post-purchase vs post-support vs NPS survey), ask channel (email vs in-app vs SMS); optimization tips: top referrers get VIP treatment — dedicated support, early access, public recognition). Include a sample referral email sequence for referred prospects.

**Expected output:** A complete referral program with structure, timing, process, materials, tracking, rules, and optimization framework
**Pro tip:** The best referral programs make it EFFORTLESS for customers to refer. Pre-written messages, one-click sharing, and automatic reward delivery remove friction. Every additional click in the referral process reduces participation by 50%. Test your own referral flow as a customer and count the clicks

### 4.32 Sales Discovery Question Bank
**Use case:** Master the art of sales discovery with proven question frameworks
**Best for:** SDRs, AEs, sales professionals

Act as a sales discovery expert and training coach. I need a comprehensive question bank organized by discovery objective. For each category below, provide 5-10 specific questions with an explanation of what each question reveals and when to use it. Answer these questions about your context: what is your product/service, who is your typical buyer, and what is your average deal size.
