CATEGORY 1 — Current State and Pain Discovery (understand what they're doing now and why it's not working):
- "Walk me through how you're currently handling [process]. What's working and what's not?"
- "What's the biggest frustration you have with your current solution?"
- "If you could wave a magic wand and change one thing about [current process], what would it be?"
- "How much time/money is the current approach costing you?"
- "What have you tried to fix this that didn't work?"
- "What would happen if you didn't solve this problem — what's the cost of inaction?"
- "How does this issue affect other parts of your business?"
- "Who else is impacted by this problem?"

CATEGORY 2 — Desired Outcome and Vision (understand what they want instead):
- "If this project is successful, what does that look like six months from now?"
- "What specific outcomes are you hoping to achieve with a new solution?"
- "How would you measure success for this initiative?"
- "What would need to be different for you to consider this project a home run?"
- "What's the ideal timeline for achieving these results?"
- "What would be a realistic and a stretch goal for this project?"
- "How does achieving this outcome support your broader business goals?"

CATEGORY 3 — Decision Process and Authority (understand how decisions get made):
- "Besides yourself, who else will be involved in evaluating solutions?"
- "What's your process for evaluating and selecting a vendor?"
- "Have you gone through a similar evaluation before? What did you learn?"
- "What criteria will you use to make the final decision?"
- "What timeline are you working with for making a decision?"
- "Who has the final sign-off on this budget?"
- "What would need to happen for this to become a priority?"

CATEGORY 4 — Budget and Resources (understand financial constraints):
- "Have you set aside a budget for this initiative?"
- "What range are you expecting for a solution like this?"
- "How do you typically budget for projects like this?"
- "What's the ROI threshold you need to justify the investment?"
- "What other priorities are competing for the same budget?"
- "If this solution delivered [specific value], would that justify the investment?"
- "How does budget approval work in your organization?"

CATEGORY 5 — Competition and Alternatives (understand what they're comparing you to):
- "What other solutions are you considering?"
- "What do you like about the other options you're looking at?"
- "What's not working or missing from those alternatives?"
- "What would make you choose one solution over another?"
- "What's your experience been with other vendors in this space?"
- "What do you wish your current solution did better?"
- "If you decided not to move forward with any solution, what would you do instead?"

CATEGORY 6 — Urgency and Timeline (understand how important this is now):
- "What prompted you to start looking now?"
- "What's the cost of delaying this decision?"
- "What's your timeline for implementing a solution?"
- "Is there a specific deadline driving this?"
- "What milestones need to happen before you can make a decision?"
- "What could accelerate or delay this timeline?"
- "How has the urgency of this changed over the past few months?"

For each question, provide: what the question uncovers (the insight it generates), when to ask it (early discovery vs late discovery vs qualification), and what to listen for in the answer (green flags, red flags, follow-up opportunities).

**Expected output:** A comprehensive discovery question bank with 40+ questions organized by 6 categories, each with insight, timing, and listening guidance
**Pro tip:** The best discovery question is one where you have NO idea what the answer will be. If you're asking questions where you already know the answer and are just waiting to launch into your pitch, you're not doing discovery — you're performing. Real discovery means being genuinely curious and letting the prospect's answers shape your approach

### 4.33 Sales Contract and Legal Review Checklist
**Use case:** Navigate the contract and legal review phase of the sales process efficiently
**Best for:** AEs, sales leaders, legal teams

Act as a sales contract specialist. I need to manage the legal review phase of my sales process more effectively. Answer these questions: what is your standard contract, what are the most common legal objections, and who is your legal contact. Then create a sales contract management guide: Pre-Contract Checklist (before sending contract: verbal agreement on all key terms confirmed — price, scope, duration, payment terms; internal approval obtained if discounting; all prospect information correct — company name, billing address, contacts; required attachments included — SOW, SLA, DPA, terms; correct contract template selected based on deal type), Common Contract Terms and Negotiation Levers (Term: 12 months standard, month-to-month premium, annual prepay discount; Auto-renewal: standard, remove only at higher price; Pricing: fix for term, annual increase cap, volume discounts; Payment Terms: net 30 standard, net 60 at discretion, upfront discount incentive; SLA: standard uptime guarantee, premium SLA at additional cost; Data Processing: standard DPA, custom DPA for enterprise; Termination for Cause: standard 30-day cure period; Termination for Convenience: not standard, only for enterprise at premium; Limitation of Liability: standard cap = fees paid in 12 months, negotiate to 2x or unlimited for data breaches; Indemnification: mutual standard, customer indemnification only for IP; Non-solicitation: standard, typically 12 months post-termination; Confidentiality: standard, 2-5 year term; IP ownership: customer owns their data, we own our IP; Professional services: standard T&M, fixed price at premium), Legal Objection Handling Playbook (objection: "our legal team needs to review standard terms" — response: provide redlined version of your standard changes (pre-approved legal changes), offer to join legal call; objection: "we need unlimited liability" — response: explain standard market practice, offer compromise like 2x fees or carve-outs for data/confidentiality; objection: "we need a shorter term" — response: offer month-to-month at 15% premium or annual with 30-day out clause; objection: "we need more favorable payment terms" — response: offer net 60 for annual prepay or net 45 for standard; objection: "our security team needs a full review" — response: provide SOC2 report, security questionnaire, DPA, offer security call), Contract Approval Workflow (deal value thresholds: under $10K — rep discretion with manager notification; $10K-$50K — manager approval; $50K-$250K — VP sales approval; over $250K — executive approval. Discount levels: standard discount up to 10% — rep discretion; 10-20% — manager approval; 20-30% — VP approval; over 30% — exec approval. Non-standard terms: any deviation from standard contract requires legal review. Approval turnaround targets: standard: 24 hours; non-standard: 48-72 hours; complex: 1 week), and Post-Signing Contract Management (signed copy stored in CRM; contract key terms extracted: renewal date, price, discount approval, auto-renewal status; renewal reminder cadence: 90 days out — start renewal conversation; 60 days out — send renewal proposal; 30 days out — finalize terms; compliance check: are we delivering what's committed in the contract — SLA, features, support). Include a contract negotiation priority matrix: what's standard (don't give away), what's negotiable (trade for value), what's flexible (use to close).

**Expected output:** A contract management guide with pre-contract checklist, term negotiation levers, legal objection playbook, approval workflow, and post-signing management
**Pro tip:** The best way to speed up legal review is to provide your "standard changes" upfront. Most legal negotiations follow predictable patterns. Create a redlined version of your contract with the most commonly requested changes pre-accepted. This turns a 2-week legal review into a 2-day confirmation

### 4.34 Sales Training New Hire Ramp Plan
**Use case:** Design a structured ramp plan for new sales hires to reach productivity faster
**Best for:** Sales leaders, sales enablement, HR

Act as a sales enablement and training specialist. I need to design a ramp plan for new sales hires. Answer these questions: what is your sales role (SDR, AE, AM), what is your product complexity, what is your average ramp time to quota, and what training resources do you currently have. Then create a comprehensive new hire ramp plan: Pre-Start (before day 1 — send welcome package, schedule overview of first week, assign pre-reading: product documentation, competitor overview, company values, sales playbook, set up tools and accounts), Week 1 — Foundation (company overview: mission, values, culture, org structure; product training: product demo, key features, use cases, customer personas; market overview: industry, competitors, positioning; sales process overview: stages, tools, metrics, expectations; tools setup: CRM, comms, prospecting tools, documentation; shadow experienced reps on calls; daily check-ins with manager), Week 2-3 — Product Deep Dive (advanced product training: in-depth features, technical architecture, integration capabilities; competitive training: head-to-head comparisons, objection handling, battle cards; customer training: case study deep-dives, customer calls listening, customer journey understanding; demo practice: deliver demo to manager and peers, get feedback; certification: pass product knowledge assessment; continue shadowing), Week 4-5 — Sales Skills Training (discovery training: question frameworks, call structure, qualification criteria; demo training: outcome-based demo, stakeholder management; objection handling: common objections, responses, role-play; prospecting (for SDRs): outreach sequences, research techniques, multi-channel approach; pipeline management: CRM hygiene, stage management, forecasting; roleplay certification: pass full sales cycle simulation), Week 6-8 — First Deals with Support (begin working own leads/pipeline with manager supervision; co-sell with experienced rep on first 2-3 deals; manager reviews first proposals before sending; daily pipeline review with manager; weekly skill practice session; begin attending team meetings and contributing), Month 3-4 — Ramp to Independence (manage own territory/accounts with reduced supervision; weekly pipeline reviews shift to bi-weekly; begin handling objections independently; prepare for first QBR or account review; full execution responsibility; manager shifts from coaching to oversight), Month 5-6 — Full Ramp (carry full quota; quarterly business review with manager; identify ongoing development areas; begin mentoring newer team members; participate in team initiatives; evaluate ramp completion — consistent performance at 80%+ of quota), Training Completion Criteria (for each phase: specific skills demonstrated and assessments passed; manager sign-off required for each phase progression; clear criteria for "graduating" from ramp to full independence — e.g., passed product certification, delivered 5 qualified demos with coach sign-off, built 3x pipeline in territory). Include a 90-day check-in template with specific questions about what's working, what's missing, and what support they need.

**Expected output:** A comprehensive 6-month new hire ramp plan with weekly activities, training topics, certifications, and success criteria
**Pro tip:** Most ramp plans focus too much on PRODUCT training and not enough on PROCESS training. A new rep who knows your product inside out but doesn't know your sales process will fail. Spend equal time on "what to say" and "how to sell." The first deal a new rep closes should feel formulaic because they followed the process, not because they were naturally talented

### 4.35 Subscription Renewal and Retention Playbook
**Use case:** Systematically manage renewals and improve customer retention
**Best for:** Customer success, account management, sales

Act as a subscription retention specialist. I need to create a renewal and retention playbook for my recurring revenue business. Answer these questions: what is your product, what is your renewal cycle (monthly, annual, multi-year), what is your current renewal rate, and what is the typical renewal process. Then create a complete renewal playbook: Renewal Timeline (Day -90: start renewal conversation — reach out to customer, schedule renewal meeting, begin value assessment; Day -60: present renewal proposal — pricing for next term, any changes, upsell opportunities; Day -45: address objections and negotiate — handle any concerns, discuss multi-year options; Day -30: finalize terms — get verbal commitment, send contract; Day -15: follow up on outstanding items — legal, procurement, budget approval; Day -7: ensure contract signed before expiration; Day -0: renewal goes live, send welcome-back note, transition to next term), Customer Segmentation for Renewals (expansion candidates — high usage, high NPS, growing company: focus on upsell/cross-sell during renewal; retention candidates — moderate usage, neutral NPS: focus on value demonstration and risk reduction; at-risk candidates — declining usage, low NPS, competitor contact: focus on root cause, recovery plan, flexible terms; passive candidates — low engagement but paying: focus on engagement, understand why they're not using; churn candidates — no usage, negative sentiment: focus on win-back or graceful exit), Value Review Meeting Structure (before the meeting: review customer's usage data, support history, business outcomes achieved; meeting agenda: 5% — check-in and relationship; 20% — value delivered: metrics, outcomes, ROI since last review; 30% — challenges and opportunities: what's working, what's not; 20% — product roadmap: what's coming that benefits them; 15% — renewal discussion: next term, pricing, changes; 10% — next steps and action items), Churn Risk Mitigation Playbook (early warning signs to monitor: login frequency dropping below threshold, feature usage declining, support tickets increasing with negative sentiment, NPS detractor, no response to check-in emails, competitor mentioned in conversation, decision-maker leaving company, budget freezes in industry. For each risk: specific intervention — personalized outreach, executive sponsorship, discount, feature training, usage review, customer health plan), Renewal Negotiation Guidelines (retain vs replace: what's the customer worth vs what will it cost to keep them — don't over-discount to retain low-value unhappy customers; retention offers: discount 10-20% for annual commitment; offer additional services or training; flexible payment terms; pilot new features for free; early renewal incentive; "save" desk: who handles retention offers and their discount authority), and Lost Customer Analysis (when a customer churns: conduct exit interview — why did you leave, what could we have done, where are you going; document churn reason in CRM; categorize churn type: value not realized, competitor win, no longer need, budget loss, poor service, product unfit, usage declined; analyze churn patterns monthly: are there common themes, customer segments, or timing patterns?; action on insights: if a specific feature gap keeps causing churn, escalate to product; if onboarding is the issue, improve onboarding; if pricing is the issue, review pricing strategy). Include a "save" email and call script template for at-risk renewals.

**Expected output:** A renewal playbook with timeline, segmentation, value review, churn risk playbook, negotiation guidelines, and churn analysis
**Pro tip:** The best time to prevent churn is the day AFTER the customer signs, not the day BEFORE renewal starts. If your first renewal conversation is at Day -90 and the customer hasn't been engaged since onboarding, you've already lost them. Continuous value delivery throughout the term is the only reliable retention strategy

### 4.36 Upsell and Cross-Sell Strategy
**Use case:** Identify and execute upsell and cross-sell opportunities within existing accounts
**Best for:** Account managers, customer success, sales

Act as an expansion revenue specialist. I need to create an upsell and cross-sell strategy for my existing customer base. Answer these questions: what products/features do you have, what are your customer segments, what is your current expansion revenue percentage, and what triggers typically lead to upsells. Then create a complete expansion playbook: Expansion Opportunity Identification (usage-based triggers: feature adoption milestones reached, usage approaching plan limits, power user behavior detected, new team members added; time-based triggers: pre-renewal (90 days before), post-onboarding (30 days after), post-success (after positive milestone — case study, testimonial, NPS promoter), post-funding (company raises money, new leadership); account-based triggers: company growth (hiring, new offices), new use case discovered, department expansion beyond current scope, competitor churn at account), Product Tier Mapping (for each tier: features included, how to identify when a customer has outgrown their current tier — specific usage thresholds, feature needs, pain points; upgrade path: what to say, what to show, what proof to share. Example: "You've been on the Starter plan for 6 months and you're now using 8 out of 10 available seats. You mentioned needing advanced reporting. The Growth plan includes unlimited seats and custom reports."), Customer Segmentation for Expansion (high potential: high usage, high engagement, growing company, executive sponsor — proactive outreach; moderate potential: steady usage, stable company, good relationship — trigger-based outreach; low potential: low usage, low engagement, at-risk — focus on retention first, not expansion; new customers: focus on onboarding and time-to-value before expansion conversation — typically 90-day minimum before discussing upsell), Expansion Conversation Framework (qualify before you pitch: confirm they're getting value from current product, identify unmet needs or limitations, understand their growth trajectory, find the champion for expansion; discovery questions: "what features do you wish you had?", "what limitations are you hitting with your current plan?", "what would be possible if you had [specific capability]?", "where do you see your usage growing in the next 6 months?"; presentation: connect expansion to their stated needs, show specific value for their use case, share case studies of similar customers who upgraded; handle objections: "we're fine with what we have" — response: "I'm glad it's working — here's what customers who felt that way discovered when they upgraded..."), Cross-Sell Approach (identify complementary products: what other needs do your customers have that your other products solve; cross-sell timing: best after they've achieved value with current product, not during onboarding; bundle pricing: offer discount for bundling multiple products — makes both stickier; internal referral: introduce the customer to the other product's team; cross-sell proof: show how other customers combine your products for better results), and Expansion Metrics (NRR — Net Revenue Retention rate; expansion MRR — monthly recurring revenue from upgrades; upsell conversion rate — % of customers upsold; time-to-first expansion — how long before customers upgrade; expansion revenue per customer; product adoption score — leading indicator of expansion readiness; NPS of expansion customers vs non-expansion). Include a quarterly account review framework for identifying and tracking expansion opportunities.

**Expected output:** A complete upsell and cross-sell strategy with opportunity identification, product mapping, segmentation, conversation framework, and metrics
**Pro tip:** The best time to introduce an upsell is when the customer has just had a WIN with your product — they achieved a goal, solved a problem, or got a positive outcome. Their perceived value of your product is highest right after a success moment, making them most receptive to expanding their investment

### 4.37 Sales Commission and Compensation Plan Design
**Use case:** Design a sales compensation plan that motivates reps and aligns with business goals
**Best for:** Sales leaders, revenue operations, HR

Act as a sales compensation consultant. I need to design a commission and compensation plan for my sales team. Answer these questions: what roles are you compensating (SDR, AE, AM, CSM), what is your average deal size and sales cycle, what is the quota per rep, and what is the desired on-target earnings (OTE) for each role. Then design a compensation plan: Compensation Philosophy (pay for performance — variable comp tied to outcomes; market competitive — OTE at 50th-75th percentile for role; simple to understand — reps should be able to calculate commission in 30 seconds; aligned with company goals — what you pay for is what you get; uncapped upside — top performers should earn significantly more), Commission Structure Options (percentage of revenue: rep earns X% of closed revenue — simple, aligns with company; tiered: higher commission rate above quota — 10% at 100%, 15% at 120%+ — motivates overperformance; accelerators: rate increases as reps exceed quota — 100% = 10%, 125% = 12%, 150% = 15%; flat rate per deal: each closed deal pays fixed amount — simple, good for high-volume low-value; gross margin-based: commission based on deal profitability — prevents discounting; MBOs: management by objectives — bonus for specific behaviors (demo quality, pipeline generation, customer satisfaction). Recommend a blended approach for most roles), Quota Setting (top-down: company target divided by number of reps; bottom-up: sum of territory potential; market-based: territory market share targets; blended: use all three with equal weighting. Quota should be challenging but achievable: 60-70% of reps should hit quota in a normal quarter. Ramp quotas: new reps start at 50% of full quota, ramp to 100% over 3-6 months), SDR Compensation (base salary higher (60-70% of OTE) since role has less direct control over close; commission on qualified meetings booked or pipeline generated (not closed revenue); meeting qualified criteria: meeting held with qualified prospect (BANT), meeting attended; pipeline credit: 5-10% of first-year deal value when pipeline closes; SPIFFs for specific campaigns or focus products), AE Compensation (base salary lower (40-50% of OTE) since role has more control over outcomes; commission on closed revenue with accelerators; gate structure: 80% attainment pays no commission (ramp protection), 80-100% pays base rate, 100%+ pays accelerated rate; clawback: commission forfeited if deal churns within 90 days; overpayment recovery: if deal doesn't pay, commission reversed), AM Compensation (base salary moderate (50-60% of OTE); commission on retention (renewal value) and expansion (upsell/cross-sell); renewal commission: 2-5% of renewal value; expansion commission: higher rate like AE (10-15%); pool model: shared commission pool based on team retention rate — encourages collaboration), Plan Governance (plan period: annual with quarterly check-ins; mid-year adjustments only for material market changes; plan documentation: written plan provided to each rep with examples; dispute resolution: formal process for commission disputes; cap discussion: uncapped for most roles, but consider soft cap for windfall deals; plan communication: launch meeting, one-pager, Q&A session, manager training on explaining plan). Include a sample commission calculation example for each role.

**Expected output:** A complete sales compensation plan with philosophy, structure, quota setting, role-specific plans, and governance
**Pro tip:** The most common compensation plan mistake is complexity. If a rep can't calculate their commission in 30 seconds, the plan is too complex. The second most common mistake is changing the plan mid-year — nothing destroys motivation faster. Set the plan annually and only change it for the next year, regardless of performance

### 4.38 Sales Data and CRM Hygiene Standards
**Use case:** Establish data quality standards and CRM hygiene practices for the sales team
**Best for:** Revenue operations, sales leaders, CRM admins

Act as a sales operations specialist. I need to improve data quality and CRM hygiene in my organization. Answer these questions: what CRM do you use, what are the current data quality issues, who is responsible for data entry, and what are the consequences of poor data. Then create a CRM hygiene program: Data Quality Standards (for each entity define required fields and formats: Contacts — full name, title, email, phone, company, LinkedIn, source; Accounts — company name, domain, industry, size, revenue, location, phone; Opportunities — deal name, amount, stage, close date, owner, source, competitor, next step; Activities — type (call, email, meeting), date, notes required, outcome, follow-up scheduled. Required fields must be completed before save. Dropdown fields over free text to ensure consistency), Data Entry Rules (enter data within 24 hours of interaction; log all customer-facing activities — calls, emails, meetings; opportunity stage changed only when exit criteria are met; notes should be useful for anyone reading them — not just for the rep; attach relevant documents to opportunity record; update contact information when you discover changes; deactivate duplicates not delete (or follow merge process); archive old records per retention policy), Data Quality Monitoring (automated reports: records missing required fields, opportunities with no activity in 30+ days, leads not contacted in 7+ days, duplicate detection report, stage duration exceeding limits; manual audits: quarterly random sample audit (50 records) by sales ops; data quality scorecard per rep: % of records with complete data, % of activities logged, timeliness scores, duplicate rate; team dashboard: overall data health score, trends, worst offenders, improvement areas), Cleaning Cadence (daily: reps review and update their records; weekly: sales ops runs automated quality reports; monthly: data cleanup — merge duplicates, update stale records, archive old opportunities; quarterly: full audit — complete data review, process improvements, training updates; annual: major cleanup — data archive, field deprecation, process redesign), Training and Accountability (new hire training: CRM basics — required fields, process, expectations; quarterly refresher: common mistakes, new features, best practices; role-specific training: what each role needs to track and how; accountability: data quality score as part of performance review; consequences for repeated violations: coaching → written warning → formal improvement plan; rewards for excellent data hygiene: recognition, small bonus, or SPIFF for highest quality score), and Process Integration (build data entry into sales process — don't separate "doing sales" from "entering data"; use automation where possible: auto-log emails, auto-create contacts from email signatures, auto-enrich from LinkedIn; integrate with other tools: marketing automation imports leads, support tool logs tickets, billing tool logs payments; make CRM the system of record — all tools push data to CRM, CRM is source of truth for reporting). Include a CRM data quality scorecard template.

**Expected output:** A CRM hygiene program with data standards, entry rules, monitoring, cleaning cadence, training, and process integration
**Pro tip:** The best CRM adoption strategy is to make CRM the path of least resistance for reps. If entering data is EASIER than not entering data, adoption will follow. This means tight integrations with email, calendar, and dialer, pre-populated fields, and mobile-friendly interfaces. If CRM is a "chore," it will always be deprioritized. If it's a "tool that makes my job easier," it will be used

### 4.39 Sales Kickoff (SKO) Planning Guide
**Use case:** Plan and execute a high-impact Sales Kickoff event
**Best for:** Sales leaders, enablement, event planners

Act as a sales kickoff event strategist. I need to plan an SKO for my sales team. Answer these questions: how many attendees, how many days, what is the budget, and what are the key objectives for the event. Then create a complete SKO plan: Event Objectives (primary goals: align team on strategy for the year, launch new products/initiatives, build skills through training, celebrate past success, build team culture and connections. Priority rank these objectives to guide agenda design), Agenda Structure by Day (Day 1 — Welcome and Strategy: morning — keynote from CEO/CRO on company vision and strategy; afternoon — product roadmap and new launches, competitive update, market trends; evening — welcome reception and team building; Day 2 — Skills and Enablement: morning — breakout tracks by role (SDR, AE, AM, leadership) for role-specific training; afternoon — cross-functional sessions (product, marketing, customer success alignment); evening — awards dinner and celebration; Day 3 — Planning and Execution: morning — territory planning, goal setting, account mapping; afternoon — team meetings, pipeline review, Q&A with leadership; closing — call to action, motivation, team photo), Content Development Plan (for each session: owner (who presents), duration, format (presentation, workshop, panel, fireside chat), preparation timeline (script, slides, handouts, rehearsal dates), content requirements: speaker training, slide template, AV needs, printed materials, attendee pre-work. Create a content production calendar with milestones 8 weeks, 4 weeks, 2 weeks, and 1 week before SKO), Breakout Track Design (by role: SDR — prospecting techniques, messaging, qualification; AE — discovery, demo, negotiation, closing; AM — account planning, expansion, retention; Leadership — coaching, pipeline management, strategic selling. By skill level: new hires — fundamentals, product training; mid-level — advanced skills, complex deals; top performers — mentorship, leadership development), Logistics and Operations (venue: capacity for all sessions plus breakouts, AV requirements, F&B, accommodation; budget: venue, travel, accommodation, food, speaker fees, materials, AV, social events, contingencies; technology: event app, live polling, Q&A tool, presentation management; materials: agenda, speaker bios, session descriptions, maps, branded swag; accessibility: dietary restrictions, mobility needs, remote attendance option), Engagement and Energy Management (ice breakers and energizers between sessions; networking activities: structured mixers, interest-based groups, role-based tables; participation mechanics: live polls, Q&A, workshop exercises, competitions, leaderboards; energy management: avoid post-lunch slumps with interactive sessions, stand-up activities, shorter blocks; evening activities: optional social events, interest-based outings, downtime), and Post-SKO Follow-Up (immediate: same-day or next-day survey — what worked, what didn't, top takeaways; within 1 week: recording and slide repository, key decisions communicated, action items assigned; within 30 days: SKO impact survey — what have you applied from SKO, what's changed in your approach; within 90 days: evaluate SKO ROI — did behavior change, did performance improve, did we achieve objectives; lessons learned for next SKO). Include a sample SKO agenda template and post-event survey.

**Expected output:** A complete SKO plan with objectives, agenda, content, logistics, engagement strategy, and follow-up process
**Pro tip:** The most memorable SKO sessions are not the keynote presentations — they're the moments when peers teach each other. Replace 30% of speaker slots with peer-led sessions where your top performers share their techniques. Learning from a colleague who does the same job feels more relevant and actionable than learning from an external expert or executive

### 4.40 Strategic Account Planning Template
**Use case:** Create a strategic plan for growing your most important customer accounts
**Best for:** Account managers, enterprise AEs, CSMs

Act as a strategic account management consultant. I need to create a strategic account plan for my key customer. Answer these questions: what is the account name, what is the current relationship (products used, revenue, tenure), who are the key stakeholders, and what is the growth opportunity. Then create a complete account plan: Account Overview (company profile — revenue, employees, industry, growth stage, funding, recent news; our relationship — products used, contract value, renewal date, current relationship strength (1-10); their goals — stated business objectives for the year; their challenges — biggest obstacles to achieving those goals; competitive landscape — who else is competing for this account), Stakeholder Map (identify all decision makers and influencers: economic buyer — controls budget; technical buyer — evaluates solution; user buyer — uses the product; champion — wants us to succeed; blocker — prefers competitor or status quo. For each: name, title, relationship strength (1-10), what they care about, what they think of us, engagement plan to strengthen relationship), Value Delivered Assessment (qualitative review: specific outcomes the customer has achieved with our product; quantitative review: metrics — ROI, time saved, revenue impacted, efficiency gained; success stories: 2-3 specific examples of value delivered; gaps: where we're not delivering expected value; value gap action plan: how to address gaps and get back on track), Growth Opportunities (upsell: existing product — upgrade tier or add users; cross-sell: new product they don't currently use; expansion: new department, new use case, new geography; partnership: joint go-to-market, co-selling, referral; advocacy: case study, testimonial, reference call, speaking engagement. For each opportunity: expected value ($), timeline (quarter), likelihood (low/med/high), next action, owner), Relationship Development Plan (engagement cadence: weekly: informal check-in; monthly: business review; quarterly: QBR — formal value review; annually: strategic planning session; executive sponsorship: assign exec sponsor, schedule annual exec-to-exec meeting; advocacy cultivation: identify potential advocates, nurture relationship, ask for specific support at right time; risk mitigation: identify any relationship risks — dissatisfaction, competitor engagement, personnel changes — and mitigation actions), 90-Day Action Plan (specific actions for next quarter: meetings to schedule, stakeholders to meet, proposals to prepare, value to demonstrate, issues to resolve. For each action: owner, timeframe, expected outcome, success measure), and Quarterly Review Process (what to review each quarter: progress against plan, new opportunities identified, risks emerged, stakeholder changes, competitive activity; quarterly review meeting with internal team — share learnings, adjust strategy, update plan; quarterly check-in with customer — formal QBR with value review and roadmap sharing). Include an account plan template that can be completed in 2 hours.

**Expected output:** A complete strategic account plan with overview, stakeholder map, value assessment, growth opportunities, development plan, and 90-day actions
**Pro tip:** The most valuable section of any account plan is the stakeholder map. In complex enterprise accounts, relationships with 3+ stakeholders across different functions is the strongest predictor of account growth and retention. A single relationship with one champion is fragile — three relationships across different roles is resilient. Spend 30% of your account time on relationship expansion, not just deal chasing

### 2.61 Newsletter Lead Magnet Upgrade
**Use case:** Convert one-time subscribers into engaged newsletter readers
**Best for:** Newsletter writers, email marketers
Act as a newsletter growth strategist. I need to create a lead magnet upgrade sequence that converts new subscribers into loyal readers. First, tell me your newsletter topic, who it's for, and what freebie you currently offer. Then design a 7-email welcome sequence that delivers: the lead magnet in digestible chunks, previews of the type of content you regularly send, a "best of" highlight from your archives, a personal story about why you started the newsletter, a request to whitelist your email address, a survey about what they want to learn, and a clear CTA to engage (reply, share, check a link). Each email should build anticipation for the next, create a reading habit, and make the subscriber feel special. Include subject line strategies for each email that balance open rates with click-through rates.
**Expected output:** A 7-email welcome sequence with subject lines, body copy, and engagement goals per email
**Pro tip:** Your welcome sequence is the highest-engagement moment you'll ever have with a subscriber — open rates of 50-70% are normal. Don't waste it on just a "thanks for subscribing" email. Deliver value immediately and establish what they can expect from you

### 2.62 B2B Case Study Interview Transcript
**Use case:** Conduct and transcribe a customer interview structured for case study writing
**Best for:** B2B content marketers, case study writers
Act as a B2B case study interviewer. I need to conduct a 30-minute customer interview and organize the transcript for case study writing. Provide me with a structured interview guide: opening (2 min) — thank them, explain the process, set expectations, get verbal consent; background (5 min) — what was their role, what was the situation before using our product, what were the specific challenges, what had they tried before; decision (5 min) — why did they choose our solution, what was the deciding factor, any skepticism; implementation (5 min) — how did the rollout go, what was easy/hard, how long to see results; results (8 min) — specific metrics before vs after, the biggest win, unexpected benefits, team feedback, what would they say to someone considering us; future (3 min) — how has their process changed, would they go back, what's next; wrap-up (2 min) — any final thoughts, thank them, next steps. After the guide, provide a sample transcript excerpt showing how to capture verbatim quotes, mark emotional moments, and note which sections are "golden quote" candidates.
**Expected output:** A structured 30-minute interview guide with timing and a sample transcript excerpt with quote-marking notation
**Pro tip:** The best case study quotes come from asking "what did that feel like?" not just "what happened?" Emotional language creates connection — "it was such a relief to finally have a system that worked" is more powerful than "the system improved our efficiency by 40%"

### 2.63 Content Strategy Pillar Framework
**Use case:** Build a content strategy organized around pillar topics and supporting clusters
**Best for:** Content strategists, SEO managers, editors
Act as a content strategy consultant. I need to build a pillar-cluster content strategy. Tell me your business, target audience, and primary topic areas. Then design a content pillar framework: identify 3-5 pillar topics (broad, high-value topics that define your expertise area — each pillar should be a category you want to be known for), for each pillar create 10-15 cluster topics (specific questions, subtopics, or keywords that support the pillar), content format recommendations for each cluster (long-form guide, listicle, video, infographic, podcast episode, tool/template, case study, FAQ), internal linking structure (how cluster content links to the pillar page and to each other), content funnel mapping (which clusters serve top-of-funnel awareness vs middle-of-funnel consideration vs bottom-of-funnel decision), publishing cadence (which clusters to tackle first based on search volume and competition), and measurement plan (pillar page authority growth, cluster content traffic, keyword rankings, topic influence score). Each pillar should have a definitive "ultimate guide" pillar page that links out to all cluster content.
**Expected output:** A 3-5 pillar content strategy with 10-15 cluster topics each, format recommendations, linking structure, and publishing cadence
**Pro tip:** The pillar-cluster model works because Google rewards topical authority, not individual keyword rankings. A strong pillar page that covers a topic comprehensively signals to Google that you're an authority on that subject, which boosts rankings for ALL the cluster content too

### 2.64 Product Description Copywriter
**Use case:** Write compelling product descriptions that drive conversions
**Best for:** E-commerce marketers, copywriters, product page managers
Act as a conversion copywriter specializing in e-commerce product descriptions. I need to write a product description that sells. Tell me the product name, what it does, its key features, its price point, and who it's for. Then write a complete product description following these principles: headline — benefit-driven, includes the transformation (not just the product name); opening paragraph — paint a picture of life WITH this product (the aspirational state); features as benefits — for each feature, explain what it means for the customer (use "so you can..." or "which means..."); social proof — weave in testimonials or usage stats naturally; sensory language — for physical products, describe how it feels, looks, sounds, smells; objection handling — address common concerns (quality, durability, fit, value); guarantee/risk reversal — make the customer feel safe buying; CTA — action-oriented, specific ("Add to Cart — You Deserve This"). Also include: a short version (50 words for mobile), a bullet-point version (scannable), and an SEO version (keyword-optimized). For each version, explain when to use it and why it works.
**Expected output:** A complete product description in 3 versions (full, scannable, SEO) with psychological rationale for each section
**Pro tip:** The most powerful words in product descriptions are "you" and "your." Replace every instance of "our product has" with "you get" and watch conversion rates climb. Customers don't care about your features — they care about what those features do for them

### 2.65 Content Republishing and Refresh Strategy
**Use case:** Identify and update old content to extend its lifecycle and improve rankings
**Best for:** SEO managers, content editors, bloggers
Act as a content lifecycle optimization specialist. I need a strategy for republishing and refreshing my existing content. Tell me about your content library — how many pieces, what topics, how old is the oldest content. Then create a content refresh system: content audit criteria (find content that: performed well historically but has declined, ranks on page 2 of Google (positions 7-15), is outdated (stats, screenshots, references), has broken links or outdated information, has high traffic potential but low current traffic, targets keywords that have changed intent); refresh approaches by content type (statistical update — refresh numbers, dates, and references with current data; comprehensive overhaul — rewrite 50%+ of content, add new sections, update examples; consolidation — merge 3-5 thin posts into one comprehensive guide; format change — convert text post into video, infographic, or interactive tool; repositioning — update title, meta description, and angle for new keyword or intent; deletion — remove or redirect content that can't be salvaged); republishing workflow (update content → change publish date → add "updated [date]" notice → promote as new content (email, social, newsletter) → update internal links → monitor ranking changes); promotion strategy for refreshed content (treat it as a new piece: email to subscribers, share on social media, pitch to link-building targets, update internal links); measurement (track: ranking improvement, traffic increase from refresh, engagement metrics before vs after, backlinks gained from refreshed content).
**Expected output:** A content refresh strategy with audit criteria, refresh approaches, republishing workflow, promotion plan, and measurement
**Pro tip:** The content that benefits most from a refresh is the content that ALMOST ranks on page 1. Moving from position 8 to position 3 can increase traffic 10x. Focus your refresh efforts on content in positions 5-15 that has a clear path to improvement — not content ranking on page 5 that needs a complete rewrite

### 2.66 Storytelling Framework for Business Content
**Use case:** Apply narrative storytelling techniques to make business content more engaging
**Best for:** Content creators, marketers, founders
Act as a storytelling coach for business professionals. I need to make my content more engaging through storytelling. Tell me what type of content you're writing (blog post, social post, email, presentation, video). Then teach me how to apply these storytelling frameworks: The Hero's Journey in business content — customer starts in ordinary world, faces a challenge, meets a guide (your brand), gains new abilities, transforms, returns with success; The Before/After/Bridge — describe the painful before, paint the aspirational after, show your product/service as the bridge; The Mountain Structure — rising tension as problems compound, climax at the breaking point, resolution with the solution; The Frame Shift — start with a common belief, share a counterintuitive insight, provide evidence that shifts their perspective; The Personal Anecdote — start with a specific moment, extract a universal lesson, connect to the reader's situation. For each framework, provide: when to use it, a template structure with placeholder text, a real-world example from business content, and common mistakes to avoid. Then rewrite a sample piece of my content using the most appropriate framework.
**Expected output:** 5 storytelling frameworks with templates, examples, and a rewrite of your content using one framework
**Pro tip:** The most powerful storytelling technique in business content is SPECIFICITY. "I was sitting in a coffee shop on Divisadero Street when I got the email that changed everything" is infinitely more engaging than "I was checking my email one day." Specific details create mental imagery, which creates emotional engagement

### 2.67 Podcast Show Notes and SEO Optimization
**Use case:** Write podcast show notes that rank in search and drive listens
**Best for:** Podcasters, content marketers, SEO specialists
Act as a podcast SEO and show notes specialist. I need to write show notes for my podcast episode that will rank in search and convince people to listen. Tell me your podcast name, episode title and guest, the key topics covered, and your target keywords. Then write complete show notes including: episode title (under 60 characters, includes primary keyword, creates curiosity); episode description (150-300 words, includes keyword naturally, summarizes what listeners will learn, includes guest credentials, has a clear hook); timestamps (mark 5-7 key moments with time and compelling description for each — "12:34 — The exact script [guest] uses to handle pricing objections"); key takeaways (3-5 bullet points of actionable insights); guest bio (2-3 sentences with relevance to the topic and a link to their website/social); transcript excerpt (most compelling 300-500 word section as a sneak peek); resources and links mentioned (tools, books, articles with affiliate links where appropriate); CTA (subscribe, rate, review, share); metadata (tags, categories, SEO title, meta description). Also write promotional snippets: 3 social media posts (LinkedIn, Twitter, Instagram) with platform-optimized copy, and a 3-email email sequence for your newsletter (announcement, highlight, final call).
**Expected output:** Complete podcast show notes with SEO optimization, timestamps, key takeaways, transcript excerpt, and promotional snippets
**Pro tip:** Podcast show notes are consistently under-optimized for SEO. A well-optimized episode page can rank for the guest's name, the episode topic, and related questions — often with little competition. Include a full transcript (Google indexes every word) and you'll get more long-tail search traffic than your blog posts

### 2.68 Collaborative Writing Brief
**Use case:** Write a detailed brief for delegating content creation to a writer or agency
**Best for:** Content managers, editors, marketing teams
Act as a content management specialist. I need to create a writing brief for a content piece I'm delegating. Tell me the topic, target audience, content type (blog, guide, case study, white paper), and your brand voice. Then create a comprehensive writing brief: content overview (topic, format, word count range, target audience description, primary goal of the piece — educate, convince, entertain, convert); SEO requirements (primary keyword, secondary keywords, search intent, target title (under 60 chars), meta description (under 160 chars), URL slug suggestion, internal linking targets, external linking requirements); angle and thesis (the single main argument or insight the piece should communicate, why this matters to the reader now, what makes this perspective unique or valuable); structural requirements (required sections/H2s, suggested H3s under each H2, what goes in the introduction and conclusion, recommended examples or data points); tone and style guidelines (brand voice description, do's and don'ts, example sentences in the target voice, preferred formatting (short paragraphs, subheadings, bullet points, bold for emphasis), required elements (quotes, statistics, expert opinions, original insights, visual suggestions); deliverables (first draft deadline, revision rounds included, final format (Google Doc, Word, Markdown), additional formats needed (social snippets, email summary, image captions); submission requirements (file naming convention, document sharing settings, how to submit questions, approval workflow). Include a completed sample brief for a hypothetical topic.
**Expected output:** A comprehensive writing brief template with all sections and a completed sample brief
**Pro tip:** A good writing brief is the difference between getting a draft you can publish as-is and getting a draft that needs a full rewrite. The most important section is the "angle and thesis" — if the writer doesn't know the ONE thing the piece needs to say, they can't write it effectively. Spend 20% of your brief writing time on this section alone

### 2.69 Content Distribution and Amplification Plan
**Use case:** Create a distribution plan that maximizes reach for every piece of content
**Best for:** Content marketers, editors, social media managers
Act as a content distribution strategist. I need a plan to maximize reach for my content piece. Tell me what the content is (topic, format, length), who it's for, and what channels you have. Then create a comprehensive distribution plan: owned channels (email newsletter — write 3 versions: teaser, full summary with link, key takeaway; social media — platform-specific posts (LinkedIn long-form, Twitter thread, Instagram carousel, Facebook community post); website — homepage feature, related posts sidebar, popup or banner; RSS — optimize feed for platforms like Feedly and Flipboard); earned channels (outreach to: journalists who cover this topic, bloggers who might link, podcasters who need guests, newsletter curators who share content, communities (Reddit, Hacker News, Slack groups, Facebook groups) where this would be valuable, content aggregators (Growth Hackers, Inbound.org, Medium publications); partner promotion — co-marketing with complementary brands, guest contributor republishing, cross-promotion with industry partners); paid channels (if budget allows: promoted social posts, content discovery (Outbrain, Taboola), newsletter sponsorships, retargeting to content visitors); repurposing strategy (from this one piece: create a Twitter thread, email summary, podcast script, LinkedIn carousel, infographic, video script, slide deck, executive summary, discussion questions, quote graphics); scheduling and cadence (launch day: publish and push to all owned channels; day 2-3: outreach to earned channels; day 5-7: repurpose and distribute new formats; day 14: re-share with new angle; month 1: refresh and re-promote; quarter 1: update and relaunch); measurement (track: traffic sources and conversion, engagement across channels, backlinks earned, email CTR, social shares, repurposed content performance, overall content ROI).
**Expected output:** A comprehensive content distribution plan with owned/earned/paid channels, repurposing strategy, scheduling, and measurement
**Pro tip:** Most content marketers spend 80% of their time creating content and 20% distributing it. The most successful content marketers flip that ratio. A great piece of content with great distribution will outperform a brilliant piece with no distribution every time. Schedule distribution time equal to creation time

### 2.70 FAQ Schema and Structured Data Writer
**Use case:** Write FAQ content optimized for Google's featured snippets and rich results
**Best for:** SEO specialists, content writers, webmasters
Act as an SEO technical content specialist. I need to write an FAQ section for my page that's optimized for Google's FAQ rich results and featured snippets. Tell me the page topic, the primary keyword, and the top questions your audience asks. Then create an FAQ section following these guidelines: question format (use natural language questions that match how people actually search — "how long does it take?" not "time duration"; each question should be a unique search query people use); answer format (answers should be 40-60 words (minimum 40 words for featured snippet eligibility), direct and concise (answer the question in the first sentence, then add supporting detail), include the target keyword naturally, use lists or tables where appropriate (Google prefers structured formats), cite sources for statistics or claims, be self-contained (someone reading just this answer should get complete value)); structured data markup (JSON-LD FAQ schema with mainEntity property, each question as a nested Question and Answer object, correct schema.org/FAQPage type); placement (FAQ section at the bottom of the page after main content, consider adding individual Q&As throughout the page as "question H2s" rather than a single FAQ block, which can also qualify for People Also Ask); content optimization (cover the "People Also Ask" questions that appear for your target keyword, include "not enough" questions — questions about limitations or problems that your product solves, group questions into categories of 3-5 for better user experience). Write 8-12 questions and answers for the target topic, formatted with structured data markup.
**Expected output:** An FAQ section with 8-12 Q&As optimized for featured snippets, plus JSON-LD structured data markup
**Pro tip:** FAQ schema can make your search result 2-3x larger with expandable questions, dramatically increasing click-through rate. However, Google may remove FAQ rich results for "most" pages in 2024+ — check current guidelines. Even without the rich result, well-written FAQs answer real search queries and improve user experience, which Google rewards indirectly

### 2.71 Video Thumbnail Script and Title Strategist
**Use case:** Write video titles and thumbnail text optimized for click-through rate
**Best for:** YouTubers, video creators, content marketers
Act as a YouTube growth strategist specializing in thumbnails and titles. I need to optimize the title and thumbnail for my video. Tell me the video topic, what format it is (tutorial, review, vlog, educational), and who it's for. Then generate: 10 title options using proven formats: "How to [achieve X] in [timeframe]" (how-to), "I tried [method] for [timeframe] and here's what happened" (experiment), "[Number] [adjective] ways to [goal]" (list), "Why [common belief] is [wrong/misleading]" (contrarian), "The [adjective] guide to [topic]" (comprehensive), "I [action] [specific thing] and [result]" (story), "[Celebrity/Expert]'s [framework/method] explained" (authority), "This [tool/strategy] changed how I [activity]" (transformation), "Don't [action] until you watch this" (pattern interrupt), "[Topic] in [year]: what you need to know" (timely). For each title: provide CTR prediction (low/medium/high), why it works (curiosity, benefit, urgency, specificity), and estimated search volume; thumbnail text options (3-5 short text overlays that pair with the title, each under 5 words, creates curiosity gap, uses emotional triggers — surprise, controversy, FOMO, aspiration); thumbnail visual description (what image pairs best with the title — close-up face with expression, before/after comparison, arrow or circle highlighting something, text overlay with contrasting color). Rank all options by expected CTR and provide a final recommendation.
**Expected output:** 10 optimized title options with CTR analysis, 3-5 thumbnail text overlays, and visual description recommendations
**Pro tip:** On YouTube, the thumbnail is more important than the title. 90% of best-performing videos use a thumbnail that shows a person's face with a distinct emotional expression (surprise, excitement, concern). The title should complement the thumbnail, not repeat it. If the thumbnail shows surprise, the title should explain WHY

### 2.72 Content Localization and Translation Brief
**Use case:** Prepare content for translation and localization into new markets
**Best for:** Content marketers, international growth managers, localization specialists
Act as a content localization strategist. I need to prepare my content for a new market. Tell me the content piece, the source language, the target language/market, and any cultural considerations. Then create a localization brief: content modifications needed (imagery — replace culturally specific images; examples — use local examples, case studies, and references; metrics — convert currencies, units, dates, and formats; idioms and metaphors — identify phrases that don't translate and suggest local alternatives; humor and tone — adapt humor style to local culture (self-deprecating works in UK, less in Germany); references — replace pop culture, historical, or local references with equivalents in target market); SEO adaptation (keyword research — target market keywords will differ from source; translate meta titles, descriptions, and URLs; H1 and H2 tags may need restructuring for different search intent; local search engines — Baidu for China, Yandex for Russia, Naver for Korea); legal and regulatory (GDPR for EU, local privacy laws, industry-specific regulations in target market, disclosure requirements); cultural sensitivity checklist (colors — red means luck in China, danger in US; symbols — check for offensive or inappropriate imagery; gestures and body language — what's acceptable varies; hierarchy and formality — formal vs informal address (tu vs vous, du vs Sie); gender considerations; religious and holiday awareness); quality assurance (translation review process: machine translation → human editor → subject matter expert review → native speaker final review); localization testing (in-market user testing, A/B test original vs localized content, monitor engagement metrics by market). Include a cultural considerations checklist for 5 major markets (US, UK, Germany, Japan, Brazil).
**Expected output:** A content localization brief with modification requirements, SEO adaptation, cultural checklist, QA process, and market-specific considerations
**Pro tip:** The most common localization mistake is direct translation that ignores CULTURAL context. "Saving money" resonates differently in Germany (frugality is a virtue) vs the US (saving = missing out). Your content's emotional hooks need to be adapted, not just the words. A literal translation preserves the words but loses the meaning

### 2.73 Interactive Content Concept Generator
**Use case:** Design interactive content experiences (quizzes, calculators, assessments) that drive engagement
**Best for:** Content marketers, growth marketers, product teams
Act as an interactive content strategist. I need to create interactive content that drives engagement and leads. Tell me your business, target audience, and marketing goals. Then generate 5 interactive content concepts ranked by expected engagement and lead generation potential. For each concept provide: type (quiz — "What [type] are you?"; assessment — "How mature is your [capability]?"; calculator — "How much could you save with [solution]?"; interactive guide — "Build your [plan/strategy] step by step"; tool/template — interactive worksheet, checklist, or planner); title and angle (specific, benefit-driven, curiosity-inducing); format description (how many questions/steps, what inputs, what outputs, how results are presented); lead capture integration (what info you ask before showing results — email, name, company size); personalization (how results are tailored based on user inputs — customized report, specific recommendations, benchmark comparison); social sharing mechanics (shareable results image or summary, comparison with others, challenge friends); lead qualification (how responses reveal user needs — question design as implicit qualification: what's their pain point, budget, timeline); value for user (what they learn about themselves, what specific insights they gain); implementation complexity (low — tool builder (Typeform, Interact), medium — custom landing page, high — custom development); promotion channels (where this type of interactive content performs best — social media, email, paid ads, embedded in blog posts). Calculate estimated engagement rate (time spent, completion rate, share rate) and lead capture rate for each concept.
**Expected output:** 5 interactive content concepts with full descriptions, lead capture strategy, social sharing mechanics, engagement estimates, and implementation complexity
**Pro tip:** Interactive content generates 2x more conversions than passive content because of the "endowment effect" — once someone invests time answering questions, they value the personalized results more. The key design principle: make the input process feel valuable (interesting questions, progress bar, insights along the way), not just a gate before the output

### 2.74 Content Performance Dashboard Design
**Use case:** Design a content analytics dashboard that tracks meaningful metrics
**Best for:** Content strategists, editors, marketing teams
Act as a content analytics consultant. I need to design a dashboard that measures what matters for my content. Tell me your content channels, your business goals, and how you currently measure. Then design a content performance dashboard with these views: Executive View (top-line metrics: total traffic, traffic value (estimated $ based on conversion rate × AOV), content-generated leads, content-generated revenue, content marketing ROI, trend lines for each); Channel Performance View (traffic and conversion by source: organic search, social, email, direct, referral, paid. Cost per channel if applicable. Channel comparison: which channels drive the most valuable traffic, not just the most traffic); Content Type Analysis (performance by format: blog posts, videos, podcasts, infographics, gated content, interactive. Compare: average traffic, engagement time, conversion rate, and lead quality per format); Topic Cluster Performance (traffic and conversions by pillar/cluster. Which topics are building authority (growing traffic over time) vs which are one-hit wonders); Funnel Performance (top-of-funnel content: traffic and new visitors; middle-of-funnel: email signups, content downloads, demo requests; bottom-of-funnel: trial signups, purchases, revenue attributed); Content Efficiency (cost per piece (creation + distribution), time per piece, traffic per piece, leads per piece, revenue per piece. Which content produces the best ROI); Content Decay Analysis (content losing traffic over time, content with declining rankings, content needing refresh. Prioritize by traffic decline × conversion potential). For each view: recommended chart type (line chart for trends, bar chart for comparison, table for detailed data), data source (Google Analytics, CRM, email platform, social tools), refresh frequency, and the decision each view supports. Include a sample dashboard layout description.
**Expected output:** A content analytics dashboard design with 7 views, each showing recommended metrics, visualizations, data sources, and decision support
**Pro tip:** The most valuable metric on a content dashboard is REVENUE ATTRIBUTED, but it's also the hardest to measure. If you can't do multi-touch attribution, start with last-touch (content that got the last click before conversion) and assisted-conversion (content that appeared anywhere in the conversion path). Even imperfect attribution is better than no attribution — it lets you compare content ROI to other channels on a level playing field

### 2.75 Guest Posting and Syndication Strategy
**Use case:** Build a guest posting strategy for backlinks, authority, and referral traffic
**Best for:** SEO specialists, content marketers, growth marketers
Act as a link building and guest posting strategist. I need a guest posting plan to build backlinks and authority. Tell me your website, target industry, competitors who guest post, and your content expertise areas. Then create a guest posting strategy: target publication criteria (domain authority 40+, relevant to your industry, accepts guest posts, has engaged audience, has existing backlinks to competitor content). Create a list of 20 target publications ranked by priority; pitch development (personalized outreach email: compliment a specific recent article, explain why your topic fits their audience, propose 3 specific article topics with working titles and 2-sentence summaries, include links to your best published work as writing samples); content creation for guest posts (follow each publication's guidelines exactly, write at the quality level of their best content (not the minimum acceptable), include original data, insights, or perspectives they can't get elsewhere, add 1-2 internal links to your content naturally, include a 2-sentence author bio with your target link); relationship building (connect with editors on LinkedIn, engage with their content before pitching, offer to promote your guest post to your audience, propose a recurring column or series after 2-3 successful guest posts, offer to introduce them to other experts for future content); content syndication strategy (after publishing on your site: syndicate to Medium, LinkedIn Articles, industry publications that accept republishing (with canonical tag pointing to your original). Not all syndication partners accept republished content — check their guidelines. Some publications want exclusive content — publish on their site first, then your site after 2-4 weeks); measurement (track: backlinks acquired, referral traffic, domain authority improvement, guest post conversion rate, relationships built, speaking or podcast invitations from guest post exposure). Include a pitch email template and a follow-up sequence (3 emails, 5-7 days apart).
**Expected output:** A guest posting strategy with 20 target publications, pitch development guide, relationship building plan, syndication strategy, and measurement framework
**Pro tip:** The #1 mistake in guest posting is pitching before building a relationship. Editors get hundreds of cold pitches. If you've engaged with their content for a month — commented thoughtfully, shared their posts, added value to their community — your pitch will be read instead of deleted. Build the relationship before you ask for the guest post

### 2.76 Content Marketing ROI Calculator Script
**Use case:** Calculate the ROI of content marketing efforts across channels and formats
**Best for:** Content marketers, CMOs, founders
Act as a content marketing finance analyst. I need to calculate the ROI of my content marketing program. Tell me your monthly content budget (or costs), your content outputs, and the revenue you attribute to content. Then create an ROI calculation framework: cost side (content creation: writers, designers, videographers, editors — hourly rates or per-piece costs × volume; content distribution: promoted posts, newsletter sponsorships, influencer partnerships; tools and technology: CMS, SEO tools, analytics, email platform, design tools; team salaries: full-time content team costs (salary + benefits + overhead); total monthly content investment = sum of all costs); return side (direct revenue: purchases directly attributed to content (last-click); assisted revenue: deals where content appeared anywhere in the conversion path; lead value: leads generated × lead-to-customer rate × average customer value; organic traffic value: traffic × conversion rate × AOV (estimate what you'd pay for this traffic via paid ads); brand lift: estimated value of increased brand searches, direct traffic, and branded mention growth (use survey or trend data); total monthly content return = sum of all returns); ROI calculation (simple ROI = (return - investment) / investment × 100; content marketing ROI = (attributed revenue - total cost) / total cost × 100; by-channel ROI: allocate costs and returns per channel to see which content channel performs best; payback period: how long for content-generated revenue to exceed content investment); benchmarking (compare your content ROI to industry benchmarks: average content marketing ROI is ~3-5x (300-500%). Top performers achieve 10x+ ROI. B2B content typically takes 3-6 months to show ROI, B2C 1-3 months); improvement levers (how to improve ROI: increase distribution of top-performing content, reduce spend on low-performing channels, repurpose high-performers into new formats, improve conversion paths from content to revenue, increase content efficiency (produce more with same resources)). Provide a sample calculation with realistic numbers for a B2B SaaS company.
**Expected output:** A content marketing ROI calculation framework with cost/return components, multiple calculation methods, benchmarks, and improvement levers
**Pro tip:** Content marketing ROI is notoriously hard to measure because content works indirectly and over long timeframes. Don't let perfect attribution prevent you from measuring at all. The simplest useful calculation: compare the cost of content to the cost of paid ads for the same traffic volume. If content costs less per visitor than paid ads, your content program is generating positive value even without direct revenue attribution

### 2.77 Creative Brief for Video Production
**Use case:** Write a detailed creative brief for producing a brand or product video
**Best for:** Video marketers, brand managers, content creators
Act as a video production creative director. I need to create a brief for a video production. Tell me the video type (brand film, product demo, testimonial, explainer, social ad, event recap), the target audience, the key message, and where it will be shown. Then create a comprehensive video creative brief: project overview (video title, type, duration target, primary goal, success metrics, distribution channels, deadline); target audience (demographics, psychographics, their relationship with your brand, what they care about, what would make them stop scrolling and watch); core message (the single thing the viewer must remember after watching — one sentence. "If they forget everything else, they must remember THIS"); creative concept (visual metaphor or narrative device that makes your message memorable, why this concept resonates with the target audience, reference videos that have a similar vibe, what emotion should the viewer feel); visual direction (color palette, lighting style (bright and clean vs moody and dramatic), camera movement (steady and cinematic vs handheld and authentic), composition style, graphic treatment if applicable, typography direction); audio direction (music genre and mood — energetic, emotional, professional, quirky; voiceover style — warm and conversational, authoritative, energetic; sound design — ambient sounds, transitions, emphasis effects; on-camera talent requirements — age, gender, energy, diversity considerations); script structure (hook — first 3-5 seconds that stops the scroll; problem — establish the tension; solution — show your offering; proof — social proof or demonstration; CTA — what to do next. For each section: timing (seconds), visual description, audio/script, text overlay); deliverables (final video in required formats (16:9, 9:16, 1:1, square), raw footage, color grade version, music-only version, subtitled version, thumbnail image, teaser clips (5-15 seconds for social)); production requirements (location, talent, props, equipment, permits, shoot duration, post-production timeline). Include a sample completed brief for a hypothetical brand video.
**Expected output:** A complete video creative brief with concept, visual/audio direction, script structure, deliverables, and production requirements
**Pro tip:** The most important part of a video brief is the HOOK description. If your hook doesn't grab attention in the first 3 seconds on social media or 8 seconds on YouTube, the rest of the video doesn't matter. Describe the hook in specific detail: what happens visually AND audibly in the first moments. Reference specific examples of hooks you like so the production team has a clear target

### 2.78 Blog Post to Social Media Thread Converter
**Use case:** Convert a long-form blog post into a high-engagement social media thread
**Best for:** Content marketers, social media managers, blog editors
Act as a social media content repurposing specialist. I need to convert my blog post into a Twitter/X thread that drives traffic back to the original. Provide me with your blog post content (or topic, key points, and conclusion). Then create a complete Twitter thread using this structure: Hook tweet (first tweet): a bold statement, surprising statistic, or provocative question from the post that makes people want to read more. Must stand alone as valuable. Thread tweets (5-10 tweets): each tweet covers ONE key point from the post — a specific insight, data point, or actionable tip. Each tweet should be understandable on its own but also make people want to read the next. Use formatting: line breaks, CAPS for emphasis (sparingly), emojis relevant to the content (1-2 max per tweet). Story arc: the thread should build toward a conclusion, not just be a random list of points. Include a "retweetable" tweet — a self-contained insight that someone would want to share. CTA tweet (final tweet): summarize the thread value, direct link to the full blog post, ask a question to drive engagement ("what did I miss?", "which tip will you try first?", "tag someone who needs to see this"), include relevant hashtags (1-3 max). Also include: a LinkedIn version (3-4 paragraphs adapting the same content for LinkedIn's longer format), an Instagram carousel outline (7-10 slides, each with a headline and 1-2 sentence description), and a LinkedIn newsletter teaser. For each platform, specify the best posting time and engagement follow-up strategy.
**Expected output:** A complete social thread for Twitter/X, LinkedIn post, Instagram carousel outline, and newsletter teaser, all derived from one blog post
**Pro tip:** The most retweetable tweets are ones that make people think "this is so true" or "I never thought of it that way." When converting a blog post, look for the counter-intuitive insights — the things that challenge common assumptions. Those make the best thread tweets because they create cognitive friction that drives engagement

### 2.79 Editorial Calendar and Workflow Template
**Use case:** Design an editorial calendar with content planning, production workflow, and approval process
**Best for:** Content teams, editors, marketing managers
Act as a content operations specialist. I need to design an editorial calendar and production workflow. Tell me your content volume (pieces per week/month), team size and roles, and what tools you currently use. Then create a complete editorial operations system: editorial calendar structure (monthly view: theme/topic, content type, channel, owner, writer, due date, publish date, status. Quarterly planning: content themes, campaign alignment, seasonal topics, resource allocation. Annual view: strategic initiatives, major campaigns, content pillars to build, hiring needs); content production workflow (Idea → Brief → Draft → Review → Edit → Approve → Publish → Promote → Measure. For each stage: who is responsible, typical duration, deliverables, quality gate (what must be true before moving to next stage), and tools used); role definitions (Content Strategist: topic selection, brief creation, performance review; Writer: research and drafting; Editor: technical accuracy, SEO optimization, style guide compliance; Designer: visuals, formatting, featured images; Approver: final content approval (legal, executive); Publisher: CMS upload, scheduling, distribution; Promoter: social media, email, outreach); content pipeline management (backlog: all content ideas not yet assigned. To-do: assigned content in production. In progress: being actively worked. In review: awaiting feedback. Scheduled: approved and scheduled. Published: live. Performance: published and being tracked. Status updates required at each transition); approval process (standard content: writer → editor → publisher (24-hour SLA); high-profile content: writer → editor → legal → executive → publisher (48-hour SLA); time-sensitive content: writer → editor (same-day SLA with expedite flag); approval escalation: if review SLA is missed, auto-escalate to next level); content batching strategy (recommendation: batch content by type — write all social posts for the week on Monday, all blog posts on Tuesday-Thursday, all emails on Friday. Batch by theme — one topic cluster per week. Production batching: record all videos on one day, design all graphics on one day, write all email copy on one day); tool stack recommendations (calendar and project management: Asana, Monday, Trello, or Notion; writing and editing: Google Docs or Notion with version history; design: Canva or Figma; scheduling: Buffer, Hootsuite, or Later; analytics: Google Analytics, Search Console, and platform analytics). Provide a template editorial calendar with columns and a sample monthly view.
**Expected output:** An editorial operations system with calendar structure, production workflow, role definitions, approval process, batching strategy, and tool recommendations
**Pro tip:** The most common editorial workflow bottleneck is the REVIEW stage. Content piles up waiting for the editor or approver. Solve this by instituting a "review by deadline" policy: if the reviewer doesn't respond by the SLA deadline, the content auto-advances to the next stage. Also, empower writers to publish standard content without review — use post-publishing quality checks instead

### 2.80 Event Content Strategy and Playbook
**Use case:** Plan content for a live or virtual event — pre-event promotion, live coverage, and post-event follow-up
