**Best for:** Event marketers, content teams, community managers
Act as an event content strategist. I need to create a content plan for my upcoming event. Tell me the event name, date, format (in-person, virtual, hybrid), audience, and speakers/agenda. Then create a complete event content playbook: pre-event content (announcement: save-the-date, speaker reveals, registration opening emails and social posts; anticipation: speaker spotlights (interview or Q&A with each speaker), topic teasers (what attendees will learn), attendee social proof (who's coming, testimonials from past events), countdown series (5 posts — one per day in the week leading up to the event); content types: blog posts, social media, email, LinkedIn articles, short videos — each with specific messaging per audience segment); live event content (real-time coverage: live tweeting key moments from each session, Instagram stories behind-the-scenes, LinkedIn live or recorded speaker highlights, quote graphics with speaker photos and key quotes; attendee engagement: photo booth or selfie station, hashtag campaign encouragement, attendee testimonial capture (video or written), live polling and Q&A amplification; speaker content: record all sessions, capture speaker uno interviews, take professional photos of each speaker); post-event content (immediate (within 24 hours): thank-you email with highlight recap, social thank-you posts, key statistics (attendance, engagement), call for feedback; short-term (within 1 week): session recordings and slides published, full recap blog post with key takeaways and photos, speaker interview series, highlight video (2-3 minutes), photo gallery; long-term (within 1 month): on-demand access to recordings, whitepaper or report synthesizing event insights, next event announcement, speaker content for their channels); measurement and reporting (reach: impressions, views, mentions, hashtag usage; engagement: likes, shares, comments, saves, poll responses; conversion: ticket sales, registration source analysis, email signups; content performance: best-performing content by type and channel, content that drove the most registrations, content with highest engagement). Include a content calendar timeline from 4 weeks before to 4 weeks after the event.
**Expected output:** A complete event content playbook with pre-event, live, and post-event content strategy, templates, and measurement framework
**Pro tip:** The most underutilized event content is the "behind-the-scenes" content during the event itself. Attendee-generated content (their photos, posts, and reactions) is 3x more engaging than brand-generated content. Make it easy for attendees to create content: photo-worthy moments, ready-to-share quote cards, a unique hashtag, and a photo booth with instant digital download. Their content promotes your event more authentically than anything you can create

### 4.41 Sales Proposal Writing Template
**Use case:** Write persuasive sales proposals that win deals
**Best for:** Sales professionals, business development, account executives
Act as a sales proposal specialist. I need to write a proposal for a potential client. Tell me the prospect name, company, their key needs, your solution, and the decision criteria. Then guide me through creating a winning proposal: executive summary (3-4 sentences that capture the prospect's situation, your solution, and the expected outcome. Must pass the "so what?" test); understanding their needs (demonstrate you listened — reference their specific pain points, goals, and constraints they shared during discovery); solution overview (describe what you're proposing, how it works, what's included, and clear deliverables or outcomes. Use their language and priorities); proof and social proof (case studies, testimonials, relevant experience, metrics and results from similar clients); pricing and options (3 tiers or options if applicable: good, better, best. Clear pricing with what's included at each level. ROI calculation if possible); timeline and implementation (when work starts, key milestones, delivery schedule, communication cadence); terms and next steps (payment terms, acceptance process, expiration date of proposal, what happens after they sign). Include a proposal checklist: before sending, verify that you addressed every objection raised during discovery, personalized at least 3 sections, included specific numbers and outcomes, and made it easy to say yes.
**Expected output:** A sales proposal template with executive summary, needs analysis, solution overview, proof, pricing, and next steps
**Pro tip:** The biggest mistake in proposals is making them about YOUR product. A winning proposal is about THE CLIENT'S problem and how you solve it. Use the word "you" 3x more than "we." And always include a specific, quantified expected outcome — "reduce response time by 40%" beats "improve efficiency" every time

### 4.42 Sales Objection Handling Script
**Use case:** Prepare scripts to handle common sales objections confidently
**Best for:** Sales reps, SDRs, account executives
Act as a sales objection handling coach. I need to prepare responses to objections that come up in my sales conversations. Tell me what you sell, your target customer, and the top 5 objections you hear most frequently. For each objection, create a complete response framework: acknowledge (show you heard and understand the concern without being defensive — "that's a fair concern, many of our clients felt the same way initially"); isolate (check if this is the real objection or a smokescreen — "if we could address this concern, would you be ready to move forward?"); reframe (shift perspective from problem to solution — "let me share how another client who had the same concern found it actually became an advantage"); respond (specific response to the objection with evidence, social proof, or logic); confirm (check if the response satisfied the concern — "does that address your concern?" or "how does that sound?"). Include specific phrases for common objections: "too expensive," "not now," "happy with current vendor," "need to think about it," "need to talk to my team," "send me information," "we have no budget," "not interested." For each, explain why the objection arises (is it real or a stall tactic?) and how to respond without being pushy.
**Expected output:** An objection handling script with a framework and specific language for at least 5 common objections
**Pro tip:** Most objections are either "I don't trust you yet" or "I don't see the value yet" in disguise. Instead of answering the surface objection, address the underlying concern. "Too expensive" usually means "I don't see enough value for this price" — so refocus on value and ROI, not discounts. Also, never argue with an objection — acknowledge it first, then redirect

### 4.43 Customer Testimonial and Case Study Collection
**Use case:** Collect and create compelling customer testimonials and case studies
**Best for:** Sales, marketing, customer success
Act as a customer evidence specialist. I need to collect testimonials and build case studies from my customers. Tell me about your product, your ideal customer profile, and what results customers typically achieve. Then create a system for: testimonial collection (ask at the right moment — right after a success moment, not during a renewal. Make it easy: provide a simple form with specific prompts ("what result did you achieve?", "what was the biggest difference?", "what would you say to someone considering us?"). Ask for specific numbers — "increased X by Y%," "saved Z hours per week." Offer to draft it for them — most customers will approve a draft faster than writing from scratch); case study structure (title: "[Customer] achieved [Result] with [Solution]"; executive summary: 2-3 sentences covering the customer, their challenge, your solution, and the result. The "so what" — anyone should understand the value in 10 seconds. The challenge (before): what was the customer's situation before? What specific problems were they facing? What had they tried that didn't work? Quantify the pain where possible; the solution (during): how did they implement your solution? What was the process? What made them choose you? Include quotes from the customer; the results (after): specific, quantified outcomes. Revenue, time saved, cost reduced, efficiency gained. Use before/after comparisons. Include a quote that captures the emotional impact — "it transformed how we work" is more powerful than "it saved us 10 hours"; the customer's advice: what would they tell someone considering the same solution? Use real quotes); permission and compliance (always get written approval before publishing. Have a case study release form template. Let the customer review and approve the final version. Be transparent if you offer incentives (gift card, discount) for testimonials); distribution (use testimonials on: website, sales proposals, email signatures, social media, ads, pitch decks. Case studies on: website case study section, LinkedIn articles, email nurture sequences, sales leave-behinds, industry publications). Provide a testimonial collection email template and a case study interview question guide.
**Expected output:** A customer evidence system with testimonial collection process, case study structure, permission templates, and distribution strategy
**Pro tip:** The most powerful testimonial is specific, quantified, and emotional. "We saved $50K in the first quarter AND my team actually enjoys using it" is worth 100x more than "Great product, highly recommend." Always ask customers for specific numbers and how your product made them FEEL. The feeling is what sells

### 4.44 Sales Cold Calling Script
**Use case:** Create a cold calling script that gets prospects to engage
**Best for:** SDRs, BDRs, sales development
Act as a sales development coach specializing in cold calling. I need a cold calling script for my outreach. Tell me what you sell, who you're calling (title, industry), and what makes your solution different. Then create a cold calling framework: opening (first 10 seconds). Introduce yourself and your company. State why you're calling in one sentence that shows you've done your homework. "Hi [name], this is [name] from [company]. I noticed [specific trigger event or observation about their company]. I'm calling because we help companies like yours [achieve specific outcome]." Do NOT ask "how are you?" or use a generic opener; value statement (if they engage, deliver a concise value statement in under 30 seconds. "We help [target customer] achieve [specific outcome] by [your mechanism]. For example, [similar client] was able to [specific result]." The goal is to generate curiosity, not close the deal on the call); discovery questions (prepare 3-5 open-ended questions that qualify and create value: "how are you currently handling [problem area]?", "what's the biggest challenge you're facing with [relevant topic]?", "what would it mean to your team if you could [achieve outcome]?", "have you looked at any solutions before? What held you back?"); objection handling (prepare for: "not interested" → "I understand, most people say that before they know what I have. Can I share one quick example of how we helped [similar company] and you can decide if it's relevant?"; "send me info" → "I'd love to, but information is generic and your situation is specific. Can we take 5 minutes to see if this is even relevant?"; "busy right now" → "when would be a better time? I'm flexible."); call close (always end with a specific next step — a meeting invite, a call-back time, or a specific action they agreed to. Even if they say no, try to learn: "what would need to be different for this to be worth your time?" or "who else in your organization might find this relevant?"). Include a pre-call checklist: research the prospect (LinkedIn, company news, recent funding, job change), review past interactions, identify a trigger event or common ground, set a specific goal for the call, prepare your value statement.
**Expected output:** A cold calling framework with opening script, value statement, discovery questions, objection handling, and call close
**Pro tip:** The best cold calls don't sound like cold calls. Do enough research to reference something specific about the prospect's company or situation. "I saw you just raised a Series A" or "I noticed you're hiring for 3 positions in your marketing department" shows you've done your homework and immediately separates you from the 100 other cold callers they got this week. The goal of a cold call is not to sell — it's to earn a conversation

### 4.45 Sales Forecasting and Pipeline Review
**Use case:** Build accurate sales forecasts and run effective pipeline reviews
**Best for:** Sales managers, VPs of sales, revenue operations
Act as a sales operations consultant. I need to improve my sales forecasting accuracy. Tell me about your sales cycle length, deal stages, typical deal size, and historical close rates. Then create a forecasting system: stage-based probability (assign realistic close probabilities to each pipeline stage: discovery 10%, qualification 25%, proposal 50%, negotiation 75%, closing 90%. These should be based on YOUR historical data, not industry averages); weighted pipeline calculation (for each deal: deal value x stage probability = weighted value. Sum of all weighted values = forecast. But add a "commit" category for deals with high confidence — deals where the customer has verbally committed, budget is approved, and you have a specific close date); deal inspection criteria (for each deal in the forecast, verify: contact with decision maker confirmed, budget identified and approved, timeline specific (not "this quarter" but "by June 15"), mutual next steps agreed upon, value articulated in customer's words, competition identified and positioned against); pipeline health metrics (track: total pipeline value vs. quota (3x+ coverage), weighted pipeline vs. quota (1.5x+), aging deals (stuck in same stage for 2x normal duration), new pipeline created this week/month, deals lost and why, average deal size and velocity); forecast categories (commit: high confidence, specific close date, verbal commitment from customer. Best case: all active deals close "someday" but no specific commitment. Pipeline: everything in the funnel including new opportunities. Overlay with reps' confidence ratings — "this deal feels great" vs. "this is a stretch"); pipeline review structure (weekly 30-minute individual reviews with each rep: review deals that should have closed but haven't, identify next steps for stalled deals, remove deals that are dead but still in pipeline, identify coaching opportunities based on deal stages where reps get stuck, update forecast and commit numbers); leading indicators (track activities that predict future pipeline: meetings booked, proposals sent, discovery calls completed, demos delivered, referrals requested. If activity is down, pipeline will be down in 2-4 weeks). Provide a pipeline review agenda template and a deal inspection checklist.
**Expected output:** A sales forecasting system with stage-based probability, deal inspection criteria, pipeline health metrics, and review structure
**Pro tip:** The most common forecasting mistake is optimism bias — reps believe deals are more likely to close than they actually are. Combat this by: (1) basing probabilities on historical data, not feelings, (2) forcing specific next-step commitments before counting a deal as "advanced," and (3) having a separate "commit" category that requires specific criteria beyond just "I think it will close." A good forecast is conservative, not optimistic

### 4.46 Sales Enablement Content Strategy
**Use case:** Create sales enablement materials that help reps sell more effectively
**Best for:** Sales enablement managers, marketing, revenue operations
Act as a sales enablement strategist. I need to create content and training that helps my sales team sell more effectively. Tell me about your product, sales process, buyer personas, and what your reps struggle with most. Then create a sales enablement system: buyer journey mapping (for each stage of the buyer's journey — awareness, consideration, decision — identify what the buyer is thinking, feeling, doing, and what questions they have. Map content to each stage: awareness stage: blog posts, industry reports, infographics; consideration stage: case studies, comparison guides, webinars; decision stage: ROI calculators, proposal templates, testimonial videos, free trials); battle cards (create competitive battle cards for your top 3-5 competitors. Each card: competitor name and positioning, their strengths (be honest), their weaknesses (your opportunities), common objections they raise about you, how to counter those objections, winning messages that differentiate you, proof points and case studies that demonstrate superiority). Do not bash competitors — focus on YOUR strengths and the customer's needs; objection handling guides (for each common objection: what the customer really means, the best response framework (acknowledge, isolate, reframe, respond, confirm), specific language to use, what NOT to say). Organize by objection type (price, timing, competition, status quo, authority, no need); content library structure (organize by: content type (case studies, one-pagers, proposals, presentation, videos, ROI calculators, FAQs), use case (sales call, discovery meeting, demo, proposal, negotiation), product/feature. Include a searchable index and 30-second description for each asset so reps know when to use it); sales training program (new hire ramp: week 1 product training, week 2 sales process, week 3 roleplay and certification, week 4 supervised calls. Ongoing training: weekly 30-minute skill sessions, monthly deep dives, quarterly offsite. Each session: topic (one specific skill), format (role-play, case study, guest speaker), assessment (how you'll measure learning)); enablement metrics (track: content usage (what's being used and what's collecting dust), time-to-productivity for new hires, win rate by rep and by content used, content influence on deal progression, rep satisfaction with enablement). Provide a content request template that reps can use to ask for specific materials they need.
**Expected output:** A sales enablement system with buyer journey mapping, battle cards, objection guides, content library structure, and training program
**Pro tip:** The best sales enablement comes FROM your sales team, not from marketing. Your top performers have scripts, responses, and techniques that work. Interview them, record their calls (with permission), and turn their best practices into training for the whole team. Peer-to-peer learning is 3x more effective than top-down training. Also, enablement content that doesn't get used is worse than no content at all — build a feedback loop to retire what isn't working

### 4.47 Sales Compensation Plan Design
**Use case:** Design a sales compensation plan that motivates reps and aligns with business goals
**Best for:** Sales leaders, HR, founders, revenue operations
Act as a sales compensation consultant. I need to design a compensation plan for my sales team. Tell me about your business model, average deal size, sales cycle length, team structure, and what behaviors you want to incentivize. Then create a compensation framework: compensation philosophy (decide on target pay position relative to market: 50th percentile, 75th percentile. Split between base salary and variable compensation: typical splits are 50/50 for SDRs, 60/40 for AEs, 70/30 for enterprise sales. Variable should be at risk — meaning they only earn it by achieving results); quota setting (quota should be achievable by 60-70% of reps (top performers exceed, most hit, bottom 10-20% miss). Set quotas based on: territory potential, historical performance, company growth targets, market conditions. Use a tiered approach: threshold (minimum to earn any commission, typically 50-70% of quota), target (100% of quota — full commission), accelerator (over-achievement commission rate, 1.5x-2x for exceeding 120%+); commission structure (common models: flat rate — fixed percentage or dollar amount per sale (simple, predictable); tiered rate — commission rate increases as rep hits higher tiers (e.g., 5% for first $100K, 7% for $100-200K, 10% for $200K+); gross margin-based — commission based on profitability of each deal (encourages discount discipline); bonus-based — fixed bonus for hitting specific milestones (good for behavior-based goals)). Include a clawback policy for deals that churn within 90 days; behavioral multipliers (consider adding modifiers for: new business vs. expansion (new should pay higher), product line priority (strategic products get a bonus), deal quality (high-margin deals earn more), customer satisfaction (NPS threshold to earn full commission), strategic account acquisition (bonus for landing target accounts)). Provide a sample compensation plan document with all components.
**Expected output:** A sales compensation plan with quota design, commission structure, behavioral multipliers, and sample plan document
**Pro tip:** The golden rule of comp design: when you change the comp plan, you change the behavior. If you want reps to hunt new business, pay more for net-new logos. If you want them to expand existing accounts, pay more for upsells. If you want them to protect margin, pay on gross margin not revenue. Never change a comp plan mid-year unless absolutely necessary — and if you do, over-communicate the why. Bad comp design is the #1 cause of sales team turnover

### 4.48 Sales Kickoff Event Planning
**Use case:** Plan and execute a high-impact Sales Kickoff event
**Best for:** Sales leaders, enablement, marketing events
Act as a sales kickoff event planner. I need to plan a Sales Kickoff (SKO) for my team. Tell me the number of attendees, budget (or constraints), location (virtual, in-person, hybrid), duration, and key objectives. Then create a complete SKO plan: strategic objectives (define 3-4 specific outcomes for the SKO: align the team on new strategy, launch new product or messaging, train on new skills, build team morale and culture. Every session should tie back to one of these objectives); agenda design (day 1 — vision and strategy: CEO keynote (company direction and why it matters), VP Sales address (sales strategy, goals, what's changing), product roadmap (what's coming and why it matters), customer panel (real stories and feedback). Day 2 — skills and enablement: breakout tracks by role (AE, SDR, CS, management), skill workshops (negotiation, discovery, demo, forecasting), role-plays and certification (practice the new messaging), battle card training (competitive positioning). Day 3 — planning and culture: territory and quota planning sessions, peer learning (top reps share their secrets), awards and recognition, team-building activity, closing session and call to action); session design (each session should have: clear learning objective, interactive element (not just slides), practical takeaway (something they can use Monday), time for questions, and measurable outcome (what will be different after this session). 45-minute sessions max. Include breaks every 90 minutes. No death by PowerPoint); pre-work and preparation (send pre-work 1 week before: read new materials, complete online training modules, prepare territory analysis, complete self-assessment. Pre-work ensures SKO time is spent on high-value activities, not content delivery); logistics (venue or virtual platform, travel and accommodation, dietary restrictions, accessibility needs, swag and materials, printed playbooks or digital access, photo and video documentation, social media coverage plan); measurement and follow-up (post-SKO survey immediately, knowledge retention test at 30/60/90 days, observe behavior change in pipeline reviews, track whether SKO objectives translated to metrics, 90-day reinforcement plan (monthly refresher sessions). Provide a post-SKO report template.
**Expected output:** A complete SKO plan with agenda, session designs, pre-work, logistics, and measurement
**Pro tip:** The biggest mistake in SKO planning is trying to cram too much content. Your team will remember 3 things max. Choose the 3 most important messages or skills and repeat them across multiple formats (keynote, workshop, role-play, one-pager). Everything else is noise. Also, the best part of SKO is usually the hallway conversations and peer networking — build unstructured time into the agenda. Don't over-schedule

### 4.49 Sales Technology Stack Evaluation
**Use case:** Evaluate and select sales technology tools for your stack
**Best for:** Sales ops, revenue ops, sales leaders
Act as a sales technology consultant. I need to evaluate tools for my sales stack. Tell me about your team size, sales process, current tools, budget constraints, and biggest pain points. Then create a sales tech evaluation framework: core stack components (CRM (foundation — Salesforce, HubSpot, Pipedrive); sales engagement (Outreach, SalesLoft, Groove); conversation intelligence (Gong, Chorus, ZoomInfo); prospecting and data enrichment (LinkedIn Sales Navigator, ZoomInfo, Apollo, Lusha); proposal and CPQ (PandaDoc, DocuSign, QuoteWerks); analytics and forecasting (Clari, Tableau, PowerBI); training and enablement (Lessonly, MindTickle, SalesHuddle). Map each tool to the stage of the sales process it supports); evaluation criteria (score each tool on: functionality (does it do what we need?), integration (does it connect with our existing stack?), ease of use (will reps actually use it?), implementation effort (how long to deploy?), support and training, scalability (works for 10 reps and 100 reps?), cost (total cost of ownership including implementation, training, and any add-ons)); build vs. buy analysis (for each tool, consider: can we build this with existing resources? What's the maintenance cost? How critical is it to our differentiation? Core capabilities that aren't differentiated — buy off the shelf. Proprietary capabilities that give you an edge — consider building. Small team? Buy everything. Large team with engineering resources? Build only what's strategic); implementation plan (select → negotiate → pilot (2-week trial with 3-5 power users) → feedback and adjust → full rollout → training → adoption monitoring → ongoing optimization. Include a migration plan for data from legacy systems); adoption metrics (track: login frequency, feature usage, pipeline data completeness, time saved per rep, rep satisfaction score, ROI (productivity gain vs. tool cost)). Provide a vendor scorecard template and an RFP template for requesting proposals.
**Expected output:** A sales tech evaluation framework with core stack components, evaluation criteria, build vs. buy analysis, implementation plan, and adoption metrics
**Pro tip:** The most expensive sales tool is the one nobody uses. Before buying any tool, verify that your team will actually adopt it. The best predictor of adoption is: (1) the tool solves a real pain the team feels (not one you think they have), (2) implementation takes less than 2 weeks, (3) it integrates with tools they already use, and (4) there is a clear champion who will drive adoption. Start with a small pilot before committing to an annual contract

### 4.50 Sales Negotiation Preparation Template
**Use case:** Prepare for high-stakes sales negotiations with a structured approach
**Best for:** Account executives, sales managers, enterprise sales
Act as a sales negotiation coach. I need to prepare for an upcoming negotiation with a prospect. Tell me about the prospect, deal size, what's at stake, your ideal outcome, and what you know about their situation. Then create a negotiation preparation plan: interests (what does each side REALLY want? Not positions ("we want 20% off") but interests ("they have budget pressure from their CFO"). What are their underlying needs, fears, and motivations? What are yours? The best negotiators focus on interests, not positions); BATNA analysis (Best Alternative To Negotiated Agreement — what happens if this deal doesn't close? Your BATNA: what else can you do with this time/resources? Their BATNA: what will they do if they don't buy from you (competitor, status quo, build in-house)? Your BATNA is your negotiating power. The better your alternatives, the stronger your position); reservation price (what is your walk-away point? The minimum acceptable deal. Below this, you walk. Also estimate theirs — what's the maximum they would pay? The Zone of Possible Agreement (ZOPA) is the space between your minimum and their maximum); leverage (what do you have that they need? Unique solution, time sensitivity, pain that's costing them money, scarcity (other buyers interested), authority (you're talking to the economic buyer). What do they have that you need? Deal size, strategic reference, market entry, revenue target pressure); negotiation tactics (plan your approach: anchor first or let them anchor (research shows first offer sets the range). Concession plan — what are you willing to give up and what do you want in return? Never give a concession without getting one. Be prepared for common tactics: good cop/bad cop, nibble ("can you just throw in..."), red herring (focusing on an issue they don't really care about to trade away for something real), deadline pressure ("we need a decision by Friday"), silence (the most effective tactic — whoever speaks first after an offer loses ground)); agreement template (what does a good deal look like? Price, terms, timeline, scope, legal requirements, references/social proof, support level, implementation plan, success metrics). Include a negotiation journal template to document what you learned after the negotiation.
**Expected output:** A sales negotiation preparation plan with interest analysis, BATNA, reservation price, leverage assessment, tactics, and agreement template
**Pro tip:** The most powerful phrase in negotiation is "help me understand." When the other side makes a demand you don't like, don't reject it — ask "help me understand how you arrived at that number." This does three things: (1) it buys you time to think, (2) it gathers information about their reasoning, and (3) it puts you in a collaborative rather than adversarial stance. The best negotiators are curious, not combative

### 4.51 Sales Funnel Analysis and Optimization
**Use case:** Analyze your sales funnel to identify leaks and optimize conversion
**Best for:** Sales ops, revenue ops, sales management
Act as a sales funnel analyst. I need to analyze and improve my sales funnel. Share your pipeline data: leads by stage, conversion rates between stages, deal velocity, and where deals get stuck. Then create a funnel optimization plan: funnel metrics (define each stage and calculate: volume (number of opportunities at each stage), conversion rate between stages (what % move from stage A to stage B), velocity (average days to move through each stage), value (average deal value at each stage), and the leak rate (where are you losing the most opportunities?)); bottleneck identification (analyze which stage has the most deals in absolute terms — this indicates a bottleneck where deals are piling up. Calculate the "days in stage" vs. expected. If deals are spending 2x expected time in any stage, that stage is a bottleneck. Prioritize bottlenecks by impact: deals lost at early stages (qualification) costs less than deals lost at late stages (negotiation)); conversion optimization (for each stage where conversion is below benchmark: stage 1 (lead to qualified lead) — improve lead scoring criteria, better qualification questions earlier, automated disqualification; stage 2 (qualified to demo) — reduce friction to book, improve discovery call value, provide pre-demo content; stage 3 (demo to proposal) — improve demo quality, address objections earlier, provide proposal-ready summaries; stage 4 (proposal to negotiate) — send proposals faster, include decision-relevant info in proposal, create urgency; stage 5 (negotiate to close) — improve negotiation prep, identify decision criteria earlier, handle final objections); velocity improvement (to reduce sales cycle: reduce time between stages with automation (scheduling, proposals, follow-ups), parallelize steps (legal review while negotiation, implementation plan while waiting for signature), create urgency (limited-time offers, social proof of others buying now), remove unnecessary steps (simplify approval processes, eliminate redundant meetings)); A/B testing ideas (for each funnel stage, suggest 3 A/B test ideas to improve conversion. Example: email subject lines, call scripting, proposal format, pricing presentation, follow-up cadence). Provide a funnel audit template with metrics to track at each stage and an action priority matrix (impact vs. effort).
**Expected output:** A sales funnel analysis with conversion metrics, bottleneck identification, optimization strategies for each stage, and A/B testing ideas
**Pro tip:** Most sales funnels have ONE dominant bottleneck that, if fixed, improves everything downstream. Look for the stage where the MOST deals are sitting (in absolute numbers) — that's your bottleneck. Fixing a 50% conversion at a stage with 100 deals has 10x more impact than fixing a 50% conversion at a stage with 10 deals. Also, late-stage bottlenecks (proposal to close) are more expensive per deal than early-stage ones — fix those first

### 4.52 Sales Partnership and Alliance Strategy
**Use case:** Build sales partnerships and channel alliances
**Best for:** Channel sales, partnerships, business development
Act as a channel partnership strategist. I need to build a sales partnership program. Tell me about your product, target market, and what kind of partners would complement your offering. Then create a partnership development plan: partner types to pursue (integration partners, referral partners, technology alliances, resellers, affiliate partners, strategic alliances. For each type: what they do, how they benefit, what you provide, typical deal structure); partner recruitment (ideal partner profile: serves the same target customer, complementary (not competitive) offering, established trust with their customers, motivated to add value for their clients. Recruitment process: identify 20 target partners in each category, research their business and customers, craft a personalized outreach (what's in it for THEM?), set up exploratory calls, present partnership opportunity and mutual value proposition); partnership structure (for each partner type, define: commission/referral fee structure, co-marketing commitment, co-selling process, revenue sharing model, minimum commitment or performance expectations, term and renewal conditions. Document everything in a partnership agreement template); enablement and support (partner training: product training, sales training, joint value proposition, competitive positioning. Partner resources: sales decks, case studies, demo environment, co-brandable materials, dedicated partner contact. Partner portal: self-service access to all resources, deal registration, commission tracking, communications); co-marketing and co-selling (joint webinars, co-branded content, case studies of joint customers, referral swaps, joint presence at events, partner spotlight series. For co-selling: clear process for how partners refer leads, how leads are tracked, how commissions are paid, how disputes are resolved); performance measurement (track per partner: leads generated, deals closed, revenue attributed, partner satisfaction, time to first deal, lifetime value of partner-originated customers, partner tier (based on performance). Hold quarterly business reviews with top partners). Provide a partnership one-pager template and a partner outreach email template.
**Expected output:** A sales partnership strategy with partner types, recruitment process, structure, enablement, and measurement
**Pro tip:** The most common mistake in partnerships is treating all partners equally. Pareto principle applies: 80% of partnership revenue will come from 20% of partners. Tier your partners (platinum, gold, silver) and invest accordingly. Your top 5 partners should get dedicated support, joint marketing budget, and executive attention. Your bottom 50 partners should have self-service access only. Don't let the long tail drain your resources

### 4.53 Sales Territory Assignment and Management
**Use case:** Design and manage sales territories for optimal coverage and fairness
**Best for:** Sales ops, sales management, revenue operations
Act as a sales territory design specialist. I need to create or optimize sales territories. Tell me about your team size, geographic coverage, account segmentation, and what data you have on account potential. Then create a territory management plan: territory design principles (territories should be: balanced (equal revenue potential across territories), actionable (each rep can reasonably cover all accounts in their territory with available time), logical (group accounts by industry, geography, or other natural segmentation), fair (comparable earning potential for reps in different territories), and stable (avoid changing territories more than once a year). The goal is not perfect equality but "good enough" balance with clear criteria); data-driven design (step-by-step: collect all accounts with location, industry, revenue, and potential score. Decide on design basis: geographic (zip code, city, region, state), account tier (enterprise, mid-market, SMB), industry vertical (healthcare, finance, tech), or a hybrid approach. Use a clustering approach: start with account density heatmap, then create territories around natural geographic/industry clusters, then balance for workload and potential, then iterate based on feedback); territory assignment criteria (when assigning reps to territories, consider: rep experience and skill level (top reps get strategic territories with high-growth potential), existing relationships, language and cultural fit, career development goals, commute/travel preferences. Give reps input into territory design — they know the accounts better than you); territory management (process for: handling account splits when an account spans territories, managing transitions when reps leave (temp coverage, reassignment, ramp period), territory swapping between reps (with manager approval and clean handoff), handling inbound leads that don't fit any territory (rotating or first-available rule)); territory metrics and review (track per territory: total addressable market coverage, account penetration rate, quota attainment % (target 60-70%), deal velocity by territory, account density (accounts per rep), travel time/cost, rep satisfaction with territory. Review territories quarterly, rebalance annually with a formal process). Include a territory design template and a territory swap request process.
**Expected output:** A sales territory management plan with design principles, data-driven approach, assignment criteria, and ongoing management
**Pro tip:** The most common complaint from sales reps is "my territory is unfair." Even if territories are perfectly balanced, reps will feel theirs is worse. The solution is transparency — share the data that shows how territories were designed, the criteria used, and how each territory compares on key metrics. Let reps see the process. Also, involve your top performers in territory design — they'll have better account intelligence than your CRM

### 4.54 Sales Playbook Creation
**Use case:** Create a comprehensive sales playbook for your team
**Best for:** Sales enablement, sales management, sales ops
Act as a sales playbook developer. I need to create a sales playbook for my organization. Tell me about your sales process, buyer personas, product, and what makes your sales approach unique. Then create a playbook structure: introduction and philosophy (our sales approach, our values, what makes us different, what we expect from each other, how we define winning). This sets the tone and culture for everything that follows; product knowledge (product overview: what we sell, who it's for, key features and benefits, pricing and packaging. Competitive landscape: top competitors, how we compare, their weaknesses, our strengths. Industry knowledge: key trends, common challenges, regulatory considerations. Keep this section living — update monthly); buyer personas (detailed profiles for each persona you sell to: job title and role, goals and metrics they're measured on, pain points and challenges, buying authority and process, where they get information, what they value in a solution, common objections they raise, how they make decisions. Include real quotes from actual buyers if available); sales process (stage-by-stage playbook for each step of your process: prospecting (sources, sequences, tools), discovery (questions, qualification criteria, MEDDIC or BANT framework), demo strategy (what to show, how to customize, next step), proposal process (templates, pricing guidelines, approval process), negotiation (authority levels, discount policies, concession strategy), closing (process, documentation, handoff to CS). For each stage: goal, entry criteria, actions (must-do), templates, exit criteria, common mistakes); prospecting plays (specific plays for each channel: cold email sequences (with templates), cold calling scripts, LinkedIn outreach, referral requests, event follow-up, inbound lead handling. Each play: when to use it, sequence/timing, templates, success metrics, common objections); role-specific guides (SDR guide: prospecting, qualification, meeting setting, handoff to AE. AE guide: discovery, demo, proposal, negotiation, close. CS guide: onboarding, QBRs, expansion, retention. Manager guide: pipeline review, coaching, forecasting, hiring). Include a playbook maintenance schedule (quarterly review and updates).
**Expected output:** A complete sales playbook with product knowledge, buyer personas, sales process, prospecting plays, and role-specific guides
**Pro tip:** A sales playbook is only useful if it's actually used. Avoid creating a 200-page document that sits on a shelf. Instead: make it digital and searchable, keep it to what's essential (40 pages max), include templates and scripts they can copy-paste, update it based on what's working (not what you think should work), and reference it in every pipeline review. The best playbooks are built by the team, not by management

### 4.55 Sales Goal Setting and Accountability
**Use case:** Set sales goals and create accountability systems that drive performance
**Best for:** Sales managers, sales leaders
Act as a sales performance coach. I need to set goals and create accountability for my sales team. Tell me about your team size, current performance, company targets, and what metrics you currently track. Then create a goal setting and accountability system: goal cascade (company revenue target → department ARR target → team quarterly quota → individual monthly targets → weekly activity goals. Each level should clearly ladder up to the next. Every rep should be able to explain how their daily activities connect to company revenue); SMART goal framework (goals should be: specific (exact number and timeline), measurable (tracked objectively), achievable (stretch but realistic), relevant (connects to company objectives), time-bound (clear deadline). For each rep, set: monthly quota (revenue or deals), weekly activity metrics (calls, meetings, proposals), skill development goal (one skill to improve this quarter)); leading vs. lagging indicators (lagging indicators (revenue, deals closed) tell you what already happened. Leading indicators (calls made, meetings booked, proposals sent) predict future results. For accountability, focus 70% on leading indicators (activities reps control) and 30% on lagging indicators (outcomes). If leading indicators are on track, lagging results will follow); accountability cadence (daily: 15-minute standup (yesterday's results, today's plan, one ask for help). Individual reps share their top priority. Weekly: 30-minute 1:1 pipeline review (review closed/won, closed/lost, key deals in pipeline, coaching focus for next week). Monthly: 60-minute performance review (goal progress, skill development, forecast update, recognition or corrective action). Quarterly: comprehensive business review (results vs. goal, territory analysis, skill assessment, goal reset)); coaching tied to goals (if a rep is missing targets: first, diagnose the gap (skill vs. will?). Is it a skill problem (they don't know how) → provide training, ride-alongs, call reviews. Is it a will problem (they're not trying) → have a candid conversation, set clear expectations, put on performance improvement plan. Is it a resource problem (they don't have what they need) → remove barriers, provide tools, adjust territory). Include a weekly 1:1 agenda template with coaching focus areas.
**Expected output:** A sales goal setting and accountability system with goal cascade, SMART framework, leading indicators, accountability cadence, and coaching
**Pro tip:** The most common accountability mistake is managing to lagging indicators only. If you only look at revenue at the end of the month, you've already lost. Instead, track leading indicators weekly — calls, meetings, proposals, pipeline created. These are activities the rep controls. If leading indicators are on track but lagging results are off, the problem is in the conversion (skill issue). If leading indicators are off, the problem is in the activity (effort issue). Diagnose before you coach

### 4.56 Sales Email Sequence Design
**Use case:** Design sales email sequences that get replies and book meetings
**Best for:** SDRs, sales development, demand generation
Act as a sales email sequencing specialist. I need to create email sequences for my outbound prospecting. Tell me about your target buyer, what you sell, and what's your unique value. Then create a multi-touch email sequence: sequence structure (5-8 touchpoints over 2-3 weeks. Mix of email types: social proof email (case study or testimonial relevant to prospect), value email (insight, tip, or resource related to their industry), personal email (reference to something specific about their company or role), direct ask email (clear CTA for a meeting), break-up email (respectful "I'll stop reaching out" that often gets replies). Alternate between email and other channels (LinkedIn, phone) within the sequence); email templates (for each email type, provide a template with: compelling subject line (personalized, curiosity-driven, or value-focused — 40-50 characters), opening line (reference something specific about them — recent news, job change, company milestone, content they shared), body (2-3 sentences max — focus on value to THEM, not features of your product. Use "you" language, not "we" language), CTA (one clear, low-friction ask — "worth a 15-minute call?", "is this relevant to your team?", "would you be open to a brief chat?"), signature (simple, no logos or links that trigger spam filters)); personalization strategy (level 1 — company-level: industry, size, recent news (minimal effort); level 2 — role-level: title, likely challenges, goals (medium effort); level 3 — individual-level: their content, comments, posts, background (high effort, highest response). Aim for level 2 on all outreach, level 3 for high-value targets); timing and cadence (best send times: Tuesday-Thursday, 6-8 AM or 4-5 PM local time. Space touches: day 1 (email 1), day 3 (email 2 or LinkedIn), day 5 (phone call attempt), day 7 (email 3), day 10 (email 4), day 14 (final email). End sequence after 14 days with no reply — move to nurture); tracking and optimization (track per sequence: open rate (target 40-60%), reply rate (target 3-10%), meeting booked rate (target 1-3%), unsubscribe rate (should be under 0.5%). A/B test: subject lines, opening lines, CTA types, send times, personalization depth). Provide a sequence tracker spreadsheet template.
**Expected output:** A sales email sequence design with multi-touch structure, templates for each email type, personalization strategy, timing, and tracking
**Pro tip:** Most sales emails fail because they're about the sender, not the receiver. Before writing a single word, ask: "why would this person care?" If you can't answer that in one sentence, rewrite the email. Also, the best sales emails look like a real person wrote them — they're not perfectly formatted, they reference something specific, and they sound human. Write like a human, not a marketer

### 4.57 Sales Discovery Call Framework
**Use case:** Master the discovery call to qualify prospects and uncover needs
**Best for:** SDRs, AEs, sales professionals
Act as a sales discovery specialist. I need to run better discovery calls. Tell me about your product, target customer, and what you typically learn during discovery. Then create a discovery call framework: pre-call preparation (research the prospect: recent company news, their role and background, their industry trends, common challenges in their role. Prepare your discovery questions based on what you already know. Set a specific goal for the call (qualify, set next step, learn specific information)); call structure (5% rapport building — natural connection, not forced small talk. 40% discovery — your questions (see below). 20% value demonstration — connect their needs to your solution briefly. 5% objection handling — address concerns that came up. 25% next steps — summarize, agree on action items, schedule next touchpoint. 5% closing — confirm value and book next step); question framework (use open-ended questions that start with what, how, tell me about. Follow the MEDDIC framework: Metrics — "how do you measure success in this area?", "what's the cost of the current problem?"; Economic buyer — "who else is involved in this decision?", "what's the approval process?"; Decision criteria — "what factors will determine which solution you choose?", "what does the ideal solution look like?"; Decision process — "what steps will you take to make this decision?", "what's your timeline?"; Identify pain — "what's frustrating about your current solution?", "what have you tried that didn't work?"; Champion — "who else in your organization shares this concern?", "who would benefit most from solving this?"); active listening techniques (paraphrase what they said to confirm understanding: "so if I understand correctly..."; ask follow-up questions based on what they shared; take notes (or record with permission); look for emotional language (words like "frustrated," "struggling," "exciting," "worried" — these reveal real priorities); pause after their answers — silence encourages them to elaborate); qualification scoring (after the call, score on: budget (identified and sufficient?), authority (talking to decision maker?), need (clear pain point?), timeline (specific buying window?), competition (who else are they considering?). Score 1-5 for each. Score of 20+ = high priority, 15-19 = nurture, below 15 = disqualify or gather more info). Provide a discovery call scorecard.
**Expected output:** A discovery call framework with pre-call prep, call structure, question framework, active listening techniques, and qualification scoring
**Pro tip:** The best discovery calls are 80% listening, 20% talking. If you're talking more than the prospect, you're not discovering — you're presenting. Your goal is to understand their situation so well that when you do present your solution, it feels like it was custom-built for them. The most powerful question in discovery: "what happens if you do nothing?" It reveals urgency, risk, and motivation

### 4.58 Sales Demo Best Practices
**Use case:** Deliver sales demos that convert prospects into customers
**Best for:** AEs, sales engineers, product specialists
Act as a sales demo coach. I need to improve my product demos. Tell me about your product, who you demo to, and what typically happens during your demos. Then create a demo optimization plan: pre-demo discovery (before any demo, confirm: what specific problem are they trying to solve? what have they tried? what's their decision criteria? who else will be on the call? what's their timeline?). A demo without discovery is a presentation, not a sales conversation. If you haven't done discovery yet, the demo should start with 10 minutes of discovery; demo structure (opening (5 min): agenda for the call, confirm understanding of their needs ("before I jump in, I want to make sure I'm focused on what matters to you — you mentioned X and Y are priorities, correct?"). The problem-focused opening (10 min): show that you understand their problem deeply. Use a story or example of a similar customer who had the same challenge. Do NOT start by showing your product. Start by showing you understand them. The solution demonstration (20 min): show ONLY the features relevant to THEIR needs. "You mentioned X was a challenge — let me show you how we handle that." This is customized, not generic. Tell a story with each feature — "here's how [similar customer] uses this to achieve [result]." The proof and results (5 min): case studies, metrics, testimonials relevant to their industry or situation. "Here's what [similar company] achieved in their first 90 days." Next steps and close (5 min): summary of what you showed and how it addresses their needs. Ask if it makes sense. "Based on what we discussed, does this seem like a good fit for your needs?" Then book next step); demo best practices (do: customize for each prospect, focus on outcomes not features, tell stories, use social proof, handle objections as they come, ask questions throughout, leave time for discussion, end with clear next steps. Don't: read from a script, show every feature, go over time, badmouth competitors, make the demo about you instead of them, send recording without a follow-up call); handling difficult demo scenarios (non-responsive audience: ask direct questions, involve them, ask them to share their screen for context. Technical audience: go deeper on architecture, integrations, security. Executive audience: focus on ROI, business outcomes, competitive advantage. Skeptical audience: address objections head on, share proof, offer references. Decision by committee: engage each stakeholder, identify individual needs, offer to run separate demos for different roles); post-demo follow-up (send personalized recap within 2 hours: "thanks for your time today. Here's what we covered and the key takeaways. [link to recording]. Next step: [specific action and date]." Include: what you discussed, how your solution addresses their needs, any open questions, next steps and timeline, relevant case study or content they requested). Provide a demo scorecard for self-evaluation.
**Expected output:** A sales demo optimization plan with pre-demo discovery, demo structure, best practices, difficult scenarios, and post-demo follow-up
**Pro tip:** The biggest mistake in demos is showing too much. Your prospect doesn't need to see every button and feature. They need to see that you understand their problem and have a solution for it. Show fewer features, but show them better. A great demo is 80% about the prospect's problem and 20% about your product. Also, the best demo closes are surprisingly direct: "based on what I've shown you, does this seem like a good fit?" The answer tells you exactly where you stand

### 4.59 Sales Competitive Positioning
**Use case:** Position your solution effectively against competitors
**Best for:** AEs, sales enablement, product marketing
Act as a competitive positioning strategist. I need to position my solution effectively against competition. Tell me about your product, your top 3 competitors, and what prospects typically compare you on. Then create a competitive positioning strategy: competitive landscape map (for each competitor, document: company size and funding, target market, core product, pricing model, strengths (be honest — knowing their strengths protects you from being surprised), weaknesses (where you win), typical deal size, sales cycle, ideal customer profile. Include positioning: how they position themselves in the market vs. how you position against them); differentiation framework (your key differentiators across: product capabilities (features they don't have), customer experience (support, onboarding, community), business model (pricing, flexibility, terms), brand and reputation (trust, credibility, customers). For each differentiator, rate: uniqueness (how rare is this?), value to customer (how much do they care?), provability (can you prove it?). Focus on differentiators that score high on all three); competitive response plays (for each competitive scenario, have a prepared response: "competitor X is cheaper" — "let's compare total cost of ownership including implementation, training, and support. Our customers typically find that while their initial price is lower, the total cost over 2 years is higher because of X." "Competitor X has feature Y" — "feature Y is interesting. Can I ask what specific problem you're trying to solve with it? [listen]. Here's how we address that same need differently..." "We're using competitor X already" — "that's great. What's working well for you? And what's not working as well as you'd like? Here's what our customers who switched from them typically experience..."); win/loss analysis (track every win and loss with: competitor faced, reason given for decision (buyer's words, not your assumption), deal size, deal stage when lost, sales rep involved, product features evaluated. Analyze quarterly to find patterns: are you consistently losing to one competitor? In one industry? At one stage? Use data to inform product, messaging, and training); competitive enablement (create a competitive intelligence system: weekly competitive monitoring (pricing changes, product launches, funding, hiring, customer wins), monthly competitive brief (what's changed and what it means), quarterly competitive deep-dive (full update on top 3 competitors), on-demand battle cards (one-page summaries for each competitor). Make competitive intelligence accessible to every rep). Provide a battle card template and a win/loss analysis template.
**Expected output:** A competitive positioning strategy with landscape map, differentiation framework, competitive response plays, and win/loss analysis
**Pro tip:** The best competitive strategy is to stop trying to win on their terms. If you're competing on features against a feature-rich competitor you'll always lose. Instead, compete on a dimension where they can't follow: customer experience, speed to value, niche expertise, or business model innovation. Also, teach your reps to NEVER badmouth a competitor — it makes you look insecure. Instead, acknowledge the competitor's strengths honestly, then pivot to where you win

### 4.60 Sales Professional Development Plan
**Use case:** Create a professional development plan for sales professionals
**Best for:** Sales managers, individual contributors, sales enablement
Act as a sales career development coach. I need to create a professional development plan for myself or my team. Tell me about the sales role (SDR, AE, manager), current experience level, and career goals. Then create a development plan: skill assessment (self-assessment across key sales competencies: prospecting, discovery, demo, objection handling, negotiation, closing, forecasting, account planning, communication, time management. Rate 1-5 on each. Identify 2-3 strengths to build on and 2-3 areas for improvement. Include input from manager, peers, and even prospects or customers (feedback)); development goals (set 2-3 specific development goals for the next quarter. Each goal should be: specific ("improve discovery call quality — consistently uncover budget, authority, and timeline in every discovery call"), measurable (score 4+ on discovery call scorecard for 10 consecutive calls), actionable (complete discovery call training, shadow 3 top performers, be shadowed by manager 3 times, record and self-review 10 calls), time-bound (achieve by end of quarter)); learning resources and activities (mix of learning modalities: formal training (courses, certifications, workshops), experiential (on-the-job practice, role-plays, call shadowing, ride-alongs), social (peer learning, mentoring, coaching, team discussions), self-directed (books, podcasts, videos, newsletters). Recommend 2-3 specific resources per development goal); practice and reinforcement (skill development requires deliberate practice: spaced repetition (practice a technique, get feedback, practice again), varied contexts (practice with different prospects, industries, scenarios), increasing difficulty (start with easy scenarios, progress to difficult ones), feedback loops (get immediate feedback from manager, peers, or recorded calls). Create a 90-day practice plan with weekly commitments); tracking and review (monthly check-ins on progress: what's improved, what's still challenging, what adjustment is needed. Quarterly formal review: reassess skills, update goals, celebrate wins. Use a simple tracking document: goal, action items, progress (not started, in progress, achieved, needs adjustment), evidence of improvement, next steps). Include a professional development plan template.
**Expected output:** A sales development plan with skill assessment, goals, learning resources, practice plan, and tracking
**Pro tip:** The best salespeople are lifelong learners. The moment you think you've mastered sales is the moment you start declining. The market changes, buyers change, tools change. The most successful sales professionals I know spend 3-5 hours per week on deliberate skill development — not just consuming content, but actively practicing and getting feedback. Treat your sales skills like a professional athlete treats their game — constant training, review, and improvement

### 5.21 Software Architecture Decision Record
**Use case:** Document and communicate software architecture decisions
**Best for:** Software architects, tech leads, senior engineers
Act as a software architecture document specialist. I need to document an architecture decision. Tell me about the decision you're facing, the options you considered, and what you chose. Then create an Architecture Decision Record (ADR) using this structure: title (ADR-NNN: [Decision Title]. Clear, descriptive, specific); status (proposed, accepted, deprecated, superseded. Track the lifecycle of each decision); context (what's the situation driving this decision? What constraints, requirements, or forces are at play? What's the business or technical context? Write enough that someone reading this 6 months from now understands why this decision was being made); decision (what did you decide? State the decision clearly in one sentence, then elaborate. Describe the chosen option and why it was selected over alternatives); consequences (what tradeoffs did you accept? What becomes easier? What becomes harder? What does this decision enable and what does it prevent? Be honest about downsides); alternatives considered (list each alternative considered with: brief description, why it was rejected, what would need to change for it to become viable. Show that the decision was not made lightly); related decisions (link to other ADRs that relate to this one. Show how decisions connect and form a coherent architecture); key assumptions and risks (what assumptions is this decision based on? What could prove these assumptions wrong? How will you know if this decision needs to be revisited?). Include a ADR template with placeholders.
**Expected output:** A complete Architecture Decision Record with context, decision, consequences, and alternatives
**Pro tip:** ADRs are not about being right — they're about creating an organizational memory. The most valuable ADR is often the one that was WRONG, because it documents why a path was tried and what was learned. Encourage your team to write ADRs for every significant decision, and celebrate the ones that lead to learning, even (especially) when they don't work out. A culture of documented decisions is a culture that learns

### 5.22 CI/CD Pipeline Design
**Use case:** Design a continuous integration and deployment pipeline
**Best for:** DevOps engineers, platform engineers, tech leads
Act as a CI/CD pipeline architect. I need to design a CI/CD pipeline for my project. Tell me about your tech stack, hosting environment, team size, and deployment frequency goals. Then create a pipeline design: pipeline stages (lint → test (unit, integration, e2e) → build → security scan → deploy to staging → integration tests → deploy to production. Each stage should: add value (catch a specific class of issues), be fast (under 5 minutes ideally, under 15 minutes max for the entire pipeline), and have clear pass/fail criteria. Fast feedback is the key principle — fail fast so developers know immediately); branching strategy (mainline development (trunk-based): short-lived feature branches (1-2 days max), frequent merges to main, feature flags for incomplete work. Alternative: GitFlow for projects with strict release cycles. Recommendation: trunk-based for most teams. Feature flags are better than long-lived branches); deployment strategy (blue/green deployment: maintain two identical environments, switch traffic. Zero downtime, easy rollback; canary deployment: roll out to small subset first, monitor, then expand. Good for risk-sensitive changes; rolling deployment: update instances gradually. Standard for most web apps; feature flags: deploy code turned OFF, toggle ON when ready. Best for decoupling deploy from release); pipeline tooling recommendations (GitHub Actions (best for GitHub projects, generous free tier), GitLab CI (excellent if you're on GitLab), Jenkins (most flexible, self-hosted, heavy), CircleCI (fast, good Docker support). Provide pro/cons for each based on team size and project type); pipeline optimization (parallelize independent stages, cache dependencies between runs, use incremental builds, fail fast (most critical checks first), optimize test suite (slowest tests identified and fixed), use artifact caching, compress build outputs). Include a pipeline health dashboard: build success rate, average build time, deployment frequency, time to recover from failure, change failure rate.
**Expected output:** A CI/CD pipeline design with stages, branching strategy, deployment strategy, tooling recommendations, and optimization
**Pro tip:** The most important metric for a CI/CD pipeline is NOT build time — it's "time from commit to production." If it takes 2 weeks from merge to deploy, your CI/CD is not working. Optimize for deployment frequency, not build speed. A 10-minute build that deploys 50 times a day is better than a 2-minute build that deploys once a week. Also, invest in good test coverage before you invest in deployment automation — deploying broken code faster doesn't help anyone

### 5.23 API Design and Documentation Standards
**Use case:** Design consistent, well-documented APIs
**Best for:** Backend engineers, API developers, platform teams
Act as an API design specialist. I need to design or improve my API. Tell me about your service, its consumers (internal, external, mobile, web), and the key resources/operations. Then create an API design standard: RESTful resource design (resources are nouns, not verbs: /users, /orders, /products. Use consistent plural naming. Nest resources logically: /users/{id}/orders. Use HTTP methods correctly: GET (read), POST (create), PUT (replace), PATCH (update), DELETE (remove). Consistent status codes: 200 (success), 201 (created), 204 (no content), 400 (bad request), 401 (unauthorized), 403 (forbidden), 404 (not found), 422 (unprocessable), 500 (server error)); request/response format (use JSON consistently. Standard envelope for responses: { data, error, meta, pagination }. Use camelCase for fields. Include timestamps in ISO 8601 format. Version your API (URL path: /v1/ or header-based)); pagination, filtering, and sorting (consistent pagination: ?page=1&per_page=20 or cursor-based: ?cursor=abc123. Response includes: { data: [...], meta: { total, page, per_page, has_more } }. Support filtering: ?status=active&created_after=2024-01-01. Support sorting: ?sort=created_at&order=desc. Document the shape of list endpoints with all supported parameters); error handling (consistent error format: { error: { code: "VALIDATION_ERROR", message: "Email is required", details: [{ field: "email", issue: "required" }] } }. Use descriptive error codes, not HTTP status alone. Never expose stack traces or internal details in production errors); authentication and authorization (use token-based auth (Bearer tokens) or OAuth 2.0. Include auth header format: Authorization: Bearer <token>. Document permission scopes and which endpoints require which roles. Rate limiting headers: X-RateLimit-Limit, X-RateLimit-Remaining, X-RateLimit-Reset); API documentation standards (documentation should include: endpoint URL and method, description of what it does, request parameters (path, query, body) with types, required/optional, and examples, response format with example, error codes specific to this endpoint, authentication requirements, rate limiting information. Use OpenAPI/Swagger for auto-generated, interactive docs. Keep docs in sync with code — automated documentation generation preferred over manual). Provide an API review checklist for code reviews.
**Expected output:** An API design standard covering resource design, request/response format, pagination, errors, auth, and documentation
**Pro tip:** The best APIs are boring. They follow conventions, they're predictable, and they don't surprise consumers. Before designing a creative new approach, ask: "what's the REST standard for this?" and do that instead. Your API consumers will thank you for being predictable. Also, invest heavily in your API documentation — it's the primary user interface for your API. Good documentation can 10x your API adoption rate

### 5.24 Code Review Best Practices and Process
**Use case:** Establish code review practices that improve code quality without slowing delivery
**Best for:** Engineering teams, tech leads, engineering managers
Act as a code review process consultant. I need to establish or improve code review practices on my team. Tell me about your team size, tech stack, current review process, and biggest pain points. Then create a code review system: review philosophy (code reviews are for: catching bugs and issues, sharing knowledge across the team, maintaining code consistency, mentoring junior developers, building collective code ownership. Code reviews are NOT for: testing (that's what CI/CD is for), style nitpicking (that's what formatters are for), gatekeeping (that's what maintainers are for), personal criticism. Keep reviews constructive, not performative); review checklist (create a standard checklist that every reviewer runs through: correctness — does the code do what it's supposed to?; security — are there any security vulnerabilities?; performance — any obvious performance issues?; maintainability — will this be easy to modify in 6 months?; test coverage — are there tests for the critical paths?; documentation — is the intent clear?; consistency — does it follow the project's patterns?); review size and cadence (PRs should be small: under 400 lines, under 2 hours to review, single logical change. If a PR is too large, ask the author to split it. Review turnaround time: target under 4 hours for standard PRs, under 1 hour for urgent fixes. Blocked reviews should be escalated after 24 hours. No review should sit longer than 48 hours); writing good review comments (use the "why" not "what" approach — instead of "rename this variable," say "this variable name is ambiguous — it could mean X or Y. Can we clarify?" Balance positive and constructive feedback. Start with what's working well. Use "I" statements: "I think this could be clearer if..." Ask questions instead of making demands: "what do you think about...?" Use code suggestions when the fix is straightforward); reviewing as an author (before requesting review: self-review your own diff first, run tests locally, check for leftover debug code/TODOs, write a good PR description explaining the WHAT and WHY, add inline comments on complex sections, indicate what kind of feedback you want ("focus on the architecture, not the style")). Provide a PR description template and a review response template.
**Expected output:** A code review system with philosophy, checklist, size guidelines, commenting best practices, and author preparation
**Pro tip:** The best code review is the one that happens before the code is written. Invest in: (1) good PR descriptions that explain the approach and reasoning, (2) early design discussions before implementation starts, (3) small, focused PRs that are easy to review. If a review takes more than 30 minutes, the PR is probably too big. Also, consider "async first" reviews — let reviewers read at their own pace rather than forcing real-time walkthroughs. But when a real-time walkthrough IS needed (complex architecture changes, security-sensitive code), schedule it — those conversations are valuable

### 5.25 Database Schema Design and Migration Strategy
**Use case:** Design database schemas and manage schema migrations safely
**Best for:** Backend engineers, data engineers, full-stack developers
Act as a database design consultant. I need to design or evolve a database schema. Tell me about your application's data model, access patterns, and scale requirements. Then create a schema design and migration plan: schema design principles (normalize for data integrity, denormalize for read performance (intentionally, with clear reasoning). Choose appropriate data types — use the smallest type that fits your data. Define primary keys for every table. Use foreign keys for referential integrity. Add indexes for your query patterns (not all columns — the ones you filter, join, or sort by). Use consistent naming conventions: snake_case for tables/columns, singular for table names (user, not users), plural for join tables); migration strategy (use versioned, sequential migration files: 001_create_users.sql, 002_add_email_to_users.sql, etc. Every migration must be: reversible (have both an up and down script), idempotent (safe to run multiple times), tested against a copy of production data. Never edit a migration that has already been applied to production — create a new migration to fix issues); migration safety rules (never: drop a column without checking nothing uses it, rename a column (create new + migrate + drop old instead), add a NOT NULL column without a default, run a migration that locks a table for hours on production, deploy schema and code changes in the same deployment (make them separate, coordinated steps). Use expand-contract pattern: expand (add new schema while old still works), migrate (update code to use new schema), contract (remove old schema). This enables zero-downtime migrations); query optimization guidance (explain slow queries with EXPLAIN ANALYZE. Look for: full table scans on large tables, missing indexes on join columns, N+1 query patterns, excessive data retrieval (SELECT * when you need 2 columns), inefficient sorting or grouping. Index recommendations: B-tree for general purpose, BRIN for large time-series data, GiST for full-text search, GIN for array/jsonb lookups. Create indexes for your slowest queries first); backup and recovery (regular automated backups (daily minimum), point-in-time recovery capability, test your recovery process regularly (quarterly). Document restore procedure. Store backups in a different location from your database). Provide a migration review checklist.
**Expected output:** A database design and migration strategy with schema principles, migration safety, query optimization, and backup/recovery
**Pro tip:** The most dangerous database operation is the one you think is simple. "Just adding a column" can lock a production table for hours on large tables. Always test migrations against a production-sized copy of your database before running on production. Use tools like pt-online-schema-change or gh-ost for zero-downtime schema changes on large tables. And never, ever run a migration without a rollback plan

### 5.26 Microservices Architecture Patterns
**Use case:** Design and implement microservices architecture
**Best for:** Software architects, backend engineers, platform teams
Act as a microservices architecture consultant. I need to design a microservices-based system. Tell me about your domain, service boundaries, and key requirements. Then create a microservices architecture guide: service decomposition (identify bounded contexts using Domain-Driven Design: look for natural domain boundaries (subdomains), different rates of change (parts of the system that evolve at different speeds), different scale requirements (parts that need to scale independently), different teams (Conway's Law — services should match team boundaries), different data ownership (parts that own different data). Start with bigger services and split only when there's a clear reason. Premature decomposition is the root of all microservices evil); communication patterns (synchronous: REST or gRPC for request-response. Use for queries, commands that need immediate confirmation. Keep synchronous chains short — avoid deep call chains that create latency and fragility. Asynchronous: message queues (RabbitMQ, Kafka) for events, commands that don't need immediate response. Use for: inter-service notifications, data replication, background processing, eventual consistency workflows. Event-driven: publish events when state changes, let other services subscribe. Best for loose coupling. Each service should use a mix appropriate to its needs); data management (database per service — each service owns its data store. No direct database access from other services. Use the API only. Eventual consistency is the default — accept that data may be temporarily inconsistent across services. Use Saga pattern for distributed transactions: choreography (each service publishes events and reacts to others) or orchestration (a coordinator service manages the workflow). Compensation actions undo partial work if a saga fails); service discovery and resilience (service discovery: DNS-based (simplest), or service registry (Consul, etcd, Kubernetes). Health checks — every service should expose a /health endpoint. Circuit breakers — when a downstream service fails, stop calling it for a while (give it time to recover). Retry with exponential backoff — retry failed calls with increasing delay. Bulkheads — isolate resources so failure in one service doesn't cascade. Timeouts — every external call MUST have a timeout); observability (distributed tracing: trace a request across services (Jaeger, Zipkin, OpenTelemetry). Centralized logging: all services log to a central system (ELK, Loki). Metrics: track service-level KPIs (request rate, error rate, latency percentiles). Dashboards: visualize service health. Alerts: notify on anomalies. Without observability, microservices are unmanageable). Include a service template with standard health check, metrics, and logging setup.
