**Expected output:** A microservices architecture guide with decomposition principles, communication patterns, data management, resilience patterns, and observability
**Pro tip:** Do NOT start with microservices. Start with a monolith. A well-structured monolith is better than a badly decomposed distributed system. Extract services only when: (1) the monolith has clear, stable domain boundaries, (2) you have a team structure that matches the service boundaries, (3) you have CI/CD and observability in place, (4) you have a specific scalability or organizational need that the monolith can't satisfy. Most teams that start with microservices end up with a distributed monolith — the worst of both worlds

### 5.27 Test Strategy and Quality Assurance
**Use case:** Create a comprehensive test strategy for your software project
**Best for:** QA engineers, engineering managers, tech leads
Act as a QA strategy consultant. I need to design a test strategy for my project. Tell me about your tech stack, team size, release frequency, and current testing gaps. Then create a comprehensive test strategy: test pyramid (unit tests (70% of test effort): test individual functions, methods, and components in isolation. Fast (milliseconds), reliable, and targeted. Mock external dependencies. Integration tests (20%): test how components work together — API endpoints, database access, external service integration. Slower (seconds) but catch real interaction bugs. End-to-end tests (10%): test complete user workflows through the entire system. Slowest (minutes) and most fragile, but catch bugs that unit/integration tests miss. This is the classic test pyramid — invert at your own risk); test coverage philosophy (don't chase 100% code coverage — it's a vanity metric that leads to testing trivial code. Instead: cover all critical paths, all error paths, all public APIs, and all business logic. Test behavior, not implementation — tests that break when you refactor are testing the wrong thing. Use coverage to find UNTESTED code, not to set targets); types of testing (functional: does it work correctly? (unit, integration, e2e, manual QA). Non-functional: does it work well? (performance, load, stress, security, accessibility). Regression: did we break anything? (automated regression suite, visual regression, smoke tests). Acceptance: does it meet requirements? (user acceptance testing, dogfooding). Release: is it ready to ship? (smoke tests, canary analysis, rollback readiness)); testing in CI/CD (every push triggers: lint → unit tests (under 3 min) → build → integration tests (under 10 min) → security scan. On merge to main: all of the above + e2e tests + performance benchmarks. On deploy to staging: full test suite + manual exploratory testing. On deploy to production: smoke tests + canary analysis + monitoring); testing culture (developers own test quality — QA enables, but developers are accountable. Write tests as part of the definition of done. No code merges without tests. Test review is part of code review. QA focuses on: exploratory testing, test strategy, automation infrastructure, test data management, and quality metrics); test maintenance (treat tests as production code — review them, refactor them, keep them clean. Remove flaky tests immediately (they erode trust in the entire suite). Flaky test triage process: identify, quarantine, fix, or delete. Test debt is real debt — pay it down regularly). Include a test plan template with test case structure.
**Expected output:** A comprehensive test strategy with test pyramid, coverage philosophy, test types, CI/CD integration, and culture
**Pro tip:** The most expensive test is the one that fails randomly. A flaky test that passes 90% of the time is worse than no test at all — because it teaches developers to ignore test failures. When you find a flaky test, your ONLY options are: (1) fix it immediately, (2) quarantine it (skip it and create a ticket to fix), or (3) delete it. Do not leave flaky tests in your suite. They are a cancer that metastasizes over time and destroys trust in the entire testing process

### 5.28 Production Incident Response Runbook
**Use case:** Create a runbook for responding to production incidents
**Best for:** DevOps, SRE, on-call engineers, platform teams
Act as an incident response specialist. I need to create a runbook for handling production incidents. Tell me about your infrastructure, monitoring tools, team structure, and past incident patterns. Then create an incident response runbook: incident severity levels (SEV-1 (critical): service down or severely degraded, active customer impact, data loss or security breach. Response: immediate, all hands, 15-min SLA for initial response, executive notification. SEV-2 (high): significant feature degradation, partial outage, performance issues affecting many users. Response: within 1 hour, dedicated team, 2-hour SLA for mitigation. SEV-3 (medium): minor feature issues, cosmetic bugs, isolated user impact. Response: next business day, standard team, no SLA. SEV-4 (low): internal tool issues, documentation errors, tech debt. Response: next sprint, normal prioritization); incident response process (detect: monitoring alert, user report, or automated health check. Triage: assess severity, notify appropriate team, decide response level. Respond: stop the bleeding (rollback, feature flag off, traffic redirect), stabilize the system, communicate status. Investigate: find root cause, gather evidence, determine scope of impact. Resolve: apply fix, verify resolution, monitor for recurrence. Learn: incident review (blameless postmortem), document what happened and why, update runbook and monitoring, implement preventive measures); communication templates (incident notification: "INCIDENT: [SEV-1] [Service] — [brief description of impact]. Responders: [names]. Time: [timestamp]. Status: [investigating/mitigated/resolved]. Updates: every [X] minutes." Status update: "Update on [incident]: [what we know], [what we're doing], [expected resolution time], [any workarounds for users]." Resolution: "Incident resolved: [cause], [fix applied], [impact summary], [next steps for prevention]. "); on-call best practices (clear escalation path: primary → secondary → team lead → engineering director. Handoff procedure: document current state before handing off, verbal handoff for ongoing incidents. Well-rested on-call: no more than 1 week on-call per rotation, at least 2 weeks off between rotations. Follow-the-sun for 24/7 coverage if needed. On-call should not have other commitments during their shift); postmortem process (blameless postmortem culture: incidents are system failures, not people failures. Postmortem structure: incident summary, timeline of events, root cause analysis, what went well, what went wrong, action items with owners and deadlines. Publish postmortem internally — transparency builds trust and prevents repeat incidents). Provide an incident report template and a postmortem template.
**Expected output:** An incident response runbook with severity levels, response process, communication templates, on-call practices, and postmortem process
**Pro tip:** The most important incident response rule is: stop the bleeding FIRST, investigate SECOND. In the heat of an incident, engineers often want to understand root cause before applying a fix. This is wrong. First priority is always to restore service — rollback, feature flag off, redirect traffic, whatever it takes. Investigate root cause after users are unblocked. A perfect root cause analysis is worthless if your customers were down for an extra 30 minutes while you did it

### 5.29 Technical Debt Management Strategy
**Use case:** Manage and reduce technical debt systematically
**Best for:** Engineering managers, tech leads, CTOs
Act as a technical debt management consultant. I need to create a strategy for managing technical debt. Tell me about your codebase age, team size, current debt issues, and business constraints. Then create a technical debt management plan: debt classification (types of technical debt: code debt (poor structure, duplication, dead code), design debt (architectural issues, over-engineering, wrong abstractions), test debt (missing or poor tests, flaky tests), documentation debt (missing or outdated docs), infrastructure debt (outdated dependencies, manual processes, configuration drift), knowledge debt (undocumented decisions, knowledge silos, bus factor). Not all debt is bad — some is strategic (taken intentionally to meet deadlines) — track that separately); debt quantification (use a simple scoring system: impact on development speed (1-5: 5 = slows everyone down daily), impact on quality (1-5: 5 = causes production bugs), cost to fix (1-5: 5 = requires major refactor), risk of fixing (1-5: 5 = high risk of breaking things). Total score = impact + cost + risk. Prioritize items with high impact and low cost first — low-hanging fruit builds momentum and trust in the debt reduction program); the "boy scout rule" (leave the code cleaner than you found it. When you touch a file, spend 10-20% of your time improving its quality. Over time, this compounds into significant improvement without dedicated debt sprints. This is the most sustainable approach to debt management); dedicated debt reduction (the 20% rule: reserve 20% of each sprint for debt reduction. Debt sprints: occasional dedicated sprints focused entirely on debt. Track progress: debt item count, debt score trends, development velocity changes, bug rate changes. Don't try to fix all debt at once — focus on the hotspots that are causing the most pain); debt prevention (prevent future debt through: code review that catches shortcuts, definition of done that includes quality criteria, architecture decisions that consider long-term maintainability, automated quality gates in CI/CD, regular dependency updates, documentation as part of development). Provide a technical debt register template with scoring columns.
**Expected output:** A technical debt management strategy with classification, quantification, reduction approaches, and prevention
**Pro tip:** The best way to make technical debt manageable is to prevent it in the first place. The "it's just a quick hack, we'll fix it later" promise is almost never kept. "Later" is a myth — either fix it now or accept that it becomes permanent debt. If you must take on debt for a deadline, document it explicitly (in a debt register, with a clear plan to address it), and make sure the person who approved the deadline knows the cost. Technical debt with a plan to repay is a business decision. Technical debt without a plan is just negligence

### 5.30 Open Source Contribution and Management
**Use case:** Contribute to open source projects or manage your own open source project
**Best for:** Developers, engineering teams, open source maintainers
Act as an open source strategy consultant. I need to contribute to or manage an open source project. Tell me about your experience level, the project you want to work with, and your goals. Then create an open source guide: finding projects to contribute to (start with projects you already use — you know their domain and pain points. Look for "good first issue," "help wanted," or "beginner-friendly" labels. Use sites like Up For Grabs or CodeTriage. Match project activity: active (recent commits, responsive maintainers, community), sustainable (has contributing guidelines, code of conduct, clear issue templates), and welcoming (responds positively to new contributors)); making your first contribution (start small: documentation fixes, typo corrections, test improvements, simple bug fixes. Read CONTRIBUTING.md thoroughly before doing anything. Set up the development environment following project instructions. Join the community (Slack, Discord, mailing list) and introduce yourself. Comment on an issue you'd like to work on to let maintainers know. Submit a focused, well-tested PR. Respond graciously to feedback — even if it feels harsh); running an open source project (clear README: what the project does, how to install/use it, how to contribute, license. Contributing guidelines: how to set up dev environment, coding standards, PR process, issue reporting guidelines. Code of conduct: expected behavior, reporting process, enforcement. Issue templates: bug report, feature request, question templates that guide contributors to provide useful information. PR template: description, testing done, related issues, checklist. Release process: semantic versioning, changelog, release notes, distribution/publishing); maintainer sustainability (set boundaries: define your availability, use automation to reduce workload (dependabot, stale bot, CI), batch reviews (set aside specific time for PR review), delegate (add maintainers, empower contributors, use triage team). Maintainer burnout is real — protect your time and energy. It's OK to say no. It's OK to take a break. It's OK to archive a project that no longer serves you); building a community around your project (responsive and respectful communication, regular releases and updates, acknowledge contributors publicly (README, release notes), create contribution opportunities (good first issues, hackathons, mentorship), foster a welcoming, inclusive environment, share credit generously). Include a CONTRIBUTING.md template and a GitHub issue template.
**Expected output:** An open source contribution and management guide covering finding projects, making contributions, running a project, and building community
**Pro tip:** The most underappreciated contributions to open source are NOT code. Documentation, triage, community management, design, and testing are all critical to a project's health. If you're new to open source or have non-coding skills, these are excellent ways to contribute. Maintainers often appreciate documentation contributions MORE than code because nobody enjoys writing docs. A well-documented project that's hard to use is better than an undocumented project that's elegant

### 6.11 Customer Health Score Framework
**Use case:** Build a health scoring system to identify at-risk and high-potential customers
**Best for:** Customer success, account management, renewals
Act as a customer health scoring specialist. I need to create a health scoring system for my customer base. Tell me about your product, typical customer journey, and what data you track. Then create a health score framework: health score components (product usage — login frequency, feature adoption, time in product, key actions taken. Support interactions — ticket volume, severity, response time satisfaction, resolution time. Engagement — meeting attendance, email response rate, NPS/satisfaction surveys, community participation. Outcomes — goal progress, time-to-value, ROI achieved, key milestones. Account health — payment history, contract duration, stakeholder changes, competitive pressure. Each component gets a score of 1-10, weighted by importance); scoring methodology (weighted average approach: determine relative importance of each component (e.g., product usage 35%, support 20%, engagement 15%, outcomes 20%, account 10%). Normalize each metric to a 1-10 scale using percentile or benchmark-based scoring. Calculate weighted score. Define thresholds: green (healthy, 7.5+), yellow (at risk, 4.5-7.4), red (critical, below 4.5). Review and adjust weights quarterly based on correlation with renewal outcomes); health score automation (build automated scoring using product analytics, support ticketing system, CRM data, and survey tools. Set up alerts when a customer's score drops by more than 1 point in a week, enters yellow or red zone, or shows specific negative triggers (key user stops logging in, support tickets spike, executive sponsor leaves)); health score-driven actions (for each segment: green — nurture and expand: share best practices, invite to events, offer beta features, ask for referral; yellow — intervene: schedule strategic review, provide additional training, assign escalation support, identify root cause of decline; red — save: executive sponsor outreach, create formal recovery plan, offer extended trial or discount, escalate to leadership. Define specific playbooks for each health score segment); health score review process (weekly automated review of all accounts with score changes >1. Monthly manual review of all yellow/red accounts by CS team. Quarterly comprehensive review of all accounts with segment strategy. Track: health score trends over time, % of accounts in each segment, correlation between health score and renewal, intervention effectiveness). Provide a health score calculation template and a segment-specific action playbook.
**Expected output:** A customer health score framework with component definitions, scoring methodology, automation, and action playbooks
**Pro tip:** Health scores are only useful if they PREDICT behavior, not just DESCRIBE it. A good health score should tell you which customers will renew (or churn) with 80%+ accuracy, 30 days before they make that decision. Back-test your health score against historical data — did customers who churned have a low score before they churned? If not, your scoring methodology needs work. Also, leading indicators (usage trends, engagement changes) are more predictive than lagging ones (survey scores, support volume)

### 6.12 Customer Onboarding Experience Design
**Use case:** Design an onboarding experience that gets customers to value faster
**Best for:** Customer success, product management, growth
Act as a customer onboarding specialist. I need to design or improve my customer onboarding experience. Tell me about your product, ideal customer profile, and what "time to value" looks like. Then create an onboarding experience design: onboarding principles (get to value fast — the first experience should deliver value in minutes, not hours. Remove friction — every step should be necessary, every question should matter. Guide, don't overwhelm — progressive disclosure: show only what they need at each step. Celebrate progress — acknowledge milestones, show progress toward outcomes. Personalize — different customer segments need different onboarding paths. Set expectations — tell them what to expect and how long it will take); onboarding journey map (awareness stage (before purchase): pre-boarding email sequence (welcome, what to expect, what to prepare), access to pre-onboarding resources (setup guides, prerequisites checklist). Activation stage (first login): account setup (guided, minimal fields), initial configuration (wizard, not form), quick-start flow (first meaningful action in under 5 minutes), success moment (something positive happens — data appears, first output generated). Adoption stage (first week): guided tour of key features (3-5 core features max), first milestone (complete a key workflow), check-in (automated message or call), resource library (contextual help, not generic docs). Expansion stage (first month): advanced feature introduction (1 per week), best practices sharing, peer community invitation, first business review scheduled); time-to-value optimization (map the ideal path from signup to first value. Identify: every step, every click, every decision they must make. Remove unnecessary steps. Pre-fill where possible. Provide templates and defaults. Offer onboarding calls or automated walkthroughs. Measure time-to-value for each segment. Set a target: "90% of customers should achieve first value within 7 days"); onboarding interventions (types of interventions: in-app guidance (tooltips, checklists, progress bars), email sequences (automated, behavior-triggered), chat/phone support (on-demand or scheduled), live onboarding calls (for high-touch segments), customer success manager (for enterprise). Match intervention intensity to segment: low-touch (automated only), mid-touch (automated + email + optional call), high-touch (dedicated CSM + live onboarding)); onboarding metrics and improvement (track: activation rate (% who complete key actions), time to first value, onboarding completion rate, drop-off points (where do customers abandon onboarding?), support requests during onboarding, satisfaction score post-onboarding. A/B test onboarding flows: compare guided vs. self-serve, video vs. text, call vs. no call. Continuously improve based on data). Provide an onboarding journey map template and an onboarding email sequence.
**Expected output:** A customer onboarding experience design with principles, journey map, time-to-value optimization, and interventions
**Pro tip:** The most important metric in onboarding is NOT completion rate — it's TIME TO FIRST VALUE. How quickly does a new customer experience the "aha moment" where they realize your product is worth paying for? Every day between signup and first value is a day they might churn. Optimize ruthlessly for speed. If you can get a customer to value in 5 minutes instead of 5 days, your retention will increase dramatically. Remove every step that doesn't directly contribute to delivering value

### 6.13 Customer Success Quarterly Business Review
**Use case:** Plan and execute effective Quarterly Business Reviews (QBRs) with customers
**Best for:** Customer success managers, account managers, CS leadership
Act as a QBR specialist. I need to plan and run a Quarterly Business Review with my customer. Tell me about the customer, their goals, our product usage, and the relationship history. Then create a QBR framework: QBR objectives (align on goals — are we still focused on the right outcomes? Review value delivered — what results has the customer achieved? Identify gaps — what's not working or not being adopted? Plan next quarter — what should we focus on next? Strengthen relationship — build executive sponsorship and trust. A QBR is NOT a status update. It's a strategic conversation); QBR agenda (90-minute format: 5 min — welcome and agenda (set the tone: "this is your time to review our partnership"); 15 min — business review (what's happened in their business this quarter? What changed? What's the current state of their priorities? Let them talk first — listen more than you present); 20 min — value delivered (show the results they've achieved using your product. Quantify as much as possible: ROI, time saved, revenue impact, efficiency gained. Connect product usage to business outcomes); 15 min — challenges and opportunities (what's not working? What features are underutilized? What new needs have emerged? Be honest about shortcomings. Your vulnerability builds trust); 20 min — roadmap and planning (what will we focus on next quarter? New goals, new use cases, expansion opportunities. Set specific, measurable objectives for next quarter); 10 min — feedback and close (collect feedback on your partnership. Ask: what should we start/stop/continue? Summarize action items with owners and dates)); QBR preparation (gather data: usage analytics, support ticket history, NPS/satisfaction scores, previous QBR notes, product roadmap relevant to this customer. Prepare: value summary deck, usage trends analysis, expansion opportunities identified, strategic questions to discuss); value realization summary (for each QBR, create a clear value narrative: what the customer's situation was before (baseline), what outcomes they've achieved (quantified), what they would have done without you (the alternative), what's possible next (expansion opportunities). The goal is to make the customer feel that their investment is paying off and they'd be worse off without you); QBR follow-up (within 24 hours: send thank-you with summary of key points, action items and owners, and next QBR date. Within 1 week: share any additional data or resources promised. Throughout the quarter: track progress on action items, reference QBR commitments in regular touchpoints). Include a QBR presentation template with slide-by-slide guidance.
**Expected output:** A QBR framework with objectives, agenda, preparation guide, and follow-up process
**Pro tip:** The best QBRs are 80% listening, 20% presenting. Your customer knows their business better than you do. Your job is to connect their business outcomes to your product's value. The most powerful QBR question: "if you could change one thing about our partnership, what would it be?" The answer will tell you everything you need to know about the health of the relationship. Also, never surprise your customer in a QBR — if there's bad news, share it before the meeting

### 6.14 Customer Escalation Management Process
**Use case:** Handle customer escalations effectively and turn crises into loyalty
**Best for:** Customer support, customer success, account management
Act as a customer escalation management specialist. I need to handle a customer escalation. Tell me about the customer, the issue, what's been tried so far, and the customer's emotional state. Then create an escalation management process: escalation definition and levels (Level 1: standard support issue. Handled by support team within SLA. Level 2: complex issue requiring specialist or engineering involvement. Expected resolution within 2 business days. Level 3: critical issue — major product bug, security concern, VIP customer at risk of churn. Requires executive involvement and 24/7 attention. Level 4: crisis — public relations issue, legal concern, data breach. Requires CEO and legal involvement. Clear criteria for each level — not based on customer volume/intensity but on objective factors); escalation response protocol (acknowledge immediately — within 1 hour of escalation, acknowledge the issue. "Thank you for raising this. I understand this is important and we're taking it seriously." Name the escalation coordinator — one person owns the issue end-to-end. Set expectations — "here's what we know, here's what we're doing, here's when you'll hear from us next." Over-communicate — provide status updates proactively, even if there's no new information. Every 4 hours for critical issues, every 24 hours for high priority); de-escalation techniques (listen actively without interrupting, acknowledge their frustration ("I understand why you're upset, and I would feel the same way"), apologize sincerely without being defensive ("I'm sorry this happened. Let me make it right"), separate emotion from problem — validate feelings while working the issue, focus on solutions not blame, do not make promises you can't keep, under-promise and over-deliver on resolution timing); escalation coordinator responsibilities (single point of contact for the customer, coordinate internal resources (support, engineering, product, leadership), provide regular status updates to customer and internal stakeholders, document everything (timeline, actions, decisions), drive issue to resolution, conduct post-mortem after resolution. The escalation coordinator has authority to pull in any resource needed to resolve the issue.); post-escalation recovery (after resolution: follow up within 24 hours to confirm everything is working, conduct internal postmortem (what happened, why, how to prevent), consider service recovery (credit, discount, extended trial) based on severity, schedule executive check-in for major escalations, update processes and documentation to prevent recurrence, train team on lessons learned). Include an escalation request form and an escalation response template.
**Expected output:** An escalation management process with defined levels, response protocol, de-escalation techniques, and recovery process
**Pro tip:** The way you handle a crisis determines whether you lose the customer forever or earn their loyalty for life. Customers who had a problem that was handled WELL are MORE loyal than customers who never had a problem at all. The key is: acknowledge fast, communicate proactively, take ownership, and go above and beyond in recovery. A great escalation response can turn a detractor into a promoter. Also, always apologize even if it wasn't your fault — "I'm sorry you experienced this" is not an admission of guilt, it's an expression of empathy

### 6.15 Customer Success Playbook Creation
**Use case:** Create a comprehensive customer success playbook for your team
**Best for:** Customer success leaders, CS operations, enablement
Act as a customer success playbook developer. I need to create a CS playbook for my organization. Tell me about your product, customer segments, CS team structure, and key customer journey stages. Then create a playbook structure: playbook philosophy (our approach to customer success, our values, what we're measured on (retention, expansion, satisfaction), how we define customer health. This section sets the foundation for all plays that follow); lifecycle plays (playbooks for each stage of the customer journey: onboarding (day 1-30) — welcome call, setup completion, first value achievement, training plan; adoption (month 1-3) — feature adoption goals, best practices sharing, regular check-ins, success milestones definition; growth (month 3-12) — QBRs, expansion opportunities identification, advocate development, product feedback loop; renewal (month 11-12) — renewal readiness assessment, value reinforcement, contract negotiation, multi-year commitment); segment-specific plays (enterprise plays: executive engagement, governance meetings, custom training, dedicated support, strategic roadmap alignment. Mid-market plays: regular check-ins, group training, community participation, self-serve resources. SMB plays: automated engagement, in-app guidance, digital touch, scalable support. Each segment needs different intensity, different tools, and different success metrics); trigger-based plays (event-driven plays that automatically trigger when specific conditions are met: usage drops below threshold → reach out with training or re-engagement; support ticket spikes → check-in and offer proactive help; executive sponsor leaves → immediate outreach and relationship rebuilding; feature adoption is low → targeted training or use case exploration; NPS survey is negative → personal outreach within 24 hours; customer achieves milestone → celebrate and ask for testimonial); tool and resource guide (CRM and CS platform usage: how to log activities, update health scores, track tasks. Communication templates: email templates for each play, call scripts for key touchpoints, presentation templates for QBRs. Escalation guide: when and how to escalate, who to escalate to, escalation template); performance standards (definitions of success for each role: CSM: retention rate, expansion revenue, health score coverage, QBR completion rate. Support: CSAT, first response time, resolution time, ticket volume trends. Team: NRR, churn rate, time-to-value, adoption benchmarks). Include a play template for creating new plays as the team learns what works.
**Expected output:** A customer success playbook with lifecycle plays, segment-specific plays, trigger-based plays, and performance standards
**Pro tip:** A playbook is only valuable if it's LIVING — updated based on what actually works, not what you think should work. The best plays come from your top performers. Give them a template and ask them to document their best practices. Review and update the playbook quarterly. Retire plays that don't work. Add new ones as you learn. A playbook that sits on a shelf is worse than no playbook — it gives the illusion of process without the reality

### 6.16 Customer Churn Analysis and Prevention
**Use case:** Analyze why customers churn and build prevention strategies
**Best for:** Customer success, data analytics, revenue operations
Act as a churn analysis specialist. I need to understand why customers are churning and prevent it. Share your churn data: who churned, when, why (if known), and what their usage looked like before churning. Then create a churn analysis and prevention plan: churn analysis framework (categorize churn by type: voluntary churn (customer actively decides to leave) — worse product, cheaper competitor, no longer need, poor support, budget cuts. Involuntary churn (payment failure, account issues) — expired credit card, failed payment, admin deactivation. Identify patterns: which segments churn most (industry, company size, plan type, acquisition channel), which behaviors precede churn (usage decline, support ticket increase, champion leaves, not engaging with CS), when do customers churn (after how many months, after which events)); churn root cause analysis (use the "5 Whys" technique to dig past surface reasons. When a customer says "too expensive," ask: why was price an issue? Because they didn't see enough value. Why didn't they see enough value? Because they weren't using the key features. Why weren't they using key features? Because onboarding didn't highlight them. The real root cause is poor onboarding, not price. Conduct exit interviews with churned customers: "what was the primary reason you left?" "what could we have done differently?" "what would need to be true for you to come back?" "what product/were you using instead?" Build a churn database tracking root causes and patterns); leading indicator identification (find the early warning signals that predict churn 30-60 days before it happens. Common leading indicators: login frequency drops below threshold, feature adoption plateaus or declines, support ticket volume spikes, NPS drops, executive sponsor leaves company, no engagement with CS in 45+ days, competitor mentioned in conversation, renewal conversation gets pushed or deprioritized. Score and weight these indicators to create a churn prediction model. Validate against historical data); prevention strategies (for each churn root cause, design a prevention play: lack of adoption → enhanced onboarding, feature training, use case exploration; poor support → support quality improvement, faster response times, proactive outreach; price sensitivity → value reinforcement, ROI reporting, flexible pricing options; competition → competitive differentiation messaging, switching cost creation, relationship strengthening; no longer need → explore adjacent use cases, offer migration to different plan, graceful offboarding); win-back program (for customers who already churned: segment by reason for churn, timing (recent vs long-gone), and value (high-value vs low). Create win-back campaigns: email sequence with product improvements since they left, case studies of similar companies succeeding, exclusive offer for returning customers, personal outreach from CS leader. Track win-back conversion rate and recovery value). Provide a churn analysis dashboard template with key metrics.
**Expected output:** A churn analysis and prevention plan with root cause analysis, leading indicators, prevention strategies, and win-back program
**Pro tip:** The best churn prevention is detection BEFORE the customer decides to leave. By the time a customer tells you they're leaving, their mind is 90% made up. Build systems that detect leading indicators (declining usage, disengaged stakeholders, support pattern changes) 30-60 days before renewal. At that point, you still have time to intervene. Also, not all churn is bad — if your product isn't right for a customer, it's better for both sides if they leave gracefully. Happy churned customers become referrals

### 6.17 Customer Advocacy and Referral Program
**Use case:** Build a customer advocacy program that generates referrals and social proof
**Best for:** Customer success, marketing, community
Act as a customer advocacy program designer. I need to create a program that turns happy customers into advocates. Tell me about your product, ideal customer profile, and current referral activity. Then create an advocacy program: advocate identification (find your best candidates: high NPS promoters (9-10), long-tenured customers (12+ months), high product usage, customers who have given unsolicited positive feedback, customers who actively participate in community/champions program, customers with relevant industry influence (bloggers, speakers, well-connected). Create an advocate score: NPS (25%), tenure (15%), usage (20%), engagement (20%), influence (20%). Focus on the top 10% of scored customers); advocacy program tiers (create a tiered program with increasing benefits: tier 1 — supporters: share social media posts, provide testimonials. Rewards: early access, swag, community recognition. Tier 2 — promoters: participate in case studies, speak at events, join advisory board. Rewards: exclusive events, product roadmap input, executive access. Tier 3 — champions: reference calls for prospects, co-marketing, product feedback. Rewards: advisory board, compensation/commission, partner status. Higher tiers should require more commitment and offer more rewards); advocacy activities (what advocates can do: provide testimonials (written or video), participate in case studies, join reference calls for prospects, speak at user conferences or webinars, write reviews on G2/Capterra, contribute to community (forums, Slack), refer other customers, participate in beta programs, join customer advisory board, co-create content (blog posts, whitepapers). Make it easy — provide templates, coaching, and support for each activity); referral program design (structure: referral reward (discount, gift card, or cash for both referrer and referee), referral tracking (unique link or code), automated fulfillment, terms and conditions (limit per customer, eligibility criteria). Promote the program: in-app notification, email signature, QBR mention, post-purchase follow-up. Optimize: test different reward amounts, test ask timing (right after success moment), test promotion channels, measure referral conversion rate and cost per acquisition); program measurement (track: advocate acquisition (new advocates per month), advocate engagement (activities completed per advocate), referral volume and conversion rate, case study and testimonial pipeline, reference call request fulfillment rate, program ROI (revenue from referrals and advocacy vs. program cost)). Provide an advocate outreach email template and a referral program one-pager.
**Expected output:** A customer advocacy program with identification, tiers, activities, referral design, and measurement
**Pro tip:** The best time to ask for a referral is immediately after a success moment — right after a customer achieves a goal, gives positive feedback, or says "this is amazing." Their willingness to refer is highest when they're actively feeling the value. Automate these requests based on in-app triggers (completed a key workflow, achieved a milestone, hit a usage threshold). And always make it easy — a referral link they can share in one click converts 3x better than asking them to manually introduce you

### 6.18 Customer Success Metrics and Reporting
**Use case:** Build a customer success metrics dashboard that drives action
**Best for:** CS operations, CS leadership, data analysts
Act as a CS metrics specialist. I need to build a metrics and reporting system for my customer success team. Tell me about your business model (subscription, usage-based, etc.), current data sources, and reporting needs. Then create a CS metrics framework: core CS metrics (retention metrics: logo retention rate (customers retained / customers at start), net revenue retention (revenue from existing customers including expansion - contraction - churn / revenue at start), gross revenue retention (revenue retained excluding expansion). Growth metrics: net new ARR (expansion revenue - contraction revenue - churned revenue), expansion rate (upsells + cross-sells / existing revenue), customer lifetime value (average revenue per customer / churn rate), payback period (CAC / monthly recurring revenue). Health metrics: NPS (customer satisfaction survey), CSAT (support satisfaction), health score distribution (% green/yellow/red), feature adoption rate (% of customers using key features). Efficiency metrics: churn rate (customers lost / total customers), time-to-value (days from signup to first value), CAC ratio (customer acquisition cost), CSM book of business size (accounts per CSM)); metric segmentation (slice all metrics by: customer segment (enterprise, mid-market, SMB), plan tier (basic, pro, enterprise), industry vertical, acquisition channel, customer age (0-3 months, 3-12 months, 12+ months), region, product line. Different segments will have different benchmarks. Track trends within each segment, not just aggregate. The aggregate hides important variance); reporting cadence and audience (daily automated dashboard: health score changes, new at-risk accounts, new expansion opportunities, support ticket trends. Weekly team report: weekly highlights (wins, risks), segment health trends, action items from review meetings, pipeline of QBRs and renewals. Monthly business review: comprehensive metric summary, trends and insights, segment deep-dive, forecast and risks, recommendations. Quarterly board report: NRR, churn, health score trends, program ROI, strategic initiatives progress. Executive dashboard: top-line KPIs, forecast confidence, risk summary, expansion pipeline); action-oriented reporting (every metric should lead to a decision or action. A report that shows churn increased is useless. A report that shows "churn increased because segment X had usage drop 40% in the 30 days before renewal" drives specific action. For each metric, define: current value, target, trend (improving/declining/steady), segment breakdown, insight (what this means), action (what to do about it), owner (who's responsible)); data infrastructure recommendations (tool stack: CRM (source of truth), product analytics (usage data), support platform (ticket data), survey tool (NPS/CSAT), BI tool (dashboard and reporting), CS platform (health scores, workflows). Automate data pipeline to reduce manual work. A single source of truth prevents conflicting numbers. Data quality: ensure data is clean, consistent, and complete. Document definitions clearly. Deduplicate. Set up data validation rules). Provide a CS metric definition table with calculation formulas.
**Expected output:** A CS metrics and reporting framework with core metrics, segmentation, reporting cadence, and action-oriented reporting
**Pro tip:** The most important CS metric is not churn rate or NPS — it's NET REVENUE RETENTION (NRR). NRR tells you whether your existing customers are growing or shrinking. An NRR above 100% means your existing book is growing even without new customers — that's a healthy business. Below 100% means you need new customers just to stay flat. Track NRR by segment, by age, and by CSM. It's the single best measure of customer success effectiveness

### 6.19 Customer Success Team Structure and Hiring
**Use case:** Design your customer success team structure and hire the right people
**Best for:** CS leadership, founders, HR
Act as a CS organizational design consultant. I need to structure my customer success team. Tell me about your customer base size, segments, current team, and growth plans. Then create a team design: CS team models (pooled model: CSMs own all accounts within a segment. Best for: small teams, simple products, homogeneous customer base. Pros: simple, clear ownership. Cons: doesn't scale, CSMs become bottlenecks. Specialized model: different roles for different lifecycle stages (onboarding specialist, adoption CSM, renewal specialist). Best for: complex products, long lifecycle, high volume. Pros: expertise, efficiency. Cons: multiple handoffs, customer may feel passed around. Hybrid model: CSM owns relationship but specialists handle specific stages. Best for: mid-market to enterprise. Pros: relationship continuity + specialist expertise. Cons: coordination overhead. Tiered model: different service tiers mapped to customer value (digital touch for SMB, pooled CSM for mid-market, dedicated CSM for enterprise). Best for: broad customer base with varying value. Pros: efficient resource allocation. Cons: complexity in managing tiers); key CS roles (CSM: owns customer relationships, drives adoption, manages health, identifies expansion opportunities. 1 CSM per $1-2M ARR book of business, or 30-50 accounts for mid-market, 5-15 for enterprise. Onboarding specialist: owns first 30-90 days, drives time-to-value, hands off to CSM after adoption threshold met. 1 per 50-100 new customers per month. Renewal specialist: owns renewal negotiations, contract management, pricing. 1 per $5-10M renewal book. CS ops: builds processes, tools, analytics, reporting. 1 per 10-15 CSMs. Customer support: handles ticket-based issues. 1 per 500-1000 active users. Community manager: builds peer community, facilitates connections. 1 per 1000+ active community members); hiring criteria (for CS roles, look for: empathy — ability to understand customer perspective; business acumen — understands ROI, can discuss business outcomes; communication — clear, concise, persuasive; product aptitude — learns technology quickly; problem-solving — can diagnose issues and drive resolution; organization — manages multiple priorities and accounts; resilience — handles difficult customer situations without getting discouraged. Sample interview questions: "tell me about a time you turned an unhappy customer around," "how would you explain the value of our product to a non-technical executive," "how do you prioritize across 30 accounts with competing needs?"); career progression (create clear career paths: associate CSM → CSM → senior CSM → principal CSM → CS manager → director of CS → VP of CS. Alternate paths: CSM → product (customer insights become product requirements), CSM → sales (relationships turn into expansion revenue), CSM → success operations (tooling and process), CSM → community (peer programs and events)); team culture and values (CS team culture principles: customer-first (we advocate for the customer internally), data-informed (we use data to drive decisions, not opinions), proactive (we reach out before customers have to reach out), collaborative (we work across teams to serve the customer), continuous improvement (we learn from every interaction)). Include a CS job description template for each role.
**Expected output:** A CS team structure guide with models, roles, hiring criteria, career progression, and culture
**Pro tip:** The best CSMs are NOT former support agents or salespeople. They are relationship-builders who combine business acumen with product knowledge and genuine empathy. Look for people who can have strategic conversations with executives AND show a customer how to use a feature. This combination is rare — invest in training to develop it. Also, the best CS leaders have usually been CSMs themselves. Don't promote someone into CS management who hasn't done the role

### 6.20 Customer Success Automation and Scaling
**Use case:** Automate customer success processes to scale without adding headcount
**Best for:** CS operations, CS leadership, revenue operations
Act as a CS automation specialist. I need to automate and scale my customer success operations. Tell me about your current processes, team size, customer count, and what's most repetitive. Then create a CS automation plan: what to automate first (prioritize automation candidates: low complexity (easy to automate), high frequency (happens often), low customization (same for most customers), high volume (touches many customers). Best candidates: health score monitoring and alerts, email sequences (onboarding, check-ins, renewal reminders), support ticket triage and routing, data aggregation and reporting, survey collection and analysis, meeting scheduling and reminders, renewal quote generation, feature adoption tracking and outreach, contract documentation); automation tooling (recommended tech stack for CS automation: CS platform (Gainsight, Totango, ChurnZero, Catalyst) — health scores, playbooks, workflows; Email automation (Intercom, Customer.io, HubSpot) — triggered email sequences; Data integration (Zapier, Workato, Tray) — connect tools without code; Scheduling (Calendly, Chili Piper) — automated meeting booking; Document automation (PandaDoc, DocuSign) — renewal quotes, contracts; Survey (SurveyMonkey, Typeform, Delighted) — NPS, CSAT collection. Evaluate build vs. buy — for most companies, buying is faster and cheaper than building CS automation); digital-touch engagement (automated engagement sequences that scale to hundreds or thousands of accounts: triggered email campaigns (based on usage, health, lifecycle stage), in-app messaging and guidance (product tours, tips, announcements), automated check-in emails ("it's been a while — here's what's new"), milestone celebrations (anniversaries, achievements, feature adoption), re-engagement campaigns for dormant users, educational content drip (tips, best practices, use cases), renewal reminders and value reinforcement); playbook automation (convert your manual plays into automated workflows: play trigger (user stops logging in) → auto-create task for CSM and send re-engagement email → if no response in 7 days, escalate notification to manager → if response, route reply to CSM with context. For each play: trigger, actions (automated and human), timing, escalation rules, success criteria. Start with 5-10 highest-impact plays and expand based on results); balancing automation and personal touch (rules for when to automate vs. when to use human touch: automate information delivery (reports, updates, reminders), automate data collection (surveys, usage reports), automate simple triggers ("low usage" alerts). Use human touch for: strategic conversations (QBRs, expansion discussions), sensitive situations (escalations, churn risk, complaints), relationship building (executive engagement, personal check-ins), complex problem-solving (custom solutions, training). The goal is to free CSMs from repetitive work so they can focus on high-value interactions). Provide an automation opportunity assessment template.
**Expected output:** A CS automation strategy with automation candidates, tooling, digital-touch engagement, playbook automation, and human touch balance
**Pro tip:** The most common mistake in CS automation is automating a BAD process — you just get bad results faster. Before automating anything, make sure the manual process works well. Map it, optimize it, document it, THEN automate it. Also, be careful not to automate away the human connection. Your customers should feel like you care about them, not that they're in a machine. Personalize automated messages, use variable fields, reference specific behaviors, and always give them a way to reach a real person

### 6.21 Customer Success and Product Feedback Loop
**Use case:** Create a feedback loop between customer success and product teams
**Best for:** CS leadership, product management, customer operations
Act as a CS-product feedback loop specialist. I need to build a system where customer insights drive product decisions. Tell me about your current product development process, how feedback is collected, and where the gap is. Then create a feedback loop system: feedback collection channels (sources of customer feedback: support tickets (issues and frustrations), CSM conversations (feature requests, pain points, workarounds), QBRs (strategic needs, feature gaps), NPS survey comments (quantitative + qualitative), usage data (features used/ignored, drop-off points), sales discovery (reasons customers chose you or lost to competitors), churn interviews (why they left), product reviews (G2, Capterra, App Store), community forums (questions, requests, complaints), beta feedback (what's working/not in new features)); feedback triage and prioritization (collect all feedback in a central repository (productboard, Canny, Airtable). Tag each item with: source, customer, severity (showstopper, important, nice-to-have), frequency (how many customers affected), revenue impact, strategic alignment. Score: frequency (1-5) x impact (1-5) x alignment (1-5) = priority score. Triage weekly. Route: bugs to engineering immediately, small feature requests to product backlog, strategic insights to product roadmap discussions, and trends to quarterly planning); CS role in product development (CS should be involved in: product discovery (CSMs participate in customer research calls), beta programs (CS nominates and manages beta customers), feature prioritization (CS provides customer impact data), release feedback (CS gathers early reactions to new features), roadmapping (CS input on what customers need most). Give product team direct access to CS data: health scores, usage patterns, churn reasons, feature adoption rates. Create a "CS says" channel in the product team's Slack/discord); closing the loop with customers (when a customer's feedback leads to a product change: notify them personally: "you asked for X — it's now live." Show their impact: "your feedback helped us improve X for all customers." Invite them to beta new features. Ask for feedback on the implementation. Customers who see their feedback implemented are 3x more likely to be promoters. They feel heard and valued. If feedback can't be implemented, explain why — "we decided not to build X because of Y reason, but here's an alternative approach"); feedback loop metrics (track: feedback items collected per month, feedback items implemented per quarter, average time from feedback to implementation, customer notification rate (how many customers were told their feedback was implemented), CS satisfaction with product responsiveness, product team satisfaction with CS input quality). Provide a feedback intake form template and a feedback triage rubric.
**Expected output:** A CS-product feedback loop with collection channels, triage, CS involvement, customer communication, and metrics
**Pro tip:** The most important rule of the CS-product feedback loop: CLOSE THE LOOP WITH THE CUSTOMER. There's nothing worse than giving feedback and never hearing what happened. Even if you can't implement their request, tell them why. "We decided not to build X because it doesn't align with our product direction, but here's an alternative approach that might help" is infinitely better than silence. Customers who feel heard stay longer, even if their specific request isn't fulfilled

### 6.22 Customer Success for SaaS Metrics Deep Dive
**Use case:** Understand and optimize key SaaS metrics from a customer success perspective
**Best for:** CS leaders, SaaS founders, revenue operations
Act as a SaaS metrics specialist. I need to understand and improve my company's SaaS metrics from a CS perspective. Tell me about your business model, pricing, customer segments, and current metrics. Then create a SaaS metrics analysis: core SaaS metrics explained (ARR (Annual Recurring Revenue): predictable annualized revenue from subscriptions. MRR: monthly version. Track both. NRR (Net Revenue Retention): revenue from existing customers (including expansion) minus churn and contraction, divided by starting revenue. Best single measure of product-market fit. Above 120% = exceptional, 100-120% = good, below 100% = leaking. Churn rate: customers lost / total customers. Monthly churn of 3-5% is typical for SMB, 1-2% for mid-market, under 1% for enterprise. Annual churn is more meaningful for annual contracts. LTV (Customer Lifetime Value): average revenue per customer / churn rate. Should be 3x+ your CAC. CAC (Customer Acquisition Cost): total sales and marketing cost / new customers acquired. Payback period: CAC / MRR per customer. Should be under 12 months. Magic number: (new ARR in quarter / prior quarter sales & marketing spend) x 4. Above 1.0 = efficient growth, 0.5-1.0 = OK, below 0.5 = inefficient); metric relationships (how CS metrics connect: churn increases → NRR decreases → LTV decreases → payback period increases → less budget for growth. Conversely: health score improves → churn decreases → NRR increases → more budget for growth. Healthy CS operation creates a virtuous cycle: better retention → higher LTV → more growth budget → better product → better retention. Unhealthy CS creates a vicious cycle: higher churn → lower LTV → less growth budget → worse product → higher churn); benchmark comparison (common SaaS benchmarks by ARR tier: sub $5M ARR: NRR 100-120%, monthly churn 3-5%, LTV:CAC 3:1, payback 12-18 months. $5-20M ARR: NRR 110-130%, monthly churn 1-3%, LTV:CAC 5:1, payback 6-12 months. $20M+ ARR: NRR 120%+, monthly churn under 1%, LTV:CAC 8:1+, payback under 6 months. These are benchmarks, not targets. Your specific numbers depend on your business model, market, and segment); leading vs. lagging metrics (lagging metrics (what happened): churn rate, NRR, LTV, revenue. Leading metrics (what will happen): health score trends, usage trends, engagement metrics, NPS trend, support ticket trend, expansion pipeline, renewal at-risk %, time-to-value. Focus CS team on leading indicators — they can influence these today. Lagging indicators are tomorrow's report card); creating a SaaS metrics dashboard (recommended dashboard: top row — NRR, churn rate, LTV, health score distribution. Second row — NRR trend by segment, churn reason breakdown, expansion revenue pipeline, feature adoption rates. Third row — CSM performance: book health, QBR completion, response times, satisfaction scores. Refresh weekly. Share with executive team monthly. Use data to drive decisions, not just report them). Provide a SaaS metrics glossary with formulas.
**Expected output:** A SaaS metrics deep dive with core metrics explained, metric relationships, benchmarks, leading indicators, and dashboard design
**Pro tip:** The single most important SaaS metric for CS is NET REVENUE RETENTION (NRR). It tells you whether your customer base is growing or shrinking in value. An NRR above 100% means your existing customers are spending more over time — that's the hallmark of a great product and great CS. Below 100% means you're on a treadmill where new customers are needed just to stay flat. Every CS initiative should ultimately impact NRR. If it doesn't move NRR, question whether it's worth doing

### 6.23 Customer Success for Product-Led Growth
**Use case:** Adapt customer success practices for a product-led growth business model
**Best for:** CS leaders in PLG companies, growth teams, product managers
Act as a PLG customer success specialist. I need to design CS for a product-led growth company. Tell me about your product, whether it's free/freemium/paid, and how users typically discover it. Then create a PLG CS strategy: PLG customer journey differences (in PLG: users try before they buy, value must be delivered before payment, the product itself drives acquisition, retention, and expansion. The CS role shifts from "managing accounts" to "accelerating value" across a large, often anonymous user base. Key differences: CS engages later in the journey (after self-serve has run its course), digital-touch is primary, human interaction is reserved for high-value accounts or critical moments, the product experience IS the primary CS channel); digital-led engagement (build value delivery into the product: in-app guidance (tooltips, checklists, walkthroughs), milestone celebrations (congratulatory messages when users achieve key actions), triggered education (offer help when users get stuck or hit new features), feature announcements (what's new and why it matters), usage-based tips ("you've been using X a lot — have you tried Y?"), upgrade prompts at value moments ("you've created 3 campaigns — upgrade to unlock advanced targeting"). The product should guide users from signup to value to upgrade without human intervention); CS interventions in PLG (human CS should intervene at specific moments: user requests help or contact, account reaches high usage/value threshold and is hand-raised to sales, account shows expansion potential (multiple team members, approaching limits), account shows churn risk (usage decline, no login), account is a named enterprise target, user is a potential champion or advocate. Create escalation triggers that auto-assign CS or sales based on user behavior and value); monetization and expansion in PLG (freemium to paid conversion: identify the "aha moment" that correlates with conversion, guide free users toward this moment, offer upgrade at the moment of maximum value. Self-serve to sales-assisted: define criteria for hand-raise (account size, usage patterns, feature requests, company profile), create a smooth transition from self-serve to human touch, ensure the handoff adds value, not friction. Expansion: in-app upsell prompts at value moments, automated outreach when approaching plan limits, CS outreach for high-potential accounts); metrics for PLG CS (track: activation rate (% that reach aha moment), time to aha moment, free-to-paid conversion rate, self-serve vs. sales-assisted revenue split, expansion rate from self-serve vs. assisted accounts, NPS by engagement model (digital vs human), feature adoption rates across all users, time from signup to first human touch). Provide a PLG CS intervention trigger framework.
**Expected output:** A PLG customer success strategy covering digital-led engagement, CS interventions, monetization, and PLG-specific metrics
**Pro tip:** The biggest mistake in PLG CS is treating it like traditional CS. In PLG, the product IS the primary engagement channel. Your in-app experience needs to deliver value, guide adoption, and identify expansion opportunities WITHOUT human intervention. Human CS in PLG should be a scalpel — used sparingly and precisely at the moments that matter most. If you need a CSM for every customer in a PLG model, you don't have a PLG model — you have a sales-led model with a free trial

### 6.24 Customer Success for Enterprise Accounts
**Use case:** Design a customer success approach for enterprise accounts
**Best for:** Enterprise CSMs, CS leadership, account management
Act as an enterprise customer success specialist. I need to manage and grow enterprise accounts. Tell me about your enterprise customers, their contract values, number of stakeholders, and typical engagement model. Then create an enterprise CS approach: enterprise account characteristics (key differences from SMB/mid-market: longer sales cycles (6-18 months), multiple stakeholders (5-20+ people involved in decision), higher contract value ($50K-$1M+ ARR), complex implementations (3-12 months), stringent security/compliance requirements, executive-level relationships needed, lower tolerance for issues, higher expectations for support, longer retention (3-5+ years average), higher expansion potential (2-5x initial contract over time). Enterprise CS requires a different skill set and engagement model than SMB or mid-market); enterprise engagement model (dedicated CSM (often called an "Account Executive" or "Customer Success Director") owns the relationship. Engagement cadence: weekly check-ins during onboarding/implementation phase; monthly operational reviews (usage, health, support, project status); quarterly business reviews (strategic, executive-level value discussion); annual executive summit (multi-year planning, partnership review). In addition: ad-hoc engagement based on triggers (issues, expansion opportunities, risks). The enterprise CSM should know every key stakeholder by name and understand their individual goals and concerns); executive stakeholder management (map all stakeholders at the customer: economic buyer (CFO, VP) — cares about ROI, business outcomes. Decision-maker (VP, Director) — cares about team productivity, operational efficiency. Champion (manager, IC) — cares about day-to-day use, features, support. Technical buyer (CTO, IT) — cares about security, integration, compliance. Users (individuals) — cares about ease of use, reliability. For each stakeholder, document: their role in the decision, their key priorities, their level of engagement, their satisfaction, what they need from you. Engage each at the appropriate level — you don't have weekly calls with all 20 stakeholders, but you should have a relationship with each); enterprise value realization (create a formal value realization program: at onboarding, establish baseline metrics (current state before your product). At each QBR, measure progress against baseline (quantified ROI, time saved, revenue impact). At renewal, present cumulative value delivered (total ROI over the partnership. Create a value deck that the customer's executive sponsor can use to justify continued investment internally. Enterprise customers need to justify your cost to THEIR leadership. Give them the ammunition); expansion strategy for enterprise (identify expansion opportunities: new use cases within current departments, expansion to new departments, upsell to higher tier, cross-sell to other products, global/regional expansion, additional seats/licenses. Map expansion opportunities for each enterprise account. Track in a "whitespace" analysis. Create a 12-month expansion plan for each key account). Provide an enterprise account planning template.
**Expected output:** An enterprise customer success strategy covering engagement model, stakeholder management, value realization, and expansion
**Pro tip:** Enterprise CS is about relationships, not processes. Your process should support the relationship, not replace it. The best enterprise CSMs know their customer's business as well as their own. They know the customer's goals, challenges, politics, and priorities. They can have a strategic conversation about business outcomes, not just product features. They are trusted advisors — and trust is earned through consistent, reliable value delivery over time. Enterprise customers don't buy software, they buy outcomes. Make sure you're delivering outcomes, not just managing accounts

### 6.25 Customer Success for Early-Stage Startups
**Use case:** Build customer success from scratch at an early-stage startup
**Best for:** Startup founders, early CS hires, startup operators
Act as a startup CS advisor. You're an early-stage startup (pre-product-market fit to $1M ARR) building your customer success practice from scratch. Tell me about your product, current customer count, team, and biggest challenge. Then create a startup CS blueprint: CS before you have a CS team (as a founder or early employee, YOU are the CS team. Every customer interaction is product research, customer research, and relationship building. In early stage: respond to every support request personally within 1 hour, call every customer who churns within 24 hours, log every feature request in a shared doc, use customer calls to validate your product direction, build relationships with your first 10-20 customers. Your first customers will tell you what to build — listen to them); when to hire your first CS hire (signs you need dedicated CS: you have 20+ paying customers and growing, support requests are pulling you away from product development, customers are churning and you don't know why, you have data but no time to analyze it, you're missing expansion opportunities because you're too busy putting out fires. First CS hire should be: customer-obsessed, comfortable with ambiguity, able to build processes from scratch, willing to do whatever it takes (support, onboarding, QBRs, renewals). Hire for aptitude and attitude over experience — startup CS is different from enterprise CS); building CS processes from scratch (start with the minimum viable CS: health score — simple red/yellow/green based on login frequency and support tickets (spreadsheet is fine); onboarding — welcome email + 30-minute setup call + day 7 check-in (calendar link + email template). Support — respond within 4 hours, use a shared inbox or simple ticketing tool. Feedback loop — capture every feature request in a shared doc, review weekly with product. Renewal — 30-day reminder email before subscription ends. That's it. Add process only when you need it, not before); metrics that matter for startup CS (don't track 20 metrics. Track 5: activation rate (% who reach value within 7 days), weekly active users (are they coming back?), support response time (within 4 hours is good), NPS (score + verbatim comments = gold), churn reason (one sentence per churned customer). Everything else is noise at this stage. Review these metrics weekly. When something changes, ask why); founder-led CS mindset (for the first 50-100 customers, the founder should be deeply involved in CS. Benefits: you'll understand your customers' problems intimately, you'll build the product they actually need, you'll create raving fans who become your best salespeople, you'll know when to hire your first CS person, you'll set the customer-obsessed culture for the company). Include a startup CS checklist with what to do at each stage (0-10 customers, 10-50, 50-100, 100+).
**Expected output:** A startup CS blueprint with pre-hire CS, hiring triggers, process building, key metrics, and founder-led mindset
**Pro tip:** For early-stage startups, customer retention is more important than customer acquisition. A churning customer is worse than no customer — they cost you acquisition effort AND tell others not to buy. Spend as much energy on your first 10 customers being successful as you do on acquiring the next 10. The best early CS strategy: over-deliver on value, respond instantly to issues, and build personal relationships. Your first customers should feel like partners, not transactions
